# Prologue: The Genealogy of the Gods

## Ayamde — In the Beginning

Before there was stone — there was the Word. Before the Word — there was Silence. And in this Silence, in a depth that cannot be measured by fathom or by age, dwelt **Gmerthi** — the One, the Nameless, He Who Is.

He did not create the world. He breathed it out.

And when the breath of Gmerthi touched the darkness, the darkness parted — and there appeared the sky, green with primordial freshness, and the earth, dark from rains not yet shed, and between them — mountains. Always mountains. For mountains are the ribs of the earth, and without them it would crumble into the sea, as flour spills from an unclenched palm.

Thus the Caucasus arose.

---

The first among the children of Gmerthi was **Armazi** — and he was fashioned from copper and thunder, from the gleam of mountain ice and the sound of a broken tree. Armazi stood over Mtskheta — a guardian at the confluence of rivers, where the Aragvi flows into the Kura — and his eyes burned like emeralds, and in his hands flashed a sword that needed no blow to strike: a glance was enough.

Armazi was the god of the sky, war, and order. Beneath him — but not below him — stood **Zaden**, the god of fertility, he who makes grain sprout beneath the snow and knows when the grape is sweet and when it is bitter. Zaden did not wage war. He only looked upon the earth with love — and that was enough.

---

From another substance — from mountain mist and lunar silver — was created **Dali**.

She lived where the forest ends and the rock begins. In Svaneti they called her Dali. In Samegrelo — Tkashi-Mapa, Queen of the Forests. Everywhere she was the same: beautiful to the point where a hunter, meeting her gaze, would forget the way home; generous to the point where a mountain ibex would walk out to meet him; dangerous to the point where that same ibex could be the last thing he ever saw.

Dali's hair was golden — not because it was beautiful, but because in the mountains, when the first ray touches the snow, it is precisely that color. She lived in a golden cave at the summit, reached by a single path — and that path is erased after the first snowfall.

Dali took mortal lovers. This was not cruelty — it was her nature, as it is the nature of fire to burn. She demanded one thing: fidelity. Not physical — spiritual. The hunter whom she loved could not boast her name. Could not take more game than he could carry. Could not utter her name aloud among people. For Dali was a secret — and a secret told ceases to be a secret.

Those who betrayed her died in the mountains. It is said their bones still lie on some passes.

---

The son of Gmerthi — **Kopala** — was created for war with the darkness.

The *Devi* came into the world before humans. Not evil by birth — but alien, strange beings, an anti-world, the reverse side of creation. They lived in caves, stole cattle, sent diseases, confused roads, and muddled the count of the stars. Gmerthi watched this for a long time — with a patience incomprehensible to the mortal mind, because mortals do not know what eternity is.

Then he sent Kopala.

Kopala received his weapon from the smith-god **Pirkushi** — an iron bow whose string rang like lightning a second before the strike. Beside him walked **Iakhsari** — the strongest of the heavenly warriors, he whose voice in the mountains people still hear in the roll of thunder. Together they traversed the Caucasus from Adjara to Khevsureti, driving the *devi* from caves and gorges, cleansing the earth for humans.

The mountains remember this war. That is how it differs from the wars of men.

---

There was also fire.

His name was **Pirmet** — "Fire-Life" in a language older than all languages. He lived in the sky, among the stars, among the gods — and was their property. But people sat in darkness and froze, and cooked meat by holding it over the weak breath of animal blood.

Then came **Amiran**.

Amiran was a demigod — born of a mortal woman and the world of the gods. Some say his mother was Dali, the goddess of the mountains. Others — that she was a simple huntress. But all versions share one thing: Amiran could not see human suffering and remain apart. This is what destroyed him. This is what glorified him.

He stole the fire. He gave it to people. He taught them to forge iron — and with this single deed changed the fate of millennia: for a people who know how to forge will not submit to those who do not.

And for this he was chained to Mount **Khvamli**.

---

Khvamli is not just a peak. It is a place where the sky touches the earth — where the rock rises vertically for hundreds of meters, where eagles do not fly into the caves because they fear something inside. Amiran lies there to this day. His dog Kursha licks his chains — slowly, year after year, and has almost gnawed through them. But every year, on New Year's or on Maundy Thursday, blacksmiths strike their anvils — and the chains grow anew.

A bitter irony: the disciples of Amiran, the people to whom he gave the craft of smithing, with their own hands keep him captive.

---

**MZE** — the Sun — is feminine in the Georgian language. This is no accident: the sun gives life, it does not take it. **MTVARE** — the Moon — is masculine, pale and changeable, now waning, now waxing, inconstant as all things masculine. This pair is not the Greek Apollo and Artemis, nor the Egyptian Ra and Thoth. It is a different logic of the universe: in it, life belongs to the feminine principle, and the count of time to the masculine.

The twins **Gatsi and Gaim** maintained the balance between worlds. The sisters **Ainina and Danina** guarded the thresholds of homes and women in childbirth in their hour of need.

---

There was another layer — a dark one.

**Achopintre** — the spirit of the forest, chaos, that which lives between the trees when the forest becomes too dense for a human path. Not evil — but alien. The forest was never a friend to man in Georgian mythology: it was a trial.

**Kaji** — demons of the deep sea of Colchis. There were two kinds: land-dwelling, malevolent ones, and water-dwelling ones, strangely friendly to humans. The *Kaji* knew metallurgy — they were the first smiths of darkness, before Amiran. Perhaps that is precisely why Amiran defeated them: he took their knowledge and gave it to people.

And finally — the line of **Aeëtes and Medea**. Aeëtes, King of Colchis, was the son of Helios, the Sun. This made him a demigod — closer to the sky than any mortal king. His daughter Medea was a *pharmakís* — a witch, a physician, a sorceress, whose pharmacy stretched from the alpine meadows of Khvamli to the shores of the Black Sea. In the veins of this family flowed the blood of gods, mixed with the blood of the earth — and this mixture gave power over both life and death.

---

Here they are — the gods of Georgia. Not abstractions, not allegories. Concrete beings with concrete characters: Armazi is harsh and just; Dali is beautiful and deadly; Kopala is fearless; Amiran is compassionate to the point of ruin; Mze is warm; Mtvare is cold; Achopintre is unpredictable; the Kaji are ambiguous.

This is not Olympus with its marble facades and ceremonial myths. This is a mountain pantheon — living, dangerous, real.

And above all this — quiet, invisible, unutterable — Gmerthi. The One. He Who Is.

This book is about what happened when people lived alongside these gods. About what they built. About what they left in stone, in caves, in the count of the stars. And about a mountain — one mountain — that remembers everything.

Its name is Khvamli.

---

*The book begins.*

---

# Chapter 1. In the Shadow of Great Civilizations

There are maps that lie not by what they show, but by what they do not show.

Open any popular atlas of the ancient world. Here is Egypt — with pyramids, pharaohs, the Nile. Here is Mesopotamia — with ziggurats, cuneiform, Gilgamesh. Here is Greece — gods, philosophers, tragedies. Somewhere on the edge of the map, in the upper right corner, where the artist grew tired of drawing — a thin strip between the sea and the mountains. A caption in small print: "Colchis" or "Iberia." Sometimes simply — "barbarians."

This is how Georgia looks on most maps of the ancient world.

Periphery. Buffer. Transit territory.

I grew up with these maps. I studied from textbooks where the Caucasus was mentioned exclusively as an obstacle — a mountain barrier separating "real" civilizations. And for a long time, I, a physician and researcher, accepted this picture as a given. Until I began to ask questions.

---

## A Paradox in Plain Sight

Allow me to pose a simple question, one I long had no answer for.

Prometheus — one of the most important myths of Western civilization. The thief of fire, the giver of knowledge, the sufferer chained to a rock. Aeschylus wrote a tragedy about him. Shelley — a poem. Goethe — a drama. Beethoven — music. Hundreds of artists have depicted his chained figure. The motif of Prometheus permeates all of European culture from antiquity to the present day.

But here is what is strange: the Greeks themselves said this myth was not Greek. It came from the east. From the Caucasus. Prometheus is chained to a "Caucasian rock" — it is stated directly in the sources.

The Golden Fleece. The Quest of the Argonauts — one of the oldest and most enduring myths of the Greek world. Jason, the ship *Argo*, Medea, the unsleeping dragon. This myth formed the basis of Apollonius Rhodius's *Argonautica* — a text read and copied for twenty centuries. Medea became a symbol of passion, witchcraft, and female tragedy: Euripides, Seneca, Corneille, Grillparzer, Anouilh — all wrote about her.
But the action takes place in Colchis. In Georgia.

Medea is a Colchian. Aeëtes is the king of Colchis. The Golden Fleece is kept in Colchis, in the grove of Ares, on the banks of the river Phasis—a river we now call the Rioni.

Now imagine that almost all of ancient Greek theater is written about events taking place in Russia or Iran, while in those very countries these plots are considered foreign. Absurd? This is precisely what is happening with Georgia.

---

## Why Have Historians Overlooked This?

This is not a conspiracy theory or the grievance of a small country. It is a methodological problem with a perfectly rational explanation.

**First reason: The colonial lens of the 19th century.**

Academic history as a discipline was formed in the 19th century in Western Europe. German, French, and British scholars created the paradigm of "great civilizations"—Egypt, Greece, Rome, Mesopotamia. Everything else was peripheral by definition: not because it was so in reality, but because there was no money, no expeditions, no political interest to study the Caucasus seriously.

Marie-Félicité Brosset—a brilliant French orientalist who in the 19th century undertook the translation of Georgian chronicles—was an exception, almost an anomaly. His works remained unclaimed for decades: there was no one to read them, no school, no successors.

**Second reason: The language barrier.**

The Georgian language is not Indo-European. It is Kartvelian—belonging to a separate language family, unrelated to Greek, Latin, or Arabic. For a European scholar of the 19th–20th centuries, entering the Georgian mythological tradition meant mastering not just a new language, but a completely different system of thinking, a different lexicon of the sacred.

Georgian texts remained closed to the outside world not because they were hidden, but because no one could enter that door.

**Third reason: The Soviet period.**

Seventy years of Soviet rule in the Caucasus meant that part of the scientific data was classified, part was adapted to Marxist methodology, and part simply was not published in languages accessible to the global scientific community. We will return to this—in the story of the 1948–1949 expedition to Khvamli. But it is already important to understand: the Soviet period created a vast archive of knowledge, parts of which remain closed to this day.

**Fourth reason: The stratigraphy of myth.**

Georgian mythology is not a single canonical text like the *Iliad* or the Bible. It is a living tradition that existed in oral circulation in different regions of the country, in different dialects, with regional variations. Researchers have been recording it since the 19th century, but systematization remained incomplete. To understand the Georgian pantheon, one had to compare Svan texts with Mingrelian, Khevsureti with Kakhetian—and within this layering, it was easy to lose the main thread.

---

## What Lies Behind the "Periphery"

Let's count.

Prometheus—a Caucasian myth assimilated by Greek literature. Greek sources themselves acknowledge this: the "Caucasian rock" is not a metaphor but a geographical indication.

The Golden Fleece and the voyage of the Argonauts—a story unfolding in Colchis, modern western Georgia. Strabo in his *Geography* (1st century BC) locates it quite specifically: the river Phasis, the Caucasus mountains, the capital at the foot of the mountains.

Medea—a Colchian princess, priestess of Hecate, possessor of pharmacological knowledge that Ovid describes with the precision of a physician (*Metamorphoses*, Book VII). Her rejuvenation of Aeson is not a fairy tale about magic; it is a description of a real practice with real plant ingredients gathered "in the Caucasus mountains."

The Nart epic—one of the world's largest epic cycles, giving rise to variants among Ossetians, Abkhazians, Adyghes, Chechens, and Ingush. It intersects with Georgian legends about Amiran in dozens of plot points. This epic is a Caucasian phenomenon, and its scale is comparable to the Germanic or Scandinavian ones.

Metallurgy. The Caucasus is one of the world's oldest centers of metalworking. The bronze culture that emerged here in the 4th–3rd millennia BC was so advanced that its artifacts are found throughout the Near East. The "people of iron" in myths is not a metaphor. It is a technological fact.

Putting it all together, we get a picture that is difficult to call peripheral. This is—one of the sources.

---

## The Problem of Self-Perception

There is another, subtler factor that is spoken of less often. This is—the historical self-perception of the Georgians themselves.

Georgia is a small country. For centuries, it existed under the pressure of huge neighbors: Persia, Byzantium, the Mongols, the Ottoman Empire, Russia. This history of constant survival formed a certain cultural reflex: to value what was managed to be preserved (language, writing, Orthodoxy), but not to claim a place in world history. The tactic of survival—is modesty.

But the modesty of a survivor is a poor tool for historical science.

I am a Georgian doctor. I practice integrative medicine, meaning the combination of different traditions, different knowledges. And I have long noticed: in medicine, as in history, the most dangerous mistake is to overlook a symptom simply because it does not fit the expected picture. "This cannot be"—is the worst diagnostic argument.

When I began to look at Khvamli—one specific mountain in Imereti—and ask questions, the symptoms began to accumulate with unexpected speed. Astronomical data recorded by Prince Vakhushti in the 18th century. The secret Soviet expedition of 1948 with the order to "present the report personally to Comrade Stalin." Petroglyphs that scholars cautiously date to the 6th–5th millennia BC. Jules Verne, who wrote in 1883 about "the rock of Khomli, to which Prometheus was chained." The etymology of the name, rooted in concepts of light and radiance.

Each of these facts individually is a curious detail. Together, they form a question that cannot be ignored.

---

## The Caucasus as a Generator, Not a Corridor

The standard view of the Caucasus in history: a corridor between civilizations. A route from Eurasia to the Near East. A place through which traders, conquerors, and ideas passed.

I propose a different model: the Caucasus as a generator of civilization.

Not a passive corridor—an active source. Not a place through which ideas pass—a place where ideas are born.

I will gather evidence for this model throughout the book. But already now, in the first chapter, I want to point out a paradox that has haunted me for years: why does Western civilization readily acknowledge the Greek origin of myths that the Greeks themselves called Caucasian?

The answer—most likely—is that the Greeks had writing. They had Homer, Hesiod, Aeschylus, Apollonius. Their texts were preserved, translated, studied. The Georgian tradition—oral until the 5th century AD—remained invisible to that part of the world which could only read what was written in a familiar language.

But oral tradition is not a weakness. It is another way of storing knowledge. A way that is sometimes more reliable than writing: books burn, manuscripts are lost, libraries are destroyed. But what lives in the memory of a singer, in the ritual of a blacksmith, in the name of a mountain—holds on more firmly.

---

## What Lies Ahead

This book is a detective story. But a detective story of a special kind: with real clues, real places, and questions whose answers have not yet been found.

We will begin with a mountain.

One specific mountain in Imereti. Height—2194 meters. A double cuesta—a geological formation that makes it resemble a natural fortress. Vertical cliffs. Caves with petroglyphs. Underground lakes. A secret Soviet expedition. Astronomical data. And a name in which something is encoded that was known even before writing appeared.

Its name is Khvamli.

I invite you there.

---

*Continuation—in the next chapter.*

---

# Chapter 2. The Khvamli Phenomenon

There are places that choose people themselves.

Not the other way around. It is not that a person comes to a mountain with the intention of making it sacred—the mountain does this itself, by its form, its position in space, its ability to create a certain field of attention around itself. Khvamli is precisely such a place.

Before speaking of myths and gods, before opening archives and quoting chronicles—one must see the mountain. Because everything else is secondary: people called it sacred not arbitrarily, not because they read about it in a book, but because the mountain made it inevitable.

---

## The Double Cuesta

In geology, there is a term that is almost never used in everyday speech but very accurately describes what is before you: **cuesta**.

A cuesta is an asymmetrical mountain ridge with one gentle slope and one steep, cliff-like slope. It forms where layers of rock lie not horizontally but at an angle, and harder rocks protect softer ones from erosion. The result is not just a mountain, but a form with a pronounced "front" side: from one side you can ascend, from the other—a vertical cliff before which even an experienced climber freezes.

Khvamli is a **double cuesta**. Not one asymmetrical form, but two, mirroring each other. Between them—a plateau-like summit, wide enough for a person to live and work on. On the outside—from all sides—vertical walls, in places exceeding three hundred meters. No army could take this mountain by storm. No enemy could climb onto the plateau without the knowledge of those at the top.
Nature created the perfect fortress.

But not only a fortress.

The flat summit, towering above the Rioni valley, is open to the sky on all sides. There is not a single other peak that blocks the horizon. This means that from the summit of Khvamli, one can observe the full horizon—from sunrise to sunset, from north to south—without any optical interference. Any object appearing on the horizon: a star, planet, comet, the sun on a specific day of the year—is visible from here with perfect precision.

Nature created the perfect observatory.

A fortress plus an observatory—this is no longer just a mountain. It is an institution. A place where one can store knowledge and the people who carry it, while remaining safe from those who would wish to destroy that knowledge.

---

## Geology: What Happened Here

The geological history of Khvamli begins about one hundred and fifty million years ago, in the Jurassic period, when a shallow sea stretched across the territory of western Georgia. Layer by layer, limestones settled—the primary rock that constitutes the mountain. The limestones of Khvamli are special: dense, light-colored, crystalline. On a sunny day, they reflect light with such intensity that the mountain literally glows—visible for tens of kilometers.

Then the sea receded. The Caucasus Mountains began to rise—a process that continues to this day at a rate of several millimeters per year. The rock strata, which lay horizontally on the seabed, began to tilt, crack, and shift. This is precisely how the asymmetry of the cuesta formed: the harder limestone layers created a steep cliff on one side, while the softer rocks were gradually eroded on the other.

But the most important event occurred later—when water began to dissolve the limestone from within.

Khvamli is a karst mountain. This means that inside it lies an extremely complex system of cavities, caves, underground rivers, and lakes. Rainwater, seeping through cracks in the limestone, dissolved the rock for millennia, creating corridors, halls, and vertical shafts. Some caves—in particular, Tekenteli and Boga—descend hundreds of meters deep and contain underground reservoirs of the purest water.

The vertical caves of Khvamli are natural refrigerators: the temperature inside is constant, around ten degrees Celsius, regardless of the season. Humidity is high. Without access to light, without exposure to oxygen—what enters inside is preserved. For millennia. This is what those who lived here utilized.

---

## What "Khvamli" Means

I am a physician, and I have always been interested in the names of diseases: each name carries a history, sometimes longer than the history of the disease itself. The same is true for toponyms.

"Khvamli" is a word that resists simple explanations.

Sulkhan-Saba Orbeliani, a Georgian lexicographer of the 17th–18th centuries who compiled the first Georgian explanatory dictionary, recorded the root **khvam-** in meanings related to light, radiance, brilliance. Not just light as a physical phenomenon—but light as a guide, a beacon, a signal, something by which one orients oneself in space.

This is important in the context of what we know about the mountain. The white limestone walls of Khvamli, reflecting the sun, are a natural beacon, visible from a great distance in the Rioni valley. On a clear day, a traveler in Kutaisi could navigate by this glowing summit in the west as confidently as by a lighthouse at sea.

The root *khvam-* can be traced in a number of other Georgian words related to concepts of brightness, manifestation, visibility. Some researchers cautiously connect it with Indo-European roots related to light: Avestan *xvar-* (sun), Persian *xorshid* (sun). This is a linguistic hypothesis requiring caution—but it is not without meaning in the context of astral cults, which will be discussed later.

There is another etymological line. In Mingrelian—a language that has preserved a significant layer of archaic vocabulary from the Colchian substrate—there is a word related to the concept of "a place of observation," "a lookout point." If this kinship is real, then the mountain's name translates not simply as "shining" or "bright"—but as "that from which one looks" or "that at which one looks." A dual meaning: a mountain-beacon and a mountain-observatory in one word.

A name as a program—this happens with places that have long lived in human memory.

---

## Vakhushti and the Star Khomli

In the 18th century, Prince Vakhushti Bagrationi—a Georgian geographer and historian, one of the most educated men of his time—compiled a detailed description of Georgia. His "Atlas of the Georgian Kingdom" and "Description of the Georgian Kingdom" remain to this day among the most important sources for the historical geography of the country.

In this text, Vakhushti mentions something astonishing. He writes that Mount Khomli (as he calls Khvamli—an older form of the toponym) is identified with the planet **Venus**. Not compared to it—precisely identified. The mountain and the star bear the same name, the same function, the same cult.

The French orientalist Marie-Félicité Brosset, who worked with Georgian manuscripts in the 19th century, paid close attention to this. In his translations and commentaries, he recorded this identification as something requiring explanation—but he never offered a full explanation. The time, tools, context—all of this was still lacking in the 19th century.

Today we can say more.

Venus is not simply the "evening star." In the astral theology of the ancient Near East, Venus held a special place: it was the only celestial body (besides the Moon) visible in both the evening sky (Hesperus to the Greeks) and the morning sky (Phosphorus/Lucifer). This made it a symbol of transition—between night and day, between death and rebirth, between darkness and light.

In the Sumerian tradition, Venus was Inanna—the goddess of war and love, who descends into the underworld and returns from there renewed. In the Babylonian—Ishtar. In the Canaanite—Astarte. In each tradition: transition, cycle, rebirth.

If Khvamli was identified with Venus—this is not a poetic comparison. It was a precise religious program. The mountain was a place where the rising of Venus was observed. Where the points of its cycle were marked. Where rituals related to transition were conducted—perhaps between seasons, perhaps between life and death, perhaps between the earthly and the celestial.

Researcher Gognashvili, studying the orientation of geological and man-made objects on the Khvamli plateau, discovered correspondences that cannot be coincidental: certain rock outcrops and niches are oriented in such a way that their use as astronomical markers would have been entirely possible. The synodic cycle of Venus—584 days—requires multi-year systematic observations. Such observations are only possible in a permanent, protected, specially equipped place.

The double cuesta—an ideal natural fortress.
White limestone walls—a beacon.
The open horizon on the summit—an observatory.
Vertical caves—repositories of knowledge and relics.

All of this is not a series of random coincidences. It is a functional system.

---

## The Mountain Above the River

There is another aspect often overlooked: Khvamli's position in the landscape.

The mountain stands above the Rioni valley—the main river of western Georgia, the very one ancient authors called the Phasis. From the summit of Khvamli, the valley is visible as if in the palm of one's hand: the meanders of the river, the glint of water, the floodplain meadows, and somewhere there, on the horizon—the Black Sea.

In the Neolithic and Bronze Age, according to paleogeographers, the water level in the Rioni was significantly higher than today. During spring floods, water covered a substantial part of the valley—and Khvamli rose above this watery plain like an island. An island in the literal sense.

The image of an island rising from primordial waters is one of the universal cosmogonic images. The primordial mound (Benben) of Egyptian cosmogony, rising from the waters of Nun. The Sumerian Abzu—the primeval ocean from which the land emerges. In each tradition: water as chaos, the mountain as the first act of creation.

Khvamli could have been such a place—real, physical, in the perception of Neolithic people who saw it exactly like that: a mountain above water, the first point of solidity in a world of flood.

This is not an arbitrary interpretation. This is what people saw.

---

## The Riddle That Waits

I stand before Khvamli and think about layers.

The first layer—geological: one hundred and fifty million years of limestone, water, plate movement.

The second layer—ecological: the specifics of the microclimate, the unique biodiversity of the alpine zone above a subtropical valley, plants that grow only here.

The third layer—astronomical: observations of Venus, the Sun, the starry sky from the open summit.

The fourth layer—mythological: Amiran chained in a cave, Aeëtes ruling Colchis from this mountain, Prometheus looking from this cliff at the Caucasus.

The fifth layer—historical: a secret Soviet expedition, a classified report, an order to Stalin.

And beneath all these layers—petroglyphs. Drawings, scratched into the stone five to six thousand years ago. People by a pillar. Hunters and beasts. Symbols whose meaning we have not yet deciphered.

Khvamli is not just a mountain. It is an archive, created by those who understood: the most reliable repository is one that cannot be destroyed without destroying everything else.

The stone remembers.

---

*Next chapter: The Argonauts in Colchis.*

---

# Chapter 3. Myth Becomes Geography: The Argonauts in Colchis

Around 250 BC in Alexandria—a city that was then the world center of scholarship—a young poet and scholar named Apollonius wrote the "Argonautica."

This was not improvisation. Apollonius worked as a historian: he collected sources, compared versions, studied geographical descriptions. Behind him stood the Library of Alexandria—the largest archive of knowledge in the ancient world. He knew about Colchis what was known to an educated Greek of the 3rd century BC: oral traditions, earlier poetic versions of the myth, reports from merchants and travelers who had been to the shores of the Pontus Euxinus.
We open the *Argonautica* and read: Jason and his companions sail to the mouth of the river Phasis. They sail upstream. Ahead are the Caucasus Mountains. To the left and right are shores overgrown with forest. Somewhere there, in the depths of the country, is the capital of King Aeëtes.

Phasis is the Rioni. The Caucasus Mountains are the mountains visible from Kutaisi to this day. The land of Aeëtes is Colchis.

This is not a metaphor. This is Georgia.

---

## Strabo as a Witness

If Apollonius is a poet, then Strabo is a scholar. A geographer and historian who lived in the 1st century BC – 1st century AD, he compiled the *Geography* — a monumental work in seventeen books, describing the world known to him with the meticulousness of a researcher.

Strabo writes about Colchis in the eleventh book. And here is where it gets most interesting: he does not merely retell the myth of the Argonauts — he tries to explain it.

According to Strabo, the Argonauts sought wealth in Colchis. Real wealth, not fairy-tale. He mentions the practice of gold panning: the inhabitants used sheepskins, dipping them into mountain rivers — the finest grains of gold settled in the fleece. This is the "Golden Fleece" in the most direct, practical sense.

But Strabo does not stop there. He describes Colchis as a rich country — with fertile lands, dense forests, rivers full of fish, with access to the sea and trade routes leading deep into the Caucasus. In his description, it is not the edge of the *oikoumene*. It is a country worth sailing across the entire Mediterranean for.

And most importantly: Strabo localizes everything very specifically. The river Phasis is the border between Asia and Europe in his system. The capital of Aeëtes is at the mountains. The sacred grove with the fleece — somewhere in the depths of the country, in the mountains.

In the mountains of Imereti. At the foot of Khvamli.

---

## Who is Aeëtes

Aeëtes — the king of Colchis — holds a special place in Greek mythology. He is the son of Helios and the Oceanid Perseis. His sister is Circe, the very sorceress from the island of Aeaea, where Odysseus ended up after the fall of Troy. His daughter is Medea.

This family is a semi-divine line descending from the Sun.

In Greek logic, this is important: son of the Sun means a bearer of solar knowledge. Solar knowledge is astronomy, the calendar, prediction of seasons, understanding of time. In prehistoric cultures, this was the most valuable of practical knowledge: knowing when to sow, when to expect floods, when summer begins — meant the difference between harvest and famine.

But in the Georgian tradition, Aeëtes appears somewhat differently. In *Kartlis Tskhovreba* — the principal Georgian chronicle — there are mentions of kings of Colchis who ruled at the foot of the sacred mountain. The connection to the Sun in these texts is not merely metaphorical — it is ritualistic. The king ruling under the sacred mountain derived his legitimacy from it, just as the Egyptian pharaoh derived his from Ra.

The sacred mountain as a source of royal power is not a Georgian specificity. It is a universal structure found in dozens of cultures. But in the case of Colchis, this structure has a specific address.

---

## Where Was the Palace of Aeëtes Located

This question has occupied scholars for centuries. Let's try to figure it out.

The *Argonautica* describes the route quite specifically: The Argonauts enter the mouth of the Phasis (the mouth of the Rioni, modern-day Poti), sail upstream, pass several days of sailing, then see "mountains in the distance." The capital of Aeëtes is at the confluence of two rivers, in the shadow of the mountains.

Kutaisi stands precisely there: in the place where the Rioni valley narrows, the mountains come close, and on a clear day, the peak of Khvamli is visible from the city's hills to the west. The distance is about thirty kilometers. In antiquity, given a different terrain and water level, this distance could have been perceived differently.

But there is an important detail. Apollonius writes about the "Grove of Ares," where the fleece is kept. The grove is at a mountain, inaccessible to a casual person, guarded by a dragon that never closes its eyes.

A dragon that never sleeps is a metaphor. But a metaphor for what?

An impregnable cliff with vertical walls, the summit of which cannot be reached without special knowledge of the route. Khvamli is precisely such a cliff: without ropes and equipment, climbing to the plateau is practically impossible, and in times when there was no equipment at all — absolutely impossible.

The dragon guarding the fleece is the mountain that cannot be climbed.

---

## Medea as *pharmakís*

Here I must make a digression that is important for understanding the entire story.

Medea in the Greek tradition is a *pharmakís*. This word is usually translated as "sorceress" or "witch," which is fundamentally incorrect. *Pharmakís* is a cognate of *pharmakon*, which means both poison and medicine (a fundamental duality that the Greek language captures in one word). *Pharmakís* is a person who works with *pharmakon*. In modern terms — a pharmacologist. Or, if you will, an integrative physician.

Medea is a priestess of Hecate, the goddess of crossroads, the moon, and herbs. Hecate is the patroness of that which is "between": between life and death, between light and darkness, between the familiar and the foreign. That is precisely why she is the goddess of magic: magic is work with boundaries.

But the practical content of Medea's activity is work with plants. The Caucasus is one of the world's centers of biodiversity. The alpine zone in Colchis, where alpine meadows border subtropical forests, creates unique conditions: plants that grow only here, with alkaloids found nowhere else.

When Ovid in Book VII of the *Metamorphoses* describes the rejuvenation of Aeson — Jason's father — by Medea, he lists the ingredients of her potion with a detail that reads like a pharmacological recipe: "roots which she cut with a bronze sickle in the Caucasus mountains," "seeds, flowers, dark juices of herbs." This is not fairy-tale magic — it is a medical protocol.

Medea rejuvenated an old ram by boiling it in a cauldron with a herbal decoction — and it emerged a lamb. This is, in all likelihood, the primary narrative from which the quest for the Golden Fleece grew: the skin of an animal that had undergone a renewal ritual was a symbol of the same rejuvenation technology possessed by Aeëtes and his daughter.

The Argonauts came to Colchis not just for gold. They came for knowledge.

---

## The Real Colchis

Georgian scholars have accumulated a significant corpus of data on the real Colchis as a state formation.

The Kingdom of Colchis existed approximately from the 8th to the 6th century BC — precisely in the period when Greek colonists began actively settling the shores of the Black Sea and contacting the local population. Colchis traded with Greek *poleis* — primarily in flax, hemp, wax, resin, and metals.

Metals — this is important. Colchian metallurgy of the Bronze Age was one of the most developed in the region. The Colchian type of axes, swords, ornaments — a recognizable archaeological type that spread throughout Transcaucasia and partly to the Near East. "The people who tamed iron" in myths is not a metaphor or hyperbole.

David Braund in his monograph *Georgia in Antiquity* (1994) — one of the best Western academic works on the subject — analyzes the testimonies of Greek authors about Colchis and concludes: it was a real state formation with a developed social structure, trade connections, and political institutions. Not a barbarian periphery — a partner and counterpart in the Greek world.

Ptolemy places the river Phasis (Rioni) at the center of his map of the Caucasus. This is not a cartographer's accident. It reflects the real state of affairs: the Rioni was the main transport artery of Colchis, along which trade flows moved.

---

## Khvamli as the Center of Colchis

Now let's try to connect all the lines.

Colchis — a real state. Its capital, in all likelihood, was located on the site of present-day Kutaisi or near it. Its sacred mountain — Khvamli — rises above the valley thirty kilometers to the west.

This distance is not accidental. In most ancient states, the sacred center was placed outside the political center: the capital — the place of human power, the sacred mountain — the place of divine power. Delphi from Athens — one hundred seventy kilometers. Olympus from Mycenae — three hundred. Distance is part of the architecture of sacred geography: close enough to go on pilgrimage, far enough that the path to the sanctuary is a journey, not a stroll.

Khvamli and Kutaisi are the perfect distance for such a system.

If we accept this, much falls into place.

The Grove of Ares with the Golden Fleece — a sacred territory at the foot of Khvamli. The dragon that never sleeps — the cliffs of the mountain. Aeëtes, son of the Sun — a king receiving legitimacy from the sacred mountain. Medea, knowledgeable of Caucasian plants — a priestess whose knowledge is connected to the specific biotope around Khvamli.

And Amiran himself, chained inside the mountain — the keeper of knowledge that cannot be taken out, but to which one can come if one knows the way.

Apollonius's *Argonautica* is, perhaps, not a poetic fiction. It is an encrypted history of how Greek sailors reached one of the most closed sacred and technological centers of the ancient world. About what they found there. And about the price that had to be paid for that access.

Medea left with them. But her knowledge remained here.

In the mountain. In the stone. In the petroglyphs awaiting their reading.

---

*Next chapter: In Search of the Rock of Prometheus.*

---

# Chapter 4. A Language Without Kinship

> "Language is not merely a means of communication. It is a geological cross-section in which millennia of a people's life have been deposited. To understand who lived on this land — read what they called what they saw."
>
> — Jacob Grimm, “On the Origin of Language,” 1851

---

Imagine you are a cartographer drawing a map of the world's languages. You carefully color the regions: blue for Indo-European (from Ireland to Bangalore), red for Semitic (from Malta to Arabia), yellow for Turkic (from Turkey to Siberia), green for Uralic (Finns and Hungarians united by one ancient proto-tree). Gradually, the map fills up—with large, confident patches.

And suddenly—a small area in the Caucasus. You reach for the blue paint—no, it doesn't fit. For the red—no. For the yellow—absolutely not. You look at your palette and realize: there is no color for this place. You need to mix a new one.

These are the Kartvelian languages.

---

## An Isolate in the Heart of Eurasia

In linguistics, there is the concept of a "language isolate"—a language or group of languages with no proven genetic relationship to any other language family. The most famous isolate in Europe is Basque: several hundred thousand people in the Pyrenees speak a language unrelated to Spanish, French, or any other known language in the world. The Basques are a linguistic "living fossil" of pre-Indo-European Europe.

The Kartvelian language family is the Caucasian analogue of Basque. Four languages: Georgian (about 4 million speakers), Mingrelian (about 500,000, western Georgia), Svan (about 30–40,000, highland Svaneti), and Laz (from 30 to 200,000, some living in Turkey on the Black Sea coast). Together they form a single language family—with a common ancestor, which linguists call "Proto-Kartvelian," and which existed, by conservative estimates, around the 5th–4th millennia BCE.

The Proto-Kartvelian language is not related to the Indo-European proto-language. Not related to Semitic. Not related to Turkic, Mongolic, Uralic, or any other known proto-language of modern linguistics. It is an independent branch of human speech that grew in the South Caucasus and did not spread its roots to the west, east, or south.

The Kartvelian language family is a linguistic isolate on a planetary scale.

---

## Ergativity as Archaism

To appreciate the depth of Kartvelian linguistic independence, one must speak of a structure that distinguishes these languages from most others.

The Georgian language has an ergative alignment. What does this mean in practice? In the languages we are accustomed to (Russian, English, German), there are "nominative" and "accusative" cases: "I see a bear"—"I" in the nominative, "bear" in the accusative. The subject is always marked the same way, no matter what it does.

In ergative languages, everything is different. The subject of a transitive action (the one who does something to an object) is marked differently than the subject of an intransitive action (the one who simply exists or moves). "The man walks" and "the man kills a deer"—in these two sentences, "the man" is marked differently because his relation to the action differs. The object of a transitive action coincides in marking with the subject of an intransitive one.

This sounds like an abstraction, but behind it lies a fundamentally different concept of action and responsibility. Ergative languages seem to ask not "who does?" but "who bears responsibility for affecting the world?" This is not just grammar. It is a worldview.

Ergative alignment is considered one of the oldest grammatical systems, preceding the younger accusative (familiar to us) structure. Languages with ergative systems exist all over the world—in Australia, Central Asia, the Caucasus, Mesoamerica—and their distribution on the map looks like fragments of a much wider prehistoric grammatical zone. The Kartvelian ergative is one of the traces of how people spoke before the Indo-European expansion changed the linguistic map of most of Eurasia.

When we hear the Georgian language, we hear a structure of thought older than most languages in the world.

---

## Four Languages—One Family

What does the Kartvelian language family look like from within?

Georgian—the eldest and most documented. Writing has existed since the 5th century CE (possibly earlier), with a continuous literary tradition from the 11th–12th centuries. Shota Rustaveli's "The Knight in the Panther's Skin" (12th century) is a masterpiece written in a language that a Georgian-speaking reader today understands roughly as a Russian-speaking reader understands Pushkin.

Mingrelian—the closest relative of Georgian. Together they form the "Karto-Zan" subgroup. The Mingrelians historically lived in western Georgia, in Samegrelo—that very Colchis. Their language has preserved a significant layer of archaic forms absent in literary Georgian, and a special vocabulary related to the sea, river, and forest—that is, to the landscape of Colchis.

Laz—the language of the Laz people, living on the Black Sea coast, on both sides of the current Georgian-Turkish border. Laz and Mingrelian are the closest relatives (sometimes united in the "Zan" subgroup). The Laz are direct descendants of the population of ancient Colchis, and that part of the Laz who ended up in Turkey was gradually assimilated, preserving their language as a relic.

Svan—the most archaic of the four languages, preserving grammatical features of the Proto-Kartvelian language in a virtually untouched state. The Svans live in highland Svaneti—isolated valleys where historically conquerors did not penetrate and reformers did not reach. As with the Khevsurs, mountain isolation proved to be a preserving factor. The Svan language is a living fragment of linguistic depth reaching back to the Neolithic.

All four languages are parts of one tree. But this tree stands apart from all other forests.

---

## Kartvelian Lexicon in Sumerian

Now—the most controversial and fascinating section of this chapter. Because we will talk about parallels.

The Soviet orientalist Igor Mikhailovich Diakonov—one of the most authoritative specialists in the Sumerian language in the 20th century—in his comparative studies recorded a number of lexical parallels between Sumerian and the Kartvelian languages. We are not talking about borrowings (i.e., words that passed from one language to another in historical times)—but about possible substrate convergences, i.e., traces of contacts that could have occurred in deep prehistory.

Diakonov identified from seven to twelve stable parallels that cannot be explained by chance coincidence. Let us cite several of the most indicative ones.

Sumerian **čur-** / **sur-** — "water," "to flow," "stream." Georgian **c'uri** — "drop," "jet," Mingrelian **curi** — "rain," "stream." This is not an isolated sound similarity—it is a stable phonetic and semantic correspondence.

Sumerian **kur** — one of the key words of the language: "mountain," "country," "the underworld," "foreign land." In Sumerian cosmology, *kur* is both a mountain range and the underground realm of the dead. Georgian **k'ari** — "gate," "entrance." Mingrelian **k'uri** — "slope," "steepness." We dare to suggest: words meaning "mountain" and "entrance through a mountain" could have had a common prehistoric root—in an era when, for people living near the Caucasian ridge, a mountain pass was precisely the boundary between one's own and the foreign, the living and the dead.

Sumerian **diĝir** — "god," "deity," "heavenly power." Georgian **ğmerṭi** (Gmerthi) — "God," the supreme being of the Georgian pantheon. The phonetic distance between *diĝir* and *ğmerṭi* is significant—but here it is important to note the structural parallelism: in both cases, we are dealing with the concept of "heavenly power present in the world," expressed by a root with velar consonants.

Diakonov cautioned: this is not proof of a genetic relationship between Sumerian and the Kartvelian languages. Sumerian is an isolate, just like Kartvelian, and we cannot claim they share a common ancestor. But stable parallels exist. They can be explained by contacts in prehistoric times—trade, migration, ritual. Or, even more interestingly, they may point to a common linguistic substrate that existed in ancient Western Asia before the formation of the language families known to us.

The Georgian scholar Ivane Javakhishvili developed similar ideas in the 1920s–1930s, although his works were largely obscured by Soviet Marxist linguistics. Today, this topic is being revisited with new tools—computer analysis of corpora, machine comparison of phonological systems. The results are preliminary, but they do not refute Diakonov's hypothesis.

---

## What a Linguistic Isolate Means

Let us return to the map from the beginning of the chapter. Why is it important that the Kartvelian languages are an isolate?

Because language is not just a speech tool. It is a biological and cultural trace of a people in history. When two peoples live side by side for a long time, their languages inevitably influence each other: words, grammatical constructions, phonemes are borrowed. If the Kartvelian languages have no genetic connection to any of the surrounding families—this means that the peoples who spoke these languages lived in the Caucasus long enough and in sufficient isolation for their language to retain its independence.

This is not isolation in the sense of a lack of contacts. Georgia has always traded, fought, and exchanged with its neighbors. But contacts did not lead to linguistic assimilation. The Kartvelian family survived and preserved its structure—which in itself is evidence of unusual cultural resilience.

Linguists estimate the time of existence of the Proto-Kartvelian language—that is, the time when the ancestors of all four modern languages spoke one dialect—to be around the 5th–6th millennia BCE. This is the Neolithic era. It is the time of agriculture, the first permanent settlements, the first ritual centers. It is the very era to which the Shulaveri-Shomutepe culture on the territory of present-day Georgia and Azerbaijan dates back—which we will discuss in more detail in the next chapter.

A linguistic isolate is not an anomaly. It is evidence. Evidence that here, in the South Caucasus, long before the Indo-European expansion, long before the Bronze Age and states—there existed its own, independent, internally connected civilization. With its own concept of action and responsibility, encoded in ergative grammar. With its own name for God. With its own word for water and mountain.

This is not a periphery. This is—one of the sources.

---

## The Language Barrier as a Historical Obstacle

As we noted in Chapter 1, the language barrier is one of the reasons why Georgia fell out of the field of view of Western science. Now we can formulate this more precisely.

It is not only that Georgian is a difficult language (although the script, of course, requires special study). It is that the Kartvelian languages represent a different structure of thought. One can enter it through a dictionary and grammar, but to truly enter it—to understand the intuition of the language, its way of organizing the world—is incomparably more difficult.

Researchers who worked with Georgian texts without a deep understanding of the language often came to erroneous conclusions precisely because they read the words but did not hear the structure. Leonti Mroveli, Vakhushti Bagrationi, Sulkhan-Saba Orbeliani—these authors thought in a Kartvelian way, and their texts cannot be translated into Russian or English without losing a layer of meaning.

This language barrier is not a wall behind which secrets are hidden, accessible only to the initiated. It is a historical accident that can be corrected. But correction requires effort—and recognition that there is something beyond the barrier.

Something spoken of by the stones on Mount Khvamli. Stones carved by people who thought in a language without kin.

---

*Sources and Notes*

¹ Diakonov I.M. “Paths of History. From the Earliest Man to Our Days.” Moscow: Vostochnaya Literatura, 1994. His Sumerian-Kartvelian parallels are considered in the context of prehistoric contacts.

² Klimov G.A. “Etymological Dictionary of the Kartvelian Languages.” Moscow: USSR Academy of Sciences, 1964. A standard academic source on Kartvelian etymology.

³ Javakhishvili I.A. “Fundamental Questions of the History of the Georgian People.” Tbilisi, 1913–1928 (in 4 vols.). A foundational work, partially available in Russian translations.

⁴ Dixon R.M.W. “Ergativity.” Cambridge University Press, 1994. A standard academic overview of ergative systems.

⁵ Gamkrelidze T.V., Machavariani G.I. “The Sonant System and Ablaut in the Kartvelian Languages.” Tbilisi: Metsniereba, 1965. A key work on the historical phonology of the Kartvelian languages.

⁶ Harris A.C. “Kartvelian Syntax in Cross-Linguistic Perspective.” New York: SUNY Press, 1982. Comparative syntax of the Kartvelian languages.
---

*Next chapter: The Chronology of the Rupture.*

---

# Chapter 5. The Chronology of the Rupture

> "History is not a thread, but a network. When the network tears in one place, it is the apparent rupture that disappears, not the real connection. The historian's task is not to find the beginning of the thread, but to reconstruct the place where it was deliberately cut."
>
> — Fernand Braudel, *The Mediterranean and the Mediterranean World in the Age of Philip II*, 1949

---

There is a curious paradox in the history of Colchis. When Greek sailors in the 6th century BCE began actively sailing the Pontus Euxinus and founding colonies on its shores, they encountered here an already established civilization: with a state, metallurgy, trade connections, and a mythology rooted in impenetrable antiquity. They named the country Colchis and recorded everything they could about it.

But what was there before the Greeks? What lay behind that mature culture they encountered?

The answer to this question lies in the chronological rupture. In the period between two well-documented eras: the early Kartvelian Neolithic culture and the historical Colchian Kingdom about which Apollonius wrote. Between them lie millennia, which Western scholarship long called the "Dark Ages of the Caucasus." But this darkness is not an absence of history. It is an absence of convenient sources.

---

## Shulaveri-Shomutepe: A Flourishing Without Witnesses

Let us begin at the beginning—as far as possible.

In the 1960s, Soviet and Azerbaijani archaeologists, during excavations in the valleys of the Kura and Aragvi rivers, discovered what later became known as the **Shulaveri-Shomutepe culture**. Its dating is approximately 6000–4000 BCE, i.e., the Neolithic and the beginning of the Chalcolithic. Its area spans southern Georgia and northern Azerbaijan, although traces spread wider.

This is one of the oldest agricultural cultures of the Caucasus.

Shulaveri-Shomutepe settlements consisted of round or oval houses built close together: a typical form of early farming communities in the Near East, where such layout combined defensive function and space economy. The inhabitants cultivated emmer wheat, barley, and grapes. Grapes are especially important: it is precisely within the Shulaveri-Shomutepe area that the oldest evidence of winemaking in the world has been discovered, dating to around 6000 BCE. Georgia's claim to the status of the "cradle of wine" rests not on legend, but on a stratigraphic layer.

The inhabitants of these settlements made pottery with characteristic ornamentation, worked bone and flint, and raised livestock. Their cultural profile is sedentary, agricultural, organized. Not hunter-gatherers at a crossroads, but people who invested labor in a specific land and remained on it.

Who were they linguistically? Did they speak a precursor to the Proto-Kartvelian language? Science cannot answer this question with certainty. But the geographical coincidence of the Shulaveri-Shomutepe area with the later area of Kartvelian languages is a fact that is difficult to ignore. Continuity is possible.

---

## Colchian Bronze: Maturity Without Chronicles

Let us skip ahead several centuries. Between 3000 and 1000 BCE in western Georgia, what archaeologists call the **Colchian Bronze Age culture** takes shape. This is one of the most developed metallurgical centers of the ancient world.

What do we know about Colchian bronze?

First and foremost—its distinctiveness. Colchian bronzes differ from contemporary Near Eastern and Anatolian analogues in alloy composition, casting technology, and artifact typology. Colchian axes, daggers, and bracelets represent a distinct style, recognizable at a glance: characteristic geometric ornaments, specific proportions, often zoomorphic handles and finials. This style spread throughout Transcaucasia and penetrated the Near East.

Metallurgy requires several things simultaneously: raw materials, knowledge, labor organization, and trade routes for distribution. The presence of a developed bronze culture means all of this existed. The Caucasus is rich in ore deposits—copper and tin, from which bronze is alloyed. Mountain rivers, including the Rioni and its tributaries, provided access to the sea. Labor organization implies a social structure, i.e., a state in embryonic form or something equivalent.

Colchis of the 2nd millennium BCE is not an empty space on the map. It is a functioning civilization.

But it left no written sources accessible to external researchers. Everything we know about it comes from the earth: from excavated graves and settlements, from chance finds, from typological analysis of artifacts. There are no chronicles. No royal inscriptions. No archives.

More precisely: if they existed—they have not yet been found. Or not identified. Or they rest in the caves of Khvamli.

---

## The Indo-European Expansion and Its Shadow

The period between 3500 and 1500 BCE in Eurasia is an era of great linguistic and cultural redistribution.

The steppe cultures of the northern Black Sea region—primarily what is called the "Yamnaya culture" and its descendants—began an expansion in all directions. To the west, they brought Indo-European languages into Europe. To the south—into Anatolia and Iran. To the east—into Central Asia. This was not a single military campaign, but a slow, centuries-long pressure: migrations, mixtures, elite replacements, language shifts.

For the Caucasus, this pressure had specific consequences.

From the north came Proto-Iranian and later Proto-Armenian tribes. From the south—the Hurro-Urartian world, which formed from a synthesis of local and incoming elements. Anatolia to the west was occupied by the Hittites—Indo-Europeans who created one of the greatest powers of the Bronze Age. The Caucasus found itself in a ring of pressure.

How did the Kartvelian culture react? Apparently—in the same way Georgians reacted to pressure in historical times: not by assimilating, but by absorbing. Borrowing words and technologies, preserving language and identity structure. It is a working strategy—but it leaves traces precisely in what changed, not in what remained.

It is precisely in this period, it seems, that the first Indo-European borrowings entered the Georgian language—predominantly from Proto-Iranian or a Proto-Hittite substrate. These are words from the spheres of animal husbandry, metallurgy, and exchange trade. They did not displace the Kartvelian foundation but overlaid it with a thin layer, like dust on a bookshelf.

---

## Why the 6th Century BCE is a Problem

And so we approach the chronological rupture that gives this chapter its title.

For Western scholarship, Colchis "appears" in the 6th century BCE—at the moment when Greek colonists began actively sailing the Pontus Euxinus and leaving records. It is then that the first reliable written evidence emerges: mentions of the Colchians, of the city of Dioscurias (present-day Sukhumi), of trade relations. It is to this period that the formation of the mythological corpus belongs, which Apollonius would later rework into the *Argonautica*.

Before the 6th century BCE—silence. For Western sources.

But the silence of sources is not the silence of history. It is the silence of those who recorded. And the Greeks recorded. And they began recording only when they arrived themselves.

What was happening in the Caucasus before the Greek colonies? We have already listed: the Neolithic Shulaveri-Shomutepe culture, Colchian bronze, the gradual formation of state structures. But all of this has no names. No names of kingdoms, names of kings, dates of victories. These are "Dark Ages" not because there was no light there—but because our flashlights have not yet reached there.

Hittite chronicles of the 2nd millennium BCE mention the land of **Colchi** (or a similar toponymic formation) among the northeastern neighbors with whom trade and diplomatic relations were conducted. If this identification is correct—and most specialists consider it probable—then Colchis as a political entity existed already in the middle of the 2nd millennium BCE. Eight hundred years before the Greeks "discovered" it.

Assyrian sources of the 9th–8th centuries BCE mention the land of **Kullha** among the lands north of Urartu. Again—an obvious coincidence with Colchis.

This is not a "rupture" in history. It is a rupture in the availability of sources for one specific, historically privileged circle of researchers.

---

## Urartu as Mirror and as Mask

Between the 12th and 6th centuries BCE, south of the Caucasus, there existed the powerful state of **Urartu**—with its capital near present-day Van (eastern Turkey). Urartu is not an Indo-European kingdom. Its language is an isolate, linguistically close to Hurrian. This state actively contacted Caucasian peoples and left a significant corpus of cuneiform inscriptions.

In Urartian chronicles, Caucasian neighbors appear regularly—under names that are not easy to identify, because the transmission of Caucasian toponyms through cuneiform inevitably distorts the original sound. But researchers identify a number of mentions that with sufficient probability refer to the territory of present-day Georgia: the kingdom of **Diaokhi** (likely in the area of present-day Adjara or Samtskhe), the land of **Kvirila** (named after the river?), and others.

More importantly: Urartian kings made regular military campaigns northward, towards the Caucasus, and brought back copper, livestock, and captives. This means that north of Urartu there existed a civilization rich enough to be worth plundering and organized enough to resist.

Caucasian bronze was mentioned above as material evidence. Urartian chronicles provide it with indirect written confirmation.

---

## Reconstructing Continuity

Let us attempt to reconstruct the chronology, relying on what we have.

**6000–4000 BCE** — Neolithic farming communities of the Shulaveri-Shomutepe type in the valleys of the Kura and Rioni rivers. Probable predecessors of the Kartvelian peoples. The first winemaking.

**4000–2500 BCE** — Chalcolithic and Early Bronze Age. Gradual development of metallurgy, growth of settlements. The Kura-Araxes culture covers much of Transcaucasia—a complex cultural synthesis with elements of local and Near Eastern traditions.

**2500–1500 BCE** — Flourishing of the Colchian Bronze Age culture. Unique metallurgy, trade connections with Anatolia and the Near East. Possibly the first state formations on the territory of western Georgia.

**1500–700 BCE** — Strengthening of political structures. Mentions in Hittite and Urartian sources. The first traces of what would later become the Colchian Kingdom.

**700–400 BCE** — The Colchian Kingdom in the form recorded by Greek sources. Trade relations with Greek colonies on the Black Sea. Formation of the mythological corpus.

This is not a rupture. This is continuity—simply of varying degrees of illumination. Early periods are visible only through the archaeologist's spade. Later ones—through the pen of a Greek geographer. Between them lie several centuries of Urartian cuneiform and one very important mountain that remembers everything.
---

## Why This Matters for Khvamli

As we saw in Chapter 2, Khvamli is not merely a summit but a functional system: a fortress, an observatory, a repository. For such a system to emerge and function, generations of people who know its purpose are required. Generations who transmit this knowledge.

The chronology we have reconstructed in this chapter provides this possibility. If the Kartvelian culture existed continuously in this territory from the 6th millennium BCE—and the data allows us to suggest this—then Khvamli could have been a sacred center for four, five, six thousand years. With adjustments, reinterpretations, stratifications—but continuously.

This conclusion is important for another reason. The petroglyphs we will examine in Chapters 7 and 8 date to the Early Bronze Age—approximately 3000–2000 BCE. This is the darkest part of our chronological scale—the part where there are neither Greek texts, nor Urartian chronicles, nor systematic excavations. And it is precisely here that the stone speaks for everything else.

The silence of sources is not an emptiness. It is a space yet to be filled.

---

*Sources and Notes*

¹ Lordkipanidze O. «Phasis: The River and Kingdom. Studies in the History and Historical Geography of Ancient Colchis». Stuttgart: Steiner, 2000. The standard monograph on historical Colchis.

² Kushnareva K.Kh. «The Southern Caucasus in Prehistory: Stages of Cultural and Socioeconomic Development from the Eighth to the Second Millennium BC». University of Pennsylvania Museum, 1997. A comprehensive overview of the prehistoric archaeology of the region.

³ Kohl P.L. «The Making of Bronze Age Eurasia». Cambridge University Press, 2007. A broad comparative context for Colchian bronze.

⁴ Melikishvili G.A. «Urartian Cuneiform Inscriptions». Moscow: USSR Academy of Sciences, 1960. Publication and analysis of Urartian sources mentioning Caucasian peoples.

⁵ Japaridze O.M. «Archaeological Excavations in Trialeti». Tbilisi: Georgian SSR Academy of Sciences, 1969. Key data on early Colchian metallurgy.

⁶ Braund D. «Georgia in Antiquity: A History of Colchis and Transcaucasian Iberia, 550 BC–AD 562». Oxford: Clarendon Press, 1994. The best academic overview of Colchis in the historical period.

---

*Next Chapter: The Methodological Trap.*

---

# Chapter 6. The Methodological Trap

> "The map is not the territory. But when the map is drawn by someone who has never been to the territory—that is not merely a mistake. It is a verdict."
>
> — Alfred Korzybski, "Science and Sanity", 1933

---

In medicine, when a doctor misses a diagnosis, it is called "diagnostic bias." The bias occurs not because symptoms are absent. They are present. They are even noticeable. But the doctor does not see them because they already have a concept—a template into which they fit everything they see. Symptoms that do not fit the template are not recorded, not analyzed. They are, as it were, nonexistent.

In historiography—the same mechanism. Only there it is harder to correct, because the patient does not come for a follow-up appointment.

Three centuries of studying the Caucasus by Western science have bequeathed to us several fundamental methodological biases which, layered upon one another, have created a stable image: Georgia as a periphery, the Caucasus as a corridor, Kartvelian culture as a recipient, not a source. This chapter I wish to devote to analyzing these biases—not to accuse Western science of malicious intent, but to understand how the trap is constructed. For without this understanding, one cannot escape it.

---

## The First Bias: The Colonial Optics of the 18th–19th Centuries

Academic history as an institution took shape in an era when the words "civilization" and "Western Europe" were perceived as almost synonymous.

The Enlightenment thinkers of the 18th century—Voltaire, Montesquieu, Herder—constructed their concepts of progress around the European model. Egypt, Greece, Rome were part of this model—as predecessors, as the ancient foundation of the European spirit. Everything else was located on the periphery: the farther from Athens and Rome, the less "civilized" what happened there seemed.

This was not merely a prejudice—it was a theory. Montesquieu in "The Spirit of the Laws" specifically explained why mountain peoples are inclined towards freedom but not towards civilization: mountainous terrain hinders trade, trade is the foundation of statehood, statehood is a prerequisite for civilization. By this logic, the Caucasus was doomed to remain barbaric: too many mountains, too few trade routes (by European standards).

The first academic expeditions to Georgia in the 18th–19th centuries—German, French, Russian—arrived with a ready-made theory and collected data that confirmed it. What did not confirm it was not published or was published in marginal sections of reports.

Jean-Chrétien de Besse, Moritz Wagner, Gustav Radde—a German zoologist who worked for years in Tbilisi and left valuable ethnographic observations—all of them saw a living culture but interpreted it within an evolutionary paradigm: "early stage," "primitive type," "precursor." The paradigm did not allow them to see an independent civilization—only an embryo of something more mature.

---

## The Second Bias: The Russian Imperial View

From 1801, Eastern Georgia, and from 1810, Western Georgia as well, became part of the Russian Empire. This had direct consequences for the production of historical knowledge.

The Russian academic tradition of the 19th century had a dual attitude towards Georgia. On the one hand—with genuine scientific interest: the first systematic excavations in the Caucasus were Russian, the first translations of Georgian texts—also. Marie-Félicité Brosset, a French orientalist, worked at the St. Petersburg Academy of Sciences and it was there that he gained access to Georgian manuscripts. His translations of "Kartlis Tskhovreba" and the works of Vakhushti Bagrationi—this is Russian academic infrastructure in the service of Kartvelology.

On the other hand—a rigid political framework. Georgia within the Russian Empire could not claim a historical autonomy contradicting the narrative of "voluntary accession" and "civilizing mission." Georgian sources were interpreted within the framework of Russian state ideology: Georgia is a younger brother, saved from Persian-Ottoman oppression, grateful to St. Petersburg for its protection.

Within this framework, the myth of Amiran could not be a "source" of the Promethean myth—because that would make Greek, and through it European, cultural heritage dependent on the Caucasian. This was unacceptable.

The Russian imperial view did not destroy Georgian historical science—it filtered it. What fit was published; what did not fit remained in manuscripts or archives.

---

## The Third Bias: Soviet "Depoliticization" of Mythology

The Soviet period brought new constraints—and new opportunities.

Opportunities: large-scale excavations, state funding for fieldwork, the creation of Georgian academic institutes. It was precisely in the Soviet period that many key sites were discovered and studied—the Trialeti burial grounds, the necropolises of Colchis, the rock art of a number of regions.

Constraints: the Marxist ideological framework, which required fitting all data into the theory of "stages of social development." Mythology—according to Marx and Engels—was a "fantastic reflection of real relations of production." This meant that myths were interesting only insofar as they illustrated class struggle or a stage of social development. Their independent content—symbolic architecture, mythological parallels, astral cults—was at best a marginal topic, at worst "bourgeois mysticism."

Result: Soviet Georgian science accumulated a colossal volume of primary data—field diaries, artifact catalogs, site descriptions—with significantly weaker interpretive engagement. The data existed, the concept did not, or the concept was inherently limiting.

Another effect of the Soviet period was the "depoliticization" of Georgian history through its inclusion in the narrative of "fraternal peoples." In this narrative, there was no place for the thesis of Colchis as a "source" of Near Eastern civilizations: this contradicted the centralizing ideology, which made Moscow the center of everything, and before it—Rome, Athens, and Babylon.

---

## The Language Barrier as a Methodological Wall

As we showed in Chapter 4, the Kartvelian languages are an isolate. This means: to work with Georgian sources, one had to specifically learn a language unrelated to anything familiar. For a Western scholar of the 19th–20th centuries, this was a barrier of rare height.

But this barrier is asymmetric—and in this lies its methodological cunning.

Georgian scholars read Greek authors—Apollonius, Strabo, Herodotus—in the original or in translation. They compared Greek testimonies about Colchis with their own sources: the chronicles of Leonti Mroveli, the geography of Vakhushti, the dictionary of Sulkhan-Saba Orbeliani. They had a complete picture from both sides.

Western scholars saw only the Greek side. They knew that the Greeks wrote about Colchis. But they did not know that the Colchians themselves wrote about themselves. Because it was written in a language they did not read.

Leonti Mroveli—a Georgian chronicler of the 11th century, author of the section "Life of the Kartlian Kings" in "Kartlis Tskhovreba"—compiled a detailed genealogy of Georgian peoples, tracing back to the mythical forefather Kartlos and his brothers. This genealogy is not merely poetic fiction: it records the real self-awareness of a people, their ideas about their own origin and connections with neighboring civilizations. It contains indications parallel to Greek sources about Colchis—but read from within, not from without.

Vakhushti Bagrationi (1696–1757)—a Georgian prince, historian, and geographer who spent most of his life in exile in Russia—compiled the first systematic geographical atlas of Georgia and "Description of the Kingdom of Georgia." His work is based on knowledge of both Georgian chronicles and Western European cartographic tradition. He would have been an ideal intermediary between two worlds. But his works were partially translated into Russian (Brosset translated some fragments), into other European languages—almost none.

The trap is simple: if a Georgian scholar wrote about Khvamli—but in Georgian—for external science, it is as if it does not exist.

---

## What Was Not Translated: Losses in Transmission

Let us consider specifically what from the Georgian corpus of sources remained outside international scholarly circulation.

"Kartlis Tskhovreba"—the principal Georgian chronicle, a compilation of the 11th–18th centuries, including material from the 5th to the 18th century. Partial translations into Russian have existed since the 19th century. Individual parts were translated into English in the 20th century (Metreveli, Thomson). A complete academic translation with commentary into any Western European language still does not exist.

Sulkhan-Saba Orbeliani. His "Dictionary"—the first Georgian encyclopedic dictionary (early 18th century)—contains etymological explanations of toponyms, theological articles, and mythological information of primary importance. Not translated. Available to specialists in the original.

"Visramiani"—a Georgian prose romance of the 12th century, an adaptation of a Persian source, containing a layer of Colchian mythological allusions. Translated into Russian, but practically unknown in Western academic circles.
Georgian hagiographic texts of the 5th–10th centuries. The lives of Georgian saints are not merely ecclesiastical literature. They are a record of folk beliefs, topographical data, mythological structures, recoded into a Christian narrative. This encoding is an exceptionally informative source on the pre-Christian religion of Georgia. It has been scarcely studied outside of specialist circles.

The total volume of Georgian written sources from the 14th–18th centuries, not translated into any major Western language, is estimated to be several thousand pages. These are not marginalia—this is a corpus.

---

## The Trap: If It's Not in Latin, It Doesn't Exist

All of the above can be reduced to one fundamental formula, which I would call the "Latin trap."

The Western academic tradition is the heir to the Greco-Roman written civilization. It knows what was written in Greek or Latin, and subsequently—in languages that inherited the Greco-Latin writing system: Italian, French, English, German. What was not written in this system "does not exist" for it in a pragmatic, working sense.

Georgia has had a written tradition since the 5th century AD. The Georgian alphabet is one of the few in the world created specifically for a single language and which has preserved its form virtually unchanged for one and a half thousand years. Georgians wrote in their own language—with their own alphabet. They did not need Latin transliteration to preserve their knowledge.

This is precisely what became the trap. They preserved it—for themselves. For the outside world, this knowledge remained invisible.

Correcting this situation is not a rhetorical task. It is concrete work: translations, commentaries, academic editions, international collaborations. Part of this work is already underway. But the greater part is still ahead.

---

## Escaping the Trap

A methodological trap is not a verdict. It is a diagnosis. And a diagnosis is already halfway to a cure.

What is needed to escape the trap?

First—recognition of the asymmetry. Kartvelian sources exist, they are informative, they are neither worse nor better than Greek or Latin ones—they are simply different. An academic tradition that does not work with these sources is incomplete. Not flawed—specifically incomplete.

Second—translations. Systematic, academic, with commentaries. At least of the key texts: *Kartlis Tskhovreba*, Vakhushti, Sulkhana-Saba.

Third—multidisciplinary. The petroglyphs of Khvamli cannot be read using only linguistic tools. Archaeologists, astronomers, geneticists, geologists, specialists in comparative mythology are needed. This is already happening—slowly, with insufficient funding, but it is happening.

It is to these petroglyphs that we now turn. Because stone speaks without a language barrier. Stone is understood by all who have the patience to listen.

---

*Sources and Notes*

¹ Brosse M.-F. «Histoire de la Géorgie depuis l'Antiquité jusqu'au XIXe siècle». St.-Pétersbourg: Académie Impériale des Sciences, 1849–1858. A fundamental work on Georgian history, still relevant today.

² Metreveli R.V. (ed.) «Kartlis Tskhovreba». Tbilisi: Publishing House of the Academy of Sciences of the Georgian SSR, 1955 (in Georgian); Thomson R.W. (tr.) «Rewriting Caucasian History: The Medieval Armenian Adaptation of the Georgian Chronicles». Oxford, 1996. An academic translation of part of the material into English.

³ Orbeliani S.-S. «Dictionary of the Georgian Language». Tbilisi: Metsniereba, 1966 (facsimile edition of the 18th-century original). The primary source of Georgian lexicography.

⁴ Lang D.M. «A Modern History of Soviet Georgia». New York: Grove Press, 1962. A classic Western overview showing how political frameworks influenced historiography.

⁵ Lordkipanidze M. «Georgia in the XI–XII Centuries». Tbilisi: Ganatleba, 1987. An academic overview of medieval Georgia.

⁶ Chubinashvili N.G. «Georgian Metalwork Art». Tbilisi: Academy of Sciences of the Georgian SSR, 1959. A fundamental work on medieval Georgian art, containing rich iconographic material.

---

*Next Chapter: The Petroglyphs of Khvamli—The First Corpus.*

---

# Chapter 7. The Petroglyphs of Khvamli—The First Corpus

> "The rock does not lie. It does not know how to lie. It has no language—only a surface that someone once wanted to make into a non-chronicle. And that someone was smarter than we think."
>
> — André Leroi-Gourhan, "Hand and Word," 1964

---
