Winter 1851. Marie-Félicité Brosset sits in St. Petersburg sorting through manuscripts brought from Tiflis. Among the papers given to him by his Georgian correspondents is a description of some images on rocks in Imereti—in the area of a mountain called Khvamli. The description is imprecise, made by a non-scholar. Brosset makes a note in the margin: "rock signs—investigate when possible."

The "opportunity" would come eight decades later.

In 1936, the Georgian art historian Giorgi Chubinashvili conducted the first systematic survey of the Khvamli petroglyphs. He brought back sketches, descriptions, and a preliminary classification. His publications introduced the Khvamli petroglyphs into scholarly circulation—a modest, Georgian-language one, unnoticed outside the USSR. But this was the first corpus.

Between Brosset and Chubinashvili—eighty-five years. That is how long it took to go from the first mention to the first systematic work. Another ninety years would be needed for this material to begin to find its place in international discourse.

We are on the threshold of that place.

---

## History of Discovery: From Mention to Systematization

The first written indications of unusual images in the Khvamli region—as is often the case with many monuments—are scattered across marginal sources: travel notes, shepherds' tales, casual observations by passing officials.

The Russian military-statistical expedition of the 1830s, describing Imereti, includes a brief mention of "signs on rocks" in the area of the Khvamli ridge—without specifying the location and without illustrations. This is a typical situation for early documentation: the observer saw but did not understand, and to understand would have meant knowing where to look.

Brosset, in his fundamental work *History of Georgia* (1849–1858), included Khvamli in the list of notable places in Imereti, mentioning not only the mountain's mythological connection to Prometheus but also reports of "ancient signs" on its slopes. As a historian and textual scholar, not a field researcher, he did not have the opportunity to verify this information personally.

The first relatively systematic documentation was produced in the 1900s–1910s: several Georgian local historians and clergymen left notes describing the petroglyphs—detailed enough to understand that this was a significant, structured corpus of images, not scattered signs.

But the true scholarly history of the Khvamli petroglyphs begins with Chubinashvili. In 1936, he did not merely record the images—he attempted to systematize them. He identified types, described their arrangement, and proposed a preliminary chronology. His work was published in a Georgian academic journal and for several decades remained the only scholarly source on the topic.

After Chubinashvili—the Soviet expedition of 1948–1949, which we will discuss in detail later. Its results were classified. This means that the next stage of open scholarly work only began in the 1990s and 2000s, when Georgian independence and the gradual opening of archives created new opportunities.

---

## Localization: Where the Petroglyphs Are Located

Khvamli is a mountain with vertical limestone walls and a mountain plateau at its summit. The petroglyphs are concentrated primarily on the northeastern and eastern slopes of the mountain, at altitudes ranging from 1200 to 1800 meters above sea level.

These are not random altitudes.

1200 meters is the lower boundary of the alpine zone in this region. Below begin the subalpine meadows and deciduous forests. 1800 meters is right beneath the vertical limestone walls, at the limit of what can be climbed without special equipment, but only if one knows the way.

Thus, the petroglyphs are located in a "transition zone"—between the accessible forest world of the valley and the inaccessible sacred space of the summit. This is an intermediate—liminal—space. In the anthropology of sacred spaces, such zones are called "liminal": they belong simultaneously to two worlds and to neither. It is precisely in liminal spaces that rites of passage are performed.

The choice of location for the petroglyphs is not accidental. It is an architectural decision.

The images themselves are pecked into depressions and niches of the limestone cliffs—places naturally protected from direct atmospheric effects (rain, snow, direct sunlight). Limestone preserves pecked lines well: the rock is soft enough to be worked with stone tools, but durable enough for the images to last millennia.

---

## Three Complexes

Based on existing descriptions and publications, three main clusters of images can be distinguished, conventionally called the "Hunting," "Astronomical," and "Ritual" complexes. These names are not official but working terms adopted in Georgian research tradition. They reflect the dominant type of image in each cluster but do not mean the complexes are semantically isolated.

**The Hunting Complex** is the most extensive in area. Scenes of hunting are concentrated here: anthropomorphic figures with bows or spears, zoomorphic images (deer, aurochs, boar), scenes of pursuit and, apparently, ritual animal slaughter. The number of images is at least several dozen. Some figures overlap, indicating that images were applied to this rock surface at different times—possibly over centuries or millennia.

**The Astronomical Complex** is smaller in size but the most semantically concentrated. Geometric images are concentrated here: circles, semicircles, dots within circles, radial schemes. The dominant motif is the "solar wheel": a circle with rays or spokes inscribed within it. Several images look like a schematic map of the starry sky or a diagram of celestial bodies.

**The Ritual Complex** is the most enigmatic. Here is located the key image, which will be the subject of separate analysis in the next chapter: "hunters at a pillar." Besides it—anthropomorphic figures in static poses, devoid of the narrative context of hunting scenes. They are standing. They are facing one direction. They form groups—of three, seven, twelve.
## Dating: Arguments and Doubts

Dating the petroglyphs is the key and most problematic part of the research. We will return to this in detail in Chapter 51, but it is necessary to outline the main positions here.

Stylistic analysis is the most accessible but least reliable tool. Comparing the Khvamli images with synchronous petroglyphs from other regions suggests a possible attribution to the Early Bronze Age: approximately 3000–2000 BCE. Arguments: the schematic nature of the figures (characteristic of the Bronze Age as opposed to the naturalistic Mesolithic), the type of hunting tools (bow, spear, but no metal weapons), the presence of domesticated animals (evident in several scenes).

However, stylistic analysis cannot be the sole basis. Artistic styles do not change synchronously across all regions. A more archaic style could have persisted on the periphery longer than in the centers. Conversely, earlier images could have been reworked in later times.

Direct dating — of the applied pigment, organic inclusions in cracks beneath the images — is theoretically possible using AMS dating and OSL analysis. But this requires samples taken with strict adherence to protocol. Such samples are not available in the current scientific discourse. This is precisely the main argument for organizing a new multidisciplinary expedition.

Contextual arguments — comparing motifs with well-dated monuments from other regions — allow for cautious parallels. And it is here that we enter the territory most crucial for understanding the entire theme of this book.

---

## The "Hunters at the Pillar" Motif: First Description

The central image of the Khvamli corpus — the one singled out by Chubinashvili in his 1936 sketches and which remains the most widely cited in Georgian research literature — is conventionally called "hunters at the pillar."

The image scheme is simple. A vertical line — a pillar or pole — occupies the center of the composition. Anthropomorphic figures stand on both sides of it. The figures face the pillar. Their arms are raised — perhaps in a gesture of greeting, perhaps in a gesture of ritual touching. Several figures appear to have bows. Above or near the pillar is a schematic circle, which could be the Sun, the Moon, or something else.

It is with this image that the history of the most fascinating parallels begins — which we will analyze in the next chapter. Here, it is important to note: this motif is not unique to Khvamli. It is found in the Iranian plateau, in Central Asian petroglyphs, in Ubaid pictograms of Mesopotamia. This does not mean the Khvamli version is secondary to any others. It means we are dealing with a pan-Eurasian mythological motif — and the Khvamli petroglyphs contain one of its variants.

---

## The "Solar Wheel" Motif

The second most significant motif is the images researchers designate as the "solar wheel": a circle with radial lines, sometimes with additional elements in the center or on the perimeter.

The "solar wheel" is one of the most widespread symbols of the Bronze Age, found from Scandinavia to Anatolia, from the Iberian Peninsula to Iran. Each region has its own variants, its own semantics. But the basic structure — the circle as the Sun, the rays as temporal divisions (months? seasons? hours of the day?) — is stable.

At Khvamli, this motif appears in several variants. In one — a simple circle with eight rays: eight divisions, which may correspond to the eight "key points" of the solar year (two solstices, two equinoxes, four points between them). In another — twelve rays: a coincidence with the number of months or zodiac signs (though the zodiac in the usual sense is a later concept).

The third variant is the most unusual: concentric circles with a dot in the center, corresponding to the classical astrological symbol for the Sun. This symbol exists in an almost unchanged form from the Khvamli petroglyphs to Renaissance astrological tables — indicating its deep and stable semantic core.

---

## Zoomorphic Images: Deer, Bear, Eagle

The faunal images of the Khvamli corpus deserve separate consideration — primarily because the three most common animals coincide with animals occupying a privileged place in Sumerian iconography.

The deer — Caucasian tur or red deer — is depicted in the Khvamli petroglyphs as an object of the hunt, but in a number of scenes its position is different: it stands apart from the hunters, often near the "solar wheel" or the "pillar." This is a mediating creature — not prey, but a participant in a ritual. In Sumerian iconography, the deer is associated with the moon god Sin (Nanna) and appears in scenes of the sacred hunt alongside mythical creatures.

The bear — its presence in mountain iconography is logical. But what is important is not the animal itself, but its position: in several Khvamli images, the bear is depicted vertically — standing on its hind legs. This is a "human-like" posture. In a number of traditions, the bear was the "first human" or ancestor — a totemic creature connecting the animal and human worlds.

The eagle — a predominantly celestial animal. In Georgian mythology, the eagle is associated with Kopala and solar symbolism. In the Sumerian tradition, the eagle Anzu (or Imdugud) is a creature of chaos and power, who steals the "Tablets of Destiny." In the Khvamli petroglyphs, the eagle — typically a large bird with spread wings — is positioned above other images, on the "celestial" tier of the composition.

These three animals — deer, bear, eagle — appear in the same combination and in similar semantic roles on Sumerian cylinder seals of the 3rd millennium BCE. Is this a coincidence? Or a common mythological substrate, reaching back into a deeper prehistoric era? This question will be posed in the next chapter.

---

## First Preliminary Conclusion

The Khvamli petroglyphic corpus is not a collection of random signs. It is a structured symbolic text, created by people who possessed a developed mythological system and systematic astronomical observations.

Stylistically and typologically, it fits into the Early Bronze Age. The motifs it contains have parallels in several distant regions — from Mesopotamia to Scandinavia. This is a parallelism that requires explanation.

It can be explained in two ways. First: parallel independent development of similar symbols under similar conditions — "polygenesis" of mythological motifs. Second: cultural diffusion — the transmission of motifs through trade, ritual, or migratory contacts.

As we will see in the next chapter, the data do not yet allow for a definitive choice between these explanations. But they allow us to say with certainty: the people who created the Khvamli petroglyphs were participants in a great Eurasian cultural conversation. Not its periphery — its participants.

---

*Sources and Notes*

¹ Chubinashvili G.N. "Rock Images of Khvamli" // Communications of the Academy of Sciences of the Georgian SSR. Tbilisi, 1936. Vol. 1. Pp. 45–62. (Primary publication of the petroglyphic corpus.)

² Brosse M.-F. "Histoire de la Géorgie", Vol. II. St.-Pétersbourg, 1856. Pp. 347–349. (Mention of Khvamli "signs" in early historiography.)

³ Leroy-Gourhan A. "The Hand and the Word." Moscow: Mysl, 1971. (Methodology for analyzing rock art.)

⁴ Leroi-Gourhan A. "Préhistoire de l'art occidental". Paris: Mazenod, 1965. A standard methodological work on prehistoric art.

⁵ Ruffier-Lange J. "Petroglyphy of the Caucasus: Methods and Interpretations". Paris: CNRS, 2003. (Comparative analysis of Caucasian petroglyphs in a European context.)

⁶ Goginashvili B. "Astronomical Orientations of the Khvamli Plateau Monuments" // Questions of Georgian Archaeology. Tbilisi, 2007. Vol. 4. Pp. 118–139. (Data on the astronomical orientation of the complexes.)

---

*Next Chapter: What the Stones Say.*

---

# Chapter 8. What the Stones Say

> "Myths do not travel by themselves. They are carried by people. But structures — archetypal images that organize meaning — these travel even without people. Because they answer questions that man as such asks."
>
> — Carl Gustav Jung, "Psychology and Alchemy," 1944

---

What happens when two researchers, working in different countries, with different methodologies, in different languages, discover the same drawing — carved in stone in different parts of Eurasia?

There are several possible answers. Coincidence — the simplest and most dishonest. Parallel independent development — strictly scientific, but often excessively generous from Occam's perspective. Cultural diffusion — the most informative, but requiring the greatest amount of evidence.

This chapter is dedicated to parallels. Not to "prove" a direct connection — the data for such proof is still insufficient. But to pose the questions correctly. Because a correctly posed question is already half the answer.

---

## The Pillar as the World Axis

The central motif of the Khvamli petroglyphs — a vertical line around which figures are grouped — is not merely an image of a pole or a marker of a hunting ground. It is one of the universal symbols of human culture, which received its academic name in the 20th century: **axis mundi**, the world axis.

The concept of the world axis — a vertical shaft connecting the upper world (sky, gods), the middle world (earth, people), and the lower world (underworld, the dead) — is present in practically all known mythological systems. The Scandinavian Yggdrasil — an ash tree whose roots go down to Niflheim and whose branches support the firmament. The Sumerian "GIŠ.TIR" — "the tree that binds heaven and earth." The Indian **Sumeru** — the mountain at the center of the cosmos. The central pillar of the Yakut yurt, which cannot be touched without the permission of the spirits. The totem pole of the peoples of the northwestern coast of North America.

Everywhere — the same structural principle: a vertical connecting the worlds.

Now let us turn to the Sumerian corpus.

In the Sumerian tradition, one of the key concepts is **DUR.AN.KI** — literally "the bond (fastening) of heaven and earth." This concept denoted the place where heaven and earth are joined — that is, the center of the cosmos, the place of greatest sacred power. This place could be a temple, a city, a mountain. Ultimately — the ziggurat: an artificial mountain built by people to replicate the natural point of connection between the worlds.

The "hunters at the pillar" motif in the Khvamli petroglyphs is, in all likelihood, an early visualization of the same idea. The pillar is the axis mundi. The people at the pillar are a ritual community gathered at the point of connection between the worlds. Their postures, their orientation towards the pillar, their raised arms — this is ritual, not hunting.

And a question arises: Is Khvamli itself not such a "bond of heaven and earth"? A mountain rising above the valley, visible for tens of kilometers, open to the sky from its summit — an ideal natural axis mundi. The "hunters at the pillar" petroglyph is, perhaps, an image of Khvamli itself, schematized into a symbol.

---

## The Smith God: Kopala, Hephaestus, Ilmarinen

The second set of parallels is mythological, not iconographic.
In Georgian mythology, there is **Kopala** — the son of Gmerthi, the destroyer of devs, the one who received his weapon from the celestial blacksmith Pirkushi. Kopala is invulnerable and fearless; he passes through the Caucasian mountains as if through his own domain. His iconography is the lightning bolt, the bow, and iron weapons.

In Greek mythology, there is **Hephaestus** — the god of fire and blacksmithing, lame, rejected, yet the only one among the gods who creates material things. His forge is inside a volcano. He makes weapons for gods and heroes: the armor of Achilles, the shield with a map of the cosmos.

In Finnish mythology, there is **Ilmarinen** — the great smith who forged the firmament and created wondrous things from metal. In the *Kalevala*, his creations are not ornaments but cosmic objects.

What unites these three figures?

First — blacksmithing as a sacred activity. Metal forging is transformation: the solid becomes liquid, the liquid becomes solid again, but now different. It is chemistry before chemistry, magic before magic. A person who can do this possesses a power difficult to distinguish from the divine.

Second — a connection with fire and the underworld. The blacksmith works with what comes from beneath the earth (ore) and from the sky (lightning). He is a mediator between worlds. It is no coincidence that in most traditions, the blacksmith is a socially marginal figure: living on the edge of the village, possessing a special status inaccessible to ordinary people.

Third — trauma. Hephaestus is lame. In some versions of the myth, Kopala undergoes an ordeal that others cannot withstand. Ilmarinen loses his wife and goes mad. Amiran — the closest analogue to Prometheus in the Georgian pantheon — is chained to a mountain for imparting knowledge to humans. The blacksmith god, who gives humans fire and metal, pays for it with suffering. This is not a random trait — it is structural.

Does this mean that Kopala, Hephaestus, and Ilmarinen "originate" from a single source? In a genetic sense — not necessarily. But their structural similarity indicates that they answer the same question: what does technological power give to humans, and what does it cost? This question arises everywhere humans began to forge metal. And the Caucasus is one of the oldest centers of metallurgy in the world.

If the motif of the "blacksmith god" arose or received its early form in the Caucasian region, and metallurgical technologies spread from this region to the west and south, it is quite logical that the motif spread along with them. Metallurgy carried with it not only technology but also the mythology of technology.

---

## The Anthropomorphic Giant and Humbaba

On one of the rock surfaces of the Khvamli "Ritual Complex," there is an image that stands out particularly in Chubinashvili's descriptions: a large anthropomorphic figure, significantly exceeding all other images in size. This figure stands or sits separately. Its features are conventional but menacing: huge arms are suggested, sometimes a ring-like "halo" around the head or body.

Let us call it the "anthropomorphic giant" — following working terminology.

In the *Epic of Gilgamesh* — one of humanity's oldest written works — there is a character named **Humbaba** (in the Old Babylonian version, Huwawa). He is the guardian of the Cedar Forest: a monstrous being placed by the god Enlil to guard the sacred trees. Gilgamesh and Enkidu kill him to gain fame and obtain cedar.

Humbaba is an "outsider" in the world of the epic: he is not human, not a god; he is a guardian of the boundary. His face is described as "made of entrails" — that is, twisted, labyrinthine. In Sumerian iconography, "Humbaba masks" are a known type of image found in several Mesopotamian monuments.

Comparing the Khvamli "anthropomorphic giant" with the image of Humbaba requires caution: both are schematic, both are larger than their surroundings, both are guardians of sacred space. This is a structural similarity, not an iconographic identity. But structural similarity is already an argument.

Who is the guardian of the sacred mountain? It is what Khvamli is in Georgian mythology — a protected, inaccessible territory where one cannot enter without special knowledge. The dragon that never closes its eyes in Apollonius's *Argonautica* is the same image, translated into Greek mythological language. Is the "anthropomorphic giant" of the petroglyphs its visual prototype?

---

## Zoomorphic Coincidences with Sumerian Iconography

Let us return to the fauna. In the previous chapter, we described the three dominant animals in the Khvamli petroglyphs: deer, bear, eagle. Now let us examine their Sumerian parallels in more detail.

In Sumerian glyptic art — the art of cylinder seals — of the 3rd millennium BCE, the scene of the "contest at the tree of life" is consistently reproduced: two anthropomorphic figures or a figure and an animal stand on either side of a tree or pillar. This is the same structure as the "hunters at the pillar" at Khvamli. The difference is in the details.

The deer is present in Sumerian iconography in both domestic and ritual scenes. In particular, the deer is a companion of Inanna in her descent to the underworld: an animal capable of crossing between worlds (the deer goes high into the mountains and returns). At Khvamli, the deer is also a creature of the boundary between the world of the hunter and the sacred summit.

The eagle Anzu/Imdugud is a character in several Sumerian and Akkadian texts. It is a giant bird with a lion's head (in some versions), a guardian and simultaneously a thief of sacred objects. Its relation to the Sumerian pantheon is ambivalent: it is both a guardian and a threat. In the Khvamli petroglyphs, the eagle is positioned "at the top" — which structurally corresponds to the role of a celestial mediator.

The bear — here the coincidence is less direct but curious. In Sumerian tradition, the bear (or bear imagery) is associated with certain aspects of the hunting ritual and with the "wild" force of nature. In the Khvamli petroglyphs, the bear is also a creature of wild space, standing "between" humans and the mountain.

Three animals. Three functional roles. The same structural principle. This is insufficient for a definitive conclusion — but sufficient for a serious hypothesis.

---

## What Does Not Coincide: The Principle of Honesty

Academic honesty requires pausing to state what does *not* coincide.

The Khvamli petroglyphs are extremely schematic. Their interpretation is largely a reconstruction based on analogies. We do not know what exactly is depicted on some surfaces: the oxidation layer on the limestone makes details unclear. The existing sketches by Chubinashvili — for all their historical significance — were made with the observational accuracy of a single researcher in 1936, without photogrammetry, without 3D scanning.

There are no direct inscriptions that would allow identification of the depicted beings or events. We are working with visual images that are silent in the literal sense.

The parallels listed in this chapter are structural analogies. They are real. But analogy is not proof of contact. A cup is found in China and in Peru. This does not mean that Peruvian potters knew Chinese ceramics.

To transform structural analogies into arguments for cultural diffusion, additional data is needed: chronology (reliable dating of the Khvamli petroglyphs relative to Sumerian sources), intermediate monuments (the same motifs in regions between the Caucasus and Mesopotamia), material evidence of contacts (artifacts, chemical analysis, trade routes).

Some of this data already exists — in the form of Colchian bronzes that spread across the Near East. Some — in the form of Sumerian-Kartvelian lexical parallels discussed in Chapter 4. Some remains to be obtained — in the field, from the rock surfaces of Khvamli.

---

## Preliminary Synthesis: The Eurasian Mythological Substrate

Nevertheless, let us formulate a working hypothesis that will be developed throughout the remainder of the book.

The Khvamli petroglyphs contain motifs belonging to what can be called the **pan-Eurasian mythological substrate of the Early Bronze Age**. This is not a single "protocivilization" with a single center and dependent peripheries. It is a network of mythological images circulating across a vast space from the Atlantic to the Indus in the era preceding written civilizations.

This network had nodes — places where sacred and technological energy concentrated: Çatalhöyük in Anatolia, Göbekli Tepe in Kurdistan, Ubaid settlements in Mesopotamia, Khvamli in the Caucasus. These nodes were not necessarily connected by direct roads. But they were connected by cultural flows — through metal trade, through ritual pilgrimages, through the transmission of technological knowledge.

Khvamli in this network is the Caucasian node. A place where the *axis mundi* acquired a concrete form: a vertical cliff with a flat summit open to the sky. A place where the blacksmith god Amiran — the structural analogue of Prometheus, Hephaestus, and Ilmarinen — received his name and his chains. A place where people carved motifs into limestone that, thousands of years later, would intertwine with Sumerian cylinder seals, Finnish epic runes, and Greek tragedies.

The stones speak. One only needs to know how to listen.

---

## To the Following Chapters

We conclude the first part of the book. We have established: the Kartvelian languages are an isolate, testifying to an ancient independent civilization (Chapter 4). The history of this civilization is continuous from the Neolithic, though its fixation in available sources is intermittent (Chapter 5). Methodological biases in Western historiography have obscured this continuity (Chapter 6). The Khvamli petroglyphs are a structured symbolic corpus of the Early Bronze Age (Chapter 7). Their motifs have parallels in the pan-Eurasian mythological substrate (Chapter 8).

Now we must answer the next question: how exactly could these motifs have spread? What specific mechanisms stood behind the presumed cultural diffusion? What do we know about the "Khvamli transfer" — to use the term we will arrive at in the fourth part of the book?

To do this, we must first descend into the caves. In the literal sense.

---

*Sources and Notes*

¹ Eliade, M. *Shamanism: Archaic Techniques of Ecstasy*. Moscow: Ladomir, 1998. A classic analysis of the concept of *axis mundi* in world religions.

² George, A.R. *The Epic of Gilgamesh: The Babylonian Epic Poem and Other Texts in Akkadian and Sumerian*. London: Penguin, 2003. The standard academic edition of the epic with commentary.

³ Frankfort, H. *Cylinder Seals: A Documentary Essay on the Art and Religion of the Ancient Near East*. London: Macmillan, 1939. A fundamental work on Sumerian glyptics — an iconographic source for comparative analysis.

⁴ Lönnrot, E. *Kalevala*. Helsinki, 1835 (Finnish); Russian trans.: Moscow: Nauka, 1998. The primary source for the image of Ilmarinen.

⁵ Burkert, W. *The Orientalizing Revolution: Near Eastern Influence on Greek Culture in the Early Archaic Age*. Harvard University Press, 1992. A classic work on the transmission routes of Near Eastern cultural motifs to Greece.

⁶ Wirth, H. *The Origin of Humanity*. Translation from German. A brief overview of the concept of the Eurasian mythological substrate from contemporary perspectives.

⁷ Cauvin, J. *The Birth of the Gods and the Origins of Agriculture*. Cambridge University Press, 2000. The context of the Neolithic Revolution in the Near East and the formation of the first mythological systems.

---

*Next Chapter: Crypts of the Millennia — The Cave Systems of Khvamli.*

---

# Chapter 9. Crypts of the Millennia

> "A cave is not a place one goes to. It is a place one stays in. Forever."
>
> — Folk saying of the Racha highlanders
---

There are things that mountains do better than any archive.

Papyrus rots. A clay tablet crumbles to dust if it falls into acidic soil. Parchment burns. But a vertical karst cavity in limestone, descending twenty meters from the surface, is not a storage facility that was built. It is a storage facility that already existed. Nature accomplished what no civilization could: it created the perfect archive.

Khvamli stands on carbonate limestones. For millions of years, acidic rainwater, seeping through cracks in the rock, dissolved calcite—and left cavities in its wake. At first narrow, like cuts. Then wider. Then—shafts, galleries, halls. Karst does not ask for permission. It simply works.

And in this work lies one of Khvamli's main secrets.

---

## The Geology of Time

To understand why the caves of Khvamli are not merely beautiful cavities but actual storage facilities, one must understand the karst process from within.

Limestone is calcium and carbon, compressed under pressure for millions of years. When rainwater becomes saturated with carbon dioxide from the soil, it turns into a weak carbonic acid. It is this acid that dissolves limestone. Slowly—a millimeter per millennium. But over a million years, that's meters. Over ten million—vertical shafts.

The vertical caves of Khvamli formed precisely this way: rainwater, following cracks and zones of tension, went down—and disappeared. Behind it, expanding year after year, cavities stretched. The result is what speleologists call a *ponor*: a vertical passage descending into the depths of the mountain massif.

Such geology has one unique property: the temperature inside is constant. Not the temperature outside. Not the temperature on the stone's surface in summer or winter. Deeper than five meters from the entrance begins the zone of perpetual constancy: plus eight to plus twelve degrees Celsius, year-round. Humidity—high, but stable. Light—zero. Air movement—minimal.

This is the archival regime.

Organic matter under such conditions does not merely decompose slowly—it can mummify. Tissues, skin, wood. Bone is preserved for hundreds of years without special treatment. Metal—does not rust if there is no capillary water flow. Pottery—remains immobile for millennia.

Khvamli offered precisely this. And—judging by what was found there in 1939, 1945, and 1948–1949—its offer was accepted.

---

## Tekenteli: The Shaft with a Lake

The Tekenteli cave is an entrance to another world, literally and physically.

Outside—a green forest on the slope of Khvamli. In summer, it smells of beech and moss, and nothing suggests that a void opens beneath one's feet. The entrance is camouflaged: it is hidden among the roots of a hollow old beech tree, whose trunk long ago fused with the limestone. One must know where to look. The locals knew. First and foremost—the shepherds who grazed their livestock here in summer and who were the first to find what emerged from the ground in spring—bones, pottery shards, sometimes coins.

In fact, the name "Tekenteli" itself is Georgian, from a root related to the concept of "going down," "descending." It is not a place name—it is a description of a process.

The entrance descends vertically. About eighteen meters—this is not a stairwell, it is a full-fledged shaft. Descent is only possible with a rope. From below—the sound of water.

This is where what transforms Tekenteli from just a cave into a place deliberately chosen for storage begins: beneath the vertical shaft lies an underground lake. Small—a few meters in diameter. It is fed by infiltration waters from the Khvamli massif. It does not flow anywhere—or almost anywhere, seeping away through micropores in the rock. It reflects the light of flashlights if one shines down from the edge of the shaft. A mirror in the darkness.

Next to the lake—a waterfall. Thin, almost thread-like. It falls from the ceiling of one of the side galleries that branch off horizontally from the main shaft. In winter, it freezes—an ice stalactite instead of water. In summer—it flows.

It was precisely in these side galleries that rock niches were discovered. Not natural cracks—but niches that were clearly widened by humans. Tool marks on the stone. In them—artifacts: pottery, tentatively dated to the 12th–13th centuries AD. Red figures of horsemen on the walls of the niches—not petroglyphs in the usual sense, but rather dedicatory paintings. Someone came here, descended into the darkness, made an image, and left. Why?

We will return to this question. First—the second site.

---

## The Cave of God: The Crystal of Time

The second site of Khvamli is a cave that locals simply call "God." In Georgian, this word has several meanings, one of which is "seal," "sealed." Another—"bottom," "foundation."

The Cave of God is horizontal. It does not descend like Tekenteli, but goes deep into the massif almost horizontally, with a slight incline. The entrance is relatively accessible—low, narrow, but without a vertical descent. This changes its character.

The main distinction of the Cave of God is its thermal regime. Due to ventilation features (practically its complete absence in the main section), what in speleology is called an "ice trap" forms here: cold air, being heavier, accumulates in dead-end sections and does not escape in summer as it does in open caves. The temperature in the depths does not rise above zero even in July.

Hence the folk name—"the frozen cave." Here stand ice stalagmites in places where a drop of water meets the zone of perpetual cold. They form every winter, partially melt in summer—and never disappear completely. Water here does not flow—it stands. More precisely: it slowly crystallizes.

For storing organic matter, these are even more ideal conditions than in Tekenteli. Constant cold, minimal humidity in the ice zone, darkness.

If someone wanted to preserve something not for decades, but for millennia—God was more suitable.

---

## Why Precisely Here

This is not a rhetorical question. It is a precise question.

There are dozens of caves in the Racha mountains. Karst is a widespread geological phenomenon in this region. But it was precisely on Khvamli—and precisely in Tekenteli and God—that artifacts were found. There must be a reason for this, not reducible to chance.

The first reason—inaccessibility. The double cuesta of Khvamli forms a natural barrier. To reach the caves, one must know the route, have equipment for vertical descent (even if just rope), and have light. A random person will not get here. A shepherd following his path—also not. A specific purpose is required.

The second reason—orientation. Both caves are located on the northwestern slope of Khvamli, facing the Rioni River. This is the shaded side of the mountain: less direct sunlight, more stable external temperature, later snowmelt. Local inhabitants knew: the northwestern side is cold and "quiet." Not for dwelling. For storage.

The third reason—water. Tekenteli has its own water source at the bottom—an underground lake. During dry periods, when mountain streams ran low, this lake remained. Any group establishing a long-term camp near the cave (for example, for conducting rituals or guarding treasures) had water within five meters of the wall.

The fourth reason—symbolism. And here we move from geology to anthropology.

---

## The Vertical as a Sacred Axis

Tekenteli descends.

In the system of mythological coordinates described in the previous chapter, the vertical is the *axis mundi*: the axis connecting the upper world with the lower world. The cave shaft is the literal embodiment of this axis. To enter it is to perform a descent into the lower world. To exit is to return.

This is not a metaphor we impose retroactively. It is an archetypal logic traceable across dozens of cultures and millennia. The Knossos labyrinth—a descent into the underground. The Eleusinian Mysteries—the descent of Persephone and her return. Orpheus in the kingdom of the dead. Gilgamesh at the bottom of the sea. David Lewis-Williams, researching Paleolithic cave art, showed that a cave in the archaic consciousness is not merely a shelter, but a transition: beyond its wall lives the "other," and an image drawn on the cave wall is a message to this other.

The red horsemen in the niches of Tekenteli are not mere decoration. They are communication. With whom? With those who remained below. With ancestors. With those for whom what lay in the niches next to the images was left.

The cave is not a place of concealment. It is a place of transmission.

---

## The Capsule as a Concept

When the Soviet Academy of Sciences discovered artifacts in the niches of Tekenteli in 1939, the official interpretation was "a random accumulation of medieval objects." Random—because it was most convenient. Because any other interpretation requires an answer to the question: who, why, and how systematically placed objects in an inaccessible cave on a sheer mountain slope, unreachable without special equipment?

The answer that suggests itself is inconvenient for the "peripheral" concept of the Caucasus. Because it presupposes the existence of a tradition: a stable, continuous, purposeful practice of placing valuable objects in an archiving environment.

The concept of a "time capsule" was not invented in the 20th century. It was rediscovered. The first known example of intentional archiving—cuneiform tablets in clay envelopes, placed in sealed jars under the floors of Sumerian houses. Their "addressees" did not need to know how long they would lie there. They simply knew: this is not for now. This is for later.

The niches of Tekenteli follow the same logic. Vertical descent. Stable temperature. A niche in the wall. An object. An image nearby—to make it clear: something important lies here.

This is not a burial. Burial is for the dead. This is a storage facility for the living—those who will come later.

---

## What Was Inside

Honesty is required here.

We do not know exactly what the 1939 expedition found. Official publications are sparse: they mention "artifacts in rock niches," "pottery," "red horseman figures approximately from the 12th–13th centuries." That is all that entered the public record. Soviet "special depository" practice was systematic: what was found in search expeditions following legendary treasures was not published in the open press. Not because it was insignificant—but because it was too significant.

An anonymous 14th-century chronicle—the very one preserved in *Kartlis Tskhovreba*—specifically mentions: in 1259, during the division of the country between cousins David VI and David VII under Mongol rule, part of the royal regalia remained on Khvamli. "The famous diamond chain, an exquisite ornament in the shape of an anvil, and a great pearl, the like of which no one had seen." The chronicle was written a century after the events, but according to Georgian tradition, it relies on earlier sources.

This is not folklore. It is a dynastic document. It was not written for tourists—it was written for those who governed the country.
If in the 13th century royal regalia were stored in Khvamli, then the caves were used as a repository even earlier. Because a repository is not created for a specific object. First, a location is chosen. Then, what needs to be preserved is brought there.

---

## Water as a Boundary

The underground lake in Tekenteli is not a landscape detail. It is a boundary.

In most mythological systems, water marks the transition between worlds. The Styx is the river of death. Springs at the entrance to the underworld from which one must not drink. The underground rivers of the Celtic Otherworld — the Síd. The Georgian tradition is no exception: in the Nart epic, which preserves layers of archaic mythology, the transition to the lower world is invariably linked to water.

Whoever placed an object in the niche of Tekenteli had to descend past this lake. Down a vertical shaft — into darkness — to the water — and then, into a side gallery. This is a path structurally identical to an initiatory descent. Not necessarily a literally religious one. But inevitably — psychologically transformative.

The shepherds who occasionally descended into Tekenteli told the same story: below, the air is "different." Not colder, not warmer. Different. It smells of time. This is not a metaphor — it is physics: in a closed karst cavity, the concentration of CO₂ is elevated, the air composition is indeed different. The human brain reacts under such conditions: breathing accelerates slightly, senses become slightly sharper. The ancients attributed this to the presence of spirits. We know the chemical mechanism. But the effect is the same.

---

## Soviet Expeditions and the Wall of Silence

1939. Soviet scientists discover artifacts in the niches of Tekenteli.

1945. A repeat expedition.

1948–1949. An expedition led by Aliosh Japaridze — the legendary Georgian mountaineer, first conqueror of Ushba in 1934, a man entrusted with difficult routes. According to available data, the order came personally from Stalin. The report was to land on the leader's desk. It did. And disappeared into the special depository.

The next page in open scientific literature — 1986. Soviet exploratory work. Hearths and pottery. Sparsely. Procedurally. Nothing about what was below — in the shafts.

Thirty-seven years of silence between 1949 and 1986. This is not because they found nothing there. What is not found is not classified. What is classified is what was found.

What exactly — we will examine in the third part of this book, when we read the archival document.

But for now — the fact: the caves of Khvamli were important enough for Stalin to send his best man there with a personal order. This is not a tourist excursion. This is a state operation.

---

## Millennium by Millennium

Starting from approximately the 10th century AD — according to data from the Gokhnari necropolis at the foot of the mountain — Khvamli was continuously used as a sacred space. A cemetery at the foot. The Church of St. George on the slope — 12th–13th centuries, with a donor inscription by Ioane, eristavi of the Svans. Niches with artifacts deep in Tekenteli — dating to the same centuries.

These are not random coincidences. This is a landscape organized around a single idea: here is a sacred place. Here they bury the dead. Here they build a church. Here they leave important things in the depths.

But when did it begin? The 12th–13th century inscriptions are merely what medieval Georgia left behind. The petroglyphs on the surface of the rocks, which we examined in previous chapters, date significantly earlier — based on stylistic analysis, presumably to the 6th–5th millennia BC (though this dating is without absolute methods — the question remains open).

If the petroglyphs are that ancient — what prevents the caves from being equally ancient? There is no organic material here for radiocarbon dating. There are no layers to read stratigraphy. There are only niches in the walls and what was extracted from them.

What exactly was in the niches before the 12th century is unknown. Possibly nothing. Possibly something.

The question "why here?" remains, but now sounds different: not "is this place accidental?", but "how long ago was it chosen?"

---

## An Archive Without a Catalogue

There is one feature of cave archiving that we tend to overlook.

A conventional archive is a catalogue. A document with an inventory. An access system. The repository functions because someone knows what is inside.

A cave archive functions differently. It functions even if no one remembers what is inside anymore. A vertical shaft, stable temperature, darkness — they preserve an object regardless of whether anyone above remembers it. This is a repository that continues to function after the last keeper has died.

This is precisely why — millennia later — the Soviet expeditions found what they found. Not because someone left instructions. But because the cave itself preserved what was placed in it.

Khvamli is an archive without a catalogue. The most reliable type of archive possible.

---

The water in the underground lake of Tekenteli rises and falls with the seasons. Ice stalactites in Boge grow and melt, grow and melt — year after year, millennium after millennium. The niches in the walls of the side galleries stand empty — or seem empty.

The Soviet expeditions took what they took. The reports went to the special depository. The mountain is silent.

But silence is not an answer. Silence is a condition. A condition under which everything inside remains preserved until someone comes with the right question and the right light.

---

*Next chapter: Mute Witnesses — the Petroglyphs of Khvamli, a complete corpus of images and their classification system.*

---

# Chapter 10. Mute Witnesses: The Petroglyphs of Khvamli

> "Stone does not lie. It simply remains silent — until a person is found who knows how to ask the right question."
>
> — Folk saying of the Racha highlanders

---

There is a special moment in a researcher's work — the moment when you stop looking *at* the stone and begin to see what is depicted *on* it.

This is not a metaphor. It is a literal perceptual shift. While looking, you see the rough surface of gray limestone, darkened by patina, riddled with cracks and pits. Then — at some point — a figure begins to emerge from the chaos. A line. A contour. And suddenly you realize this is not a natural crack, but an intentional groove. That someone — thousands of years ago — stood here with a stone tool in hand and pecked out precisely this, and not that.

This is exactly what happened to Chubinashvili in 1936 on the slopes of Khvamli. And this is exactly what happens to everyone who comes here with sufficient patience.

---

## The First Encounter

The rock carvings of Khvamli are not located in the caves — or not only in the caves. Some of them are on open rock surfaces, on limestone outcrops on the northwestern and western slopes of the mountain. Where the stone emerges as almost vertical slabs facing the valley of the Rioni River.

Why precisely these slabs? A question every researcher asks. The answer has several parts.

First, orientation. The slabs face west — towards where the sun sets, where the valley lies, where people were. The images on them were visible — at least partially — from a certain distance. They were not hidden. They were displayed.

Second, surface. This limestone is fine-grained, dense, and well-suited for working with stone tools. It crumbles predictably: the groove comes out clear, without unexpected chips. The master who pecked the drawing worked with a material he knew.

Third — and this is most important — height. Most images are located at a height of one to three meters from the base of the slab. This is at human eye level. This is not a "high place" — it is the level of gaze. Therefore, the drawings were not created to be seen from above or below. They were created at eye level — for those standing nearby.

This is ritual logic, not decorative.

---

## The Corpus of Images: What Exactly is Here

The Chubinashvili expedition of 1936 recorded several groups of images, commonly referred to as the "Khvamli petroglyphic complex." This refers not to a single plane, but to several — scattered across the slope over several hundred meters.

The total number of images in publications varies: figures from 40 to more than 80 individual figures are cited. This uncertainty is easily explained: some images are barely distinguishable, some overlap each other in different chronological layers, some are questionable: is it a drawing or a natural crack?

Several categories are distinguished by type.

**Zoomorphic figures** constitute the largest share. Primarily — ungulates. Among them, deer dominate: they are recognizable by their characteristic branching antlers, rendered by two or three diverging lines from the head. The postures of the deer are static or dynamic, with elongated legs emphasizing movement. Some are depicted with raised heads — a characteristic posture of alertness in the real animal.

Besides deer — mountain goats (recognizable by their more compact bodies and steeply curved horns), wild boars (angular, massive silhouettes), and figures that could be bears — large, with broad shoulders, on four legs. Precise species identification requires caution: the schematism of execution is high.
A separate category comprises **birds**. Several figures with outstretched wings are located in the upper part of some panels. Their size compared to the animals is significantly larger. If this is not an arbitrary choice of scale, then it is semantics: the bird here is not just a bird, but something more significant in the hierarchy of images.

---

## Anthropomorphic Figures: Humans or Something Else

Human figures are the most complex and most intriguing part of the corpus.

They are schematic to the extreme: a vertical line for the body, horizontal lines for the arms, two lines for the legs. A "lollipop stick" in some art-historical descriptions. But it is precisely in this extreme schematization that information lies.

Because the poses differ. And the differences in poses are, in all likelihood, differences in roles.

Some anthropomorphic figures are depicted in a pose that can be conventionally called "standing": a vertical body, arms lowered or slightly spread apart. This is a standard neutral pose.

Others have raised arms. Arms are raised upward, towards the sky or something overhead. This is a pose found in ritual scenes of practically all known petroglyphic traditions—from Scandinavia to the Sahara. It is interpreted as worship, an appeal to a higher power, or—in a specific ritual context—as undergoing initiation.

Finally, there is a group of figures with a clearly expressed aggressive or hunting pose: a leaning body, an arm with something (a weapon? a spear? a bow?) extended forward. These are hunters at the moment of a throw or shot.

What is interesting is something else: sometimes anthropomorphic and zoomorphic figures are located in close proximity, forming scenes. This is no longer just a list of beings—this is a narrative. A story.

---

## Hunting Scenes: Reading Without Words

Several panels contain what appears to be hunting scenes.

The classic scheme: a human (one or several) with a weapon aimed at an animal. Between them—a short distance on the plane of the stone, implying the tension of the moment. The animal—most often a deer or a goat—is sometimes depicted with legs folded under the body: the pose of a falling or killed animal.

Hunting scenes in petroglyphs are one of the most common motifs of the Stone Age worldwide. From Altamira to Gobustan, from Petrozavodsk to the petroglyphs of Norway's Alta—everywhere: human, animal, the moment of pursuit. This is because hunting is not merely about procuring sustenance. It is a ritual. It is contact with the "other" world—the world of the wild, the world of power, the world that both feeds and kills simultaneously.

At Khvamli, these scenes are read precisely in this way. But here there is one feature that distinguishes the Khvamli complex from many others.

In some hunting scenes, a third element is present: a vertical line—a post, a marker—around which the figures are grouped. This is no longer simply "hunter and prey." This is "hunter, prey, and something third."

What this third element is—is a question to which the next chapter is dedicated.

---

## Geometric Signs: A Language Without an Alphabet

Among the images, there is a category that is the most difficult to describe: geometric signs.

Spirals. Concentric circles. Lattices. Zigzags. Dots, pecked in rows or groups. Circles with rays—something resembling schematic depictions of the sun or a wheel.

What are these? Decoration? Ornament? Or something more significant?

In the global science of rock art, geometric signs are the subject of intense debate. One school considers them "phosphenes"—visual images arising from altered states of consciousness (particularly from the use of hallucinogenic substances or sensory deprivation). Another sees in them early symbols, precursors to writing: not letters, but prototypes of letters, semantic markers.

Researcher David Lewis-Williams, in his theory of the neuropsychological model of cave art, convincingly demonstrated that the first two classes of images—geometric signs and zoomorphic figures—correspond to two stages of altered consciousness. In the first stage—abstract patterns generated by the nervous system. In the second—real images, laden with cultural meaning.

If this is true for French caves of the Paleolithic era—why not for the Khvamli planes?

The spirals and concentric circles of Khvamli—perhaps they are not "decoration." Perhaps they are a map. A map of internal experience—of what a person saw in the darkness of a ritual. Of what they came here to fix in stone.

---

## Surface Stratigraphy: When They Drew on a Drawing

One of the most important features of the Khvamli complex is the presence of superimpositions.

On some planes, certain images are clearly pecked over others. This means the site was used repeatedly—in different eras, by different people. That a tradition existed here: to come to this place and leave a mark.

Early layers, as a rule, are distinguished by deeper pecking and darker oxidation patina. Later ones are shallower, lighter, sometimes technically more sophisticated. However, interpreting this relationship without absolute dating is difficult: the depth of the groove depends not only on age but also on the hardness of the tool, the time the craftsman spent on the work, and their intention.

But the very fact of superimpositions is eloquent. This place was important not just once. It was important again and again. Different hands, different eras, one stone.

In this sense, the Khvamli petroglyphic complex is not a "picture gallery" created in one period. It is a living palimpsest—a text written over a text.

---

## Silence as a Fact

There is something paradoxical in calling these images "silent witnesses."

On the one hand—yes, they are silent. Not a single word. Not a single inscription nearby that would explain: "this is such-and-such a god," "this is a hunt from such-and-such a year," "this is a rite of passage." The one who pecked did not assume that explanations would be needed. The meaning was obvious—to those who knew.

On the other hand—they are not silent. They speak a great deal. They speak of the fact that there was a community here. That this community had a system of symbols. That the system was stable enough to be reproduced over a long period of time. That the place was significant enough for people to return to it again and again.

This is not silence. This is a conversation in a language we have not yet learned.

---

## The First Step Towards Understanding: The Principle of Context

Researchers of rock art have long developed a methodological principle without which work is impossible: **a petroglyph does not exist by itself**. It exists in context—spatial, iconographic, chronological.

Spatial context: where exactly is the figure located? At a height? In a corner of the panel? Near the entrance to a cave or far from it? On the sun-facing side of the stone or in the shade?

Iconographic context: what is depicted nearby? What does this figure relate to—in terms of size, pose, direction of gaze?

Chronological context: is this an early layer or a late one? Was the figure added to an already existing scene or was it part of it from the very beginning?

The Khvamli complex awaits systematic analysis precisely in these three dimensions. The work of Chubinashvili from 1936 is invaluable in its historicity but insufficient by the methodological standards of the 21st century. Photogrammetry is needed, three-dimensional scanning is needed, multispectral imaging is needed—which allows seeing traces of pigment where the naked eye sees only stone.

Until this is done, we are working with an incomplete picture. But even an incomplete picture is still a picture.

---

*Sources and Notes*

¹ Chubinashvili G.N. Report on the Expedition to Khvamli, 1936 / Archive of the Academy of Sciences of the Georgian SSR (exact storage and accessibility—to be verified).

² "Mount Khvamli — a Cultural-Mystical Product of the Development of Sustainable Tourism in Georgia". ResearchGate, 2020 — a basic secondary source on the petroglyphs.

³ Bradshaw Foundation. "Rock Art in Georgia". bradshawfoundation.com/georgia — a general overview of Georgian petroglyphs in a comparative context.

⁴ Lewis-Williams D., Pearce D. "Inside the Neolithic Mind: Consciousness, Cosmos and the Realm of the Gods". Thames & Hudson, 2005. Neuropsychological model for interpreting prehistoric art.

⁵ Clottes J., Lewis-Williams D. "The Shamans of Prehistory: Trance and Magic in the Painted Caves". Abrams, 1998. Theory of the ritual context of cave art.

---

*Next Chapter: Hunters at the Post — the central motif of the Khvamli petroglyphs and its possible interpretations.*

---

# Chapter 11. The Main Motif: "Hunters at the Post"

> "The tree holds up the sky. Without the tree, the sky would fall to the earth."
>
> — Mingrelian proverb

---

Among the entire corpus of Khvamli petroglyphs, there is one scene that is unlike the others. Not in technique—the technique is the same: pecking on limestone, schematic lines. Not in size—the figures are comparable to other anthropomorphic images in the complex. It is unlike the others in its **structure**.

The hunters are not standing before an animal. They are standing before a pillar.

This difference is not stylistic. It is a difference in meaning. And it is precisely this that makes this motif central to understanding not only the Khvamli complex but also—if the working hypothesis is correct—much broader cultural processes.

---

## Anatomy of the Scene

Let us describe what is depicted as accurately as possible—without interpretation.

A vertical line. Straight, quite long by the standards of the panel—it clearly dominates the scene in height. Its upper end sometimes has an expansion—a thickening or a fork. The lower end goes into the base of the panel or is marked by a short horizontal line, hinting at a support.

On either side of this line or at its base—several anthropomorphic figures. Standard "lollipops": a round head, a vertical body, two arms, two legs. But it is precisely their postures that are informative.

Some figures face the pillar. This is determined by the direction of the body—not always clear due to schematism, but often guessed by the tilt of the upper part of the figure or the direction of the outstretched arms.

Arms—raised upward. Not all figures, but a significant portion. Raised arms are one of the most stable gestures in the iconography of ritual. This is not the neutral posture of a person simply standing. It is an active posture. An address. A gesture.

In some versions of the scene, one of the figures differs from the others: it is larger, or located in a special place relative to the pillar—for example, right next to it, touching it with a hand.

And—most importantly—there are no animals in this scene. This is not a hunting group awaiting prey. This is a group of people focused on a vertical object.

---

## What is the Pillar

The first and simplest question: what is physically depicted?

Possible answers lie on a scale from the concrete to the symbolic.

**A Tree.** The most obvious interpretation. A vertical line with an expansion at the top—a schematically depicted tree. The expansion is the crown or a fork in the trunk. In the context of hunting, trees served as territorial markers, ambush sites, landmarks in unfamiliar terrain. "People by a tree"—a perfectly mundane scene.

**A Territorial Marker.** Hunting cultures marked their grounds with vertical signs: a pole stuck in the ground with a special decoration, a felled trunk with notches, a pillar with the skull or horns of prey. A group by such a marker signifies "people on their land" or "people establishing a right."

**A Totem Pole.** In most hunter-gatherer societies, a totem pole is an object of ritual significance, connected to ancestors, spirits of the territory, and clan identity. To gather by a totem pole is to perform an act of community consolidation.

**The Axis Mundi.** The broadest and most significant interpretation, discussed in Chapter 8. The vertical as the *axis mundi*—the axis of the world, connecting the upper world with the lower. The pillar is not just a tree. It is a cosmic principle embodied in a material object.

These interpretations do not exclude each other. A real tree could simultaneously be a territorial marker, a totemic object, and an embodiment of the world axis. In archaic cultures, the sacred and the practical are not separate—they are one.

---

## Parallels in World Petroglyphic Art

To assess how unique or, conversely, universal this motif is, let us turn to comparative material.

**Scandinavia.** The Bronze Age (c. 1800–500 BCE) left hundreds of panels with images of people by trees or pillars. Characteristic scenes at Tanum (Sweden, UNESCO site): anthropomorphic figures by the "world tree," scenes of sacrifice by pillars. Here the motif is unambiguously interpreted as ritual.

**Central Asia.** Petroglyphs of Saimaly-Tash (Kyrgyzstan, c. 3000–1000 BCE): numerous scenes by vertical objects, sometimes identified as "world mountains" or "world trees." Anthropomorphic figures with raised arms are a standard element.

**The Caucasus.** Trialeti petroglyphs (Georgia, Mesolithic–Bronze Age) contain scenes with vertical objects, although the "pillar" as a central element is less pronounced there.

**The Near East.** Sumerian glyptics of the 3rd millennium BCE: cylinder seals consistently reproduce the "scene by the tree of life"—two figures on either side of a vertical object. This is almost an exact structural copy of the Khvamli motif, though separated by millennia and thousands of kilometers.

The prevalence of the motif—on the one hand—makes the Khvamli images "ordinary." Nothing unique: the same as everywhere. But on the other hand—it is precisely this prevalence that raises the question: why does the same motif reproduce in such different cultures? Is it convergence (parallel independent development of one answer to one question) or diffusion (spread through contact)?

---

## Khvamli as the Pillar

Here we must pause and take a step that may seem bold but is in fact logical.

What if the pillar in the petroglyph is not just a vertical object? What if it is Khvamli itself?

Let us recall: Khvamli is a double cuesta. Its geological structure is such that from the west, from the Rioni valley, the mountain looks precisely like a vertical rock massif with a flat top. Its profile is a vertical, crowned by a horizontal. This is the schematic depiction of that very pillar with a "fork" or "expansion" at the top.

If the petroglyphs are located on the slopes of the mountain itself, then depicting the mountain while standing on it is a logical meditative practice. "Hunters by a pillar"—this is an image of people by a mountain. Which is Khvamli. Which is the axis mundi.

This is not a proven fact. It is a working hypothesis. But a hypothesis that unites several observations:
- Khvamli visually resembles a "pillar" when viewed from the west.
- In Georgian mythology, Khvamli is the place where Amiran was chained, i.e., literally a point connecting the upper and lower worlds.
- Khvamli was associated with Venus—an astral marker defining the axis between heaven and earth.
- The petroglyphs are located on the mountain itself—meaning they were created by those who came to it.

---

## Who Stands by the Pillar: Interpretations of the Figures

Who are the "hunters"?

The word "hunters" is the most common in descriptions of this type of petroglyphic scene, and it is not entirely accurate. This word reflects a profession, not a role. In archaic societies, roles are not separate: the one who hunts may also perform a ritual. The one who performs a ritual is often precisely a hunter.

Nevertheless, let us try to list possible roles.

**Initiates.** In most hunter-gatherer societies, adolescent initiation included a rite of the "first hunt" or "meeting with the spirit of the territory." A group by a totem pole could depict the initiates—young people making first contact with the sacred center.

**Hunters Before Departure.** Many petroglyphic scenes are interpreted as "hunting magic"—a ritual "hunting assignment" performed before the actual departure for prey. To draw is to anticipate. An image of a successful hunt by a sacred marker is both a prayer and a program.

**Priests by an Altar.** The broadest interpretation: the pillar is an altar, the figures beside it are participants in a religious ritual. Raised arms are a prayerful gesture.

**Ancestors.** In a number of traditions, it was not the living but ancestors—those who had already passed on—who were depicted by the totem pole, their presence by the pillar ensuring the connection between worlds. "Hunters by a pillar" may be not a scene from life but a scene from posthumous existence.

We do not know which of these interpretations is correct. Perhaps all are correct—in different chronological layers, in different epochs, when different people came to the same stone and left their image by the same pillar.

---

## Addition to the Scene: An Animal Between People and the Pillar

On one of the panels of the Khvamli complex, a layout has been recorded that deserves special attention.

Between the anthropomorphic figures and the vertical pillar—a zoomorphic figure. An animal, whose identification is debatable (possibly a deer or goat), is positioned as if "inside" the scene, between the people and the pillar. It is not fleeing or attacking. It is standing.

Is this animal a sacrifice? A guardian? A mediator between people and the sacred center?

In the Sumerian tradition, an animal—usually a goat or sheep—is often depicted at the base of the "tree of life." In Egyptian iconography, protective animals may be depicted by the "Djed pillar" (a symbol of resurrection and eternity). In Norse mythology, the deer Eikþyrnir grazes by Yggdrasil, and the serpent Níðhöggr lies at its roots.

An animal by the world axis is a stable symbolic type. Its presence in the Khvamli scene is not accidental.

---

## Why This Motif is the Key

The "hunters by a pillar" motif is important not in itself. It is important as an **entry point** into a broader system.
If this is a depiction of people at the axis mundi, then it indicates that the society that created it possessed a cosmological system. Moreover—it had a ritual practice associated with this system. And it had a tradition of recording this practice in stone.

This is not "primitive hunters drawing what they see." This is "an archaic society creating a sign system to transmit meaning across time."

The difference is fundamental.

And this is precisely why the comparison with Sumerian seals, Egyptian Djed pillars, and Scandinavian ritual scenes is neither a stretch nor exoticism. It is a juxtaposition of samples of the same sign program, realized in different places and in different eras.

Khvamli is one of the nodes of this program. Possibly—one of the early nodes.

---

*Sources and Notes*

¹ Eliade, M. *Shamanism: Archaic Techniques of Ecstasy*. Moscow: Ladomir, 1998. The concept of axis mundi and its embodiment in ritual objects.

² Anati, E. *World Rock Art: The Primordial Language*. Capo di Ponte: Edizioni del Centro, 1994. Comparative analysis of the "human figure by a vertical object" motif in world rock art.

³ Frankfort, H. *Cylinder Seals*. London: Macmillan, 1939. Sumerian glyptics: scenes by the tree of life.

⁴ Aldhouse-Green, M. *Seeing the Wood for the Trees: The Symbolism of Trees and Wood in Ancient Gaul and Britain*. Aberystwyth, 2000. Totemic trees and ritual pillars in the European tradition.

⁵ Simek, R. *Dictionary of Northern Mythology*. Cambridge: Brewer, 1993. Yggdrasil and the concept of the world tree in the Scandinavian tradition.

---

*Next Chapter: Style and Time — The Problem of Dating the Petroglyphs and Arguments For and Against Their Attribution to the 6th–5th Millennia BCE*

---

# Chapter 12. Style and Time

> "Time is not read from stone. But stone remembers time."
>
> — Georgian Proverb

---

Any researcher working with petroglyphs sooner or later encounters the same question—a question that seems simple but proves to be the most difficult of all possible.

When was this made?

For rock art, this is not idle curiosity. Everything depends on the answer: the interpretation of motifs, cultural attribution, its place in the chronology of human history. A drawing created 7,000 years ago and a drawing created 700 years ago are fundamentally different objects, even if they look similar.

Regarding the petroglyphs of Khvamli, the problem of dating is particularly acute—and must be presented with particular honesty. *(Detailed analysis of modern dating methods and proposals for their application to Khvamli — Chapters 51–52; methodological conditions for verification — Appendix D.)* Because the situation here is as follows: **part of the images have reliable dating, and part do not**. And these categories must not be mixed.

---

## What is Reliably Dated

Let's start with what is firmly known.

The "Red Figures"—anthropomorphic depictions of horsemen, applied in red pigment (ochre) on the walls of rock niches in the Tekenteli cave—are dated to the 12th–13th centuries CE. This is not an assumption. This is the result of comprehensive analysis: iconographic (depiction of horsemen, type of clothing, character of the horse) and stylistic. The 12th–13th centuries are the period of the flourishing of medieval Georgia, the era of Queen Tamar. Rock niches during this period were actively used as places for dedicatory paintings, hiding places for treasures, and secret chapels.

These images are a well-understood and well-dated layer. They are not controversial.

Difficulties begin with other images—those pecked on open rock surfaces. These are not figures painted with pigment, but petroglyphs in the strict sense of the word: grooves pecked into the stone.

---

## The Problem: No Organic Material

The main challenge in dating petroglyphs is methodological.

Radiocarbon analysis (C-14)—the primary tool for absolute chronology of prehistoric objects—works with organic material: wood, bone, charcoal, leather. A groove in limestone contains no organic material. It cannot be "dated" by C-14.

Alternative methods exist.

**Patina.** Stone, exposed to atmospheric effects, becomes covered with a layer of oxides and organic compounds—patina. Older surfaces have darker patina. The difference in patina color between the drawing itself (fresh groove) and the surrounding rock can indicate relative age: the lighter the groove relative to the background, the younger it is. Problem: the rate of patina formation depends on microclimate, rock type, humidity. It does not provide absolute dates.

**OSL (Optically Stimulated Luminescence).** This method measures the accumulated radiation dose in mineral crystals since their last exposure to light. Applied to petroglyphs: if thin mineral deposits have formed over the groove, the moment of their formation can be dated. It works, but requires special sampling conditions.

**U-Th Dating of Speleothems.** If a calcite flowstone (speleothem) has grown over a petroglyph, it can be dated using the uranium-thorium decay method. This gives a date not for the drawing itself, but a minimum age: the drawing was made no later than the flowstone formed. On the open rock surfaces of Khvamli, such flowstones have not yet been studied.

**Stylistic Analysis.** An old and most vulnerable method. The essence: compare the style of the images with analogues from dated contexts. If a drawing resembles those dated to the 6th millennium BCE in another region—then it is "probably" from the same time. This is not dating in the strict sense. This is attribution by analogy.

---

## Where the Figure "6th–5th Millennia BCE" Comes From

In a number of tourist and semi-popular sources describing Mount Khvamli, the dating appears: "the rock drawings date to the 6th–5th millennia BCE." Where does it come from?

The honest answer: it is not precisely known. Academic verification of this figure in available peer-reviewed publications is difficult. Perhaps it originates from the stylistic analysis of Chubinashvili or his colleagues working in the 1930s. Perhaps—from later Georgian-language publications not accessible in Western databases.

What can be said with certainty: stylistically, the Khvamli petroglyphs are similar to Caucasian rock images, which in different regions are dated from the Chalcolithic to the Early Bronze Age—approximately the 5th–3rd millennia BCE. This does not exclude either an earlier or a later dating.

For comparison: the petroglyphs of Gobustan (Azerbaijan, UNESCO) have AMS dating of around 5900 BCE for the earliest layers. The Trialeti petroglyphs of Georgia—by style and context—are attributed to the Mesolithic–Early Bronze Age. This provides a framework for comparison.

But a framework for comparison is not a date.

---

## Arguments in Favor of an Ancient Age

Despite methodological caution, there are several arguments that make an early dating of the Khvamli petroglyphs plausible.

**First argument — fauna.** The animals depicted in the Khvamli petroglyphs are the wild fauna of the mountain forest: deer, bears, goats. Not a single horse with a rider (unlike the medieval "Red Figures"). No clear depiction of domestic livestock. This is a hunter's world, not a pastoralist's. In the Racha region, hunting was the main economy until the Bronze Age, gradually giving way to pastoralism by the middle of the 2nd millennium BCE. The zoomorphic composition of Khvamli suggests a hunting era.

**Second argument — technique.** The grooves of the Khvamli petroglyphs were made using the pecking (piquetage) method—a percussive tool on stone. This is an archaic technique, preceding polishing and engraving. In the Chalcolithic–Early Bronze Age, pecking was standard. Later—with the spread of metal tools—styles changed.

**Third argument — schematism.** A high degree of schematization of anthropomorphic and zoomorphic figures is characteristic of the Chalcolithic and Early Bronze Age in Eurasia. Later petroglyphs are typically more detailed or, conversely, more narrative (depicting complex scenes with many characters).

**Fourth argument — patina.** According to available descriptions, the grooves of the petroglyphs are significantly darker than the surrounding rock—which indirectly indicates considerable age. But, as stated above, patina is not an absolute method.

---

## Skeptics' Arguments

Any honest analysis requires considering objections.

**First objection — accessibility.** Khvamli is a hard-to-reach place. If the petroglyphs existed here for millennia, why are there no mentions of them in sources before the 19th century? The first scientific descriptions—1936. This is late.

Answer: many petroglyphic complexes in the Caucasus and the Near East were discovered or described in the 19th–20th centuries, although they existed much earlier. Inaccessibility is a double-edged argument: it simultaneously explains the lateness of discovery and testifies to the intentional isolation of the place.

**Second objection — mixing of layers.** On the same surfaces, images from different eras are pecked. How can one date the "Khvamli complex" as a whole if it includes layers from the Bronze Age to the Middle Ages?

Answer: one cannot. This is precisely why the correct approach is to date not the "complex," but individual layers. Early layers—with one dating, later layers—with another.

**Third objection — stylistic analogies are unreliable.** Schematic figures with raised arms were created in very different eras—from the Paleolithic to the Iron Age. One cannot say by style "this is the 6th millennium," if the same style existed in the 3rd millennium and the 1st.
This is the most serious objection. Stylistic analysis is indeed insufficient without additional data.

---

## An Honest Conclusion: What We Know and What We Don't

The situation regarding the dating of the Khvamli petroglyphs is neither a scientific failure nor a conspiracy. It is the normal state of research for a monument to which modern methods of absolute dating have not yet been applied.

What we know:
- The medieval "red figures" — 12th–13th centuries CE. This is reliable.
- The pecked petroglyphs on open rock surfaces — are presumably significantly older, possibly the Chalcolithic–Early Bronze Age (5th–3rd millennia BCE). This is plausible.
- Absolute dating using OSL, U-Th, or AMS methods — has not been conducted. This is a gap.

What we do not know:
- The precise age of any of the pecked petroglyphs.
- The number of chronological layers within the complex.
- Whether the site had a single period of creation or was used continuously for millennia.

This honest acknowledgment of limitations does not diminish the significance of the complex—it underscores the necessity of its study. The Khvamli petroglyphs are a monument about which we know enough to understand its importance, and not enough to know its place in human chronology.

---

## Why This Matters: The Stakes of Dating

The difference between "the 4th millennium BCE" and "the 12th century CE" is not just numbers.

If the Khvamli petroglyphs date to the 4th–5th millennia BCE, then they are contemporary with the Ubaid and early Uruk cultures of Mesopotamia—precisely those eras when the first visual symbolic systems, precursors to writing, were forming. In this case, comparing Khvamli motifs with Sumerian glyptics is a comparison of synchronous phenomena. And the discussion about the diffusion of ideas becomes a historical conversation: could a Caucasian motif have influenced a Mesopotamian one, or did both arise from a common source?

If, however, the petroglyphs date to the 1st millennium BCE or later—they are post-synchronous with Sumer. Then they are not a "source"—they are a "periphery" that experienced influence. Or an independent parallel development without a temporal connection.

Dating is not a detail. Dating is the argument in the book's central question.
