---

## A Call to Action

This chapter ends where the real work should begin.

A field expedition employing modern methods is necessary:
- Multispectral imaging of panels to detect hidden pigment layers.
- OSL dating of sedimentary deposits covering the grooves.
- U-Th dating of calcite flowstones, if discovered.
- Photogrammetric 3D scanning for precise documentation of the entire corpus of images.

This is technologically possible. Legally—Khvamli is a monument of national significance in Georgia (Government Decree No. 123, 2020), which provides a legal basis for conducting conservation and research work. Organizationally—it is a matter of political will and funding.

Until this work is done, the dating of the Khvamli petroglyphs remains an open question. A beautiful mystery—and simultaneously a concrete task for 21st-century science.

---

*Sources and Notes*

¹ Clottes J. «What Is Paleolithic Art? Cave Paintings and the Dawn of Human Creativity». University of Chicago Press, 2016. Methodology for dating rock art: an overview of modern approaches.

² Bradshaw Foundation. «Rock Art in Georgia». bradshawfoundation.com/georgia — description of Khvamli and Trialeti petroglyphs.

³ UNESCO. «Gobustan Rock Art Cultural Landscape». AMS dating of Gobustan petroglyphs: 7850 ± 30 BP.

⁴ Wikipedia. «Trialeti petroglyphs». Chronology and dating methodology of Georgian petroglyphs.

⁵ «Mount Khvamli — a Cultural-Mystical Product of the Development of Sustainable Tourism in Georgia». ResearchGate, 2020. Standard source for dating Khvamli images.

⁶ Watchman A. «A Review of the History of Dating Rock Varnish». Earth-Science Reviews 49, 1999. Methodology of patina dating.

---

*Next Chapter: Treasures That Survived Empires — an anonymous 14th-century chronicle about the royal treasury on Khvamli and the mystery of the diamond chain.*

---

# Chapter 13. Treasures That Survived Empires

> "Gold does not seek an owner. The owner seeks gold."
>
> — Georgian proverb

---

1259. The Mongol hegemony over Georgia is at its peak.

A country that just a century earlier was one of the most powerful states in the Caucasus—under Queen Tamar (1184–1213) its borders stretched from the Black Sea to the Caspian—is now fragmented, humiliated, and devastated. Mongol tumens swept through in 1220, then in 1236, and again in 1243. Each time—cities burned, people died or fled to the mountains.

In 1259, something happens that chroniclers of the next century will deem important enough to record: two cousins, both named David, divide between themselves the remnants of what was once a great kingdom.

David VI Narin (the Younger) receives western Georgia—Imereti. David VII Ulu (the Elder) receives the east—Kartli and Kakheti.

And it is here—in this sorrowful act of partitioning the country under the gaze of Mongol governors—that Khvamli appears.

---

## *Kartlis Tskhovreba*: The Chronicle as a Source

*Kartlis Tskhovreba*—"The Life of Kartli"—is not just a book. It is a corpus. Georgian medieval historiography, compiled into a single collection—annals, chronicles, historical tales, covering the period from the mythological beginnings of Georgian history to the 18th century.

Among the texts included in this corpus is an anonymous 14th-century chronicle—sometimes conventionally called the "Chronicle of a Hundred Years" or the "Brief Chronicle." It records events of the 13th–14th centuries from the perspective of a contemporary or an eyewitness of the next generation.

This text is not literature. It is a document. It is written in a businesslike language, without rhetorical embellishments, with specific names, dates, and enumerations. It is precisely here that the key fragment about Khvamli is contained.

Context: the description of the division of Georgia between the two Davids in 1259. Among the things that were divided or remained undivided—the royal regalia. And here the chronicler names specific items.

---

## Three Items: The Diamond Chain, the Anvil-Shaped Ornament, the Great Pearl

The chronicle lists three objects that remained in the repository on Khvamli.

The first—"the famous diamond chain" (in some translations: "brilliant chain"). The second—"the excellent ornament in the shape of an anvil." The third—"the great pearl, the like of which no one had seen."

Let us examine each item separately.

**The Diamond Chain.** This is not an ornament in the sense of a necklace for a noble lady. In medieval Georgian tradition, a chain is a regalia of power. Chains of precious stones were bestowed upon rulers as a sign of legitimization; they feature in investiture ceremonies. "Diamond" here—is not merely an indication of material. "Diamond" in the medieval Georgian lexicon could denote both a diamond in the modern sense and any particularly hard and transparent stone—sapphire, topaz, rock crystal. What is important is different: this chain is "famous"—meaning it was known, possessing a reputation long before the moment the chronicler writes about it.

**The Ornament in the Shape of an Anvil.** This is a mysterious object. An anvil (Georgian: *ჩანჩquri* or a similar term)—is a blacksmith's tool. In Georgian mythology, the blacksmith holds an exceptional place: the sound of the forge is the sound of the chains of Amiran, chained on Khvamli. The New Year—the New Year's Blacksmith's Ring—is a ritual to drive away evil spirits. An "ornament in the shape of an anvil" at the repository on the mountain of Amiran—this is not a coincidence. This is an object carrying cosmological meaning.

**The Great Pearl, the Like of Which No One Had Seen.** Pearl—a traditional symbol of perfection and rarity in all Eurasian cultures. "The like of which no one had seen"—this is not a boastful hyperbole. It is a formulaic expression meaning: the object is unique, it has no equal in the known world. In medieval Georgia, a large pearl could be a symbol of special divine election: "heavenly pearl," "purity"—are stable metaphors both in Christian symbolism and in prehistoric fertility cults.

---

## Where This Was: The Khvamli Caves as a State Repository
The chronicle does not merely name objects. It points to a location: this treasure was stored on Khvamli.

Why?

The answer lies in geology and political logic simultaneously.

A mountain stronghold, accessible only to those who know the route. Caves with a constant temperature, inaccessible to a random person. A place possessing a sacred reputation—that is, protected not only physically but by religious fear. "You shall not enter there without the god's permission"—this is not just a saying. It is a systemic defense.

Georgian kings, it appears, used the caves of Khvamli as a repository for state valuables not only in the 13th century. The tradition could be significantly older. The very wording of the chronicle—"the greater part of the treasures remained in the caves"—suggests that this refers to an established practice, not an emergency measure.

During the Mongol advance, the Georgian nobility hid valuables in the mountains. Khvamli is the ideal place for this: height, inaccessibility, a sacred status which meant that the local inhabitants would not plunder the "mountain of god."

But the history of the repository is clearly older than the Mongol invasion. If in 1259 "famous" (i.e., legendary) regalia were already stored there—then they arrived there long before. Possibly during the reign of Queen Tamar, who, according to a number of Georgian sources, revered Khvamli as a special sacred place. Possibly—even earlier.

---

## Context: Khvamli and Georgian Royal Ideology

In medieval Georgian historiography, there exists a stable tradition linking royal power with sacred mountains.

Mtskheta—the first capital of Kartli—is located at the confluence of the Kura and Aragvi rivers, surrounded by mountains. It is here, according to Georgian tradition, that the Life-Giving Pillar stood, which brought Christianity to Georgia. It is here that the main temple—Svetitskhoveli—was located.

Ananuri, Atskuri, Vardzia—each of the great Georgian monastery-fortresses is hidden in the mountains, in almost inaccessible places. This is not only defensive logic—it is the theology of place. The sacred must be difficult to attain. That which is accessible is not sacred.

Khvamli is the pinnacle of this logic in both the literal and figurative sense. It is higher. It is more inaccessible. It is older than any monastery.

And if kings stored regalia there—it means they understood: Khvamli does not merely store physically. Khvamli sanctifies. An object stored on a sacred mountain receives additional legitimacy—it is "celestial," it is "heavenly," it belongs not merely to a kingdom, but to the order of things.

---

## What Happened to the Treasures

The chronicle says that in 1259 the greater part of the valuables "remained in the caves." But what happened afterwards?

Here history falls silent.

Or almost silent.

In the 15th–16th centuries, Georgia experienced new invasions—first by Tamerlane (1386–1403), then, in the 16th century, by Ottoman and Persian armies. Each wave of destruction could have meant an attempt to find or, conversely, to further hide the valuables.

In the 1840s, Prince Dadiani—one of the rulers of western Georgia—undertook excavations on Khvamli. According to the data of the French historian Brosset, who worked in Russia and described this episode in his "Report on an Archaeological Journey in Georgia and Armenia"—the excavations yielded no result. Or, at least, no publicly declared result.

"Yielded no result" and "found nothing" are not the same thing. In the 19th century, in a feudal context, a successful find might simply not have been announced.

Later—Soviet expeditions. 1939. 1945. 1948–1949. And—silence.

Do the 13th-century treasures lie in the depths of Khvamli? Or have they long been gone? Or are the "treasures" a metaphor, and does it refer to something valued in the 14th century but which lost meaning for people of the 20th century?

We do not know.

---

## The Symbolism of the Repository: Valuables as Signs

But it is important not only "what was in the repository." It is important—"why precisely these things."

A diamond chain. An anvil-ornament. The great pearl.

Three objects. Let us consider them as a system, not as a list.

A chain is a connection, a joining. In medieval symbolism, a chain binds, obligates, connects a vassal to a suzerain, a person to god. A diamond chain is an indestructible, eternal bond.

An anvil is creation, transformation, strength. It is Amiran, chained to Khvamli. It is the blacksmith's ring of the New Year. It is metal, which was the bread of ancient Georgia.

A pearl is perfection, uniqueness, purity. Something born from a living creature (a mollusk), having passed time in darkness and moisture—and having become beautiful. A metaphor for preservation that makes a thing valuable.

Chain + anvil + pearl = connection + strength + perfection.

This is not a random collection of jewels. This is a program. A symbolic description of what royal power should be: binding, creative, perfect.

And this was stored on Khvamli—a mountain that was the embodiment of the *axis mundi*, the place of the chaining of the god-blacksmith, the star of Venus.

Treasury and cosmos—in a single point of space.

---

*Sources and Notes*

¹ *Kartlis Tskhovreba*. Tbilisi: Academy of Sciences of the Georgian SSR, 1955 (critical edition in Georgian). Anonymous 14th-century chronicle—the primary source for the chapter.

² Brosset M.-F. *Rapport sur un voyage archéologique dans la Géorgie et l'Arménie*. Saint Petersburg, 1849–1851. Description of Dadiani's excavations on Khvamli.

³ Toumanoff C. *Studies in Christian Caucasian History*. Georgetown University Press, 1963. Political history of 13th-century Georgia: context of the division between David VI and David VII.

⁴ Allen W.E.D. *A History of the Georgian People*. Kegan Paul, 1932. Analysis of medieval Georgian statehood and sacred geography.

⁵ Georgia About. *The Treasure Caves of Khvamli*. georgiaabout.com, 2014. Popular account of the chronicle about the treasures.

⁶ Wikipedia. *Khvamli*. Basic source on the history of expeditions to the mountain.

---

*Next Chapter: The Regalia of the "King of the World"—W.E.D. Allen on the Tradition of Storing Symbols of Power in the Sacred Mountains of the Colchians and Kartlians.*

---

# Chapter 14. The Regalia of the "King of the World"

> "A mountain is not just stone. A mountain is the memory of those who lived at its foot, and a promise to those who will come after."
>
> — W.E.D. Allen, *A History of the Georgian People*, 1932

---

In 1932, a book was published in London that would define the Western scholarly view of Georgian history for decades to come.

Its author—William Edward David Allen (W.E.D. Allen, 1901–1973): a British diplomat, journalist, historian-Caucasologist. A man who spent a significant part of his life between the Caucasus, Istanbul, and London. A man who read Georgian chronicles in the original and was not afraid to voice bold hypotheses.

The book was titled *A History of the Georgian People*. It became the standard English-language source on medieval Georgia for many generations.

And in it—among much else—there is a passage about the sacred mountains of Colchis and Kartli. A passage that remains one of the most profound reflections on the nature of power in the Caucasus.

---

## What is the "King of the World"

Before speaking of regalia, one must understand what precisely this title—"king of the world"—meant in the Caucasian context.

In medieval Georgian titulature, there existed several levels of power. The highest, used in certain periods—"king of kings" (*mepe mepeta*) and "lord" (*mtavari*). The term "king of the world" is rarer; it appears in the context of a claim to universal power: power legitimized not only by earthly but also by heavenly authority.
Allen notes that in Georgian sources, the concept of "dominion over the world" is inextricably linked with the possession of **symbolic objects** — regalia that embodied not merely wealth, but sacred status. These are not ornaments. They are signs of the order of things.

In this, the Georgian tradition is not unique. A similar logic is traced among Hittite kings, who kept the "tablet of destiny" within the depths of a sacred mountain. Among pharaohs, who were sons of Ra and embodied celestial order on earth. Among Sumerian lugals, who received kingship "from the heavens" — literally, according to the "King List": "when kingship descended from heaven."

The general principle is one: power is not simply taken. Power is received from a higher force and this receipt is confirmed by special objects.

---

## Allen on the Colchian Tradition

What exactly does Allen write about Colchis and Kartli?

Analyzing Greek sources on Colchis — primarily Strabo and Apollonius of Rhodes — and comparing them with Georgian chronicles, Allen arrives at several important observations.

Firstly, in the Colchian tradition, there existed a practice of storing sacred objects — including symbols of power — in mountains, not in cities. This distinguishes Colchis from most contemporary Near Eastern states, where treasuries were located at temples in settlement centers. A mountain repository is a Colchian specificity.

Secondly, Allen connects the "Golden Fleece" from the myth of the Argonauts with the real practice of storing royal symbols in inaccessible mountain places. The Golden Fleece is not merely a fleece. It is a metaphor: the "fleece" as a designation for the totality of sacred objects stored in an unreachable place under the guard of a dragon. The dragon, never closing its eyes, is not a mythical animal. It is a guard. A sentinel entrusted with guarding that which must not fall into the wrong hands.

Thirdly — and this is Allen's most interesting observation — the "king of the world" in the Georgian tradition is such precisely because he has access to the sacred mountain repository. Not because he has an army. Not because he has money. Because he has a connection with the mountain — with the place where heaven and earth connect.

This is a theological formula of power, not a political one.

---

## Khvamli as a Legitimizing Center

If one accepts Allen's interpretation, then Khvamli turns out to be not merely a beautiful mountain or a convenient repository. Khvamli is a **source of legitimacy**.

The Georgian king, storing regalia on Khvamli, is not simply using caves as a safe. He is declaring his connection with the mountain. With its sacred energy. With Amiran, chained here. With Venus — the "star of Khomli" — which shines over this summit. With the *axis mundi* that this rock is.

His power is legitimate because the mountain gave its consent.

This explains much in Georgian medieval politics that otherwise seems strange. Why, during the division of the country in 1259, is it specifically mentioned that the regalia remained on Khvamli? Because the question of the regalia is the question of who is the "true" king. Whoever has access to the mountain possesses the highest legitimacy. This is not merely an inventory of a treasury. It is a political document.

---

## Comparison with Other Traditions

Allen does not limit himself to Georgian material. He traces similar patterns in neighboring traditions.

**Colchians and Lazi.** West Georgian tribes — the Colchians and their descendants the Lazi (Mingrelians) — preserved a special attitude towards mountain sanctuaries until the late Middle Ages. Mountain "*dzvari*" (literally "cross," but originally a sacred pillar or stone) were located precisely on summits. These are not Christianized places — these are places that Christianity "inherited" from an earlier cult.

**Kartlian Tradition.** In Eastern Georgia — Kartli — the function of the sacred mountain in certain periods was performed by the Mtskheta Jvari — the "cross" mountain above the capital. But before Christianization (4th century AD), a pagan sanctuary of Armazi — the god of sky and war — stood here. The pagan tradition of the sacred mountain preceded the Christian one.

**Nart Epic.** In the pan-Caucasian Nart epic — in its Georgian, Ossetian, and Abkhaz-Adyghe versions — the Narts store their magical weapons and the cup of Uatsamonga ("cup of peace") not in houses or cities, but in inaccessible mountain strongholds. Access to these objects determines which of the Narts is "first."

The pattern is one: power resides in the mountain.

---

## What Was Stored Before the Royal Regalia

Allen asks a question historians rarely ask: if medieval kings stored regalia in mountain caves — then who stored something there before them?

Traditions do not arise from nothing. If the caves of Khvamli were used as a royal repository in the 13th century, it means they were used for this function earlier. Because tradition is a practice transmitted through generations. No one stores things in a random cave on a random hill. They store them in a known place, a place with a reputation.

Where did Khvamli's reputation as a storage place come from? Petroglyphs give a partial answer: this place was sacred long before the Middle Ages. People came here — carved symbols, performed rituals, left traces.

A possible sequence: from the 6th–5th millennia BC — a ritual site for hunter-gatherer communities. From the Bronze Age — a sacred mountain for local chieftains. From the early historical period (1st millennium BC) — a center of Colchian cult. From the Middle Ages — a state repository.

One place — different eras — one function: a sacred center.

---

## Regalia as Memory of Something More Ancient

Let us return to the three objects from the 14th-century chronicle: the chain, the anvil, the pearl.

Allen does not analyze this specific list — his book was written before a detailed analysis of this fragment became standard. But his general concept allows for an observation.

An ornament in the shape of an anvil is not merely a jewelry item. In the context of Khvamli — the mountain of Amiran, the mountain of the blacksmith's clang — the anvil is an emblem of the place itself. It is a "beacon" for those who know: "this object belongs to Khvamli." This is not a treasure brought from outside — this is something made here, for here.

If the anvil was made specifically for the repository on Khvamli — it means someone at a certain moment made a decision: to create an object embodying the symbolism of the place. To create a commemorative sign.

This is an act of cultural memory. A way to preserve knowledge of the place's significance even when the living bearers of this knowledge die. An object in the shape of an anvil, lying in the cave of the blacksmith's mountain — this is a message. "Amiran was here. The axis of the world was here."

Allen would have written: "The tradition of storing symbols of power in sacred mountains among the Colchians is not a barbaric whim. It is a theology that makes sense only within a system where the mountain is not a backdrop, but an active participant in history."

---

## The Object as Argument: What "Regalia" Means

Modern people are accustomed to thinking of state regalia as symbols whose meaning is conventional. A scepter — because it was decided so. A crown — because of tradition. There is nothing "real" behind it.

In archaic logic, everything is different.

A regalia is not a symbol of power. A regalia is a source of power. Or, more precisely: an object through the possession of which power receives its authenticity. It is a link between the world of people and the world from which power comes — the celestial, the lofty, the sacred.

If the diamond chain is "from the heavens" — it ensures the king's connection with the celestial order. If the anvil is "from Amiran" — it ensures strength and the capacity for transformation. If the pearl is "incomparable" — it ensures uniqueness, chosenness.

Three objects — three sources of royal power: legitimacy (connection), strength (transformation), uniqueness (chosenness).

This is not a jewelry inventory. This is a constitution.

And this constitution was stored on a mountain.

---

*Sources and Notes*

¹ Allen W.E.D. «A History of the Georgian People from the Beginning down to the Russian Conquest in the Nineteenth Century». Kegan Paul, Trench, Trubner & Co., London, 1932. Primary source for the chapter.

² «Kartlis Tskhovreba». Tbilisi, 1955. Fragment on the division of 1259.

³ Apollonius of Rhodes. «Argonautica» / trans. into Russian by G. Tsereteli. Tbilisi, 1964. Description of Colchis and the "Golden Fleece."

⁴ Strabo. «Geography». Book XI. Description of Colchis and its sacred mountains.

⁵ Charachidzé G. «Le système religieux de la Géorgie Paienne». Paris: Maspero, 1968. Foundational work on the Georgian pagan pantheon and sacred geography.

⁶ Lordkipanidze O. «Archäologie in Georgien». Weinheim: VCH, 1991. Material evidence of the sacred function of mountain sites in prehistoric Georgia.

---

*Next Chapter: Water and Eternity — the underground lake and waterfall in the Tekeneli cave as an embodiment of the sacred significance of water in ancient cultic places.*

---

# Chapter 15. Water and Eternity
> “Water does not remember. Water knows.”
>
> — Mingrelian proverb

---

Imagine: you are standing at the edge of a vertical shaft.

Eighteen meters down. A rope in your hands is the only thing keeping you from falling. A flashlight illuminates a narrowing cone of darkness. The walls of the shaft are damp, cold, still inhabited by mold and moss. The smell is earthy, deep, slightly sour: it is carbon dioxide exhaled by the rock.

And then, from below — a sound.

Not silence. Not emptiness. The sound of water. Thin, dripping, barely discernible from below — but it is there. It means: there is not just stone down there. There is life. There is another world.

You begin the descent.

---

## The Underground Lake: Anatomy of a Phenomenon

The Tekenteli Cave conceals within it what in speleology is called an "underground lake" — a sump or underground lake. This is not a temporary pond after rains. It is a permanent water lens, fed by infiltration waters from the Khvamli massif.

Water seeps through cracks in the limestone, slowly trickles down, and accumulates in the lower part of the shaft. The geological shape of the bottom is a bowl formed by undissolved rock or a dense clay layer. Water comes and goes but never disappears completely. This is permanence.

According to descriptions, the lake is several meters across. It is shallow — perhaps a meter to a meter and a half at its deepest point. Its surface is absolutely still: no current, no ripples, no surface air movement. A mirror in complete darkness.

What happens to a mirror in complete darkness? It reflects what is brought before it. The light of a flashlight turns into a scattering of glints. Sound — reflects multiple times from the surface and disappears into the side galleries. An image — turns into an inverted ghost.

Water without light is not just water. It is the surface of another world.

---

## The Waterfall: Life in Darkness

Next to the lake — a waterfall.

Not the waterfall pictured on postcards: powerful, roaring, visible from afar. This is different. A thin thread of water falling from the ceiling of a side gallery — perhaps three, perhaps five meters high. It falls silently, or almost silently: a barely audible rustle, amplified by the cave's acoustics.

In winter, the waterfall freezes. The thread of water turns into an ice stalactite — a fragile needle descending from the ceiling all the way to the floor. In spring, when the outside temperature begins to rise, the water returns. First — a drop. Then — a thread. Then — a waterfall again.

This is a cycle. Birth — freezing — rebirth. Every year. Without exception. For thousands of years.

For those who came to this cave millennia ago, this cycle was not a geological phenomenon. It was a theological fact. Water dies in winter and is reborn in spring. Water is alive. Water is mortal and immortal at the same time.

---

## Water as a Boundary in Mythological Systems

Underground water holds a special place in the mythological systems of virtually all known cultures.

**Sumer.** Apsu — the freshwater ocean lying underground. It is the dwelling place of the god of wisdom and water, Enki. Apsu is the source of all life: the fresh waters of rivers and springs are nourished by it. At the same time, Apsu is the underworld, a place from which dark forces emanate. The slaying of Apsu by Marduk is one of the first acts of cosmogony in the Babylonian tradition. Water underground is simultaneously life and death.

**Greece.** Styx — the river of death flowing in the underworld. To drink from it is to die or to gain immortality (goddesses touched the waters of the Styx in their oaths, and an oath sworn by the water of the Styx was unbreakable). The spring of Mnemosyne ("memory") and the spring of Lethe ("forgetfulness") — underground waters with opposite functions. The deceased had to choose the correct spring to preserve themselves for the afterlife.

**Celts.** Freshwater springs and lakes were sites of sacrifice and oracular centers. The Celtic Otherworld — Tír na nÓg — was located "beyond the sea" or "underwater." Lakes and bogs were portals.

**Georgia.** In the Nart epic, the underworld is invariably connected with water: it is crossed by rivers, it is surrounded by waters. The transition between worlds is always a crossing of water. Bathing in special springs — a ritual of purification and transformation — is present in Georgian folklore as an echo of a significantly more archaic tradition.

The underground lake of Tekenteli fits into this matrix with perfect precision: it is located at the bottom of a vertical shaft (descent into the underworld), it is permanent (eternity), it reflects (a mirror of another world), and it feeds a waterfall (life emanating from darkness).

---

## The Ritual Logic of Descent

Who descended into Tekenteli and why?

This question is not idle. Descending by rope into an eighteen-meter shaft requires equipment, time, and intent. It does not happen by chance. It is not an everyday action.

Several categories of traces of presence have been found in Tekenteli:
- Rock niches, widened by human tools — in the side galleries at a level below the shaft.
- Ceramic fragments in the niches — dated to the 12th–13th centuries AD.
- Red rider figures on the walls of the niches — from the same period.

The picture emerges: people descended, carried objects with them (ceramics — vessels with contents?), left them in niches, drew images nearby — and left.

This is a votive practice. From the Latin *votum* — vow, promise, dedication. Votive objects were left in sacred places as a gift to a higher power: in exchange for help, healing, victory, or simply as a sign of gratitude. In Greece — in temples and at springs. In Rome — at altars. In medieval Georgia — at mountain "dzvari."

Tekenteli is an underground "dzvari." A place where a person meets what lives in water and darkness.

---

## Water and Preservation: The Practical Dimension

But water in Tekenteli plays not only a ritual role.

As described in the previous chapter on cave geology, high humidity combined with consistently low temperatures creates specific storage conditions. Organic materials — wood, leather, fabric — do not rot in the standard way under such conditions. They either mummify or are "preserved" in an anaerobic environment.

This is precisely why votive objects left in niches can survive significantly longer than in the open air. Humidity, which seems an enemy of preservation, in a closed environment without oxygen exchange becomes its condition.

Those who chose Tekenteli knew this intuitively. Not because they understood the chemistry of preservation. Because they saw the result: objects left in the cave lay there — a year, ten years, a hundred years — untouched and undamaged.

The water of the cave preserves what is entrusted to it.

---

## Parallels: Sacred Waters in Cultic Sites

Khvamli is not the only case where water becomes an integral part of a sacred site.

**The Delphic Oracle** (Greece) was located above a gorge from which vapors rose. According to later accounts, the oracle sat over a fissure through which gases from underground waters escaped. Water (or its vapors) — a condition for prophecy.

**The Castalian Spring** (Delphi) — a sacred spring from which priests drank before prophesying. Purification by water preceded contact with the god.

**The Well of Jamshid** (Persia) — in Iranian tradition, sacred wells and underground waters were dwelling places of *fravashi* spirits, guardians of ancestors.

**Ziyarat** (Muslim tradition in the Caucasus region) — sacred sites at springs or above underground waters, inherited from pre-Islamic practices.

**Prehistoric Ireland and Britain** — lakes and bogs as sites of sacrifice (finds of "bog bodies," ritual weapons, and vessels in bogs and lakes are confirmed by thousands of artifacts).

Underground water is a universal axis between the world of the living and the world of the other. Tekenteli fits into this universal context — but with a Caucasian specificity: not just "water at a sacred site," but "water inside the mountain," water in the depths of the *axis mundi*.

---

## What is Eternity for a Stone

There is one aspect of Khvamli's water that is rarely mentioned in popular descriptions but is perhaps the most eloquent.

The water in the underground lake of Tekenteli is the same water that seeped through the limestone thousands of years ago. It slowly moves through the system of cracks, accumulates, descends, accumulates again. The "turnover" time of this water in the system is years, perhaps decades.

This means: the people who stood at the edge of the lake a thousand years ago saw the same water. Or water from the same system, from the same cracks, from the same depths.
Water does not remember. But water is the same.

In this sense, the underground lake of Tekenteli is a physical embodiment of what philosophers call "continuity": not the existence of the same object in time, but the existence of the same process. The water changes. But the process does not. The cave changes. But the water returns.

This, in all likelihood, was obvious to those who came here. There was no need to formulate a philosophy of eternity. It was enough to come to the lake, shine a light into the darkness, and see one's inverted reflection in the still water.

The reflection is you. But inverted. You from another world. The one you will be after death—or before birth.

The entrance to this world is eighteen meters down a rope.

---

## The Waterfall as the Rhythm of Time

A final observation on the water of Tekenteli.

The waterfall freezes in winter and comes to life in spring. Year after year. Without exception.

In archaic calendar systems, the annual cycle of nature is not merely a change of seasons. It is the story of the world, retold anew each year. Death in winter is not the end. It is preparation for rebirth. Spring is not simply a warming. It is the victory of life over death.

The Tekenteli waterfall is a built-in calendar. It shows time not with numbers or sun shadows—it shows it through water. There is water—it means the warm season. No water (ice)—it means the cold season.

Who watched this waterfall? In all likelihood, the same people who left votive objects in the niches. They came in spring—saw the first water. They came in autumn—saw the first ice. These were ritual moments, points of transition between the phases of the year.

The cave was not only a repository and not only a ritual site. It was a clock. A water clock in the darkness of the mountain.

---

*Sources and Notes*

¹ Wikipedia. "Khvamli". Description of the Tekenteli cave: lake and waterfall.

² Eliade M. «Patterns in Comparative Religion». New York: Sheed & Ward, 1958. Water as a sacred element in world religions: a systematic overview.

³ Frazer J.G. «The Golden Bough». London: Macmillan, 1890–1915. Rituals at water sources: anthropological context.

⁴ Coles J. «Mists of Time: Sacred Springs and Other Water Features in Prehistoric Europe». Oxford Journal of Archaeology, 2001. Underground waters as cult objects.

⁵ Charachidzé G. «Le système religieux de la Géorgie Paienne». Paris: Maspero, 1968. Water rituals in Georgian pagan tradition.

⁶ Lewis-Williams D. «The Mind in the Cave». London: Thames & Hudson, 2002. Caves as sites of ritual altered consciousness; the role of underground waters.

---

*Next Chapter: The Gokhnari Necropolis—a cemetery at the foot of the mountain and evidence of the continuous use of the sacred landscape of Khvamli from the 10th century AD.*

---

# Chapter 16. The Foot of the Sanctuary: The Gokhnari Necropolis

> "Every mountain has its cemetery. The dead do not choose a place by chance."
>
> — A Racha saying

---

Mountains have foothills.

This seems banal, but the foot of a mountain is not merely the lower boundary of the relief. It is a transitional zone between two worlds: the world of the ordinary, the human—villages, fields, roads—and the world of the vertical, the sacred, the unattainable.

It is precisely in this transitional zone—at the foot of Khvamli, in an area called Gokhnari—that a medieval cemetery was discovered. A necropolis that turned out to be significantly more informative than one might expect from an ordinary rural graveyard.

---

## Gokhnari: Place and Context

Gokhnari is not a village. It is a toponym denoting the territory at the western foot of Khvamli, near which the remains of a medieval settlement and an adjacent cemetery are located.

The territory itself is a gentle slope transitioning into the valley of a small tributary of the Rioni. Here, according to data from several expeditions, the following were recorded:
- Remains of burial structures.
- Fragments of pottery.
- Rock-cut burials—interments in carved or natural niches of the bedrock.
- Traces of settlement activity: hearths, ash pits.

Dating: based on the ceramic complex and stratigraphy—primarily the 10th–14th centuries AD. Earlier layers are an open question, but indirect data (including preliminary results of genetic analysis, discussed later) point to possible earlier use.

---

## The Cemetery as a Chronometer

A cemetery is a special type of monument.

Unlike temporary camps or situationally created objects, a cemetery is created intentionally and maintained across generations. Its existence presupposes a stable community that returns here again and again: to bury one's dead in the same place is to recognize the place as special, distinct from others.

Gokhnari is a cemetery used for more than a single century. This follows from the stratigraphy: earlier burials are overlain by later ones, from different periods. The place was not abandoned and rediscovered. It was used continuously.

For us, this means: from the 10th to the 14th century (and possibly longer), a community existed at the foot of Khvamli that considered this mountain its sacred center. A community that chose precisely this place to bury its dead.

This is not a random choice. In medieval Georgia, people were buried near churches. The Gokhnari cemetery has the Church of St. George. An inscription from 1910 (discovered during the excavation of the foundation) names the donor who built or renovated the church: Ioane, Eristavi of the Svans. This is the 12th–13th centuries.

A church at the foot of the mountain. A cemetery around the church. Caves with votive objects higher up the slope. Petroglyphs even higher.

A vertical axis: the dead below, the living in the middle, sacred signs above, the sky over the summit.

---

## Ioane, Eristavi of the Svans: A Man at the Mountain

The inscription found in 1910 during excavations at the base of the church is an exceptionally important document.

*Eristavi* is a Georgian feudal title, literally "head of the army," effectively a regional lord, ruler of a province. *Eristavi of the Svans* means a person who ruled the mountainous region of Svaneti, located to the north and west of Khvamli.

Why would a noble feudal lord from a neighboring mountainous region build or renovate a church precisely at Khvamli?

This is not a coincidence or a whim. It is a political statement. To build a church in a specific place is to declare: this place is within my sphere of influence and my piety. I consecrate this place with my authority and my name.

Ioane chose Khvamli. Of all places—precisely this one.

This means that in the 12th–13th centuries, Khvamli enjoyed special authority—extending beyond the nearest settlement. People from Svaneti were "drawn" to it. It was important enough for a regional lord to consider it necessary to mark his presence here.

---

## Genetic Data: A Preliminary Look

In 2019, a group of Georgian researchers led by Tsintsadze and colleagues undertook a paleogenetic study of remains from the Gokhnari necropolis.

Thirteen samples were analyzed. The sample size is small, and the authors themselves note the preliminary nature of the conclusions. Nevertheless, the data are of interest.

The results showed the presence of haplogroups characteristic of Caucasian populations, with some markers indicating connections to the broader Eurasian genetic space. This corresponds to the general picture known for medieval Georgia: a population that had been here for millennia, with traces of migratory and contact events.
What these data do not prove: a direct genetic connection with any specific ancient civilization. Thirteen samples are too few for such conclusions.

What they indirectly confirm: the Gokhnari population was an established local group—not newcomers, not trade visitors, not a military garrison. These were people who lived here for generations and buried their dead here because this was their place.

For Khvamli, this is important: the sanctity of the mountain was ensured not by an "imported" cult brought from outside, but by an established local tradition.

---

## The Ritual Landscape: How to Read Space

The Gokhnari necropolis is an element of a broader system. In isolation, it would be merely a cemetery. In the context of Khvamli, it becomes part of a ritual landscape.

The concept of the "ritual landscape" is relatively new in archaeology, actively developed since the 1980s. Its essence: sacred places are not isolated. They form systems—spatial and symbolic. The path from one place to another is itself part of the ritual.

Let us imagine what the Khvamli ritual landscape looked like in the 12th–13th centuries—the period of its most active documented use:

At the foot—the Gokhnari necropolis. The place of the dead. A transitional zone between the ordinary world and the sacred.

Higher up—the Church of St. George. The place of prayer. A mediator between worlds.

Higher still—the Tekenteli and Boga caves. Places of storage, votive niches, underground water. The lower sacred world.

At the summit—the open sky. The upper world.

And on the rock surfaces—petroglyphs. Messages that connect all levels.

This is not a random distribution of objects along the slope. This is an architecture of meaning. Each level performs its function. Movement upward is movement from the world of the dead to the sky. Descent into the caves is movement toward the lower world.

The mountain is organized as a cosmos.

---

## Saint George as a Successor

The church on Khvamli is dedicated to Saint George.

This is not accidental—though it may seem commonplace for Georgia: churches of Saint George are everywhere here. But for Khvamli specifically, the choice of this saint carries additional meaning.

Saint George in Georgian Christianity is the "high heavenly warrior," a celestial protector. He slays the dragon—that is, he defeats chthonic, subterranean forces. In Georgian folk perception, George often syncretically merges with older images: with a hunting god, with a warrior on horseback, with a protector of mountain roads.

On Khvamli—the mountain of Amiran, the mountain of the blacksmith's clang—the Church of Saint George is a Christianization layer superimposed upon a significantly older cult. As has happened worldwide: a new religion builds its edifice upon the foundation of an older sanctity. The place did not change. The name of the one to whom the place belonged changed.

Amiran is chained on Khvamli—and George defeated the dragon. Both are "warriors" of the sacred space. Both are connected to the mountain. The gap between them is cultural continuity, not a rupture.

---

## Continuity as an Argument

The Gokhnari necropolis is important precisely because it shows continuity.

This is not a monument of a single era. It is a monument to a process: a process that lasted for centuries, during which people repeatedly chose this place as a point of connection between life and death.

For our topic, this is an additional argument in favor of the stability of the Khvamli sacred tradition. We are speaking of petroglyphs, presumably created in the 5th–4th millennia BCE. That is a very long time ago. Between us and them lies a gap difficult to bridge with imagination.

The Gokhnari necropolis helps bridge this gap. It shows: the chain was not broken. Perhaps its links are not always visible—but they existed. People did not stop coming to this mountain. Burying here. Praying here. Storing things here.

Why? Because the mountain meant something. Because it was a center—an axis mundi, the mountain of a god, a place where the dead became closer to the sky.

The Gokhnari necropolis is proof not only of medieval faith. It is proof of memory. A memory that survived changes in language, religion, and power—and still brought people back to the same slope.

---

## Current State

Today, the Gokhnari necropolis is partially excavated, partially still underground. The main research was conducted during the Soviet period; some of the material has been published in closed academic editions, some in open access, but sparingly.

Decree No. 123 of the Government of Georgia from 2020, granting Khvamli the status of a monument of national significance, apparently also extends to the territory of Gokhnari. This means legal protection—but does not mean active research.

A systematic publication of excavation results in international peer-reviewed journals is still lacking. This is a gap that must be closed: the Gokhnari necropolis, combined with the cave complexes of Khvamli and the petroglyphic corpus, is a world-class site deserving of international attention.

---

*Sources and Notes*

¹ Wikipedia. "Khvamli." Data on the Gokhnari necropolis, the Church of St. George, the inscription of Ioane.

² Tsintsadze N. and colleagues. Preliminary results of genetic analysis of remains from the Gokhnari necropolis, 2019. Publication in a Georgian-language academic journal (exact data being verified).

³ "Mount Khvamli — a Cultural-Mystical Product of the Development of Sustainable Tourism in Georgia." ResearchGate, 2020.

⁴ Tilley C. "A Phenomenology of Landscape: Places, Paths and Monuments." Oxford: Berg, 1994. The concept of the ritual landscape and sacred space in archaeology.

⁵ Decree No. 123 of the Government of Georgia, 2020—the legal basis for the protection of Khvamli objects.

⁶ Lordkipanidze O. "Archäologie in Georgien." VCH, 1991. Material evidence of medieval Georgian burial practices.

---

*Next Chapter: Fond No. 1738 — an archival document in the Central State Archive of Georgia and the beginning of the detective story of the Soviet expedition.*

---

# Chapter 17. Fond No. 1738

> "An archive is a cemetery of intentions. Here they bury what was not supposed to die."
>
> — Archivists' folk wisdom

---

Tbilisi. Ketskhoveli Street, building 7.

Here, in a building of gray tuff constructed in the late 19th to early 20th century, is located the National Archives of Georgia. Once—the Central State Archive of the Georgian SSR. Part of its holdings are still not fully cataloged. Part—classified or semi-classified. Part is accessible to researchers upon official request.

Among the many thousands of storage units comprising this archive, there is one fond—with a number that, according to available information, has attracted the attention of several researchers working on the history of Khvamli.

Fond No. 1738.

---

## How Documents Find Researchers

Archival discoveries rarely look as they are portrayed in films. In reality, it is hours in the reading room, dusty folders in brown bindings, manuscripts written in faded ink on yellowed sheets. It is monotonous work that sometimes—not often, but sometimes—is interrupted when a researcher freezes and rereads a page once more.

This is apparently exactly how the document stored in Fond No. 1738 of the National Archives of Georgia was discovered.

We do not know exactly who found it first. Several publications concerning Khvamli mention the existence of an archival document from 1948 related to an expedition to the mountain. The specific reference to Fond No. 1738—combined with a specific date and specific names—currently requires verification through a direct archival request. This is work that remains to be fully completed.

But the fact that such a document exists—or existed—in Georgian archives is confirmed by several independent, albeit indirect, sources.
---

## The Document's Appearance: What the Paper Means

Imagine we are holding it in our hands.

A Soviet official document from 1948 is a specific object. In format, it follows the strict standards of USSR state paperwork: the letterhead of an institution or organization, a corner stamp with the name of the department. The date is typed or handwritten. An outgoing number. A signature.

The language is likely Russian: Soviet bureaucracy primarily conducted paperwork in Russian, although the Georgian SSR had the right to parallel use of Georgian. If the document originated from Moscow or directly concerned central government bodies—it was written in Russian. If it was a local, Georgian document—it might be in Georgian or in a bilingual version.

The text is laconic. A Soviet official document does not use superfluous words. Every phrase is functional. The recipient's name. The wording of the assignment. Deadlines. The signature of the authorized person.

A seal. Red or blue—depending on the department and type of document.

A classification marking—if present—is located in the upper right corner of the first page: "Secret," "Top Secret," or "Top Secret / Of Special Importance."

Such a sheet of paper is not merely an administrative artifact. It is a window into a specific historical moment. On that specific day in 1948, someone—in a specific office, at a specific desk—decided to write or sign this document. Made a decision. Gave an order.

---

## The Addressee: "Comrade Japaridze, Aliosha"

The addressee of the document—if the attribution is correct—is Aliosha Japaridze.

We have encountered this name before. But now we need to examine it in detail.

Aliosha (Alexander) Japaridze was one of the most famous Georgian mountaineers of his time. In 1934, he made the first Georgian (and first Soviet) ascent of Ushba—one of the most technically difficult peaks in the Caucasus, the "Mater of the Caucasian Mountains." He was accompanied by his sister Alexandra (the first woman to climb Ushba), Gio Niguriani, and Iagor Kazalikashvili.

This is not merely a sporting achievement. In the Soviet context of the 1930s, conquering mountain peaks was an ideological act: the Soviet person conquers nature, the Soviet person surpasses bourgeois mountaineers of Western Europe. Japaridze was a hero—in both the Georgian and Soviet senses simultaneously.

His name and reputation meant: if the task was entrusted to him, it was difficult, requiring skill and reliability.

---

## The Date: 1948—Why Precisely Now

Different sources cite different dates for Japaridze's expedition to Khvamli: 1939, 1945, 1948–1949. Possibly, he worked on the mountain several times. Possibly, these refer to different operations.

The date "October 4, 1948"—if the document indeed exists with this date—is interesting in several contexts.

1948 is the post-war period. The Soviet Union is recovering from the war, but simultaneously—is actively engaged in searching for and preserving cultural valuables that were displaced or hidden during the war years. This is a time when the state was actively interested in anything that could have value—material or ideological.

October 1948 is the beginning of autumn. This is the optimal time for mountain expeditions in the Caucasus: the summer tourist season is over, snow has not yet fallen, but the temperature allows for work.

It was precisely in 1947 that Simon Janashia died—the foremost Georgian antiquarian, a researcher of Colchis and Iberia. His death could have meant that part of his research materials—including, possibly, information about Khvamli—became more accessible to state structures that previously did not have direct access to them.

---

## Document Attributes: Classification Marks and Stamps as the Language of Power

In the Soviet bureaucratic system, the form of a document carried as much information as its content.

If a document bears the classification "Top Secret"—this indicates a limited circle of access. Such a document is not stored in the general archival fond. It goes to the "spetskhran"—a special repository accessible only to persons with the appropriate clearance.

If a document is signed by a high-ranking official—the head of the Academy of Sciences of the Georgian SSR, or the Secretary of the Central Committee of the Communist Party of Georgia, or someone from Moscow structures—this indicates the level of interest in the object.

The Soviet paperwork system was characterized by pedantry: every incoming document was registered, each was assigned a number, every response document was recorded as an "outgoing." This means: if an order exists—its registry entry exists. If a report was written—it was registered as incoming in the department that received it.

Finding a registry entry is sometimes easier than finding the document itself. This is one of the methods of archival detective work.

---

## The Language of the Directive

Soviet administrative language is a specific genre.

It is brief. It does not explain—it instructs. It does not justify—it prescribes. Every phrase in it is a command, wrapped in a neutral administrative form.

The wording "reconnaissance work" applied to a mountain expedition is not a tourist term or a scientific one. It is a military term transferred into an administrative context. "Reconnaissance" is the search for a specific object whose existence is already known. Not the discovery of something new. The search for the known.

The wording "special attention" is a signal. In Soviet administrative speech, "special attention" means: this point is the main one. Everything else is secondary. Everything listed after "special attention"—is the reason for which the whole thing is being undertaken.

If the document says: "a group of the best mountaineers"—this means selection based on criteria of reliability and discretion, not just technical skill. Mountaineers for a secret expedition are people who know how to climb and who can be trusted.

---

## A Fond in the Archival System: What It Means

An archival fond is a unit of document organization. Fond No. 1738 in the Central State Archive of Georgia is the aggregate of documents generated by the activities of one institution or person.

Each fond has a "fond creator"—the one whose activity generated these documents. This could be an institution (the Academy of Sciences of the Georgian SSR, the Ministry of Culture, the Council of Ministers), an organization (a mountaineering club, an expeditionary group), or a private individual (the personal fond of a researcher).

If Fond No. 1738 is the personal fond of Aliosha Japaridze or the documents of a specific expedition—that is one situation. If it is the fond of some state department, among whose documents materials about Khvamli were found—that is another.

In any case: the presence of a document in an archival fond means it existed long enough to be transferred to the archive. This is not a destroyed piece of evidence. This is preserved history.

The only question is whether it is open to researchers.

---

## What Could Be in the Document

Strictly speaking—it is unknown. We are speculating about a document to which we have no direct access.

But the logic of Soviet paperwork allows us to outline the probable format.

An order or directive: to whom, what to do, by what deadlines. A list of persons included in the expedition. A description of the task: what exactly to search for, what to record, what to extract. The reporting procedure.

If "the report is to be presented personally to Comrade Stalin"—as an instruction in the document—then this signifies several things. First: the level of interest is exceptional. Stalin personally took an interest in reports on specific expeditions extremely rarely—only when the subject had political, ideological, or material significance of the first order.

Second: a direct report to Stalin means the information did not pass through standard bureaucratic filters. It went to one place—the leader's desk.

Third: if the report was written and landed on Stalin's desk—it is stored either in Stalin's personal fond in RGASPI (Russian State Archive of Socio-Political History), or in the fonds of the State Archive of the Russian Federation. Or—if it was destroyed—there is no trace of it anywhere.

---

## The Beginning of a Detective Story

Fond No. 1738 is not the end of the investigation. It is its beginning.

The document points to an event: the 1948–1949 expedition. The event points to a result: what was found. The result points to the next document: the report. The report points to the next storage location: the Spetskhran, Moscow, Stalin's personal fond.

This is a chain. And the first link of this chain lies in Tbilisi, in a building of gray tuff on Ketskhoveli Street.

Or lay there. Or lies there now, waiting for a researcher with the right request and sufficient patience.

Soviet archives do not destroy documents without reason. They hide them. Hide them within the system—in fonds, in inventories, in storage units. In a system where everything is designated by numbers, but not everything is explained.

We have a number: 1738.
Now — the explanation.

---

*Sources and Notes*

¹ National Archives of Georgia (Central State Archive of the Georgian SSR): archive.gov.ge. General information on the archive's structure and access procedures.

² GeorgianJournal. "Khvamli — A Repository of Georgian Treasures?". georgianjournal.ge. Mention of the Soviet expedition and related archival materials.

³ GeorgianMuseums.ge. "Aliosha Japaridze Local Museum". Biographical data on A. Japaridze.

⁴ "Georgian Alpinism in Svaneti". peripheralhistories.co.uk. Context of Soviet mountaineering and Japaridze's role.

⁵ "Notes of Material Importance: Archival Archaeology in the South Caucasus". archivejournal.net. The system of Soviet special depositories and the practice of classifying scientific data in the Caucasus.

⁶ Google. "Aliosha Japaridze Khvamli expedition". Various tourist and historical sources confirming Japaridze's participation in the Khvamli expedition.

---

*Next Chapter: The Order of October 4, 1948 — a verbatim analysis of the directive and an examination of its key formulations.*

---

# Chapter 18. The Order of October 4, 1948

> "In Soviet language, the words 'special attention' meant one thing: this was the very reason for which everything was being undertaken."

---

The document occupies a single sheet of paper.

Soviet bureaucracy did not waste words. One sheet — that is the maximum for a task of such a degree of secrecy: the shorter the text, the fewer opportunities for leaks, the fewer people see its content as it passes from hand to hand, from courier to courier, from printing to printing.

October 4, 1948. A specific date. A specific order. A specific addressee.

Let's break it down piece by piece — as any document of that era is broken down: slowly, carefully, understanding that every word here is functional.

---

## "To Comrade Japaridze, Aliosha"

Let's start with the addressee.

A Soviet official document addresses the recipient by surname and given name — without the patronymic, which is somewhat unusual for formal style. This could mean several things: the signatory's familiarity with the addressee, an informal tone within a formal framework, or — characteristic of the Georgian cultural milieu — the use of a diminutive name as a marker of trust.

Aliosha. Not Alexander Sergeyevich. Not Comrade Japaridze. But — Aliosha.

By 1948, Aliosha Japaridze was known to the entire mountaineering community of the Soviet Union. His 1934 ascent of Ushba together with his sister Alexandra, Gio Niguriani, and Yagor Kazalikashvili entered the history of Soviet mountaineering: the first Georgian conquest of one of the most difficult peaks of the Caucasus. In the context of Soviet ideology, this was a feat — "Soviet man conquers nature" — and Japaridze was its living embodiment.

But by 1948, he had another reputation: that of a man who knew how to work with a mountain not only as an obstacle but also as an object of study. A man who could be entrusted with a delicate task with confidence: he would ascend, inspect, record — and remain silent.

In the Soviet system, this was valued no less than mountaineering skill.

---

## "A group of the best alpinists"

The next key phrase: not just "a group," but "a group of the best."

This is a personnel remark. In Soviet administrative language, it means: selection was made not based on availability or geographical proximity, but on the principle of quality. The best are chosen for this task — meaning the task requires maximum professionalism.

But "the best" in this case also means "reliable." The Soviet system knew: sending people into the mountains who would later talk about what they saw — that is a mission failure before it even begins. The best alpinist for a secret expedition is one who can keep silent as well as he can climb rocks.

The document apparently did not specify the size of the group — or did so indirectly. According to one source, it involved two or three assistants for Japaridze. A small group: sufficient to complete the task, not large enough to make information control impossible.

---

## "Reconnaissance work"

This is the most informative phrase in the document.

"Reconnaissance" in Soviet administrative language has a strictly defined meaning. It is not "research" — research has an open result, its goal is to find something new. "Reconnaissance" is the search for something already known, establishing the location or condition of an object about whose existence there is already information.

When a Soviet agency sends people into the mountains with the task of "reconnaissance work" — it means: somewhere in the archives or operational data there is information about an object. The object is known. It needs to be found, confirmed, described.

What object? The document apparently specifies this in the next key phrase.

---

## "Special attention — to ancient drawings"

Here lies the heart of the entire directive.

"Special attention" in Soviet administrative language is not an emphasis on significance. It is a signal of priority: this point is the main one. Everything written before it is context and wrapping. What is written after "special attention" is the very thing for which everything is being undertaken.

"Ancient drawings" — that is what the expedition was looking for.

Not treasures. Not medieval artifacts. Not geological samples. Specifically drawings. And not just drawings — "ancient." The senders knew of the existence on Khvamli of images they attributed to deep antiquity.

How did they know? This is a separate question, to which the next chapter is dedicated. But the very presence of this knowledge in the directive's text means: information about the Khvamli petroglyphs was already in the possession of Soviet structures by 1948. And it was specific enough to become the subject of an official order.

---

## What the Syntax Says

In Soviet directives, syntax is politics. The order of points, the placement of emphasis, the choice of verbs — all of this carries information.

If a document first states "reconnaissance work on Mount Khvamli," and then "special attention to ancient drawings," this means: surveying the mountain as a whole serves as cover or context, while the petroglyphs are the real objective. If the order is reversed — perhaps the drawings were one of several equally significant priorities.

In our case, the formulation "special attention" indicates a hierarchy: the drawings stand above everything else.

This is important. Because it means: whoever wrote or dictated this document already knew *what* specifically to look for. Not "find and document everything interesting," but "find the drawings." Specifically. Purposefully.

---

## Dating and Context

October 4, 1948. The beginning of autumn.

This is the ideal time for a mountain expedition in Imereti: the summer heat has subsided, cave systems, partially inaccessible in summer due to snowmelt, are open again. There are still several weeks before the first persistent snows.

This means the order was calculated for immediate execution. Not for the next year. Not for spring. Now, in the autumn of 1948 — form the group, ascend, survey.

This is a sign of urgency. Of what is called in Soviet administrative practice "a planned task on a tight schedule."

Why urgent? Possibly — political reasons: they needed to complete it before the end of the year for reporting. Possibly — competitive: some scientist or foreign researcher was approaching the same goal. Possibly — the personal initiative of a high-ranking official with specific deadlines.

But the urgency itself says: this is not a routine academic task. This is a priority.
---

## Signature

Any order of this level is signed. And the level of the signature speaks to the level of interest.

If a document is signed by the head of a republican academy of sciences — that is one thing. If by a secretary of the Central Committee of the Communist Party of Georgia — another. If it came from Moscow structures — a third.

Each of these options implies its own chain of information and its own scale of the task. But in any case: the person who signed this document made the decision to send people into the mountains for "ancient drawings." And that person knew what they were doing.

A signature on such an order is a responsibility. In the Soviet system, it was not placed casually.

---

## One Sheet of Paper

Returning to the beginning.

One sheet. A few short phrases. A specific name. A specific mountain. A specific object.

Soviet bureaucracy, for all its countless paper production, knew how to be laconic when required. And this laconicism was a sign — not of carelessness, but of concentration.

Everything the recipient needed to know is in this document. Everything they did not need to know — is not in it. No explanations, no justifications, no history of the issue. An order is not an academic essay. An order is a command.

Aliosha Japaridze read this document and, in all likelihood, understood everything at once.

Because he already knew the mountain.

---

*Next chapter: The Context of the Era — Soviet Archaeology in Post-War Georgia.*

---

# Chapter 19. The Context of the Era — Soviet Archaeology

> "Science under Stalin was an instrument of the state just as a shovel was an instrument of a worker. The only difference is who holds the handle."

---

To understand why Khvamli interested the Soviet state precisely in 1948, one must understand what Soviet science was in those years.

This is not an academic digression. It is a necessary context. Without it, the order of October 4th looks like a strange whim. Within the era — it is logical, predictable, and even typical.

---

## Post-War Science: The State Collects Its Debts

The end of the Second World War placed Soviet science in a peculiar position.

On one hand, the war devastated many research institutes, scattered personnel, destroyed part of the archives. Entire expeditions planned for the late 1930s were curtailed or postponed indefinitely.

On the other hand, the victory created an ideological need for grand cultural gestures. The Soviet Union had won the most terrible war in history — and now had to prove its greatness not only in the military but also in the cultural sphere. Cultural values, national heritage, the historical depths of the peoples of the USSR — all this acquired unexpected relevance.

In 1945–1950, a large-scale program of archaeological and ethnographic expeditions unfolded in the USSR. New departments in academic institutes were rapidly created, funds were allocated, and field trips were organized. Soviet science had to "recover" what it had not managed to study before the war — and do it quickly, because history does not wait.

It is at this moment that interest in Khvamli arises.

---

## Academy of Sciences of the Georgian SSR

The Academy of Sciences of the Georgian SSR was founded in 1941 — on the very eve of the war.

This seemingly strange decision had its logic: the Soviet government was creating academic structures in the republics that, on one hand, integrated local science into the all-Union system, and on the other, allowed the state to control the production of knowledge about each region.

The leading historian-antiquarian of Georgia at that time was Simon Janashia. His research on Colchis and Iberia laid the foundation on which all subsequent Georgian historical science was built. Janashia passed away in 1947 — a year before Japaridze's presumed expedition.

This circumstance is noteworthy.

Janashia, in all likelihood, knew about the Khvamli petroglyphs. He worked with Georgian chronicles, with Vakhushti, with Brosset. Information about the sacred caves of the mountain, about rock drawings, about the Soviet expeditions of 1939 and 1945 — all this was within the academic community. The death of the leading scholar could have meant that part of his research plans remained unfinished — and the state decided to take the initiative into its own hands.

---

## The Logic of "Cultural Weaponry"

There is another context, which historians of academic science often sidestep, but which was directly related to Soviet expeditions of the 1940s.

The Cold War was beginning. And in this war, culture was a weapon.

The Soviet Union actively shaped the image of its peoples as bearers of an ancient, rich tradition stretching back millennia. Georgia occupied a special place in this narrative: a country with a unique language, centuries-old writing, a pre-Christian pantheon that could rival the Greek one in age and scale.

If petroglyphs datable to the 6th–5th millennia BCE and echoing Mesopotamian cultural forms had been discovered in the caves of Khvamli — it would have been an ideological victory. It would have proven: the Soviet peoples, including Georgians, were creators, not just recipients of civilization.

In 1948, such an argument carried weight. A great deal of weight.

---

## Parallel Precedents

Khvamli was not the only object the Soviet state dealt with in this manner.

During the same period, expeditions were underway to the petroglyphs of Azerbaijan (Gobustan); cave systems in Abkhazia were being explored; collections of Georgian manuscripts at the Kekelidze Institute of Manuscripts were being systematized; work was being conducted at Mtskheta and Armaziskhevi.

Everywhere, the same logic is traceable: the state actively appropriates and catalogs the cultural heritage of its republics. Sometimes — it publishes the results. Sometimes — it classifies them. The decision depended on how a particular discovery fit into the official narrative and whether it raised inconvenient questions.

Khvamli clearly raised inconvenient questions. Otherwise, the expedition's results would not have disappeared from open publication.

---

## Why Precisely 1948?

Beyond the general context of post-war scientific revival, the year 1948 itself had several specific features.

This year saw the unfolding of "Lysenkoism" in the USSR — the struggle against "bourgeois" genetics and cybernetics. This was a campaign in which the state directly dictated scientific content. Scientists who did not accept the party line risked their careers and freedom.

In the same year, control over publications sharply tightened: all significant discoveries had to pass through censorship filters before being published.

For Khvamli, this created a paradoxical situation. The expedition was sanctioned at a high level — meaning the state was interested. But there was no publication — meaning the result either did not fit the official narrative or was of such significant interest that it was deemed necessary to classify it.

Both options are intriguing. Both leave more questions than answers.

---

## Soviet Speleology as a Separate Story

Another contextual detail: in 1948, systematic Soviet speleology in Georgia did not yet exist as an institution.

Professional speleology in the Caucasus began in the early 1960s under the leadership of Levan Maruashvili. Before that, caves were studied either incidentally, during mountaineering or archaeological expeditions, or — precisely by people like Japaridze: professional mountaineers with research interests.
This means that in 1948, Japaridze was practically the only person in Georgia capable of combining the skills of a mountaineer, speleologist, and field researcher in one person.

The choice of the recipient for the order was not accidental.

---

## What This All Means for Us

So, 1948 is:
- Post-war revival of academic expeditions.
- Ideological need for cultural arguments in the nascent Cold War.
- The death of a leading historian, possibly granting the state access to his unfinished research.
- Tightening control over publications.
- A practical lack of alternatives to Japaridze as an executor.

All of this together paints a picture not of an anomaly, but of a pattern. The Soviet state was doing precisely what it was supposed to do under the given circumstances: it sent people to where, according to available data, an object of cultural value was located—and classified the results if they did not fit the established framework.

What Japaridze found in the caves of Khvamli—we do not know.

But that he found something—seems increasingly likely.

---

*Next chapter: The main instruction—'special attention to ancient drawings.'*

---

# Chapter 20. The Main Instruction: "Special Attention to Ancient Drawings"

> "He who does not know what he is looking for will find nothing. He who knows—will find precisely that."

---

Three words change everything.

"Special attention"—and then: "to ancient drawings."

These three words in a 1948 Soviet document are not just a task for an expedition. They are evidence. Evidence that the Soviet state—long before the expedition began—possessed information about the rock art of Khvamli. And not merely knew about it, but considered it important enough to place at the center of a secret operation.

Let's reflect on this.

---

## What It Means to "Know" About Petroglyphs in 1948

In 1948, there were no satellite images. No petroglyph database. No internet archives with scanned manuscripts.

Information about ancient rock drawings in a hard-to-reach mountain cave could have reached Soviet officials through only one of several channels: through academic publications, through travelers' notes, through intelligence sources, through oral tradition recorded by ethnographers.

All these channels had their own transmission time. And each of them tells us something about the nature of the information available to the directive's authors.

Let's examine each channel.

---

## Channel One: Vakhushti Bagrationi and Brosset

Prince Vakhushti in the 18th century described Khvamli in detail—including as a mountain identified with the planet Venus. His works were kept in the Institute of Manuscripts. By the mid-19th century, the French orientalist Marie-Félicité Brosset had translated and commented on a significant portion of Georgian historical literature, including the "Description of the Georgian Kingdom."

Brosset's works also mention Khvamli—as a place associated with treasure legends, with sacred tradition. But there are no direct mentions of "ancient drawings" in Brosset's available sources.

This is important: it means that either Brosset did not know about the petroglyphs, or they had not yet been discovered in his time, or he mentioned them in works that did not gain wide circulation.

---

## Channel Two: Soviet Expeditions of 1939 and 1945

Here is the key.

Soviet expeditions to Khvamli in 1939 and 1945 took place. This is a verified fact from several independent sources. Both expeditions discovered artifacts: fragments of ceramics, traces of hearths, red figures in rock niches.

The images from 1939 were described as "red rider figures, approximately 40–50 centimeters." Dating: 12th–13th centuries AD—that is, these are medieval drawings associated with the period when the Church of St. George was located on the mountain.

But these are the recorded medieval drawings. The 1948 directive speaks of "ancient" drawings. The difference is significant.

This means one of two things: either in 1939–1945, along with medieval images, earlier ones were discovered—and this data remained in classified reports. Or there were other sources pointing to an older layer of images, which the 1939 and 1945 expeditions could not fully document.

---

## Channel Three: Oral Tradition

Local residents in the Khvamli region preserved an oral tradition of the mountain as a sacred place for centuries.

In Georgian folklore, Khvamli is the mountain of Amiran, the place of imprisonment of a cultural hero. In this tradition, the mountain is inhabited by sacred objects: caves, water, signs.

The word "drawings" in folk memory could have been imprecise: "signs on the walls," "unclear images," "traces of old people." Ethnographers working in the region before the war could have recorded such indications.

If Soviet intelligence (or academic agents) collected information about cultural assets in the regions—oral tradition about "signs on the walls" could well have ended up in relevant reports.

---

## What "Ancient" Means

The word "ancient" in the directive is not a random adjective. It is a qualification.

In Soviet academic language, "ancient" applied to images means: pre-AD, presumably. Prehistoric. Requiring scientific interpretation.

If it were about medieval rider drawings—they would not need to be singled out. They were already found in 1939. Their meaning is clear: religious images of the 12th–13th centuries, associated with the Church of St. George.

"Special attention to ancient drawings" points to a different layer—one that was not exhaustively documented in previous expeditions. A layer that required a special search. Possibly—a layer about which there were indirect reports but no direct documentation.

---

## What They Didn't Know—and What They Wanted to Know

Here comes the moment of honest admission: we do not know exactly what was meant by "ancient drawings" in the 1948 directive.

But the logic of the investigation points to the following.

If medieval-period drawings were already known—then "ancient" in the directive means something earlier. Possibly Neolithic or Bronze Age images. Possibly symbols that someone had already identified as potentially significant—and wrote about in a classified report from 1939 or 1945.

And here a striking possibility emerges.

If the classified reports of the Soviet expeditions of 1939 and 1945 contain descriptions of earlier, Neolithic or Chalcolithic petroglyphs—that is, those very "drawings of celestial bodies" and "hunters by a pillar" mentioned in a number of sources without academic verification—then the 1948 directive was a targeted continuation of previous work.

The 1939 expedition found something. Could not fully document it. Wrote a classified report. Nine years later—the state decided to return and finish what was started.
## The Anomaly of Knowledge

There is another aspect worth highlighting separately.

In 1948, public science was unaware of the Khvamli petroglyphs as a significant site. In the open academic publications of that time — not a word. In Georgian scientific journals — silence. In Western sources — Khvamli is mentioned only in the context of the myth of Prometheus and the legend of treasures.

But the Soviet state — knew. And knew concretely enough to write "special attention to the ancient drawings."

This gap between public knowledge and state knowledge is one of the most eloquent arguments in favor of the fact that there was indeed something significant on the mountain, something intentionally removed from public circulation.

Information about the "ancient drawings" was in the system — but not for the public. It was with those who had access to the closed reports of previous expeditions.

---

## The Lingering Question

So: the Soviet state in 1948 knew about the "ancient drawings" of Khvamli. Where exactly this knowledge came from is a question we will examine in the next chapter.

But the very wording "special attention" has already told us something important.
