Not "find and describe everything that is there." Not "conduct a comprehensive survey." But — "special attention to the ancient drawings."

Someone already knew what to look for. And this knowledge means that behind this order lies a history — a history that does not begin on October 4, 1948. It began earlier. Possibly — much earlier.

---

*Next chapter: Sources of Information — How the Kremlin Knew About the Khvamli Petroglyphs.*

---

# Chapter 21. Sources of Information — How the Kremlin Knew

> "Behind every state secret stands someone who knew first — and told."

---

Tracing the path of knowledge is a special kind of detective work.

We have a fact: in 1948, the Soviet state knew about the "ancient drawings" on Mount Khvamli concretely enough to include this information in an official directive. Now we need to answer the question: where did this knowledge come from?

I propose to consider four versions — and not dismiss any prematurely.

---

## Version One: Reports of the 1939 and 1945 Expeditions

This is the most obvious and, likely, the primary channel.

The Soviet expeditions of 1939 and 1945 really existed. One source directly states: "In 1939, Stalin instructed Aliosha Japaridze to survey the Khvamli caves with two assistants." This suggests that as early as 1939, the state's interest in the mountain was sufficiently high.

The 1939 and 1945 expeditions found artifacts — pottery, traces of hearths, medieval images. But the closed reports of these expeditions could have contained significantly more. Soviet field researchers were obligated to document everything they saw — even what did not make it into open publications.

If traces of older images were noticed in 1939 — schematic signs, contour lines, unidentified symbols — this would have gone into the closed report. And a closed report could have lain in a desk drawer for nine years until an opportunity to return arose.

Nine years in the Soviet system — not long.

---

## Version Two: The Legacy of Brosset

Marie-Félicité Brosset spent considerable time in Georgia in the mid-19th century and wrote a number of works on the country's history and geography, including reports on archaeological travels.

In his texts, Khvamli is mentioned — in the context of treasure hunts, in the context of legends about the royal treasury. Brosset described Prince Dadiani's excavations in the 1840s as fruitless: they searched for treasures, did not find them. But "fruitless" for a treasure hunt does not mean "fruitless" for a search for images.

Brosset worked with Georgian manuscripts. In his archives — or in the archives of the Georgian Academy of Sciences, where his materials are stored — there could be records about something he saw or heard about the caves. Records that long remained unnoticed, and in the late 1940s came to the attention of Soviet scientists or officials.

---

## Version Three: Local Informants

In Soviet times, ethnographic expeditions regularly worked in the regions of Georgia — especially in the 1930s, during the era of systematic folklore collection.

The local residents of Racha — the district to which Khvamli belongs — preserved a living tradition about the mountain. They knew about the caves, knew about the "signs," knew about the sacred nature of the place.

In the Georgian folk language, the word "ნიშანი" — "nishani," sign — was applied to a wide variety of phenomena: drawings, notches, symbolic marks. An informant's story about "signs inside the mountain" could have been recorded by an ethnographer without understanding that it referred to something significant from an archaeological point of view. And then that report could have reached someone's attentive eyes.

---

## Version Four: Jules Verne and European Travel Notes

In 1883, Jules Verne mentioned in the novel "Kéraban the Inflexible" "the rock of Khomli, towering over Kutaisi, to which Prometheus was chained."

Verne did not travel to the Caucasus. He worked with sources. This means that by the 1880s, information about Khvamli as a sacred place associated with Prometheus was already circulating in European geographical and travel literature.

Soviet intelligence — and this is a well-documented fact — systematically studied foreign literature, travel notes, geographical descriptions for information about cultural values on the territory of the USSR. European notes on Caucasian travels were among the priority objects of study.

Perhaps it was precisely through this channel — or through its Soviet equivalent, the works of Russian and European travelers of the 19th century who visited Imereti — that information about "signs and drawings" on Khvamli became part of the official database.

---

## Synthetic Version: All Four Simultaneously

In practice — and this is likely closest to the truth — information accumulated through all four channels simultaneously.

Brosset mentioned the mountain. Ethnographers recorded legends. The 1939 expedition found something — and wrote a closed report. Academic historians — in particular, Simon Janashia before his death in 1947 — analyzed all available data.

By 1948, this conglomerate of information reached a critical mass. Someone — the name of this person we may never know — reviewed all the materials, put them together, and wrote the directive.

One sheet of paper. A few phrases. Three words: "special attention to drawings."

---

## The Key Question: What Exactly Was Known?

Here it is necessary to be honest: we do not know exactly what was contained in the information available to the Soviet state.

Perhaps it was a description of specific images — "hunters by a pillar," symbols that someone compared to something similar in Mesopotamia or Egypt.

Perhaps it was a more general indication: "on the cave walls there are unidentified signs, possibly very old."

Perhaps it was an expert opinion from one of the Soviet scientists — possibly Janashia himself or his colleagues — that Khvamli deserved a special expedition.

But the very presence of this information in the directive indicates that the question of the Khvamli petroglyphs had already been raised. By someone. Sometime. And the state decided to answer it.

---

## What This Tells Us About the Petroglyphs Themselves

There is one consequence of this analysis that is important for our understanding of the mountain.
If the 1948 directive was a reaction to real data about "ancient drawings," then these drawings exist. Or existed. They are significant enough to interest the state. They are specific enough to warrant "special attention."

They are not a product of popular imagination. They are real objects in real caves.

The fact that they have not yet been introduced into scholarly circulation is a consequence of what happened after the 1948–1949 expedition. And what happened afterward is a story about the Special Archive, about classified reports, and about a system that knew how to hide knowledge better than the most impregnable cliff.

But we will get to that.

---

*Next chapter: The Personal File of Aliosha Japaridze.*

---

# Chapter 22. The Personal File of Aliosha Japaridze

> "Mountains do not lie. A man on a mountain cannot be other than who he is."

---

In the village of Abari in the Ambrolauri municipality, in the Racha-Lechkhumi region, there is a small museum. It opened in 2017. It houses "archaeological, geological, ethnological, and other materials" collected by one man over the course of his entire life.

The museum bears the name of Aliosha Japaridze.

---

## A Biography Written by Mountains

Alexander — Aliosha — Japaridze was born in Svaneti, in a land where mountains are not an exotic adventure but a daily reality. Here, one did not need to learn to feel the rock: it came naturally, from childhood, with the first snow, with the first mountain pass.

A Svan boy, raised among tales of Dali and Kopala, among hunting legends and mountain spirits, became a Soviet mountaineer. This is not a contradiction — it is a synthesis. He carried within him both traditions: the ancient one, reaching back millennia, and the modern one, demanding technical mastery.

In 1934, he led the group that, for the first time in the history of Georgian and Soviet mountaineering, conquered Ushba — the "Mother of the Caucasus Mountains," one of the most technically difficult peaks in the entire mountain world. By his side was his sister Alexandra — the first woman to set foot on that summit. Two Japaridzes on one peak: this is not a coincidence, it is a family trait.

---

## Ushba as a Calling Card

The conquest of Ushba in 1934 made Japaridze famous in the Soviet sports world. But, more importantly for our story, it made him known as a man who could be entrusted with the impossible.

In the Soviet system, such a reputation had practical value. When the state needed a person capable of climbing where others could not — the choice fell on Japaridze.

He climbed peaks that others considered inaccessible. He knew how to work in conditions that would break others. He knew how to remain silent — a quality valued in the Soviet system above any technical qualification.

---

## Khvamli — Not the First Time

One source indicates that as early as 1939, Japaridze was sent to survey the caves of Khvamli — with two assistants, on an assignment that came from the highest level.

If this is true, then by 1948 he knew the mountain. He knew its character, knew the caves, knew what could be found there — and, perhaps, what had already been found the first time.

A repeat expedition with a similar assignment would mean one of two things: either the first expedition did not complete its work (hindered by war, lack of equipment, weather), or the results of the first expedition generated such interest that the state decided to study the object in more detail.

Both options increase the significance of what was found.

---

## The Character of the Researcher

Anyone who works seriously in the mountains knows: the mountain changes a person. It strips away the superfluous, leaving the essential.

Japaridze was not just an athlete. The museum in Abari holds "ethnological and other materials" — meaning he collected not only rock samples but also evidence of human culture: objects, images, records.

This is an important detail. A person who collects ethnological materials in the mountains is a researcher in the broad sense. Not one who completes an assignment and leaves, but one who sees, observes, documents.

Such a person in the caves of Khvamli would not have passed by "drawings on the walls." He would have stopped. He would have recorded. He would have sketched or photographed.

---

## What He Saw — We Do Not Know

Here comes the moment of honest admission.

We do not know exactly what Japaridze discovered in the caves of Khvamli in 1948–1949. His report — if it was written, and it was written because the system required accountability — went to the Special Archive. Or to Moscow. Or to both places.

What remains is the museum in Abari. The collection he gathered throughout his life. Among the exhibits there may be sketches. Photographs. Descriptions. Something he did not hand over to the state — or that the state did not deem necessary to confiscate.

This is the first point on the map for future researchers.

---

## Why Him?

Let's return to the question: why was the directive addressed specifically to Japaridze, and not, say, to an academic historian or a professional archaeologist?

The answer is simple: in 1948, there was no other person in Georgia who combined all the necessary qualities.

Technically — he could climb. Khvamli requires mountaineering skills: vertical cliffs, complex terrain, lack of convenient trails to the caves.

Methodologically — he knew how to work with material: collect samples, document, describe.

Politically — he was vetted. After the 1934 ascent and years of work within Soviet structures, he had passed all the necessary loyalty checks.

And — he knew the mountain. It was not his first visit.

---

## A Monument to Living History

Today, the Aliosha Japaridze Museum in Abari is open daily from noon to four o'clock. A small building in a small village in the mountains of Racha.

Perhaps it is there, in that museum, among the "archaeological, geological, ethnological materials," that the key to the Khvamli mystery is kept. Sketches made by his hand in the caves. Records he left not for the state, but for himself — or for those who would come after.

Mountains do not give up their secrets easily. But sometimes they give them to those who know how to ask correctly.

Japaridze knew how to ask.

His museum is a question that still awaits an answer.

---

*Next chapter: The Highest Resolution — "to report personally to Comrade Stalin."*

---

# Chapter 23. The Highest Resolution — "to report personally to Stalin"

> "In the Soviet hierarchy of power, every word had weight. The name of the leader — a weight that could break any structure."
---

There are formulations that change everything they touch.

"Report to be presented personally to Comrade Stalin."

If this phrase indeed appears in a document from Fond No. 1738—and the available evidence gives reason to believe it does—then it constitutes the strongest proof of the significance of the Khvamli petroglyphs that we possess.

Not because Stalin was wise or far-sighted. But because the Soviet system he headed functioned according to clear rules. And the violation of these rules—bypassing the multi-tiered bureaucratic hierarchy—meant something very specific.

---

## How Soviet Bureaucracy Worked

The Soviet state apparatus was a pyramid. A standard academic report traveled from the bottom to the top through all levels: field researcher → head of expedition → director of the institute → academician-secretary → president of the Academy of Sciences → the corresponding department of the Central Committee.

At each level, the document accumulated resolutions, amendments, and approvals. At each level, its content became known to a greater number of people.

Direct access to Stalin—bypassing this chain—was an exception to the rule. An exception applied in strictly defined cases:
— when it concerned matters of the highest state security,
— when the information was so secret that its passage through standard channels created a risk of leakage,
— when the subject was of personal interest to the leader.

Khvamli made it onto this list. This is a fact. And it demands an explanation.

---

## What Interested Stalin Personally

Iosif Dzhugashvili—Stalin—was a Georgian. This is a biographical fact that he spent his entire life meticulously minimizing in public, transforming himself into an "all-Soviet" and "international" leader.

But his Georgian identity never went away. It manifested in unexpected moments: in his interest in the Georgian language and literature, in a special attitude towards Georgia as "his" republic—and, according to available data, in his personal interest in the search for ancient Georgian treasures.

The legend of the Khvamli treasures is widely known. Several sources explicitly state: Stalin was interested in finding what the mountain held. This was an interest that went beyond academic curiosity.

But alongside the interest in treasures stood another interest: in history. In what could rewrite or confirm the place of Georgian (and Soviet) civilization in the global context.

"Ancient drawings" that could confirm a connection between the Caucasus and Mesopotamia or Egypt—these are not just petroglyphs. They are an argument. An ideological, cultural, political argument.

---

## What "Personally" Meant

In Soviet practice, "to report personally" meant physically bringing the report to the office. Or delivering an oral report during an audience.

It meant the document did not go through the chancellery. Was not endorsed by intermediate authorities. Did not lie in a pile of "incoming correspondence" in some department.

It went directly. From hand to hand.

This means: the content of the report was known to one—at most two—people besides the executor. Dzhaparidze knows. The person who signed the order knows. And Stalin knows.

That's it.

No one else.

---

## Where the Report Went

If the report was indeed presented personally to Stalin, then it is stored in one of the following places:
- Stalin's personal fond at the Russian State Archive of Socio-Political History (RGASPI).
- A special archive within the State Archive of the Russian Federation (GARF).
- Or—if it was destroyed after Stalin's death in 1953—it does not exist in any physical form.

The destruction of documents from Stalin's personal fond after his death is a real practice of Soviet history. Some papers were burned. Some were transferred to special fonds with restricted access. Some remain classified to this day.

This means: finding Dzhaparidze's report through Moscow archives is technically possible. But it requires an official request, access to classified fonds, and—no small amount of luck.

---

## What This Says About the Significance of the Find

Let's try to reason backwards: if we assume the report was presented, that Stalin read it—what did he see?

First possibility: the report was disappointing. Nothing substantial found. Caves empty. Drawings—medieval, nothing interesting. In this case, the matter would have been closed without consequences, and the materials—perhaps transferred to a regular academic archive.

Second possibility: the report contained something significant. Images that did not fit the standard picture. Objects or symbols that required interpretation. Something that confirmed or refuted some important version.

What happened next—silence. Complete, total, systemic silence.

It is precisely this silence that tells us it was the second possibility.

---

## Silence as an Answer

In the academic world, a positive result is published. A negative one—often too. A null result—is sometimes hushed up.

The Dzhaparidze expedition of 1948–1949 did not produce a single publication in the open press. Not one conference paper. Not even an indirect mention in Soviet academic journals of that period.

This is not a null result. A null result is not classified with such thoroughness.

This is a result that was decided not to be published. For what reason—we do not know. But the very decision not to publish indicates that the find was real.

And the fact that the report went "personally to Comrade Stalin" means: this real find was to remain where it ended up. In one copy. In one office. In one archive.

Where no one, except the very first reader, could see it.

---

*Next chapter: Spetskhran—the system of total secrecy.*

---

# Chapter 24. Spetskhran—The System of Total Secrecy

> "The most perfect way to hide knowledge is not to destroy it, but to place it within a system where no one looks for it."

---

In the Soviet system, there was a mechanism that worked better than any lock.

It was called "spetskhran"—special storage.

This is not a prison for documents. It is—another library. A parallel storage system where materials were placed that the state did not deem necessary to make accessible. Not to destroy—no, Soviet bureaucracy was meticulous about preserving documents. Simply—to store separately. Under a special order. With a special access regime.

Not everyone could get in there.

---
## How the Special Repository Worked

The system of Soviet closed archives developed gradually, starting in the 1920s, and by the 1940s had reached systemic maturity.

The principle was simple: any document that could have state, political, or strategic value was directed to a special collection with restricted access. Access required special permission—a "clearance"—issued by state security structures.

Special repositories existed at different levels: every state archive had its own closed collection; there were departmental special repositories of the MVD, MGB, and the Central Committee of the CPSU; and finally, there was the Kremlin archive—the most closed level of the system.

A document placed in a special repository was not destroyed. It was preserved. But it became invisible to the outside world. Researchers did not know of its existence. Even archivists working in the open collection of the same institution might not know that something specific was stored in the closed one.

---

## The Special Repository for Science

For academic research, the special repository meant the following: the results of an expedition conducted under a secrecy classification were not published. Field diaries, photographs, drawings, samples—all of this went into the closed collection.

A scientist who conducted a secret expedition, as a rule, did not have the right to publish its results without special permission. Such permission was rarely granted. Sometimes—it was never granted.

This meant: a person could know something important—and remain silent about it for the rest of their life. Not out of fear, although fear was certainly present. But also because the system was structured such that violating the secrecy regime meant criminal liability.

Under such conditions, silence was not cowardice—it was a condition for survival.

---

## Precedents of Soviet Classified Science on Caucasian Antiquity

Khvamli was not the only case.

In the 1930s, Soviet intelligence actively studied the material culture of the republics. Some of the found artifacts were described in open publications—with an ideological emphasis on the "rich heritage of the Soviet peoples." Some were classified—if a discovery contradicted the official narrative or created inconvenient political questions.

Inconvenient questions arose, for example, when a find indicated connections of a given people with foreign civilizations—which could be interpreted as "bourgeois nationalism" or as an undesirable argument in favor of independence.

The argument that the Khvamli petroglyphs are connected to Sumer or Egypt—is precisely such a dual argument. It could be interpreted both as "Soviet peoples are the source of world civilization" (good), and as "Georgians have a distinct cultural identity predating the Soviet period" (bad, in a certain context).

Which interpretation the compilers of the closed report chose—we do not know. But the choice was obviously made in favor of secrecy.

---

## 1991: Partial Opening

After the collapse of the USSR, Soviet archives in the former union republics came under the control of the new states. This meant partial declassification—but precisely partial.

In Georgia, the National Archive inherited the collections of the Central State Archive of the Georgian SSR, including its closed sections. Some collections were declassified. Some—were not. Some are in a transitional state: formally accessible, in practice—uncataloged or difficult to access.

Collection No. 1738—if it exists precisely under that number—could be in any of these states. This is work for a professional archivist with an official request and patience.

---

## What Does the Special Repository Hold?

Regarding Khvamli, the special repository—whether in Tbilisi or Moscow—most likely contains the following.

Dzhaparidze's report: a textual description of the expedition, its route, discovered objects. This could be a document ranging from a few pages to dozens of pages—depending on the scale of the work.

Illustrative materials: drawings, sketches, photographs. In 1948, photographic equipment was already available for expeditions. Photographs of the caves, photographs of images on the walls.

Samples: possibly small fragments of rock with images or from near them—for subsequent analysis.

Evaluative part: interpretation of the finds—what they are, when, what their significance is.

And—a signature. Or several signatures. Of those who accepted this report.

All these documents—if they exist—lie in the archive. They are not destroyed. The Soviet system did not destroy documents just like that. It hid them.

And what is hidden can be found.

---

## The Irony of the System

There is a peculiar irony in the fact that the system created to conceal knowledge is simultaneously its best preserver.

If the results of Dzhaparidze's expedition had been published in the open press—they might have been reprinted, cited, and then lost in the general flow of Soviet academic publications.

Because in the special repository, a document is protected from chance. It cannot get lost among other papers. It cannot be destroyed by a library fire. The registration system ensures its existence—even if access to it is closed.

Paradox: the most secret document is often the best-preserved document.

Dzhaparidze's report, if it existed, exists somewhere now.

It is waiting.

---

*Next chapter: A White Spot on the Map of Science.*

---

# Chapter 25. A White Spot on the Map of Science

> "The absence of publication is also information. Sometimes—the most important."

---

Open any Soviet academic journal on the history of Georgia from 1949–1960.

Look for the word "Khvamli."

It is not there.

No article. No note. No conference abstract. No mention in a footnote. Complete, absolute silence.

This is strange. Very strange—considering that by the 1940s, Mount Khvamli was known as a sacred monument, the site of the legend of Amiran, a possible repository of royal treasures. Normal Soviet academic tradition assumed: if you conducted an expedition—you published the results. Even modestly, even within the framework of the official narrative, but—you published.

Dzhaparidze—published nothing.

---

## What Should Have Been Published

Assume that the result of the 1948–1949 expedition was zero: nothing significant was found, the drawings turned out to be medieval, there are no petroglyphs of "deep antiquity."

Even in this case, standard academic practice would have suggested a brief note: "Expedition to Mount Khvamli. Survey of cave systems. Data on medieval artifacts." This is routine. This requires nothing sensational.

This would have been published—because Soviet science needed to demonstrate activity, report metrics, show "results."

The fact that nothing was published means one of two things. Either the state classified the results—and then they are significant. Or the state decided that the results should not enter public circulation for other reasons. But there simply were no "other reasons" for classifying a zero result in the Soviet system.

Silence after 1949—is a signal.
---

## Parallel Silence of the West

But Soviet science is not the only context in which this blank spot exists.

In Western academic publications, Khvamli is practically absent until the end of the 20th century. One or two mentions in the context of Caucasian mythology, one tourist article in the English-language press of the 2000s, a few academic references — and that's all.

For a mountain that is one of the richest mythological sites in the entire Caucasus — this is strikingly little. This is a blank spot of a different nature: not Soviet secrecy, but simply a lack of systematic attention.

Two blank spots — Soviet and Western — close off Khvamli from both sides. The mountain finds itself in a zone of double non-vision.

---

## Comparison: What Was Studied, What Was Not

To understand the scale of the problem, a comparison is useful.

During the same period — the late 1940s, 1950s, 1960s — Soviet science actively published materials on:
- Gobustan petroglyphs (Azerbaijan) — regular publications, international documentation.
- Trialeti petroglyphs (Georgia) — several expeditions, publications.
- Mtskheta and Armaziskhevi — large-scale excavations, monographs.
- Vardzia cave monastery — complete documentation.

Khvamli — is absent from this list.

Why was Gobustan published, but not Khvamli? Why was Vardzia studied, but Khvamli was not?

The only answer that withstands logical scrutiny: because something was found on Khvamli that was not meant to be published.

---

## 2011: The First Evidence

The first systematic public evidence of what happened on the mountain in Soviet times appears only in 2011 — from the organization "Green Alternative," which conducted work to document oral testimonies from local residents.

What the residents of Racha and Imereti told — these are not academic data. This is folk memory of "secret operations," of "people from Tbilisi," of something being taken away from the mountain.

Folk memory is imprecise. It mythologizes, shifts chronology, mixes real events with legends. But it rarely invents from nothing. Behind every story about "people from Tbilisi who were taking something away" lies some real observation.

---

## 2020: Official Recognition

In 2020, the government of Georgia adopted Decree No. 123, granting Khvamli the status of a "monument of national significance."

This is an official act that requires justification. A state does not declare places "monuments of national significance" without grounds — it is a legally significant action requiring a documentary basis.

What lies behind this decree? What data formed its foundation? Which experts prepared the justification?

The 2020 decree is another document that requires study. Because in its appendices or during its preparation, materials that were previously closed could have surfaced.

The official recognition of the mountain as a monument means: someone knows enough about its significance to take this step.

---

## The Blank Spot as a Method

For a historian and a scientist, a blank spot is not only a problem. It is a method.

When there is no information about a certain place on the map — it means information existed — and someone removed it. Or — it exists, but in an inaccessible repository.

Both options say: there is something here.

The blank spot of Khvamli on the map of Soviet and world science is not a failure of knowledge. It is a designation of a place where knowledge existed — and to which one must return.

The return begins with the archives.

---

*Next chapter: The 1949 Field Diary — What We Know.*

---

# Chapter 26. The 1949 Field Diary

> "A diary is a conversation with oneself, which sometimes becomes a conversation with the future."

---

Let us imagine this diary.

We do not know for certain if it exists in the form we imagine. But we know: Soviet field researchers kept records. It was a practice, sometimes an obligation. Dzhaparidze, as a man with a research temperament — judging by the fact that his museum holds "ethnological and other materials" — almost certainly recorded what he saw.

Let us try to reconstruct this diary — not from fantasy, but from logic.

---

## October 1948 — The Beginning

The directive is dated October 4, 1948. This means that by mid-October the group should have begun work. It is autumn in Imereti: the weather is still holding, the nights are cold, but the caves are dry and accessible.

The road to the mountain from Kutaisi — several hours in Soviet transport of 1948. Then — the ascent. For a climber of Dzhaparidze's level, Khvamli did not present technical difficulty. But with equipment — ropes, lanterns, cables for descending vertical shafts — the ascent took more time.

The first entry in the hypothetical diary could have been concrete: weather, trail condition, first impressions. Soviet field reports began exactly like that: date, weather, route.

---

## Tekenteli Cave

The main point of the expedition — Tekenteli Cave, the most significant in the Khvamli system.

Its entrance — through a hollow in a large beech tree, a tree that itself is a spatial marker, indicating the location. After the entrance — a vertical karst shaft about eighteen meters deep. Below — an underground lake and a water stream leading to a waterfall.

For a person in 1948 without modern equipment, this was a difficult descent. For Dzhaparidze — routine work.

In the diary, there would likely have been a description of the first descent: "temperature in the shaft about ten degrees," "humidity high," "lantern light reflects off the limestone walls," "sound of water heard from below."

---

## The Cave Walls

The most important thing — the walls.

In the limestone caves of the Caucasus, the walls have a special surface: smooth, slightly damp limestone. It is precisely on such a surface that petroglyphs were made — scratched with a sharp stone or covered with ochre.

In the darkness of the cave, under lantern light, the images appear differently than in daylight. Shadow makes the scratched lines more noticeable. Colored images — ochre, charcoal — more contrasting.

What did Dzhaparidze see on the cave walls?
We know about the medieval red figures of horsemen. They were documented as early as 1939. The return visit in 1948 was not looking for them—it was looking for something else. Something the directive called "ancient drawings."

---

## Symbols That Cannot Be Dated by Eye

In a cave environment without special equipment, it is extremely difficult to distinguish an image from the 12th–13th century from one from the 5th–6th millennium BCE. Both are scratched onto the same limestone. Both can have the same patina color.

But the form is different.

Medieval images are recognizable objects: horsemen, crosses, animals, sometimes inscriptions. They are legible.

Neolithic or Chalcolithic petroglyphs are fundamentally different: schematic, abstract, geometric. Dots, lines, spirals, figures composed of several elements. Alongside these are anthropomorphic schemas: a human reduced to a few lines.

If Dzhaparidze saw symbols on the walls that were neither horsemen nor crosses—he understood: this is older. How much older—he could not determine without laboratory analysis. But he could record: "images of a non-Christian character are present."

---

## Hypothetical Records

A professional researcher's field diary in the cave could have contained the following.

Schematic sketches of the images—rough croquis, a few minutes of work with a pencil. Description of location: "right wall, approximately three meters from the entrance," "ceiling in the northern part of the hall." Description of technique: "scratched," "applied with red pigment," "applied with black." Description of character: "geometric forms," "anthropomorphic figures," "animals—indeterminate."

And—most importantly: an evaluative judgment. What is this, from the field researcher's point of view? "Looks like..." or "resembles..." or "unlike anything known to me."

It is precisely this evaluative judgment—if it was made in favor of "very old" images—that determined the fate of the diary and the report.

---

## What Remains

If the hypothetical diary exists in physical form—it is in the museum in Abari. Or in an archive unknown to us.

If it does not exist—indirect evidence remains: what the museum holds. Objects collected by Dzhaparidze over his lifetime.

A researcher who goes to Abari and requests permission to study the museum's holdings—may find there what is not in any archive. Not because Dzhaparidze was hiding it. But because he kept it—where personal things are kept.

This is the next step.

---

*Next chapter: Legends and Truth—the Oral Tradition of Local Residents.*

---

# Chapter 27. Legends and Truth—the Oral Tradition of Local Residents

> "Folk memory is not history. But it always contains a grain of an event."

---

In 2011, the organization "Green Alternative" was working in the Khvamli region.

They did not come for petroglyphs or for Soviet archives. They were engaged in monitoring the ecological and cultural situation in the region. But along the way—as often happens in fieldwork—they heard stories.

They recorded them. Systematized them. Published them—to the extent their activity format allowed.

These accounts are not academic sources. They do not replace archival documents. But they are the only thing that passed through the Soviet system of silence and remained accessible.

---

## "People from the City"

In the oral testimonies of residents of Racha and adjacent regions, one constant motif appears: "people from the city"—from Tbilisi or Moscow—who came to the mountain. Went up. Did something there. And left without explaining anything.

The chronology in these stories is generally vague. "In Soviet times," "before the war," "after the war," "when I was still a boy." Age markers are often unreliable: a narrator born in 1940 might be telling what he himself heard from his father—that is, events from the 1930s.

But the very presence of this motif—"people from the city came to our mountain"—is not invented. Behind it are real observations by real people.

---

## "They Took Something Out"

The next persistent motif: "they took something out."

Boxes. Bundles. "Old things." More precisely—the narrators, as a rule, cannot specify. Not because they are lying, but because they saw the process from afar, not the contents.

In the context of Soviet expeditions, these could have been: rock samples, pottery fragments, artifacts from the medieval period, photographic equipment.

But it could have been something else. If objects were found in the caves—not just images, but physical objects—from the pre-Christian or earlier period, they could have been removed and sent to "special storage" along with the report.

The folk memory of "things taken out" is not a legend. It is an observation that became a legend over eighty years.

---

## Local Tradition About the Mountain

Alongside stories about Soviet operations exists a more ancient layer of oral tradition.

In the Khvamli region, residents remembered: the mountain is sacred. Not in the Christian sense (although the Church of St. George was there). But in a more ancient, pre-Christian sense.

Here Amiran is chained. Here his chains are held. Here his dog Kursha licks these chains year after year. Here, deep within the mountain, lives something that should not be disturbed.

This tradition is not an invention of the 19th century. It is recorded in Georgian texts from early periods, it found reflection in the records of Vakhushti, it reached Jules Verne. It lives in oral memory because it has roots.

---

## "Books Inside the Mountain"

A special motif, which we will examine in more detail in subsequent chapters, is the stories about "books" or "records" hidden inside the mountain.

In the version that sounds in various forms among the region's residents: "somewhere inside the mountain, old books are kept that must not be touched." Sometimes—"books written before times."

"Books" in this context are not necessarily literal books. In the Georgian tradition, the word "წიგნი" ("ts'igni") can mean any written or symbolic text. Images on stone are also "ts'igni," a "record," a "book."

The story of "books inside the mountain" is likely a folk interpretation of petroglyphs. People saw "signs on the walls" and interpreted them as "something written."

This is direct evidence that petroglyphs were seen. That they exist. That local residents knew about them.

---

## What Oral Tradition Adds

To academic analysis, oral tradition adds the following.

It confirms: Soviet expeditions happened. People saw them. Something was happening on the mountain in Soviet times.

It confirms: "signs" on the mountain are known to local residents. They are neither a secret nor an invention.
She adds: in folk memory, these signs are preserved as something significant — "books," "records," "that which must not be touched."

This is not proof. But it is convergent evidence, which aligns with what the archival hints tell us.

---

## Why This Matters

In historical research, oral tradition is never a primary source. But it is an indicator.

Where several independent narrators speak of the same thing — something happened. Even if the details diverge, even if the chronology is shifted, even if the images are mythologized — an event occurred.

The oral tradition of the Khvamli region says: Soviet people came to the mountain. Did something in the caves. Took something away. The mountain holds "records."

This is a version of events awaiting confirmation from the archives.

When the archives open — we will learn how accurate folk memory is.

---

*This concludes Part III. The Secret Archive — The 1948–1949 Expedition.*

*Next part: The Khvamli Transfer Hypothesis.*

---

# Chapter 28. The Khvamli Transfer Hypothesis

> "Civilization is not what is built. It is what is transmitted."

---

Before proceeding further, we must pause.

Thus far, we have spoken of the mountain. Of myths. Of caves. Of the Soviet expedition. Now — we must take a step back and formulate the central question that permeates this entire book.

This question is not "what is inside the caves of Khvamli?" — although that is important.

The main question is this: **how exactly could ideas born at the foot of this mountain have spread across the ancient world?**

The answer to this question is the Khvamli Transfer Hypothesis.

---

## Formulation

The Khvamli Transfer Hypothesis (KHT, Khvamli Transfer Hypothesis, KTH) is a hypothesis about the role of Khvamli and Colchis as a nodal center of ideational diffusion: the spread not of people, but of ideas — concepts, symbolic systems, religious images — from the Caucasus to Sumer, Canaan, and Egypt.

The key word here is "ideational diffusion."

This is not a migration theory. I do not claim that Colchians migrated to Mesopotamia and literally brought their culture with them. I do not claim that Georgians founded Sumerian civilization or built the Egyptian pyramids.

I claim something else: that certain ideas — the image of the sacred mountain, the concept of the chained hero, the cult of the morning star, metallurgy as sacred knowledge — could have arisen in the Caucasus in a specific form, and traces of this form can be traced in several great civilizations of the ancient world.

---

## Ideational Diffusion: What It Is

Anthropologists and cultural historians are well acquainted with this mechanism.

Ideas travel faster than people. A merchant passing through several cultures carries not only goods, but also words, images, stories. A myth heard among one people can take root among another — altered, yet recognizable.

The Caucasus in antiquity was a crossroads of trade routes. From the 3rd millennium BCE, copper and bronze of Colchian production are recorded across the Near East. This is a documented fact. Metal traveled. With the metal traveled craftsmen. With the craftsmen — legends about where this knowledge came from.

The myth of Amiran, chained to the mountain for stealing fire and metallurgical secrets — is a story about how knowledge costs its bearer his life. It is a story that makes sense primarily in a culture where metallurgy was sacred, guarded knowledge.

---

## Four Vectors

The KHT describes four possible directions for the spread of ideas from the Khvamli cultural area.

**The Sumerian Vector**: possible connections between Kartvelian languages and Sumerian, the concept of "Dur-an-ki" (bond of heaven and earth), the natural proto-*ziggurat*, petroglyphs as proto-writing.

**The Canaanite Vector**: the cult of the morning star — the "star of Khomli" — as a possible precursor to Sumerian Inanna, Babylonian Ishtar, Canaanite Astarte. The path of Venus as a religious program.

**The Egyptian Vector**: the image of Khvamli as an "island of creation" in the flooded Rioni valley — a parallel to the Egyptian Benben. "Son of the Sun" Aeëtes — a parallel to the Egyptian pharaoh as "son of Ra."

**The Caucasian-Iberian Vector**: Khvamli as a legitimizing axis of power for the proto-states of Diauehi and Colchis. Metallurgy as a political and religious force.

---

## Three Conditions for Verifying the Hypothesis

For the hypothesis to claim the status of a scientific theory, proof of three key conditions is necessary.

**1. Chronological Priority of Khvamli.** It is necessary to establish that the symbolic complexes of Khvamli (petroglyphs, sanctuary structure) chronologically precede their supposed echoes in Sumer and Egypt. Today, the biggest problem is dating. The earliest reliably dated rock images at Khvamli belong to the 12th–13th centuries CE. Confirming the hypothesis requires methods capable of "aging" these symbols by 2–3 millennia, which has not yet been achieved.

**2. Intermediate Links.** Archaeological evidence of contacts is required — not only trade (metal), but also cultural — which could have served as "bridges" for the transmission of ideas. These could be cult objects, specific ornaments, or traces of ritual practices at sites in Asia Minor, Northern Mesopotamia, or the Levant, demonstrating a mixture of Caucasian and Near Eastern elements.

**3. Specificity, Not Universality.** It is necessary to prove that the parallels (e.g., "mountain-axis of the world," "chained culture hero") are not universal archetypes arising independently among different peoples, but a unique, detailed complex. Specific, "necessary" coincidences in plots, attributes, and semantic connections must be identified, which cannot be explained by simple convergence.

---

## The Position of Opponents

Criticism of the Khvamli Transfer Hypothesis from the academic mainstream boils down to several fundamental objections.

**1. The Dating Problem.** The primary and most weighty argument. The lack of reliable early dating for the Khvamli petroglyphs calls into question the very principle of chronological priority. Opponents rightly point out that the similarity of symbols could be the result of later influences in the opposite direction — from the Near East to the Caucasus — or even of random coincidences.

**2. Hyper-Diffusionism.** Critics see in the KHT features of outdated hyper-diffusionism — the tendency to derive all cultural achievements from a single center. They emphasize that parallels in myths (mountain, hero, star) are easily explained by anthropological constants and independent development under similar geographical or social conditions.

**3. Linguistic Speculativeness.** Attempts to link the Sumerian language with Kartvelian languages remain a marginal hypothesis not accepted by the majority of linguists. Without a solid linguistic "bridge," the hypothesis about the transmission of complex ideas loses an important channel of substantiation.

**4. Selectivity of Evidence.** Opponents note that proponents of the hypothesis often construct chains of similarities while ignoring fundamental differences in political systems, social structure, and material culture between Colchis and the ancient kingdoms of Mesopotamia.

---

## What the KHT Does and Does Not Claim

It is important to immediately delineate the boundaries of the hypothesis.

**What the KHT is NOT:**
*   A claim about the "Georgian origin" of Sumerian, Egyptian, or Canaanite civilization. That would be nationalist fantasy, not science.
*   Proof of a genetic relationship between Kartvelian languages and Sumerian.
*   A claim that all parallels are the result of direct contacts. Some of them may be the result of parallel independent development.

**What the KHT proposes:**
First: to consider the Caucasus, and specifically the Khvamli/Colchis region, not only as a passive corridor, but also as a possible active cultural generator in deep antiquity.
Second: to acknowledge that a number of specific parallels between the Khvamli tradition and the great civilizations of the ancient world require an explanation beyond simple coincidence.
Third: as a working explanation for these parallels, consider the model of ideational diffusion through trade and cultural channels of the Bronze and Early Iron Ages.

---

## Why This Matters

If the KHT ever finds serious confirmation, it will change our understanding of Caucasian history.

In such a paradigm, Georgia ceases to be a "periphery" and becomes "one of the sources." Not the only one. Not the main one. But one of them. An active participant, not a passive observer in the processes of ancient civilization formation.

This shift in perspective is not merely an academic reformulation. It is a different history. A history in which the people living at the foot of the mountain with petroglyphs on cave walls were not savages on the outskirts of great cultures, but potential co-authors of the ideas that these cultures later embodied in stone and clay.

---

*Next chapter: Four Vectors of Civilization.*
---

# Chapter 29. Four Vectors of Civilization

> "A map is not the world. But without a map, it is easy to get lost in the world."

---

Before delving into the details of each of the four directions of the Khvamli Transfer, a general overview is necessary—a bird's-eye view that allows one to see the entire structure.

The four vectors are not just the structure of the book. They are an analytical map. Four directions along which parallels are traced between Khvamli/Colchis and the great civilizations of the ancient world.

The map does not prove the theory. It shows that the theory has structure. It is not arbitrary. Behind each direction lies a specific corpus of facts.

---

## Vector One: Sumer

Sumer — Southern Mesopotamia, the territory of modern Iraq. 4th–3rd millennium BCE. The first great civilization with writing, cities, ziggurats, and law codes.

The distance from Khvamli to Nippur—the sacred center of Sumer—is about two thousand kilometers. For the Bronze Age, when trade routes connected the Caucasus and Mesopotamia, this distance was not insurmountable.

Copper from Colchis is documented in Mesopotamia from the 3rd millennium BCE. This is a documented fact. The metal traveled through Anatolia, through Transcaucasia, along trade routes that can be conventionally called the "Copper Route" of the Bronze Age.

Along with copper, craftsmen could have moved. Along with craftsmen—legends and concepts.

The Sumerian vector of the KHT describes parallels in three areas: language, cosmology, and symbolism.

---

## Vector Two: Canaan

Canaan — the Levant, the territory of modern Israel, Palestine, Lebanon, western Syria. 2nd millennium BCE — 1st millennium BCE.

Between the Caucasus and the Levant lies Anatolia, through which trade routes have passed since prehistoric times. The Hittites, who inhabited Anatolia in the 2nd millennium BCE, were likely intermediaries in this exchange.

The Canaanite vector of the KHT is, first and foremost, the history of the cult of the morning star.

In the Georgian tradition, recorded by Vakhushti in the 18th century, Mount Khomli is identified with the planet Venus. This is an astral cult associated with the rising and setting of the "morning star."

In Sumer, Venus is Inanna. In Babylon, it is Ishtar. In Canaan, it is Astarte. All three goddesses share a structural matrix: duality (morning and evening star), connection with fertility and war, the motif of descent into the underworld and return.

Could this astral cult have originated at the foot of Mount Khomli and spread through Anatolia to the Levant? This is a hypothesis. However, the structural coincidences are precise enough to consider it seriously.

---

## Vector Three: Egypt

Egypt — the most distant point. Three and a half thousand kilometers from Khvamli to the Valley of the Kings.

At first glance—too far for direct connections. However, Egypt and the Near East were closely interconnected. Canaanite merchants traded with Egypt. Egyptian pharaohs sent expeditions to the Near East for resources.

The Egyptian vector of the KHT is not an assertion of direct contacts. It is an analysis of structural parallels.

Egyptian cosmogony: the Benben—the primordial mound that rose from the waters of the primeval chaos (Nun). This is the place where land first appeared. The first act of creation—a mountain above water.

Khvamli during the floods of the Rioni could have been perceived precisely in this way: a mountain above water, the first point of solidity in a world of flood. If the Neolithic inhabitants of Colchis interpreted this as the "first act of creation"—and if this idea could have traveled along trade routes—then we have one of the possible routes for the spread of the "Benben" concept.

This is not proof. It is a hypothesis with a landscape basis.

---

## Vector Four: Caucasian Iberia

The most obvious—and yet less studied in the context of the transfer—vector.

Khvamli was a sacred center not only for Colchis but for the entire region. The proto-states of Diaokhi (2nd–1st millennium BCE) and Colchis could have used the sacred mountain as a legitimizing center of power.

This is the internal Caucasian vector. Not diffusion outward, but formation from within.

The mountain as an axis of power. The ruler who controls the sacred mountain—controls legitimacy. Whoever owns the symbolic center—owns the political space.

This tradition can be traced from the Bronze Age proto-states to the medieval Georgian kings who, according to a 14th-century chronicle, kept their regalia in the caves of Khvamli.

---

## The Map in Action

Take a map.

Place a point on Khvamli in western Georgia.

Draw a line eastward through Transcaucasia to Mesopotamia—this is the Sumerian vector. About two thousand kilometers.

Draw a line southward through Anatolia to the Levant—this is the Canaanite vector. About one and a half thousand kilometers through active trade routes.

Extend the Canaanite line further southwest to Egypt—another thousand kilometers. The Levant in this model acts as a possible intermediary.

And draw a circle around Colchis itself—this is the Caucasian-Iberian vector. Internal.

This is the map of the KHT. Four arrows from one point.

---

## A Warning to the Reader

Before diving into the details of each vector, an important methodological note is necessary.

The KHT is a hypothesis, not an established fact. Many of the parallels that will be considered in the following chapters have justifications but are not definitively proven. Some of them may be explained by independent parallel development under similar conditions—without any direct contact.

Science requires honesty regarding the degree of proof.

But science also requires asking new questions.

The KHT is precisely such a question: what if the Caucasus was not a periphery, but one of the sources of certain cultural complexes?
Let us see how far this question can take us.

---

*Next chapter: The Linguistic Bridge — Kartvelian Languages and Sumerian.*
---

# Chapter 30. The Linguistic Bridge — Kartvelian Languages and Sumerian

> "Language is not just a way of speaking. It is a way of thinking. And ways of thinking travel."

---

Let us begin with a provocative question.

Why is the Sumerian word for copper — *urudu* — so similar to a Kartvelian compound root meaning "big hammer" plus "chief/master"?

This is not just a play on words. It is one of the fragments of the mosaic that forms the linguistic vector of the KHT. However, it is important to clarify immediately: in modern linguistics, such parallels are considered not as proof of genetic relationship, but as material for hypotheses about ancient contacts.

**The Skeptics' Position (Rubio, 1999):** G. Rubio, in his work "On the Alleged Pre-Sumerian Substratum" (*Journal of Cuneiform Studies*, 1999), convincingly demonstrated that most of the alleged "pre-Kartvelian" and "Caucasian" lexical layers in the Sumerian language are explained by typological coincidences, not historical contacts. According to Rubio, the very concept of a "pre-Kartvelian substrate" in Sumerian is a methodological error. The author of this book accepts this criticism as substantial: the KHT does not claim direct linguistic kinship, but only records structural parallels that require explanation. The linguistic argument is the most vulnerable in the KHT system and requires the greatest caution.

---

## The Georgian Language and Its Place in History

The Georgian language is one of the few in the world that does not belong to any of the major language families. It is not Indo-European. Not Semitic. Not Turkic. Not Afroasiatic.

It is Kartvelian — that is, it belongs to a separate, isolated language family formed by four closely related languages: Georgian, Megrelian, Svan, and Laz.

This isolation itself says something important: the Kartvelian languages are not a lateral branch of some large tree, but a separate tree. A very old tree with very deep roots.

In 2023, a study on the origin of the Kartvelian languages was published in the journal *Scientific Reports*. The results showed: the homeland of the Kartvelian languages was located "near modern eastern Turkey and Lake Van" — that is, in immediate proximity to the Sumerian ecumene. Dating: about five to six thousand years ago.

This means: the ancestors of the Georgians lived next to Mesopotamia precisely during the period when Sumerian civilization was forming there.

---

## Nikolai Yakovlevich Marr and the Japhetic Theory

Academician Nikolai Yakovlevich Marr (1865–1934) was one of the most influential and controversial scholars of his time.

The son of a Scottish gardener and a Georgian peasant woman, born in Kutaisi, Marr became the leading Soviet linguist. His "Japhetic theory," developed from 1912, asserted the existence of "Japhetic" languages — a related group uniting the Kartvelian languages of the Caucasus with the languages of the Mediterranean and the Near East. Marr included Sumerian in this group.

**Important Clarification:** Marr's Japhetic theory, despite its historical significance and scope, was rejected by modern comparative-historical linguistics as methodologically unsound. Its main postulates do not meet the criteria for establishing linguistic kinship accepted in science. However, at its core lay a genuine research question: why do Kartvelian languages exhibit typological parallels with the languages of the Near East?

This question, stripped of Marr's methodology, remains open to this day.

---

## Ivane Javakhishvili and a Strategic Observation

Ivane Javakhishvili (1876–1940) — the founder of Georgian historical science, the first rector of Tbilisi University, an academician.

In his research, he wrote a phrase that has not lost its significance to this day: "The deeper we look into the history of the Kartvelian languages, the clearer their genetic contacts with the languages of the ancient civilizations of the Near East: Urartian, Hittite, Sumerian, Elamite."

Javakhishvili used the word "contacts," not "kinship" — this is a fundamental difference. Not common origin, but interaction. Borrowings. Traces of a shared history, not necessarily a shared ancestor.

---

## Copper and Metallurgy: *Urudu* — Anna Meskhi's Hypothesis

Anna Meskhi, a Georgian linguist and researcher, in a series of works analyzed the metallurgical vocabulary of Kartvelian and Sumerian languages.

Her key **hypothesis** is as follows: the Sumerian word *urudu* (copper) can be decomposed into two Kartvelian roots.

First: *uro* — "big hammer," a blacksmith's tool. Second: *du* — "chief," "head," "master."

Thus, "the master's big hammer" — could have been a designation for metal in a culture where metallurgy was the craft that defined the master's status.

Copper metallurgy in the Caucasus dates to the 5th millennium BCE. This is earlier than Sumerian. If Caucasian tribes indeed transmitted metallurgical knowledge to Mesopotamia — words could have traveled along with the knowledge. **It is important to emphasize that this remains a speculative, albeit intriguing, hypothesis, not confirmed by a system of regular phonetic correspondences.**

---

## An Honest Clarification and the Position of Mainstream Linguistics

A strict clarification reflecting the consensus of modern science is necessary here.

Mainstream comparative-historical linguistics does not recognize a genetic relationship between Kartvelian languages and Sumerian. Critics, such as I.M. Diakonoff and S.A. Starostin, pointed to the absence of systematic sound correspondences and reconstructable common morphology — the cornerstones for proving kinship. In their view, individual lexical parallels are more convincingly explained by early borrowings through cultural contact or random coincidences, especially when comparing isolated languages.

*Urudu* = Kartvelian root — this is an interesting observation, but not proof of kinship. It could be a borrowing. It could be a random coincidence. It could be a trace of real, but limited, cultural contact.

We do not know for sure. And scientific honesty requires acknowledging this.

---

## The Skeptics' Position: Contact, Not Kinship

Summarizing the critical view, the following position, shared by most comparative linguists, can be outlined:

1.  **Lack of a System:** Isolated lexical convergences, even convincing at first glance (like *urudu*), are not proof of kinship without identifying a system of regular phonetic correspondences in basic vocabulary.
2.  **Priority of Borrowings:** All observed parallels between Kartvelian and ancient Near Eastern languages, including Sumerian, are satisfactorily explained by the theory of prolonged cultural contacts and borrowings under conditions of geographical proximity.
3.  **Typology ≠ Genetics:** The presence of some typological similarities (e.g., in word structure or agglutinative nature) is a common phenomenon in the world's languages and does not indicate common origin.
4.  **Burden of Proof:** The burden of proof lies with the proponents of the kinship hypothesis, and to date they have not presented evidence meeting the strict standards of comparative linguistics.

Thus, skeptics acknowledge the possibility and even probability of deep historical contacts, but completely reject the idea of genetic kinship.

---

## What We Do Know

Despite the validity of the criticism, we know the following.

Geographically: the homeland of the Kartvelian languages was located next to Mesopotamia precisely during the period of Sumerian civilization formation.

Technologically: Caucasian metallurgy preceded Sumerian, and metal from Colchis is recorded in Mesopotamia from the 3rd millennium BCE.

Linguistically: a number of metallurgical and cultural terms show parallels between Kartvelian and Sumerian languages that require explanation — even if that explanation lies in the realm of borrowings.

Typologically: Kartvelian languages and Sumerian demonstrate a number of typological similarities noted by several generations of scholars.

This is enough to say: **connections existed.** The nature of these connections — borrowings, contact, random coincidence, or something deeper — requires further, methodologically impeccable research.

And it is precisely this research that has not been conducted in full. Because Khvamli and its petroglyphs remain undated, and the main archives remain closed.

---

*Next chapter: Petroglyphs and Proto-Cuneiform.*
---
# Chapter 31. Petroglyphs and Proto-Writing

> "The first writing is a drawing that forgot it was a drawing and decided to become a word."

---

Between 3600–3200 BCE, something revolutionary happened in Sumerian Uruk.

People began to draw not what they saw—but what they thought.

The first tablets from Uruk are pictograms: concrete images of a cow, a sack of grain, a person. But gradually, the images became abstracted. The cow turned into a sign denoting not a specific animal, but the concept of "cattle in general." The sack of grain—into the concept of "grain," "harvest," "tax."

After a few centuries, pictograms turned into cuneiform. Writing—an invention that changed the world.

But what if the beginning of this process was not only in Uruk?

---

## The "Hunters by a Pillar" Motif

Among the petroglyphs of Khvamli—more precisely, among those images dated as potentially earlier than the medieval period—researchers identify a motif which, for convenience, is called "hunters by a pillar."

These are anthropomorphic figures, schematic (a few lines), standing next to a vertical object—a marker, a pillar, an axis.

This motif is not unique to Khvamli. It is found in the petroglyphic art of the entire Caucasus: in Trialeti, in Gobustan (Azerbaijan), in other mountainous regions.

But it is at Khvamli that it acquires special significance—because here it is combined with astral symbols, with images that may be connected to observing the sky.

---

## The Vertical Marker and Its Meaning

A vertical object next to a human figure is one of the most enduring archetypes of human culture.

In the Sumerian tradition, the vertical pillar is the axis of the world, "Dur-an-ki," the "bond between heaven and earth." In Nippur, the central symbol was a tall stone "kur"—"mountain"—from which all world order emanated.

On early Uruk pictograms, the sign of a pillar/column is one of the most frequent. It denotes a place, a center, a landmark.

If the hunters by a pillar at Khvamli depicted a ritual action at a vertical marker—and if this ritual was connected to observing Venus (the "sunrise—sunset" axis)—then we have a prototype of what the Sumerians would later embody in the concept of Dur-an-ki.

---

## The Bashplemi Tablet

In 2021, at Lake Bashplemi in the Dmanisi region (southern Georgia), a basalt artifact measuring 24 × 20 centimeters was discovered, bearing sixty symbols, thirty-nine of which are unique.

It was published in 2024 in the scientific journal JAHA.

The symbols on the tablet show partial similarity with Proto-Kartvelian writing from the 4th millennium BCE, with Caucasian Albanian and Georgian Mrglovani scripts, as well as with Phoenician and Proto-Sinaitic.

This is direct evidence: an independent tradition of symbol recording existed in the Caucasus. Not borrowed from Sumer. Not a copy of Mesopotamian forms. Its own.

The Bashplemi tablet has not been deciphered. We do not know what it says. But its very existence is a huge argument in favor of the Caucasus being an independent center of proto-writing.

---

## Direct Comparison: What Coincides

If we compare the recorded motifs of Khvamli petroglyphs with Ubaid pictograms (4th millennium BCE) and early Uruk cuneiform, certain parallels can be traced.

The vertical marker—in both. A person next to the marker—in both. Geometric forms: circles, lines, dots—in both.

But a strict caveat is needed here: these parallels have not been subjected to systematic scientific iconographic analysis. This is an observation, not a study.

Such a study—a direct comparison of the corpus of Khvamli petroglyphs with Ubaid and Uruk pictograms using formal methods—has not been conducted. It awaits its executor.

---

## Gabunia's Hypothesis

In the context of Caucasian proto-writing, the name of Manana Gabunia—a Georgian scholar who worked on the Trialeti petroglyphs—is often mentioned.

An important clarification: her work concerns Trialeti specifically, not Khvamli directly. The Trialeti petroglyphs are a different region, a different corpus. But Gabunia's methodology—the search for proto-writing symbols in Caucasian petroglyphs—is applicable to Khvamli as a question and a hypothesis.

If a systematic study of the Khvamli petroglyphs is ever conducted according to the standards of modern iconography—comparing Khvamli with Trialeti, with Uruk tablets, with Bashplemi symbols—it could provide an answer to the question that remains open today.

---

## What We Do Not Know

We do not know the precise dating of the Khvamli petroglyphs. Without dating, it is impossible to assert priority.

We do not know if the "hunters by a pillar" motif was a specific ritual program or simply a depiction of hunting.

We do not know if there are images at Khvamli that are directly comparable to Uruk signs.

This is an honest list of ignorance.

But ignorance is not an argument against research. It is an argument in its favor.

---

*Next chapter: Dur-an-ki—"the bond between heaven and earth."*

---

# Chapter 32. Dur-an-ki — "The Bond Between Heaven and Earth"

> "Man built towers not because he was proud. But because he needed the sky."

---

In Sumerian cosmology, there was a place that held a special position.

This was Nippur—not the richest city, not the strongest. But—the most sacred. The center of the universe. The place where heaven and earth touch.

The Sumerians called it "Dur-an-ki"—"the bond between heaven and earth."

This is not a metaphor. In Sumerian religious thought, it was a literal geographical point: the place where the vertical axis of the universe pierced physical reality.

---

## Ekur — "House of the Mountain"

In Nippur stood the main temple of the Sumerian god Enlil—lord of the atmosphere, wind, fate. The temple was called "E-kur"—"House of the Mountain."

This is not a random metaphor. On the flat Mesopotamian plain, where the horizon is flat and there is not a single peak, the mountain was a symbol of a fundamentally different world. A world inaccessible to ordinary people. A world where gods live.

A Sumerian hymn to Enlil: "he sits in Dur-an-ki and fills Ki-ur with splendor."
"Ki-ur" — the underworld. Enlil in the sacred mountain — and his power encompasses everything, from heaven to the netherworld. A vertical axis from top to bottom.

The ziggurat — an artificial mountain on the plain — is the embodiment of precisely this idea. If there are no natural mountains — we will build a mountain ourselves. So that heaven and earth could be joined here, in our city, and not somewhere in inaccessible mountains.

---

## What if the mountain was real?

This is the question that makes Khvamli special.

There are no mountains in Mesopotamia. That is precisely why the Sumerians built ziggurats.

In the Caucasus, there are mountains. Real, vertical, inaccessible, with caves, with underground lakes, with limestone walls reflecting the sun.

Khvamli is a natural Ekur. It is a natural Dur-an-ki. A place where the sky — an open plateau at the summit — neighbors the underworld — vertical cave shafts descending into darkness.

If the inhabitants of Neolithic Colchis saw Khvamli as a "pillar connecting heaven and earth" — they had a real, physical basis for this. There was no need to build a ziggurat. The mountain was already there.

When trade routes brought Caucasian craftsmen with their metal and their myths to Mesopotamia — they also brought the idea of a sacred mountain as a cosmic axis. And on the Mesopotamian plain, this idea could be realized in only one way: to build.

The ziggurat is a longing for a mountain.

---

## A parallel in Georgian religion: Jvari

In the Georgian mountain tradition — especially among the Pshavs — the sacred vertical pillar is called "Jvari".

"Jvari" literally translates as "cross" — this is a Christianized word. But the cult behind it is much older than Christianity. It is a vertical marker, the axis of ritual space, the point where heaven and earth connect for a specific community.

If in the Khvamli petroglyphs the "hunters by the pillar" are people by the Jvari, — then we are looking at a visual document of this cult, reaching back into deep antiquity.

And this cult is functionally identical to the Sumerian Dur-an-ki.

---

## Verticality as a universal code

The idea of a vertical cosmic axis — the world tree, the world mountain, the world pillar — is one of the few genuinely universal mythological archetypes.

It is traced in Scandinavia (Yggdrasil), in India (Mount Meru), in the Greek Olympus, in the Sumerian Ekur, in the Egyptian obelisk — the embodiment of the Benben.

This is either a universal psychological need of humans for vertical orientation in space. Or — a single cultural diffusion of one idea.

For the KHT, another detail is fundamentally important: in each tradition, the "axis of the world" is embodied in a specific form reflecting a specific landscape. For Mesopotamia — a tower. For Egypt — a pyramid or an obelisk. For Greece — a mountain on the horizon. For India — a distant, inaccessible peak.

For the Caucasus — Khvamli: a real mountain, with real caves, with real images on the walls.

---

## What this means for chronology

If Khvamli indeed functioned as a "Dur-an-ki" in Neolithic Colchis — and if the mountain's petroglyphs date to this same period — then we have evidence that the concept of the "world mountain" existed in the Caucasus earlier than or simultaneously with the Sumerian one.

This does not mean that Sumer "took" the idea from the Caucasus. It may mean parallel development. Or early contacts along trade routes.

But it certainly means: the axis of the world stood at Khvamli long before the first brick of a ziggurat was laid on the plains of Mesopotamia.

---

*Next chapter: The Natural Proto-Ziggurat.*

---

# Chapter 33. The Natural Proto-Ziggurat

> "Architecture is nature reinterpreted by man. Sometimes nature works faster than the architect."

---

Standing before the Ziggurat of Ur — the very one that rose thirty meters above the Mesopotamian plain, with its three terraces and a sanctuary at the summit — it is hard not to ask: where did this idea come from?

Mesopotamia is flat as a table. A horizon without a single hill. The people who lived here never saw anything that vertically soared towards the sky. Except perhaps trees — but trees are not built.

The ziggurat arose as a memory. Or as a dream. As an image of a sacred mountain that is not here — but which exists somewhere.

---

## Form as an argument

Let us compare two structures.

Ziggurat: a stepped pyramid with a flat summit, on which a sanctuary is located. Access from the outside via a special staircase or ramp. The base is wide, the summit is narrow.

Khvamli: a double cuesta with a flat summit. Vertical cliffs on all sides. Access only via one specific route requiring skill. The base — wide, the summit — a plateau.

Not identical. But — structurally parallel.

Both structures create one space: an inaccessible summit with a horizontal plateau, where the sky is visible from all sides. Both realize one idea: a sacred height, attainable not by all, but only by the chosen or the skilled.

---

## Geology as theology

In Khvamli, there is nothing man-made in its form. It is limestone that formed over two hundred million years under tectonic pressure. It is erosion that worked on the hard limestone differently than on soft rock for millennia.

The result: a double cuesta. The natural architecture of a stepped mountain with a flat summit.

For a Bronze Age human looking at Khvamli from below — this was not geology. This was intention. God created the mountain exactly like this because this is how it should be: with a summit for ritual, with inaccessible walls for protection, with caves for storing secrets.

When a merchant from Colchis came to Mesopotamia and told of the sacred mountain back home — a mountain with a flat summit where stars are observed, and with caves where knowledge is stored — the Mesopotamian listener heard a description of the perfect ritual structure.

And then built it. Out of brick. Because there were no mountains.

---

## The three terraces of the ziggurat and the three levels of the world

Sumerian ziggurats typically had three tiers — sometimes more. In religious interpretation, this corresponded to a three-level cosmology: the lower world, the middle (earth), the upper (heaven).

In Georgian mythology, the same tripartite structure exists: the underworld (where Amiran is chained), the earthly world (humans), the heavenly world (Gmerthi and the gods).

At Khvamli, this tripartite nature is physically present: vertical shafts into the cave system lead downward, the plateau at the top is open to the sky, the mountainside is the middle world, the world of humans.

The living topography of myth.

---

## The distance between idea and stone
Between the idea of “the mountain as a sacred place” and the construction of the first ziggurat lay, in all likelihood, a distance of several centuries and several thousand kilometers.

That is enough for an idea to transform. Enough for it to remain recognizable.

The Bronze Age trade routes—copper from Colchis to Mesopotamia—created precisely such a channel. Not “Georgians built the first ziggurat.” But rather—“the idea of the mountain as the world’s center traveled along with trade routes and was embodied in architecture where there were no real mountains.”

---

## What Remains a Hypothesis

Direct “architectural evidence”—proof that Sumerian architects were familiar with Khvamli or its image—is currently absent.

This is an admission. An honest one.

But the absence of evidence is not evidence of absence. The trade routes along which copper traveled existed. People walked them. Ideas traveled with them.

A ziggurat is a longing for a mountain, rebuilt in brick.

Khvamli is a mountain that did not need to be built.

---

*Next chapter: The Path of Venus—the Canaanite Vector.*

---

# Chapter 34. The Path of Venus

> “She appears first in the evening sky and last—in the morning. Each time—different. Each time—the same.”

---

There is one planet visible both at sunset and at dawn. The only one—if we do not count the Moon—a celestial wanderer that moves from one side of the horizon to the other.

The Greeks called it by two names: Hesperus (evening) and Phosphorus (morning). For a long time, it was thought they were different stars. Pythagoras, according to tradition, was the first to understand—it is one.

In the Near East, they understood this earlier. And from this understanding, they made an entire religion.

---

## Inanna—Goddess of the Double Sky

In the Sumerian religious tradition, Venus was the goddess Inanna long before the advent of writing. Her symbol—an eight-pointed star—is found on seals from the 4th millennium BCE.

Inanna was dual, like the planet itself. Morning Inanna—goddess of war. Evening—goddess of love. One and the same force, turned in different directions.

The main myth about her is the descent into the underworld. Inanna descends below, passes through seven gates, each time losing one attribute of power. Dies below. Returns.

This is the myth of Venus, disappearing from the sky for several days (during inferior conjunction with the Sun) and reappearing. A myth of death and resurrection, encoded in the planet’s motion.

---

## “The Star of Khomli”

Now—back to Khvamli.

Prince Vakhushti Bagrationi in the 18th century recorded: Mount Khomli is identified with the planet Venus.

This is not a poetic comparison. In Georgian tradition, the mountain and the star bear the same name, the same function, the same cult.

From the summit of Khvamli, one can observe the rising of Venus with perfect precision: the flat plateau is open to the horizon on all sides. On a clear day or evening, the morning or evening star is visible from here so clearly that one can mark the exact points of its appearance and disappearance.

The synodic cycle of Venus is 584 days. Eight Venusian cycles = five solar years. This is an important astronomical proportion, which Sumerian astronomers knew how to calculate. For such a calculation, long-term systematic observations from a fixed point are needed.

Khvamli was such a point.

---

## The Research of Gogiashvili

Georgian researcher Gogiashvili studied the orientation of natural and man-made objects on the Khvamli plateau relative to the horizon.

According to his data, a number of rock outcrops and niches are oriented in such a way that their use as astronomical markers—for tracking the risings and settings of Venus at specific points in its cycle—would be entirely possible.

This is not a sensational discovery. It is a cautious professional observation.

But if it is correct—then Khvamli was not simply “identified” with Venus in a mythological sense. It was used as an instrument for observing the planet.

---

## From “The Star of Khomli” to Inanna

Assume the following sequence.
