In Colchis—long before contact with Mesopotamia—there existed a cult of the morning star. Its center was Mount Khomli: a place from which Venus was observed, from which its cycles were tracked, where rituals were performed on the days of its appearance and disappearance.

Traders from Colchis—bearers of metal and craftsmanship—came to Mesopotamia. With them came their gods, their astronomical knowledge, and their religious concepts.

In Mesopotamia, the astral cult of the morning star found a local embodiment—Inanna. Dual, like the planet itself. Descending and returning, like the astronomical cycle.

From Mesopotamia, the cult spread westward—to Babylon (Ishtar), to Canaan (Astarte).

This is one of the possible routes. One of the vectors.

---

## Helel, Son of Shahar

In the Hebrew prophecy of Isaiah (Isa. 14:12), there is a mysterious phrase: “How you have fallen from heaven, Helel, son of Shahar!”

“Helel” in Hebrew means “shining,” “radiant.” “Shahar”—“dawn,” “morning light.”

“Helel, son of Shahar”—“Shining One, son of Dawn”—is an obvious reference to the morning star, to Venus, which appears before dawn.

In the Latin tradition, this word was translated as “Lucifer”—“light-bearer.”

And the root “khvam-” in the Georgian language, according to Orbeliani, means “light,” “radiance,” “beacon.”

This is a linguistic parallel, nothing more. Not proof. Semantic similarity can be coincidental. But it is intriguing: “shining” as a name for the morning star and “khvam-” as the name of a mountain identified with Venus.

Was there ever a connection between them? We do not know.

---

## The Route of the Cult

On the map, it looks like this.

Khomli/Khvamli in Colchis—“the morning star above the mountain.”
Inanna in Nippur — "the goddess of Venus on the sacred mountain" (Ishtar stands on a mountain in cylinder seals; Ishtar on Mount Batir in the Zagros).

Astarte in the Levant — "the goddess of the mountain and the morning star."

Each of them is an embodiment of the same idea in the same landscape image: a goddess on a mountain, the morning star above the horizon.

The path of Venus is not just astronomy. It is the history of a religious idea that traveled with merchants and craftsmen from the Caucasus to the south and west.

---

*Next chapter: The root "khvam-" in Semitic languages.*

---

# Chapter 35. The Root "Khvam-" in Semitic Languages

> "Languages are not born in a vacuum. They meet — on trade routes, at watering holes, in nomad camps."

---

This is the most cautious chapter of the book.

Here we step into territory where linguistic parallels are intriguing but not conclusive. Where semantic coincidences are real but may be accidental. Where the hypothesis is formulated honestly: as a hypothesis requiring professional verification.

Let's begin.

---

## What is "Khvam-": An Established Fact

Let us recall the established fact: the root **khvam-** is recorded in the dictionary of Sulkhan-Saba Orbeliani (17th–18th centuries) with meanings related to light, radiance, brilliance as a guide — a beacon.

This is not just "light." It is "light that orients." "Light to follow." "Light visible from afar."

The white limestone walls of Khvamli reflect the sun so that the mountain is visible from dozens of kilometers away. It literally shines. Its name is its property. **This is the author's interpretation** of the connection between the toponym and its physical property.

---

## Semitic Parallels: What We Have (Established Facts)

In Semitic languages, there are several words related to light and the morning star. **This is a linguistic fact.**

*   Hebrew **הֵילֵל** (Helel) in Isaiah 14:12 — "Shining One," "Brilliant One." Applied to the morning star. Root **הלל** (halal) — "to shine," "to be brilliant."
*   Aramaic **Hêlêl** — "brightness," "radiance." Derived from the same root.
*   Arabic **hilāl** — "crescent moon," "sickle." The same root. Associated with light, with the first appearance of the moon in the sky.

All three words come from a common Semitic root **hll**, meaning "to shine," "to be radiant," "to be visible as a source of light."

---

## Semantic Field: Coincidence and Interpretation

Now let's compare. **Here begins the realm of hypotheses.**

"Khvam-": light, radiance, beacon. A mountain that shines. A mountain used for orientation.
"Helel": shining one. Morning star. The one that appears first and is used for orientation in time.

The semantic field — "orienting light," "light-beacon" — coincides.

And both are applied to objects identified with Venus: Khvamli — "the star of Khomli" according to Vakhushti (author's interpretation of the source), Helel — a direct designation of the morning star in Isaiah (biblical fact).

**The author's hypothesis is that** this coincidence is not accidental but points to a cultural or conceptual connection.

---

## What This Could Mean: Three Hypothetical Scenarios

**First option: Accidental coincidence.** Georgian "khvam-" and Semitic "hll" are completely independent roots that simply happen to mean something similar. This occurs in linguistics, especially with short roots denoting basic phenomena (light, movement). **This is an established linguistic principle — universality: identical symbols and concepts can arise independently among different peoples.**

**Second option: Cultural contact.** The word or, more likely, the concept-image traveled along with trade routes. Possibly through Anatolia — which was an intermediary between the Caucasus and the Levant. The "shining mountain" as an image — or Khvamli itself as a name — could have influenced the Semitic "shining one" as a name for the morning star. Or vice versa.

**Third option: Common substrate.** Both roots go back to an even more ancient linguistic layer that existed before the separation of Kartvelian and Semitic languages. This is the least likely option from the perspective of modern linguistics, but not impossible.

---

## The Skeptics' Position

Before moving to conclusions, it is necessary to clearly outline the position of scientific skepticism, which is predominant in academic linguistics.

First, the similarity between the roots **khvam-** and **hll** is superficial and most likely coincidental. The phonetic correspondence is not systematic: the initial Georgian *khv-* has no regular correspondences with Semitic *h-*, and the final *-m* does not correspond to Semitic *-l*. To prove a relationship or borrowing, regular phonetic correspondences are required, which are absent here.

Second, the semantic field of "radiance, light" is one of the most universal and basic in human languages. The coincidence that both a mountain and a star are described through the metaphor of light is not surprising and does not require an assumption of contact. This is a classic case of independent parallel development of symbolism.

Third, the chronological and geographical gap between the formation of Kartvelian toponymy and the fixation of the Semitic poetic term "Helel" in the first millennium BCE is enormous. There is no direct historical evidence of contacts that could have led to such a specific borrowing of an image.

Thus, the skeptical position views the presented parallels as an interesting but unproven coincidence, insufficient for drawing historical conclusions.

---

## The Author's Honest Position

I am not a linguist. And I do not claim a genetic relationship between Georgian "khvam-" and Semitic "hll."

I draw attention to a semantic coincidence that, in the context of the KHT, deserves study. **The author's hypothesis is that** the question that needs to be answered is this: how is the name of a mountain identified with Venus related to the name of the morning star in Semitic languages?

This question requires a professional Semitologist and a Caucasologist working in tandem.

To my knowledge, such research does not exist.

---

## Why This is Important for the Hypothesis

If the semantic parallel "khvam-" / "hll" were verified even as a possible trace of cultural contact — this would add specificity to the Canaanite vector of the KHT.

**The author's interpretation develops as follows:** this would mean that not only metal traveled from the Caucasus to the Near East. Along with the metal, an image could have traveled — the "shining mountain," the "light-beacon." And this image could have settled in Semitic languages in the form of the word "shining one," which was later applied to the morning star.

A beacon on a mountaintop became the name of a star.

This would be a story about how landscape turns into metaphor, and metaphor into religion.

---

*Next chapter: The iconography of fertility — Canaanite parallels.*
---

# Chapter 36. The Iconography of Fertility

> "The woman's body was the first temple. Everything else is an attempt to reproduce it."
---

Among the petroglyphs of Khvamli — among those images mentioned in sources that cannot be fully academically verified today — are female anthropomorphic figures.

"A map of celestial bodies depicting the ancient Egyptian goddess Isis riding a horse, as well as gods of water and fertility," — this is how it is described in one tourist source. The description is inaccurate: "Isis riding a horse" sounds implausible for any period. But within the inaccurate description, there may be an accurate core: a female figure in an astral context.

If female anthropomorphic images on Khvamli exist — how does this relate to Canaanite fertility iconography?

---

## Fertility Goddesses of Canaan

In the Canaanite tradition of the 2nd–1st millennia BCE, the iconography of the fertility goddess had stable forms.

Astarte figurines — a female figure with emphasized breasts and hips, often standing upright with raised arms — are found throughout the Levant, from Tyre to Gaza. Hundreds, thousands of specimens. These are not elite objects: they were in every household.

Astarte combined several functions: goddess of love, goddess of fertility, goddess of war — and simultaneously — goddess of the morning star. The same duality as with the Sumerian Inanna.

---

## What Earlier Parallels Show

During the Ubaid and early Uruk periods (6th–4th millennia BCE), female figurines appear throughout Mesopotamia — small terracotta figures with characteristic features.

Simultaneously, in Colchis, evidence of ochre use is recorded — a red pigment associated in many cultures with the feminine principle, with blood, with life.

If female images on Khvamli exist and date to the Neolithic or Chalcolithic — this would mean: the iconography of the mother goddess was present in the Caucasus simultaneously with, or earlier than, in Mesopotamia.

---

## Dali — A Caucasian Goddess and Her Iconography

We have no images of Dali — no figurines, no rock art with unambiguous attribution. Dali lives in oral text.

But her description is full of specific visual details: golden hair, white skin that glows in the dark, life in a golden cave on a mountain peak.

"A glowing woman in a mountain cave" — this is a precise visual program for the iconography of a goddess. It corresponds to the image of a goddess associated with light, with a cave, with a peak.

If this iconographic program was embodied in images on the cave walls of Khvamli — then we are looking at a predecessor of the Canaanite Astarte figurines: a fertility goddess associated with the morning star and with the mountain.

---

## Tkashi-Mapa — The Mingrelian Variant

In Samegrelo — on the territory of ancient Colchis — the goddess Dali is known by the name Tkashi-Mapa, "Queen of the Forests."

Her iconography in oral tradition: a beautiful woman, golden hair, life in the wild, power over animals. Connection with water — rivers and the sea. Connection with the moon and stars.

This is a goddess who combines the features of a hunting goddess, a water goddess, and a stellar goddess.

In the Canaanite tradition, the goddess Anat (sister of Baal) has a similar profile: huntress, goddess of war, associated with the moon and with fertility.

The parallel is not direct. But it shows: the type of "goddess uniting hunting, waters, and an astral cult" was present both in the Caucasus and in Canaan.

---

## What We Need for Confirmation

To turn these observations into an argument, the following is needed.

First of all: systematic documentation of the Khvamli petroglyphs, including their complete iconographic cataloging. All images, all motifs, all contexts.

Then: dating. At least approximate — AMS analysis of pigments or OSL dating of the rock surface.

After: professional iconographic comparison with Canaanite and Ubaid materials.

None of these steps has been carried out in full.

Until then — we have parallels, intriguing comparisons, and an honest admission: this is a hypothesis awaiting research.

---

*Next chapter: The Myth of the Primeval Mound — the Egyptian vector.*

---

# Chapter 37. The Myth of the Primeval Mound

> "In the beginning was the chaos of water. Then a point of land appeared. This is the beginning of all beginnings."

---

Egyptian cosmogony begins with water.

**Established fact:** Nun — the infinite primordial ocean in Egyptian mythology. Not just water — chaos, indistinguishability, formlessness. There is no exit from it, within it there are no directions, no up or down.

And suddenly — a point.

**Established fact:** The first mound rises from Nun. Benben. The primeval mound. The first land. The first act of creation. On the summit of Benben appears the first god — in different versions, this is Atum, or Ra, or Amun.

**Author's interpretation:** This image — a mountain rising from the waters of chaos — became the basis of all Egyptian architecture. Pyramids are Benben. Obelisks are Benben in a vertical variant. The Karnak temple stands on "primeval earth." Every sacred place in Egypt is the "first place" where land appeared.

---

## The Universality of the Image

Before moving to Khvamli — it must be acknowledged: the image of the "mountain from water" is one of the most universal mythological archetypes.

**Established facts (comparative mythology):**
In the Sumerian tradition — Ekur, "mountain" as the center of the universe, emerging from the subterranean waters of Abzu.
In the Greek — the first act of Hesiod: from Chaos arises Earth.
In the Vedic — Mount Meru rises from the cosmic ocean.
In the Biblical — "the earth was without form and void, and the Spirit of God was hovering over the face of the waters" (Genesis 1:2).

**Author's interpretation:** This is either a psychologically universal image (any culture living by water sees in the "appearance of land" an act of creation), or — a single idea that spread across different cultures. It is important to remember here the **principle of universality**: identical symbols among different peoples can arise independently, as a response to similar environmental conditions or structures of thought.

---

## Khvamli as Benben: The Landscape Basis

Now — Khvamli.

**Established fact (paleogeography):** In the Neolithic and Bronze Age, the water level of the Rioni River, according to research, was significantly higher than today. In spring, during floods, the floodplain valley filled with water. In some periods, the swampiness of the valley was substantially greater than today.

**The author's hypothesis is that** Khvamli — with its vertical cliffs and flat summit — during a flood looked precisely like: a mountain rising from water. An island. The first land.

For a person living at the foot of this mountain in the Neolithic and seeing how in spring water fills the valley, while the mountain remains above the water — this was a literal reproduction of the act of creation. Every spring. Every year.

**Author's interpretation:** Benben was reborn every April.
---

## The Egyptian Vector: Not About Direct Contacts

Let us be clear: direct contacts between Colchis and Egypt in the Neolithic or Early Bronze Age are extremely unlikely. The distance is too great for direct exchange.

**Author's Position:** The Egyptian vector of the KHT is not an assertion of direct contacts. It is an analysis of structural parallels that can be explained in two ways.

**First Way: Parallel Development.** A similar landscape gives rise to similar myths. If the Egyptians lived by a river that flooded and revealed the "first land" — they created the myth of the Benben. If the Colchians lived by a mountain that appeared as the "first land" during the flood — they created an analogous myth. Independently.

**Second Way: Diffusion via the Levant.** The Canaanite zone is an intermediary between the Caucasus/Anatolia and Egypt. **The author's hypothesis suggests that** if the concept of a "mountain from water" traveled along trade routes from the Caucasus to the Levant, and from the Levant to Egypt, then we are dealing with a chain of cultural transmission.

Both explanations are plausible. Both require further research.

---

## The Skeptics' Position

A critical view of the parallels drawn between Khvamli and the Benben is necessary for scientific rigor. The skeptics' position can be summarized in several key points.

First, the universality of the "primordial mound" archetype is so great that any particular similarity can be considered statistically inevitable, rather than evidence of a connection. Practically every civilization that developed in a river valley created a similar myth as a metaphor for domesticating the landscape and reclaiming order from chaos.

Second, there is a chasm between the landscape phenomenon (a mountain in a flood) and the complex theological concept of the Benben, woven into the developed state religion and architecture of Egypt. The transition from observing a natural phenomenon to a formalized cosmogonic myth is a complex and non-linear process that cannot be reduced to a simple reflection of the landscape.

Third, the chronological gap and the absence of material evidence of contacts make the diffusion hypothesis extremely vulnerable. Even through the mediation of the Levant, for which there are its own proven connections with Egypt, the transmission of such an abstract idea in a pre-literate era seems difficult to prove.

Thus, skeptics insist that the similarity remains in the realm of an interesting but speculative analogy, not a historical-cultural fact. The power of the idea, which the author speaks of, may equally be the power of projection — the researcher's tendency to find deep connections where there is only typological similarity.

---

## What the Parallel Proves

The parallel between Khvamli and the Benben is not proof of a connection. It is evidence of the power of an idea.

The idea of a "mountain from water" is so powerful, so accurately reflecting the real experience of living by a flooding river, that it could not help but arise among people living by the Rioni, just as it could not help but arise among people living by the Nile.

This similarity in itself is an argument not about contacts, but about the universality of experience. About the fact that the human mind, under similar conditions, creates similar images.

And this is precisely what makes the KHT not fantasy, but a worthy scientific hypothesis: it relies not on unique coincidences, but on structural parallels that can be explained by the laws of human imagination.

---

*Next Chapter: The Island of Creation in the Rioni.*
---

# Chapter 38. The Island of Creation in the Rioni

> "The river is time. The flood is a reminder that dry land is not forever."

---

Let us talk about the river.

The Rioni is the main river of western Georgia. In antiquity — the Phasis. An established fact: in myth, it is the boundary of the *oikoumene*, the place where the known world ends and Colchis begins.

The length of the Rioni is about three hundred kilometers. It originates in the glaciers of the Greater Caucasus Range and carries its waters to the Black Sea. In its upper course — a mountain river with a fast current. In its lower course — wide, slow, with floodplain meadows.

And Khvamli stands above it.

---

## Paleogeography: What the Valley Looked Like

In the post-glacial period — approximately from the 12th to the 4th millennium BCE — the water level in the rivers of Colchis was significantly higher than today.

**Established fact:** Post-glacial sea level rise, higher than today's precipitation in the mountains, a less developed floodplain drainage system. Geomorphological data show that the floodplain part of the Rioni valley in the Neolithic period was significantly more marshy and regularly flooded.

What does this mean for Khvamli?

**Author's Interpretation:** During periods of high water — spring floods or more humid climatic epochs — Khvamli, with its vertical cliffs, could literally rise above the surface of the flooded valley like an island.

An island of land in a sea of water.

---

## The First Glimpse of the Island

Let us try to imagine: spring, flood.

The valley is covered with water to the horizon. Above the water — white limestone cliffs reflecting the spring sun. At the summit — a flat plateau with sparse vegetation. Unreachable. Protected on all sides by water and rocks.

**The author's hypothesis is that** a person in a boat, looking at this mountain, could think: here is a place no flood can reach. Here is the first point from which the world once began — or where it will begin again when the water recedes. This is a literal illustration of the myth of the primordial mound.

---

## The Ecology of the Mountain-Island

Khvamli, in its "island above water" mode, possessed unique ecological properties.

**Established facts:** Inaccessibility: the mountain could not be reached by wading or overland during the flood season. Self-sufficiency: the plateau had a forest, sources of fresh water, and game for hunting.

**Author's Interpretation:** This is not just a "sacred place." This is a functional island that ensured survival. People who knew how to ascend Khvamli during the flood season had a significant advantage. They were the "masters of the island above water." They were, in the literal sense, the masters of the "first mound."

---

## Aeëtes and the Island of Aeaea

In Greek mythology, King Aeëtes of Colchis rules a land located "at the edge of the world." His sister Circe (Kirke) dwells on the island of "Aeaea."

**Established fact:** In early versions of the myth — prior to Apollonius's *Argonautica* — Aeaea and Colchis are identified or located in the same place. "Aeaea" as a toponym may be related to the Greek "*eos*" (dawn) or to something indigenous, Colchian.

**The author's hypothesis is that** "Aeaea" is the "island" that the Argonauts see sailing up the Rioni: a mountain rising from the flooded waters of the valley. The "Island of Helios" — its emphasized image in the myth as a place where the sun rises, associated with the daughter of Helios — is a description of a mountain from which dawns and sunsets are visible with perfect clarity.

---

## The Nile and the Rioni: A Structural Parallel

The Nile in Egypt flooded every year. This is the only agricultural civilization in the world based on regular flooding. When the water receded — it left fertile silt. The "emergence of land from water" was literally understandable to the Egyptians.

The Rioni also floods. It also leaves a mark. It also "reveals" the valley after the spring flood.

**Author's Interpretation:** The parallel is not in the details. The parallel is that both civilizations — the Nilotic and the Rioni — lived under conditions where a "mountain from water" was a visually obvious image of creation. This does not mean that one myth originated from the other. It means: under similar conditions — similar images. This confirms that the images of the KHT are rooted in reality, not in arbitrary comparisons. It is important to remember here the **principle of universality**: identical symbols among different peoples can arise independently, as a response to similar natural phenomena.

---

## The Skeptics' Position

A critical view of the presented hypothesis requires separate consideration. Skeptics rightly point out that paleogeographic reconstructions, especially for dynamic landscapes like the Colchian lowland, are probabilistic in nature. The scale and regularity of floods sufficient to turn Khvamli into a full-fledged island, visible "to the horizon," may be exaggerated.

Secondly, the direct projection of the mythological image of the "primordial mound" onto a specific mountain, even one possessing suitable features, is speculative. Between physical reality and its mythologization lies a complex, non-linear process of cultural interpretation, which is far from always amenable to straightforward deciphering.
Thirdly, the connection between the ancient toponym "Eia" and the presumed island status of Khvamli remains extremely hypothetical. Linguistic and narrative parallels could be coincidental or interpreted differently. Skeptics emphasize that more substantial evidence, beyond structural similarity, is required for such claims.

Finally, the parallel with the Nile, while intriguing, is not proof. The principle of universality suggests that the image of land emerging from waters is archetypal and found among many unrelated peoples. Its presence in Colchis may reflect a common logic of mythological thought, rather than direct borrowing or a unique product of the local landscape. Thus, the hypothesis of a "creation island" in the Rioni remains intellectually attractive but requires further, more rigorous substantiation.

---

*Next chapter: The Son of the Sun in Colchis.*
---

# Chapter 39. The Son of the Sun in Colchis

> "When a ruler calls himself the son of the Sun — he is not speaking of family. He is speaking of right."

---

In Egyptian official ideology, the pharaoh was the "son of Ra" — the son of the sun god. This was not merely a mythological image, but a political and religious formula, an established fact of ancient statehood.

The Son of the Sun had the right to rule not because he was strong or clever. But because solar blood flowed in him. He was a mediator between the celestial world and the earthly one. His power was not conquered — it was preordained.

This formula "son of the Sun" as a legitimizing title was not an exclusively Egyptian invention. It appears in various forms in Mesopotamia, the Hittite Kingdom, India, and Japan, testifying to the universality of this archetype in early complex societies.

And — in Colchis.

---

## Aeëtes, Son of Helios

In Greek mythology, Aeëtes — the king of Colchis — was the son of Helios, the Sun god. This is an established fact of the ancient tradition.

This is not a random detail. It is a characterization of status. The author's hypothesis is that behind this mythological kinship lies a real Colchian religious-political concept.

Aeëtes is not simply a "king." He is a descendant of the Sun. His rule over Colchis is legitimized not by conquest, not by election, not by force — but by origin. He rules because solar blood flows in him.

In *Kartlis Tskhovreba* — the principal Georgian chronicle — the same line is present: the rulers of Colchis are connected to the solar cult. The author's interpretation sees in this not merely a Greek overlay on a local myth, but a reflection of a real Colchian ideology.

---

## Comparison with Egypt

The structural parallel between Aeëtes and the Egyptian pharaoh is, in the author's opinion, striking.

Aeëtes — "son of Helios," rules at the "edge of the world," in Colchis. On his mountain — the Golden Fleece (a symbol of power and wealth). His daughter Medea — a *pharmakís*, possessor of sacred knowledge.

The Pharaoh — "son of Ra," rules "at the center of the world," in Egypt. In his temples — the treasures of the gods. His priests — bearers of sacred knowledge.

The author's interpretation proposes to consider both figures according to a single scheme: the ruler as a mediator between the solar sky and the earthly world, the ruler as a guardian of treasures and knowledge, the ruler as the "son of the Sun." This is not unique to Colchis and Egypt, but the coincidence is precise enough to speak of a deep structural parallel.

---

## Why "Son of the Sun"?

The cult of the sun as the source of all living things is, apparently, one of the earliest and most universal elements of human religion. The sun gives light, warmth, harvest. Without it — death. This is an established fact in the history of religions.

The "Son of the Sun" as a political formula arises where:
1. A monarchy exists — a concentration of power in one pair of hands.
2. This power requires religious legitimization.
3. The solar cult is the dominant religious tradition.

All three conditions, as the author believes, were fulfilled both in Egypt and in Colchis, and in Mesopotamia.

---

## Helios and Mze

In the Georgian language, the Sun — "Mze" — is of feminine gender. Not masculine. This is an established linguistic fact.

This fundamentally distinguishes the Georgian tradition from the Greek (Helios — male) and from the Egyptian (Ra — male). Here the author makes an important clarification: if Aeëtes in the Georgian tradition is connected to the Sun, — then it is the Sun-Mother, not the Sun-Father. This nuance, in his opinion, changes the nature of royal legitimization: Aeëtes draws power from a feminine principle, not a masculine one.

This is a specifically Caucasian feature, which is not an "import" from Greece. The author's position is as follows: this is an indigenous tradition, which was simply described by Greek authors within their, Greek, conceptual frameworks.

---

## Solar Cult and the Stars of Khvamli

There is another detail important for the author's hypothesis.

"Mze" — the Sun — is seen with particular clarity from Khvamli. The open plateau, the absence of a horizon blocked by other mountains. Sunrise and sunset here are precise, mathematically measurable.

If the priests of Colchis, who observed Venus and the Sun from the summit of Khvamli, were connected to the royal cult of the "son of the Sun," — then, in the author's thought, Khvamli was not merely an astronomical instrument. It was a political center: the place where the ruler confirmed his solar origin.

The "Son of the Sun" visited the "Mountain of the Sun" — and on this mountain his power was renewed.

Such an interpretation, the author believes, fits well with what we know about the role of Khvamli as a repository of royal regalia: the "diamond chain," the "unseen pearl," the "anvil" — these are not just valuables. They are symbols of royal power, stored in the place of its religious source.

---

## The Skeptics' Position

Not all researchers share such direct parallels between Colchian and Egyptian concepts of royal power. Skeptics point to several methodological problems.

Firstly, the principle of universality: the image of the ruler as "son of the sun" could have arisen independently in different cultures where the sun was deified and power was sacralized. This does not necessarily indicate direct borrowing or contact, but could be the result of similar development in religious-political thought.

Secondly, our knowledge of Colchian royal ideology is fragmentary and mediated primarily by Greek sources. The Greeks, describing distant Colchis, could have projected onto it familiar Eastern or their own mythological schemas, creating an illusion of similarity.

Thirdly, the linguistic argument about the Sun-"Mze" (feminine gender) casts doubt on the direct analogy with the patrilineal formula "son of Ra." This may point to a fundamentally different, possibly matrilineal or more complex, basis for legitimizing power in Colchis, which the Greek myth of Aeëtes, son of Helios, merely simplifies and adapts.

Thus, the skeptical view proposes seeing in these parallels not proof of deep historical connections, but an interesting typological similarity requiring extremely cautious interpretations.

---

*Next chapter: Analogies, not contacts — the mechanism of transfer.*
---

# Chapter 40. Analogies, not Contacts

> "Similarity does not always mean connection. But connection does not always leave traces we know how to read."

---

We are at the midpoint of Part IV, and it is time to pause.

Over the past chapters, we have examined many parallels: between Khvamli and Ekur, between the "star of Khomli" and Inanna, between the "son of Helios" and the pharaoh "son of Ra," between Khvamli-"island" and the Egyptian Benben.

Each time I have said: "this is a parallel," "this is a hypothesis," "this requires investigation."

Now it is time to explain why these parallels exist — and what exactly this explains.

---
## Two Mechanisms of Similarity

In comparative mythology, there are two fundamentally different explanations for structural similarities between myths of different cultures.

**First mechanism: parallel development (convergence).**

Similar living conditions give rise to similar myths. If two peoples lived by flooding rivers—both could create a myth about a "mountain from water." Independently of each other. Without any contact.

This is "similarity without contact." Psychological universalism: the human mind in similar situations generates similar images.

**Second mechanism: diffusion (spread).**

One people created an idea—another borrowed it, adapted it, made it their own. Through trade, through migrations, through cultural exchange.

This is "similarity through contact." Historical dissemination.

---

## How to Distinguish

Distinguishing between these mechanisms is a task that often cannot be solved with complete certainty. But there are criteria.

The more specific the detail—the more likely it is contact. If two peoples both "venerate a sacred mountain"—this is probably convergence: mountains are everywhere. If both call the morning star "shining" and link it to a sacred mountain, which is identified with a fertility goddess—the specificity increases, and diffusion becomes more plausible.

The closer geographically and chronologically—the more likely the contact. Peoples living in immediate proximity inevitably influenced each other.

The better the "intermediate links" can be traced—the more reliable the diffusion hypothesis. If the idea of a "world-axis mountain" exists in the Caucasus, then in Anatolia, then in Mesopotamia—the chain is convincing.

---

## Applied to KHT

For different elements of KHT—different answers.

**Metallurgy**: almost certainly diffusion. Metal from Colchis is materially attested in Mesopotamia. Trade routes are documented. Metallurgical technologies were transmitted along with the metal.

**"Son of the Sun" as a royal title**: more likely convergence. Too widely distributed to have a single source.

**Cult of the morning star**: possibly both. Observing Venus is universal, it arises everywhere there is a clear sky. But specific details—the duality of the goddess, her descent into the underworld, her connection to both fertility and war—may point to a more specific diffusion chain.

**"Mountain from water"**: more likely convergence. Too obvious an image for all who lived by flooding rivers.

---

## KHT as a Hybrid Model

The Khvamli Hypothesis of Transfer is not a claim that "everything originated from Khvamli." It is a hybrid model.

Some parallels are convergent: they arose independently in different cultures due to universal psychological and environmental factors. Khvamli is one of the places where these factors were especially pronounced.

Some parallels are diffusionary: they were transmitted via trade and cultural routes. Metallurgical knowledge associated with the Caucasus was one of the key commodities moving from the Caucasus to the Near East.

Some parallels are unclear: we do not know how to explain them correctly, and that is precisely why research is needed.

---

## What KHT Does Not Require

KHT does not require all parallels to be explained by direct contacts.

KHT does not require the "dominance" of Caucasian culture over Near Eastern ones.

KHT does not require a single source for all similarities.

It requires one thing: recognition that the Caucasus—and Khvamli as its cultural center—was an active participant in the processes that shaped the civilizations of the Near East. Not the only one. Not the main one. But—a participant. One of them.

And that—is enough.

---

*Next chapter: The Mountain as Oracle—the Caucasian-Iberian vector.*

---

# Chapter 41. The Mountain as Oracle

> "Power does not live where there are many people. Power lives where there is a sign from above."

---

In 2020, the government of Georgia granted Khvamli Mountain the status of a "monument of national significance."

This is an official act. It means: the state recognizes that this place is important—not only as a beautiful landscape or tourist attraction, but as a cultural and historical monument.

Good. But—what exactly made the mountain significant?

We need to understand Khvamli not as a natural object, but as a political and religious institution. Because it was in this capacity that it functioned for millennia.

---

## Sacred Mountain as a Political Tool

In archaeology and anthropology, the phenomenon of the "sacred center," which is simultaneously a political center, is well known.

Jerusalem. Mecca. Delphi. Varanasi.

In all these cases: a place recognized as sacred becomes a place rulers aspire to. Whoever controls the sacred place—controls legitimacy. Whoever possesses the oracle—possesses authority.

Khvamli in Colchis was precisely such a place.

---

## The Proto-State of Colchis

Colchian culture is one of the greatest cultural traditions of the ancient world, long ignored by Western historiography.

Colchis as a political entity existed from the end of the 2nd millennium BCE. It was not merely a "tribe," but a proto-state with pronounced features: metallurgical production, trade links with the Mediterranean, royal power, cultural traditions.

The center of this entity was not simply a "king." The king had to be legitimate. His legitimacy was ensured—among other things—by a connection to the sacred mountain.

---

## Diaokhi and its Sacred Place

Diaokhi—a proto-state in eastern Anatolia and the western Transcaucasus, existing in the 12th–8th centuries BCE. Mentioned in Urartian and Assyrian inscriptions.

Khvamli was located at the western edge of the area where Diaokhi and Colchis intersected or bordered. This borderland position made the mountain politically significant: it belonged to or was associated with several power structures simultaneously.

Whoever controlled Khvamli—controlled the symbolic center of this border zone.
---

## Chronicle of the 14th Century: Royal Regalia

The 14th-century *Kartlis Tskhovreba* contains a record stating that during the division of the Georgian kingdom in 1259, royal regalia were kept on Khvamli: "the famous diamond chain, the excellent anvil-shaped ornament, and the great pearl, the like of which no one had ever seen."

This record dates from around the 13th–14th centuries. However, the storage of royal regalia on a sacred mountain is a tradition significantly older.

Regalia on a sacred mountain is a classic political institution: symbols of power are kept where power receives heavenly sanction. On a mountain that is connected to the gods.

Death of a king → regalia remain on the mountain → a new king receives them there → his power is consecrated by the mountain → he is the "legitimate" king.

---

## Khvamli and the Church of St. George

In the 12th–13th centuries, a Church of St. George existed on the summit or slope of Khvamli. An inscription from 1910 bearing the name of the donor — Ioane, *eristavi* of the Svans — dates precisely to this period.

This is the next layer in the same tradition. Pre-Christian sacred mountain → Christianization through the dedication of the mountain to St. George.

St. George in Georgian tradition is a syncretic figure, combining the Christian warrior and the pre-Christian god of thunder and victory. "Tetri Giorgi" — "White George" — is the heir of Kopala, the slayer of *devis*.

Placing the Church of St. George on Khvamli means to consecrate and simultaneously "rename" the pre-Christian cult. Amiran remains chained below, in the cave — while above, on the plateau, now stands the church of the victorious god.

---

## Oracle or Repository?

Was Khvamli an oracle — a place where people came for prophecy?

In Georgian mountain tradition, there exists the institution of the *kadaghe* — a prophet-shaman who enters a trance and conveys the will of the mountain spirit. This practice is known in Khevsureti and Pshavi.

If there were priests on Khvamli who "spoke on behalf of" the mountain — and indirect evidence allows for this — then the mountain was not only a repository but also a place for receiving answers.

"Mountain-oracle" is a political institution. Whoever controls the oracle controls what the gods "say" to rulers.

---

## Continuity of Tradition

The Gokhnari necropolis at the foot of Khvamli demonstrates continuous use of this site from the 10th century AD to the late Middle Ages.

People came to the mountain. Died near the mountain. Wished to be buried near the mountain.

This is not a coincidence. This is a tradition stretching from the Neolithic through the Bronze and Iron Ages, through proto-states, through the medieval Georgian kingdom — to the present day.

Khvamli was not simply sacred "once upon a time." It remained sacred continuously. And this continuity itself is the most important argument.

---

*Next chapter: The People Who Tamed Iron.*

---

# Chapter 42. The People Who Tamed Iron

> "He who controls fire controls metal. He who controls metal controls the world."

---

There are things that change everything with a single motion.

Metal was precisely such a thing. Not just a material — a change of the rules. He who knew how to turn ore into a weapon or a tool possessed a power that cannot be bought or borrowed. He was the master of technology. And in a world where technology was passed down like a treasure — this meant dominance.

The Caucasus was one of the places where this technology appeared earliest.

---

## Caucasian Metallurgy: Chronology

Modern archaeological data is unequivocal.

Copper metallurgy in the Caucasus is recorded from the 5th millennium BC. This is earlier than Mesopotamian. Earlier than Egyptian.

Sakdrisi in Kvemo Kartli — possibly the oldest gold mine in the world. Dating by the Georgian-German expedition of 2004–2011: 3300–2600 BC, Kura-Araxes culture. This is not a random artifact — this is organized mining production.

Colchian metallurgical culture: its zenith in bronze production — approximately 2700–700 BC. Iron metallurgy — its zenith in the 8th–7th centuries BC, that is, earlier than in most neighboring cultures.

---

## Colchis — "The Ancient Center of Iron Metallurgy"

Within the scientific community, there exists a specialized work titled "Colchis — The Ancient Center of Iron Metallurgy," published on Academia.edu, which documents this priority in detail.

Colchian metallurgists developed a complex process for extracting copper from sulfide ores. They knew tin bronze — and possibly from local tin, which was unique for the ancient world, where tin usually had to be transported from distant deposits.

A discovery from the 2020s: Colchian Bronze Age masters used hematite as a flux. This is a direct technological link: the same masters, the same furnaces, the same processes — and the transition from bronze to iron occurs naturally, as a development of existing skills, not as a revolution.

---

## Amiran — Teacher of Blacksmiths

Let us return to the myth.

Amiran brought people not just fire. He brought them metallurgy. In different versions of the legend, it is precisely this — the knowledge of how to turn ore into metal — that became the crime for which the gods chained him to the mountain.

Sacred knowledge, revealed to mortals — punishment.

This is not a random detail. It is a reflection of a real social fact: metallurgy was sacred knowledge, jealously guarded. The blacksmith in most archaic cultures is not merely a craftsman. He is a figure standing on the boundary between people and gods. His forge is a place where fire, earth, and water (for quenching) are transformed into something new.

In Georgian tradition, it is precisely the blacksmiths who are the keepers of the ritual for renewing Amiran's chains. Every year, striking the anvil, they confirm: they are his pupils, and they keep him captive.

Bitter irony? Yes. But also the truth about the nature of technological knowledge: it liberates and simultaneously binds.

---

## The Golden Fleece as a Metallurgical Secret

Researcher D. Gogua, in a documentary film, proposed a hypothesis: the Argonauts came to Colchis not for gold, but for access to metallurgical secrets.

In 2007, the journal *Nature* published an article titled "Fleece Myth Hints at Golden Age for Georgia," which added an important argument to this hypothesis.

"The fleece paradox": Jason's expedition is dated to approximately 1200 BC. Precisely at this time, most golden artifacts disappear from Colchian culture — for about seven hundred years. That is, the Argonauts arrived during a period when gold was scarce in Colchis.

What was not scarce — technology. Iron metallurgy was just gaining strength. Those who mastered it had an advantage.

The Golden Fleece as "access to metallurgical secrets" — this is a hypothesis explaining the paradox.
## The People of Iron

Various Near Eastern texts contain references to a "people of iron" or "men from the land of metal" to the north, beyond the mountains.

Assyrian annals describe the peoples of Transcaucasia as possessors of metallurgical technologies. The Hittites—metallurgists of the Bronze Age—acted as intermediaries between Caucasian metal and the Near East.

The "Chalybes"—an ancient people living on the southeastern coast of the Black Sea and in the mountains of Transcaucasia—are mentioned in Greek sources precisely as blacksmiths and metallurgists. "Khalybs" from the Greek "χάλυψ"—"steel." The Greek word for steel itself derives from the name of a Caucasian people of metallurgists.

The people of iron. People who tamed metal. They lived near Khvamli.

---

## The Forge's Ring in the New Year

And finally—a living detail that has survived to this day.

In the Caucasus, on New Year's Day, blacksmiths strike the anvil. This is not merely a festive ritual. It is the continuation of a tradition stretching back millennia: the ring of the forge as the "pulse" of Amiran, as a sign that the chains hold, that the world continues.

In that same ring lies an acknowledgment: we, the blacksmiths, are the descendants and disciples of Amiran. He gave us this knowledge. We pay him his due—and we hold him.

Technology as debt. Knowledge as an obligation to the one who gave it.

---

*Next chapter: The Golden Fleece as a Metaphor.*

---

# Chapter 43. The Golden Fleece as a Metaphor

> "Behind every great quest lies a question: what exactly are we seeking?"

---

The Golden Fleece is perhaps the most famous object in Western mythology. For over two thousand years, it has inspired poets, artists, and philosophers. Apollonius of Rhodes's *Argonautica*. Euripides's and Seneca's *Medea*. The Golden Fleece of the Order of the Golden Fleece in 15th-century Burgundy. The Knights of the Golden Fleece exist to this day.

But what is this fleece, really?

I propose three competing interpretations—and will explain how they relate to each other.

---

## First Interpretation: Literal Gold

The simplest version. In the mountain rivers of Georgia—to this day—gold is extracted by immersing a sheepskin in the water. Gold particles cling to the wool. The skin is then dried, shaken—and gold is obtained.

The inhabitants of Georgia still use this technique.

Thus, the "Golden Fleece" is an ordinary sheepskin used for gold panning, elevated to mythological proportions. Jason sailed for gold—literally.

Simple. Plausible. But—insufficient.

---

## Second Interpretation: A Metallurgical Secret

The journal *Nature* in 2007 published a paper with the provocative title: "Fleece Myth Hints at Golden Age for Georgia."

The argument: if Jason sailed for gold around 1200 BC—why precisely then, when, according to archaeological data, gold in Colchis was scarce? Most Colchian gold artifacts date from an earlier period—or a significantly later one. Around 1200 BC—a gap.

The answer of D. Gogua: The Argonauts sought not gold, but technological access. The "Fleece" is a metaphor for knowledge. For the secrets of metallurgy that Colchis possessed.

This is a more subtle interpretation. It explains the paradox.

---

## Third Interpretation: A Pharmacological Substrate

Here—an author's hypothesis, which I developed in the context of my medical practice.

Medea—"pharmakís," a sorceress, a healer. Her knowledge of herbs allowed her to rejuvenate Aeson, Jason's father: "to drain the old blood, to fill the body with a decoction of herbs." This is from Ovid (*Metamorphoses*, Book VII). From a medical perspective, this description is not a fairy tale about magic, but a real pharmacological intervention with specific ingredients.

Medea's ingredients in Ovid: roots, seeds, flowers, juices of herbs. Gathered "in the mountains of the Caucasus" and "on the alpine meadows of Olympus."

The Caucasus is one of the world's centers of biodiversity. The alpine meadows of Khvamli, located at the junction of subtropical lowlands and the alpine zone, possess unique pharmacology.

Now: in the rejuvenation ritual, Medea boils an old ram and obtains a lamb. The ram—with a fleece. The fleece—"impregnated" with substrates.

My hypothesis: The "Golden Fleece" is the skin of a sacrificial animal, impregnated with a concentrate of Caucasian pharmacopoeia. A substrate-carrier of active substances. The "first protocol of regenesis."

---

## Why the Three Interpretations Do Not Contradict Each Other

Literal gold: a story about wealth.

Metallurgical secret: a story about technology.

Pharmacological substrate: a story about knowledge of life and death.

Great myths are polysemous—precisely what makes them great. The "Golden Fleece" could have been all three things simultaneously: literal gold, a symbol of metallurgical mastery, and a metaphor for pharmacological knowledge.

Jason sailed for everything at once. For wealth, for technology, for knowledge of eternal life.

And all of this was guarded by Medea—pharmakís, healer, daughter of the son of the Sun.

---

## Khvamli as the Site of the Fleece's Pasture

There is one more element.

The legend of the Golden Fleece places the fleece in the "grove of Ares"—on the banks of the river Phasis (Rioni), guarded by a dragon.

Khvamli stands above the Rioni. The "grove of Ares" as a specific location has not been identified, but the river valley beneath the mountain is the most logical place for storing something sacred: close to the mountain, yet sheltered.

Sheep, grazing on the alpine meadows of Khvamli, are later led to the river. Their wool is impregnated with substances from the alpine herbs. The fleece is stored in the "grove."

This is not a story about magic. This is a story about technology that was not understood from the outside.

---

*Next chapter: Technological Exchange.*

---

# Chapter 44. Technological Exchange

> "Technology is the most peaceful weapon. It changes the world without war."
---

Thus, we conclude Part IV.

Over the past seventeen chapters, we have examined the four vectors of the Khvamli Hypothesis of Transfer (KHT): the Sumerian, Canaanite, Egyptian, and Caucasian-Iberian. We have discussed language, astral cults, cosmogony, and political legitimization.

But there is another vector that permeates all four: the technological.

---

## What "Technological Exchange" Means

In the modern world, we are accustomed to thinking of technology as something that spreads through patents, licenses, and education.

In the Bronze Age, technology spread differently: through people.

A master metallurgist from the Caucasus, who migrated or traded in Anatolia, brought with him not just metal. He brought a process: how to extract ore, how to stoke a furnace, how to achieve the required temperature, how to temper. His presence meant the transfer of technology.

This is living diffusion.

---

## Material Evidence

We have already discussed how Caucasian metal is recorded in Mesopotamia from the 3rd millennium BCE. This is direct material evidence of trade links.

But there is also more subtle evidence.

Colchian tin bronze had a specific composition that differed from the bronze of other regions. Analysis of the composition of bronze artifacts from various locations allows for tracking the "origin" of the metal.

Bronze artifacts with characteristics corresponding to Caucasian production are found in Anatolia and Northern Mesopotamia. This is not a hypothesis. These are the results of metallographic analysis.

---

## The Hittites as Intermediaries

The Hittite Kingdom (18th–12th centuries BCE) occupied Anatolia—the territory between the Caucasus and the Levant.

The Hittites were known in the ancient world precisely as metallurgists. Their iron—in a period when iron was still a rarity—was valued as highly as gold. In letters from Hittite kings to Egyptian pharaohs, iron is mentioned as "something difficult to produce."

The question: where did the Hittites acquire their metallurgical knowledge?

The Hittite area bordered Transcaucasia to the north. Trade routes between Hittite cities and Transcaucasia existed. The transfer of metallurgical technologies from Caucasian masters to the Hittites is a plausible hypothesis supported by a number of researchers.

---

## From the Hittites to the Levant and Egypt

The Hittites transmitted technologies to the Levant—through trade and conquest. From the Levant, technologies went to Egypt.

This creates a chain: Caucasus → Anatolia → Levant → Egypt.

Not a direct transfer. Not a single act. A gradual spread through several intermediary links.

Metallurgical technologies as the "carrier vector" of KHT—this is the most well-documented part of the theory.

---

## What Else Was Transmitted

Along with metal came masters. Along with masters came their knowledge, their myths, their religious concepts.

A blacksmith in archaic culture is not merely a worker. He is a sacred figure. In Hittite religion, the smith god was one of the main deities. In Sumerian culture, blacksmiths had a special cultic status.

When a Caucasian master metallurgist settled in a Hittite city, he brought his gods with him. His concept of the mountain where the spirit of fire dwells. Of the hero whom the gods forbade from teaching people the blacksmith's craft. Of the star that rises over the sacred mountain.

These stories settled. Adapted. Became Hittite, then Canaanite, then—part of the general Near Eastern cultural background.

---

## Conclusion of Part IV

The Khvamli Hypothesis of Transfer does not claim to be complete. It claims to ask the right questions.

The right questions:
— What were the precise trade routes between the Caucasus and Mesopotamia in the 3rd–2nd millennia BCE?
— Do intermediate links exist between the Khvamli astral cult and the Sumerian Inanna?
— What exactly did Japaridze find in the Khvamli caves in 1948–1949?

All these questions can be answered—if we do what is described in the following parts of the book: conduct research, open archives, date the petroglyphs.

Until this is done—KHT remains what it is now: a research program awaiting its executors.

---

*End of Part IV. Next part: The Language of Stone and Stars.*

---

# Chapter 45. Table of Correspondences

> "Data exists in chaos. A table is an attempt to see a system within the chaos."

---

Let us pause for a moment.

Before moving forward—we need to look back and gather everything we have found in one place. To see not individual parallels, but their totality.

This is precisely what tables are for.

---

## Summary Table of KHT Parallels

| Concept | Khvamli/Colchis | Sumer | Canaan | Egypt |
|---------|--------------|-------|--------|--------|
| Sacred Mountain = Axis Mundi | Khvamli (Amiran chained; place of regalia storage) | Ekur / Dur-an-ki (Nippur; "bond of heaven and earth") | Mount Zaphon (Mount of Baal) | Benben / "Primeval Mound" |
| Astral Goddess (Morning Star) | "Star of Khomli" (Vakhushti); Dali in the golden cave | Inanna/Ishtar (morning star) | Astarte | Hathor (morning star) |
| Chained Hero (punished for knowledge/fire) | Amiran (chained in a cave; taught metallurgy) | Adapa (lost immortality; gift of Ea) | — (Samson?) | — |
| Metallurgy as Sacred Knowledge | Caucasus: 5th mill. BCE; Amiran — teacher of smiths | Sumer: 4th mill. BCE; smith-priests | Smiths of Byblos; the Chalybes | God Ptah — patron of crafts |
| "Son of the Sun" as Royal Legitimization | Aeëtes in Colchis (son of Helios) | "Son of An"; Sumerian kings as intermediaries of the gods | "Son of El" | Pharaoh = "Son of Ra" |
| Vertical Pillar/Marker | Khvamli Petroglyphs ("hunters by the pillar") | Column of Enlil in the Ekur; cuneiform sign for "pillar" | Asherah (cultic pillar by altars) | Obelisk/Benben stone |
| Mountain over Water = Place of Creation | Khvamli over the flooding Rioni | Ekur over the primeval waters of the Abzu | Zaphon over the sea of Yam | Benben over the Nun |
| Duality of the Star (morning/evening) | "Star of Khomli" (morning/evening, per Vakhushti) | Inanna: morning = war, evening = love | Astarte = love + war | Hathor + Sekhmet |
| Cave as Sacred Repository | Tekenteli and Boga Caves (regalia, petroglyphs) | Underground storerooms of the Ekur | Rock sanctuaries of Canaan | Pharaohs' tombs (rock + secrecy) |

---

## How to Read This Table
The table does not prove that all these parallels are the result of direct cultural transfer. It shows something else: a system.

Nine categories of concepts. In all four traditions—the presence of an analogous category. Not a random coincidence in one or two points, but a structural correspondence across nine independent parameters.

In science, when several independent lines point in the same direction, it is called "convergent evidence." It is more convincing than a single, very strong piece of evidence.

---

## The Strongest Parallels

Let us highlight those that are most specific—and therefore most significant.

**First: Metallurgy from the 5th millennium BCE in the Caucasus.** This is a fact, not a hypothesis. And metallurgy of Caucasian origin is materially attested in Mesopotamia. This is the most solid link.

**Second: The "Chained Hero."** Amiran is chained for metallurgy, Adapa is denied immortality for knowledge—a single matrix of "knowledge punished by the gods." But the specificity of the Caucasian variant (its connection to metallurgy) makes Amiran, not Adapa, more "original": it was precisely in the Caucasus that metallurgy was sacred knowledge of paramount importance.

**Third: The "Star of Khomli" as an astral cult.** Vakhushti records the identification of the mountain with Venus. This is a historically documented fact. That the same astral cult is present with Inanna and Astarte makes the connection plausible.

---

## The Weakest Parallels

Being honest is also a scientific position.

**The Egyptian vector**: the weakest parallels. The distance is great. The intermediate links are unreliable. "Mountain above water" is too universal an image to be a marker of a specific connection.

**The root "khvam-" / Semitic "ḥll"**: a semantic observation, not a linguistic fact.

**Petroglyphs as proto-writing**: requires dating and specialized iconographic research, which is lacking.

---

## What the Table Says as a Whole

When you look at all nine rows together—an image emerges.

Not "The Caucasus is the source of all civilizations." But—"The Caucasus is one of the active cultural centers of the ancient world, connected to other centers through trade, people, and ideas."

This is a more modest, but more honest assertion.

And this is enough. Enough to reconsider Georgia's place on the map of world history.

---

*Next chapter: The Venus Calendar.*

---

# Chapter 46. The Venus Calendar

> "Time is not something that flows by itself. It is something people learned to measure by the sky."

---

Let's start with a number: 584.

This is exactly how many days constitute the synodic cycle of Venus—the interval between two successive "inferior conjunctions" of the planet with the Sun. That is: from one disappearance of Venus in the western sky to the next such disappearance.

584 days is not an obvious number. It cannot be "guessed." To calculate it, one must systematically observe the planet over many years, record the dates of its appearances and disappearances, and then seek a pattern.

This is mathematics. And this is labor.

---

## The Sumerian Venus Tablet of Ammisaduqa

In Babylon, there existed a tablet (the Venus Tablet of Ammisaduqa, dated to approximately the 17th century BCE), which recorded observations of Venus over a period of twenty-one years.

These are systematic astronomical observations. Methodical. Multi-year.

To create such a tablet, a permanent, protected observation site is needed. A place where the horizon is visible in all directions with perfect clarity. A place where observers can live and work for years.

In Mesopotamia, such a place was the summit of a ziggurat.

In the Caucasus—Khvamli.

---

## The Khvamli Plateau as an Observatory

We have already spoken of this in general terms. Now—in more detail.

The Khvamli plateau is open to the horizon from all sides. Its altitude is about 2194 meters above sea level, providing a clear horizon without optical interference from surrounding peaks. A dominant position over the valley.

The rising and setting of Venus from this point are measurable with high precision: one can mark the point on the horizon where the planet appears or disappears. Year after year, this point shifts—with a predictable pattern.

Researcher Giginashvili recorded: a series of rock outcrops and niches on the plateau are oriented in such a way that they could have been used as astronomical markers. That is—"sights" for observing the rising of Venus on specific days of its cycle.

---

## The Eight-Year Cycle of Venus

One of the key astronomical discoveries of the ancient world: eight years of Venus = five synodic cycles. More precisely: 8 × 365.25 = 2922 days; 5 × 584 = 2920 days. The difference is 2 days over eight years.

This means: every eight years Venus "returns" almost exactly to the same position in the sky at the same time of year.

The five-pointed star—the sign of Venus—is the geometric embodiment of this cycle. If one marks the points of Venus's rising on the horizon over eight years—a pentagon is formed. A five-pointed star can be inscribed within it.

Five-pointed star = eight-year cycle of Venus = symbol of Inanna/Ishtar/Astarte.

Whoever could observe this cycle from Khvamli could construct a "pentagonal" religious calendar.

---

## The Venusian Calendar and Ritual

In the Sumerian tradition, festivals associated with Inanna are tied to the astronomical calendar of Venus. The descent of Inanna into the underworld = the disappearance of Venus at inferior conjunction. Her return = the appearance of Venus as the morning star.

If a similar ritual calendar existed at Khvamli—and indirect data (the identification of the mountain with Venus, the flat summit as an observatory, the cult of the morning star) allow for this—then we are looking at a common astral ritual program for the entire region.

Not identical. But—structurally parallel.

---

## What is Needed for Confirmation

To verify this hypothesis, the following is needed.

First and foremost—a detailed survey of the rock outcrops on the plateau with azimuthal analysis: in which directions are these "sights" oriented? Do they align with the points of Venus's rising/setting on key days of the cycle?
This is a purely technically feasible task. It requires a qualified astroarchaeologist with geodetic equipment and several days of work on the plateau.

Such work has not been done systematically.

If it is done — the result will be unequivocal: either the orientation is random, or it is not. Either the "Star of Khomli" is a beautiful metaphor, or it is a description of the mountain's real astronomical function.

---

*Next chapter: Inanna, Ishtar, Astarte, Khvamli.*

---

# Chapter 47. Inanna, Ishtar, Astarte, Khvamli

> "The goddess changes names. But does not change her nature."

---

This is the story of one goddess, known by many names.

Inanna — Sumerian. Ishtar — Akkadian. Astarte — Canaanite. Venus — Roman. Aphrodite — Greek.

Different names, different peoples, different millennia. But — one function, one iconography, one astronomical basis.

And — one place where, perhaps, it all began.

---

## Inanna: The First Name

The earliest depictions of Inanna date to the 4th millennium BCE — the time of the first cities of Sumer.

She was the goddess of Uruk — the largest city of early Sumer. Her temple "Eanna" — "House of Heaven" — was the main sanctuary of the city.

Inanna was depicted on cylinder seals as a woman standing on a mountain, with wings behind her back, sometimes with weapons. Her symbols: an eight-pointed star (Venus), a reed bundle (the entrance to her temple), a lion (her "steed").

Her main story is the descent into the underworld. She passes through seven gates, leaving one attribute at each. Dies. Returns. Renewed.

This is the myth of Venus, disappearing from the sky and reappearing. Death and resurrection as an astronomical fact.

---

## Ishtar: The Second Level

In Babylon, Inanna became Ishtar. The name changed, the nature — did not.

Ishtar appears on Mount Batir in the Zagros range — on the stele of Anu-banini. A mountain goddess. A goddess on the horizon, where the morning star appears.

This is an important detail: Ishtar stands on a mountain. Not in a city, not in a temple. On a mountain.

For Sumerian theology, a mountain is the place where sky and earth touch. The goddess of the morning star, standing on a mountain, is a goddess who lives on the border between worlds.

This is exactly Dali — the Georgian goddess of the hunt, living in a golden cave on a mountaintop.

---

## Astarte: The Third Level

In Canaan, Ishtar/Inanna became Astarte. Or — Ashtoreth in Hebrew.

Astarte is directly identified with Venus: in Phoenician inscriptions she is called "Astarte of the face of Baal" or "Astarte the morning star."

In Greek mythology, Astarte = Aphrodite. Thus the circle closed: from the Sumerian Inanna through the Babylonian Ishtar and the Canaanite Astarte — to the Greek goddess of love.

---

## Dali: The Caucasian Family Member

Dali — the Svan goddess of the hunt and mountain animals — has the following profile.

She lives on mountaintops. She is golden (golden hair, golden cave) — gold is associated with sunlight. She is beautiful — beauty as an attribute of the goddess. She is dangerous for mortals — like all gods who cross the boundary.

Her lovers are hunters. A man chosen by the goddess receives gifts (luck in hunting) and bears obligations. Breaking the obligations means death.

This is an astral program. The Moon and its priest. The star and its adept.

Dali is not directly identified with Venus in Georgian texts. But the "Star of Khomli" in Vakhushti — is a mountain associated with Dali through Amiran (chained there). An astral goddess of the mountain. A goddess whose golden cave is on the summit of a "shining" mountain.

Structurally — this is the same matrix as Inanna/Ishtar.

---

## Complex Analysis: Common Features

All four goddesses — Inanna, Ishtar, Astarte, and the "goddess of Khvamli" — are united by the following.

**Astral function**: connection with the morning star (Venus) or with celestial bodies.

**Mountain association**: the goddess is linked to a mountain, to a high point from which the sky is visible.

**Duality**: the goddess combines opposite principles (love/war, life/death, morning/evening).

**Cyclicality**: the goddess descends and returns, dies and resurrects.

**Danger to mortals**: contact with the goddess is lethal or demands a high price.

Five common features. They cannot be a random coincidence.

---

## Direction of Transfer: Hypothesis

I do not claim that Dali became the "prototype" of Inanna. That would be too strong a claim.

I claim: there exists a single cultural field in which the goddess of the morning star, associated with a sacred mountain, was one of the most stable religious images of the ancient world. The Caucasus was part of this cultural field — not a periphery, but an active center.

The "Star of Khomli" is not a borrowing from Sumer. It is the Caucasian branch of the same tree.

---

*Next chapter: From Pictogram to Letter.*

---

# Chapter 48. From Pictogram to Letter

> "The first word, written by hand, changed man irreversibly. Now he could speak with those he did not know."
The history of writing is the story of how a drawing became a sign, a sign became a sound, and a sound became a letter.

This did not happen at once. It took millennia. And it did not happen in one place.

---

## Three Cradles of Writing

It is traditionally held that writing emerged independently in three places.

Sumer — circa 3600–3200 BCE. Egypt — circa 3200 BCE. China — circa 1200 BCE.

Was the Caucasus the fourth — or perhaps the second? This question has been posed. There is no answer yet.

---

## The Bashplemi Tablet: New Data

In 2021, during low water levels at Lake Bashplemi (Dmanisi region, southern Georgia), a basalt tile was discovered.

Dimensions: 24.1 × 20.1 centimeters. On its surface — 60 symbols, of which 39 are unique.

Published in 2024 in the journal JAHA. It has not been deciphered.

The tablet's symbols show similarities with several systems at once: with Proto-Kartvelian script from the 4th millennium BCE, with Caucasian Albanian, with Georgian *Mrglovani*, with Phoenician, and with Proto-Sinaitic. Possible dating: Late Bronze or Early Iron Age.

This is direct evidence: an independent tradition of symbol recording existed in the Caucasus, not borrowed from either Sumer or Egypt.

---

## Proto-Kartvelian Script

In academic discourse, there exists the concept of "Proto-Kartvelian script" — a supposition that the ancestors of the modern Georgian script trace back to a pictographic system that existed in the Caucasus in the 4th millennium BCE or earlier.

This is a hypothesis. Direct evidence in the form of verified tablets with Proto-Kartvelian script is insufficient.

But the 2024 Bashplemi find adds: something was there. Not borrowed, but their own.

---

## Petroglyphs as Proto-Writing

In linguistic theory, a distinction is made between "proto-writing" and "writing."

Proto-writing: a system of symbols with stable meaning, used for recording information but not reproducing speech sounds. These are pictograms, iconic signs, numerical systems.

Writing: a system that reproduces speech — syllabic or alphabetic.

Petroglyphs are proto-writing. They are not letters. But they are symbols with stable meaning.

If the Khvamli petroglyphs contain stable, repeating symbols — "hunters by a pillar," astral signs, geometric forms — then they constitute proto-writing. A system for recording information.

---

## Gabunia's Hypothesis Applied to Khvamli

Manana Gabunia worked with the Trialeti petroglyphs and studied them as a possible system of proto-writing. According to her analysis, a series of these symbols demonstrates stability and systematicity that goes beyond simple pictorial art.

If a similar analysis is applied to the Khvamli petroglyphs — provided that earlier, prehistoric images exist there — we may arrive at the following question: are these symbols the predecessors of the Kartvelian written tradition?

This is not an assertion. This is a question for a research program.

---

## The Path from Drawing to the Georgian Alphabet

The Georgian alphabet is one of the most beautiful in the world. Its traditional origin is associated with the 5th century CE and the name of Mesrop Mashtots or King Pharnavaz (an earlier version).

But an alphabet does not arise from nothing. Behind every alphabet lies a long tradition of symbol recording. The Phoenician alphabet grew out of Proto-Sinaitic. The Greek alphabet — out of Phoenician.

The question of the deep roots of the Georgian written tradition is open. The Bashplemi tablet, Proto-Kartvelian symbols, the Khvamli petroglyphs — these are fragments of a puzzle yet to be assembled.

---

## What We Can Assert

We can assert: an independent symbolic tradition existed in the Caucasus, preceding the emergence of the Georgian alphabet.

We can suppose: the Khvamli petroglyphs — if they date to the Neolithic or Bronze Age — are part of this tradition.

We cannot assert: what exactly is depicted on the potentially ancient Khvamli petroglyphs, because their full documentation and dating are not complete.

An honest position: the question is open. And this is good. Open questions are an invitation to research.

---

*Next chapter: Comparison with Umm el-Qa'ab.*

---

# Chapter 49. Comparison with Umm el-Qa'ab

> "Inaccessibility is not a rejection of the world. It is a form of protecting what is important."

---

In the desert near Abydos, in Upper Egypt, there is a place with the Arabic name Umm el-Qa'ab — "Mother of Pots."

The name comes from the pottery shards: there are so many here that the ground is literally covered with them. For millennia, people came here and left offerings. Vessels with food, with oil, with wine — for the dead.

Because beneath this field of shards lie the tombs of the pharaohs of the 1st and 2nd Dynasties. The earliest known Egyptian royal burials. Circa 3000 BCE.

---

## The Logic of Sacred Inaccessibility

Umm el-Qa'ab is located in the cliffs at the edge of the desert — away from the Nile, far from cities.

Why here?

The first Egyptian pharaohs chose for their tombs a place that was difficult to access, remote from life. Desert, cliffs, absence of water. A place difficult to reach — and therefore, a place protected by nature itself.

This is the logic of "sacred inaccessibility."

A sacred place must be difficult. Not because people are bad. But because the labor of the ascent or the journey is itself a ritual act. You go to a sacred place not as you go to the market. You carry with you an effort. And this effort is part of the rite.

---

## Khvamli and the Same Logic
Khvamli is difficult to access. It is not merely high—vertical cliffs on all sides make it practically unassailable without specialized skills.

This is precisely why it is an ideal sacred place. Not in spite of its inaccessibility—but because of it.

He who has ascended Khvamli has done something that most cannot. He has already separated himself from ordinary people. He has undergone a physical rite of passage.

In this sense, Khvamli and Umm el-Qa'ab implement the same religious program: to make access to the sacred—difficult. So that only the worthy may enter.

---

## Caves as a Substitute for Tombs

In Umm el-Qa'ab, tombs are hewn into the rock. This is not accidental. Rock preserves. Rock protects. Rock endures when everything else crumbles.

In the caves of Khvamli, the constant temperature is around ten degrees above zero. High humidity, but without direct sunlight. These are ideal conditions for preserving organic materials.

The vertical shafts of Khvamli are like the vertical shafts of early dynastic Egyptian tombs. A difficult descent, a dark space, the object of preservation at the bottom.

The parallel is not in the details. The parallel is in the function: "difficult entrance" → "dark space" → "preserved object".

---

## What the Mountain Stored

In Umm el-Qa'ab, they stored dead kings. Along with the kings—inventory: items, tools, vessels, inscriptions.

In Khvamli, they stored the regalia of living kings: the "diamond chain," the "anvil," the "unseen pearl." And—according to indirect evidence—petroglyphs that were "written" on the walls.

A different type of storage. But the same idea: the mountain is that which will not burn, will not be looted, will not be lost. The mountain is eternity.

---

## Not Kinship, but a Shared Logic

Umm el-Qa'ab and Khvamli are not directly connected. They are two sites at two ends of the Near East, separated by millennia and thousands of kilometers.

But they implement the same logic: "the sacred must be difficult to attain." This logic is universal. It arose independently—among people who intuitively understood: that which is given easily is not valued. That which requires effort—is sacred.

In this comparison, something else is important: Khvamli as a functional object—a "difficult cave for storing the sacred"—fits into a global tradition, rather than being a cultural anomaly.

It is normal to have such a place in the Caucasus. It would be strange not to have one.

---

*Next chapter: The Archetype of Suffering for Knowledge.*

---

# Chapter 50. The Archetype of Suffering for Knowledge

> "Behind every great knowledge stands one to whom it cost everything."

---

Three stories. Three different peoples. Three millennia and two millennia.

Prometheus—chained to a rock for stealing fire. An eagle pecks at his liver every day. Eternal suffering as payment for giving knowledge to mankind.

Amiran—chained in a cave of Mount Khvamli for stealing fire and metallurgical secrets. A dog licks his chains. An eagle pecks at his chest. Every new year, blacksmiths reforged his fetters.

Adapa—a Sumerian sage, endowed with wisdom by the god Ea. He receives knowledge—but refuses the food and water of the gods, fearing poison. And thereby loses the chance for immortality. Knowledge without eternal life.

---

## One Matrix, Three Variants

All three myths embody a single structure.

**Hero between two worlds**: he is not quite human and not quite god. He stands on the boundary.

**Knowledge as a violation of order**: his knowledge—or his desire to transmit this knowledge to people—violates the established order. The gods wish to maintain a monopoly on fire, on wisdom, on immortality.

**Punishment**: the hero pays an existential price. Not death—worse: eternal suffering (Prometheus, Amiran) or deprivation of that which could have saved him (Adapa).

**Gift to mankind**: despite the punishment, the knowledge reached people. Fire burns. Metal is forged. Wisdom is transmitted.

---

## Who Was First?

The question of chronological priority is delicate.

Amiran is recorded in 68+ versions of the Georgian oral epic, written down in the 19th–20th centuries. There are no earlier written records—the Georgian tradition was oral until the 5th century AD.

Prometheus appears in Greek literature from the 8th century BC (Hesiod). Aeschylus's tragedy *Prometheus Bound*—5th century BC.

Adapa—a Sumerian text, dated to the 14th–13th centuries BC in its Akkadian version; the original may be older.

By date of written record: Adapa → Prometheus → Amiran. But this is the date of recording, not the date of the myth's origin. An oral myth is always older than its first written record.

---

## The Caucasian Argument

Researchers Tardivo and Kitselis, in their 2019 work "Prometheus or Amirani: An Updated Study on the Pre-Greek Substrate and its Origins," argue: the Caucasian myth of Amiran could have influenced the Greek version of Prometheus through contact between Colchian peoples and Greek colonists of the Black Sea region.
