The Greeks themselves said: Prometheus was chained to a "Caucasian rock." This geographical indication is not a metaphor.

Mikhail Chikovani, in his monograph *Amiran in Chains* (1947), collected 68 versions of the legend from 1848 to 1945—and concluded: Amiran is likely older than the Greek literary version of Prometheus.

Academic caution: "likely." But—"likely" in the work of a professor with 68 verified versions—carries weight.

---

## The Specificity of the Caucasian Variant

There is one detail that makes Amiran particularly convincing in the context of the KHT.

In the Amiran version, the emphasis is on metallurgy. Not just "fire," but "blacksmithing." Amiran taught people to forge. Precisely this—is his crime, for which he is punished.

In the Caucasian context, this makes perfect sense: the Caucasus was a metallurgical center. The knowledge of the blacksmith was sacred. Its transmission to people—is a violation of the sacred monopoly.

In the Sumerian Adapa, there is no metallurgy. In the Greek Prometheus, it is present indirectly (fire → smelting). In Amiran—directly.

This makes the Caucasian myth the most "original" in the sense: it better reflects the cultural reality of the society where it arose.

---

## The Archetype as a Mirror
Why is this myth so persistent? Why does it appear in three independent traditions with a single matrix?

Because it describes a real social phenomenon.

Every culture possesses knowledge that "belongs to the gods"—that is, belongs to the elite, the priests, the masters. And there is a moment when someone breaks the monopoly and transmits this knowledge further.

This person is a hero to those who received the knowledge. And a criminal to those who guarded it.

Prometheus, Amiran, Adapa—these are the archetype of the cultural transgressor. The person who stands between gods and humans and chooses humans.

In the Caucasus—with its metallurgical tradition, with its myth of Amiran—this archetype is most material, most concrete, most grounded in reality.

This is one of the arguments of the KHT: the Caucasian version of the myth is closer to its material roots than the Greek or Sumerian ones.

---

*End of Part V. Next part: Science at the Crossroads.*

---

# Chapter 51. The Dating Problem: Between Hypothesis and Proof

> "In science, the most honest answer is 'we do not know.' And the strongest question is 'why do we not know?'"

---

It is time to speak about the problem directly and without illusions.

Everything we have discussed in the previous parts—parallels, hypotheses, structural coincidences—rests on one key premise: the Khvamli petroglyphs are truly ancient. Ancient in the sense that their creation predates the emergence of Sumerian civilization or at least coincides with it chronologically.

But here it is necessary to make a critically important admission: **the earliest RELIABLY dated images from Khvamli belong to the 12th–13th centuries AD.** Claims of an age in the 6th–5th millennia BC are currently unverified by modern scientific methods. This does not mean they are false—it means they remain in the status of an unconfirmed hypothesis.

It is precisely in this chronological chasm between the proven medieval period and the assumed Neolithic that the main challenge for the Khvamli-Tigranakert Theory (KHT) lies.

---

### Why "Style" is Not a Date

Before the advent of objective physico-chemical dating methods, rock art was dated stylistically: by similarity to known samples, by the technique of pecking, by the assumed cultural context. This approach is vulnerable. It relies on subjective perception and a limited comparative base. For the Caucasus, such a base is fragmentary.

When non-professional sources or early researchers speak of the "Neolithic of Khvamli," they almost certainly rely on stylistic analogies. But analogy is not proof. A petroglyph stylistically similar to a Neolithic one could have been created in the Bronze Age, in antiquity, or even in the Middle Ages as a conscious archaization or a copy of a lost original.

**Key conclusion:** if the Khvamli petroglyphs turn out to be medieval, this does not refute the KHT hypothesis itself about an ancient Caucasian proto-civilization. It changes its evidentiary base. In that case, the petroglyphs may not be the "primary source," but a later fixation, replica, or interpretation of a much older oral or lost material tradition. However, without an accurate date, we cannot distinguish the source from the echo.

---

### Modern Science: How Petroglyphs are Dated Worldwide

Today we have tools to replace the question "what does this look like?" with the question "how old is this?" Here are the main methods:

1.  **AMS radiocarbon analysis of pigments.** If organic binders (plant resins, animal fat, blood) were used in the paint (e.g., ochre), micro-samples can be extracted and the age of the carbon in them determined with an accuracy of several decades. This is precisely how the famous bison images in **Altamira** (Spain) and mammoths in **Chauvet Cave** (France) were dated, confirming their Paleolithic age.

2.  **Uranium-thorium dating (U-Th) of calcite crusts.** In caves and rock shelters, thin layers of calcite (speleothems) often grow over drawings. By analyzing the ratio of uranium and thorium isotopes in this mineral "crust," the minimum age of the image beneath it can be established. This method helped refine the chronology of the **Chauvet** complex (France) and the **Altai petroglyphs**.

3.  **Optically Stimulated Luminescence (OSL).** The method determines when mineral grains (e.g., quartz) in a layer covering a petroglyph were last exposed to sunlight. This gives the date of the image's "burial."

**The Khvamli Paradox:** none of these modern methods have been **applied** to the key, controversial Khvamli petroglyphs. The closest benchmark is **Gobustan** in Azerbaijan (a UNESCO site), where AMS analysis confirmed an age of around 5900 BC for some images. Khvamli, however, remains in the shadow of methodological backwardness and a lack of research attention.

---

### A Concrete Call to Action: What to Do?

The dating problem is not a dead end, but a technical task. It can and must be solved.

**Goal:** Obtain a direct scientific date for at least one key Khvamli petroglyph claiming prehistoric age.

**Method:** The priority is **AMS analysis of organic pigment components**. In their absence—**U-Th dating** of possible calcite deposits.

**Executor:** An appeal to a specialized international laboratory, for example, the **Centre for Isotope Research at the University of Groningen** (Netherlands) or the **Oxford Radiocarbon Accelerator Unit** (UK), which have extensive experience working with rock art.

**Budget and Actions:** The cost of one AMS analysis ranges from 500 to 1200 euros. It requires organizing a micro-expedition involving a licensed restorer or archaeologist for **non-contact micro-sampling** (weighing a few milligrams) in compliance with all ethical and conservation standards. This will not damage the monument but will provide a definitive answer.

Without this procedure, the debate about the age of the Khvamli petroglyphs will forever circle in the realm of speculative assumptions. With it—we will gain a point of reference. Either Khvamli will gain its confirmed ancient age and stand alongside the great monuments of the world, or we will learn that we are dealing with a unique phenomenon of medieval sacred archaism. In any case, it will be knowledge, not conjecture.

It is precisely the absence of this simple, yet decisive procedure that is the **main enemy** of a genuine understanding of Khvamli today.

---
*Next chapter: Methods of the Future.*
---

# Chapter 52. Methods of the Future

> "The tools have already been created. The question is whether we have the will to apply them."

---

The previous chapter ended with an honest admission: we do not have a date for the Khvamli petroglyphs. This is not a tragedy. It is a task.

And this task is technically solvable. The tools exist. The methods are proven. All that is needed is an expedition.

Let us consider the available arsenal.

---

## AMS Radiocarbon Analysis

Accelerator Mass Spectrometry (AMS) is the most direct method if the pigment is organic.

Many petroglyphs were created using ochre—red iron oxide. Ochre itself is inorganic and cannot be radiocarbon dated. But ochre was often mixed with organic binders: animal fat, plant oil, blood, egg white. It is precisely these organic impurities that can be dated.

The AMS method requires a sample of negligible volume—on the order of 1 milligram. For pigment on rock, this is quite feasible: a microscopic scraping is taken, which almost does not damage the image.

Method accuracy: from several centuries to—under ideal conditions—less than a hundred years. For our question (6th millennium BC vs. 12th–13th centuries AD), this is more than sufficient.

It was precisely AMS dating that was applied to the Gobustan petroglyphs in Azerbaijan. Result: 7850 ± 30 years ago. A verified figure. It does not exist for Khvamli—but it could appear.

---

## OSL Dating

Optically Stimulated Luminescence (OSL) works differently: it dates not the pigment itself, but the mineral grains in the surface layer of the rock.

Mineral grains accumulate a dose of ionizing radiation from the moment of their last exposure to light. When the grains are illuminated in the laboratory with a special light, they "release" the accumulated charge in the form of a luminescent flash. The intensity of the flash shows how much time has passed since the last irradiation.

For petroglyphs, the method is applied as follows: a sample of the surface layer is taken near the image or beneath the pigment layer. The date indicates the last exposure to light—that is, when the surface was last exposed to sunlight.

Limitation: if the surface has been repeatedly covered and re-exposed, the result may be ambiguous. But in the cave conditions of Khvamli—where contact with the external environment is limited—the method can yield a stable result.

---
## U-Th Dating of Speleothems

In caves, calcite crusts—speleothems—can form over images. This occurs gradually, as moisture saturated with carbon dioxide and calcium seeps through the rock.

The uranium-thorium (U-Th) dating method is exceptionally precise, with an error margin of a few percent. Crucially, it provides a *minimum* age. If a petroglyph is covered by a speleothem dated to 5000 years, the image is no younger than that speleothem. It is older or of the same age.

This method has been applied to date rock art in Spanish caves—in Altamira, in La Pasiega. The results changed history: it turned out that some images are older than 40,000 years.

The Khvamli caves—Tekenteli, Boga—have active speleothem formation. This makes them a potentially ideal object for the U-Th method.

---

## Photogrammetry and 3D Documentation

Before dating, documentation is necessary.

Modern photogrammetry allows for the creation of an accurate three-dimensional model of a rock surface from a series of overlapping photographs. Resolution can reach fractions of a millimeter. The depth of grooves, surface relief, tool marks—all become visible.

Such documentation solves several problems simultaneously.

First, it creates a permanent reference archive: even if the petroglyphs deteriorate over time, their 3D model will remain. Second, photogrammetry helps detect images invisible to the naked eye: faint, faded, or overlaid by later ones. Third, relative dating is possible: newer over older—this is stratigraphy, but on a two-dimensional surface.

The equipment is a standard DSLR camera plus software (Agisoft Metashape, Reality Capture). The cost is incomparably lower than that of a drilling expedition.

---

## Drone LiDAR

Laser scanning from an unmanned aerial vehicle—LiDAR—allows for seeing structures beneath vegetation cover.

This is a revolutionary method for tropical jungles (it was LiDAR that revealed hidden Mayan cities under the forests of Guatemala). For Khvamli, it is relevant for another reason: vertical walls and hard-to-reach niches. A drone can fly along a sheer cliff, scanning every centimeter of the surface at a distance inaccessible to humans.

Additionally, LiDAR detects relief regardless of lighting and surface color. Images carved into the rock without pigment—purely engraved—become visible only under oblique lighting. LiDAR registers them directly.

---

## Ground-Penetrating Radar and Microbiological Cores

Artifacts may lie hidden beneath the cave surface.

Ground-Penetrating Radar (GPR) sends radio waves into the rock and analyzes the reflections. This allows for the detection of voids, density anomalies, and possible burials—without destroying the surface.

Microbiological cores—thin cylinders drilled into the cave wall—contain layers of sediment, pollen, spores, and organic matter. Analysis of these layers reconstructs the climatic and biological history of the cave with decadal precision.

---

## What a Comprehensive Approach Will Yield

No single method can solve the task completely.

AMS will say: this pigment is this many years old. OSL will say: this surface was last exposed to light this many years ago. U-Th will say: the image is no younger than this many years. Photogrammetry will create an archive. LiDAR will find hidden features. GPR will look beneath the surface.

The totality of answers will provide what is currently lacking: a verified dating of the Khvamli petroglyphs with multiple independent methods of verification.

This is the global scientific standard. Gobustan has undergone it. Altamira has undergone it. Lascaux has undergone it.

Khvamli awaits.

---

*Next chapter: Paleogenetic Data.*

---

# Chapter 53. Paleogenetic Data

> "DNA is the language in which history is written without an editor. It does not flatter and does not remain silent."

---

In 2019, a group of Georgian geneticists published a study that went almost unnoticed outside of professional literature.

Tsintsadze, Khetsuriani, and co-authors investigated mitochondrial haplogroups from the Gokhnari necropolis—a burial complex at the foot of Khvamli, dated to the 10th–14th centuries AD.

The results were unexpected.

---

## What Was Found in Gokhnari

The Gokhnari necropolis is not just a cemetery. It is a site of continuous burial for at least four centuries, located within direct sight of Mount Khvamli. The people buried here lived at the foot of the sacred mountain.

Mitochondrial haplogroups were successfully extracted and analyzed from 13 samples. A haplogroup is a branch of the genetic tree, traceable through the maternal line. It persists for millennia.

What was discovered: among the 13 samples, haplogroups H and U predominated—characteristic of West Eurasian populations. This is not surprising: the Caucasus was and remains a West Eurasian area.

But among the samples was haplogroup J2—a marker that takes us much further back.

---

## Haplogroup J2: A Bridge Across Millennia

Mitochondrial haplogroup J2 (not to be confused with Y-chromosomal J2) is West Eurasian, with a concentration in the Near East. It is associated with the Neolithic expansion from the Levant—with the movement of farmer-herders who, around the 8th–6th millennia BC, began spreading from the Fertile Crescent.

The Caucasus was not a passive recipient of this movement—it was part of it. Recent genetic studies (Lazaridis et al., 2016; Wang et al., 2019) show: Caucasian hunter-gatherers and Anatolian farmers intermixed. The descendants of this mixture became ancestors of European and Near Eastern populations.

Simply put: the people with the Gokhnari genes were not an isolate, but part of the same West Eurasian network that included the Levant, Anatolia, and Mesopotamia.

---

## What This Means for KHT

The Khvamli Hypothesis of Transfer is built on the hypothesis of ideational connections—the transfer of ideas, images, mythological structures through trade and migration contacts.

The genetic data from Gokhnari do not directly prove these contacts. They show something important: the population of the Khvamli region was genetically connected to the same network from which Near Eastern civilizations grew.

This is a necessary condition. Not sufficient—but necessary.

If genetics had shown complete isolation—if J2 were absent among the samples, with exclusively relict Caucasian markers predominating—this would weaken KHT. But the data speak of integration, not isolation.

---

## Prospect: aDNA from Earlier Layers

The analysis by Tsintsadze and colleagues covers the medieval period—the 10th–14th centuries. This is valuable but insufficient for testing KHT, which appeals to the Neolithic and Bronze Age.

A revolutionary step would be obtaining aDNA—ancient DNA—from earlier burials in the Khvamli region. If there are Bronze Age or Chalcolithic burials at the foot of the mountain, their genetic analysis could show direct connections with Near Eastern populations of the corresponding period.

Georgian paleogenetics is actively developing. In 2021–2024, a series of works on aDNA from the Georgian Caucasus were published, covering periods from the Neolithic to the Iron Age. The Khvamli region has not yet been singled out as a separate object in these works.
This is a task for the next stage.

---

## The Y-Chromosome Question

A parallel question: Y-chromosomal haplogroups — the paternal line.

Y-chromosomal J2 is one of the most characteristic markers of the Neolithic expansion from the Near East. Its frequency in the Caucasus — particularly in western Georgia and among the Megrelians — is quite high. This indirectly points to the same conclusion: a deep historical connection with the Near Eastern genetic pool.

However, Y-chromosomal analysis requires samples from male burials with identifiable biological sex — a standard that has not yet been fully met for Gokhnari.

---

## An Honest Conclusion

The genetic data from Gokhnari are preliminary results. Thirteen samples do not constitute a representative sample. This is exploratory drilling that confirms: there is bedrock here worth drilling deeper into.

Science works precisely this way: first, a small sample that indicates a direction. Then — a full-scale study that provides an answer.

The direction — has been indicated.

---

*Next chapter: Caution — the sample.*

---

# Chapter 54. Caution: The Sample

> "The most dangerous moment in science is when there is enough data to want to believe in it, but not enough to be certain."

---

Thirteen samples.

That is the number of genetic samples successfully obtained from the Gokhnari necropolis. And it is with this number that one must be extremely cautious.

---

## Why Sample Size Matters

Statistics knows the concept of "statistical power" — the ability of a study to detect a real effect if it exists. With small samples, power is low. This means: random deviations can mimic patterns.

Thirteen samples from a medieval necropolis are results from real people. But do they represent the population? No. They represent thirteen specific individuals, whose fates could have been completely atypical.

Suppose two individuals with haplogroup J2 were found among the thirteen. That is 15% of the sample. But the actual frequency of J2 in the population could have been 5% or 40% — thirteen people do not allow this to be determined with acceptable accuracy.

---

## The Problem of Representativeness

The Gokhnari necropolis is located in a specific place: at the foot of Khvamli, along the road. The people buried here are not a random sample of the entire district's population. They are those who lived near this road, who died in this place, whose remains have been preserved to our time and became available for research.

Each of these filters introduces bias.

Good preservation of remains means: most likely, the burials were sufficiently deep and dry. Availability for research means: they were located in the excavation zone, i.e., in a specific part of the necropolis. Belonging to this place means: possibly, a certain social status, profession, or origin.

---

## The Problem of the Time Range

10th–14th centuries — that is four hundred years. Over four centuries, the population of the Khvamli region could have changed radically: migrations, Mongol invasions, demographic upheavals.

Mixing samples from different centuries into one "sample from Gokhnari" erases temporal dynamics. Two samples from the 10th century and two from the 14th century are not one population; they are two completely different historical realities.

Without precise dating of each burial within the necropolis, this semantic nuance is lost.

---

## What Can and Cannot Be Asserted

It can be asserted: among the 13 samples from the Gokhnari necropolis (10th–14th centuries), mitochondrial haplogroups H, U, and J2, characteristic of the West Eurasian genetic space, were discovered.

It cannot be asserted: that the population of the Khvamli region in the Bronze Age or Neolithic had the same genetic distribution.

It cannot be asserted: that 15% J2 in the sample corresponds to the real frequency of this haplogroup in the historical population.

It cannot be asserted: that genetics confirms KHT. Genetics creates a compatible background — not proof.

---

## Why Talk About Them Then?

Because the direction is correct.

If the 13 samples had shown complete isolation — exclusively endemic Caucasian markers without West Eurasian connections — that would have been an argument against KHT.

The fact that the data are compatible with KHT is not proof, but it is also not a refutation. It is an invitation to the next step: to take not 13, but 130 samples. To cover different periods: Neolithic, Chalcolithic, Bronze Age. To include male Y-chromosomal data alongside female mitochondrial data.

Then the picture will become clear.

---

## A Parallel with Linguistics

In linguistics, the same problem exists: the fewer preserved texts, the less reliable the language reconstruction.

The Sumerian language isolate — we have tens of thousands of tablets. Kartvelian proto-language reconstruction — we have Georgian texts from the 5th century, before that — only reconstructions. Language genetics and human genetics suffer from the same disease: a shortage of early samples.

But the disease is treatable. New excavations. New aDNA extraction methods — technology improves every few years. Samples from which DNA could not be obtained before now allow it.

---

## Conclusion

The data of Tsintsadze and colleagues (2019) are a beginning, not an end. They indicate a direction. They show that the question is worth asking.

But science demands more. Thirteen samples from a medieval necropolis are not the foundation upon which a theory can be built. This is the first test pit before full-scale excavations.

Honest science acknowledges the limits of its data. And that is precisely why KHT is a theory, not a proven fact. A hypothesis awaiting verification, not a statement seeking confirmation.

The difference is fundamental.

---

*Next chapter: The "Khvamli" Plan — a multidisciplinary expedition project.*

---
# Chapter 55. The "Khvamli" Plan

> "A good expedition is not an adventure. It is the solution of a scientific problem with pre-defined criteria for success."

---

Assume that everything necessary is available: funding, permits, a team. What exactly needs to be done?

This is not a rhetorical question. Any good expedition must have a plan with specific, measurable tasks. Here it is.

---

## Phase 0: Archival Preparation

Before going into the field, it is necessary to exhaust all available sources.

**Fund No. 1738**, Central State Archive of Georgia. Full access to the materials of the 1948–1949 expedition: the order, field diaries, photographs, sketches, the final report. If some materials are classified — submit an official request for declassification.

**Archive of the Academy of Sciences of Georgia**. Internal correspondence regarding the expedition, possible duplicate documents, the personal file of Aliosha Japaridze.

**Museum in the village of Abari**. Japaridze's personal belongings, possible photographic materials, oral testimonies of local residents preserved in oral tradition.

The goal of this phase: to know the maximum possible before arriving on site. Not to rediscover what is already discovered; not to waste resources on what is already described.

---

## Phase 1: Documentation of Petroglyphs

This is the core of the expedition.

**Photogrammetric Survey**: all surfaces with petroglyphs are photographed with at least 70% overlap. Software processing creates 3D models with a resolution of 0.5 mm per pixel. Result: a complete digital archive, independent of the physical condition of the surface.

**RTI (Reflectance Transformation Imaging)**: a series of shots under different lighting angles. Allows for the identification of images invisible under standard lighting: fine engravings, partially overlapping drawings, layers from different periods.

**Drone LiDAR**: systematic scanning of all accessible and hard-to-reach surfaces. Vertical walls, overhanging cornices, cave vaults — everything a person cannot reach.

**Pigment Spectrometry**: non-invasive XRF analysis of dye composition. Allows for the classification of pigments by type and potentially by ochre source.

---

## Phase 2: Dating

After documentation — primary sample selection.

**Microscopic Examination of Pigment**: before dating, it is necessary to determine if the pigment contains organic impurities. If yes — AMS dating.

**Sample Selection for AMS**: minimal quantity, maximum informativeness. Priority — samples from different stylistic groups: presumably ancient "astral" images and presumably medieval red horsemen. This will allow for direct verification of stylistic dating.

**OSL Dating**: samples of the surface rock layer near the images. Particularly valuable are areas where the surface was demonstrably protected from light.

**U-Th Dating of Speleothems**: search for calcite crusts overlying images. If found — this is an absolute priority. Even one sample will provide a lower age boundary.

---

## Phase 3: Genetics and Biology

A parallel track — no less important.

**Extended Paleogenetics of the Gokhnari Necropolis**: from 13 to 130 samples. Chronological distribution: different periods within the 10th–14th centuries. Parallel analysis of mtDNA and Y-chromosome.

**Search for Earlier Burials**: around Gokhnari and in the wider Khvamli area — exploratory excavations in search of Bronze Age or Chalcolithic burials. Finding even a few samples from this period would be a breakthrough.

**Microbiology of the Tekenteli Cave**: sediment cores from the cave floor. Analysis of pollen, spores, organic remains. Reconstruction of climatic and biological history. Carbon dating of organic horizons.

---

## Phase 4: Geophysics

Things that change everything may be hidden beneath the surface.

**Ground-Penetrating Radar**: systematic scanning of the territory around the Gokhnari necropolis and near cave entrances. Search for anomalies — voids, density changes, possible burials or hoards.

**Geoelectrical Survey**: in combination with GPR — a more detailed picture of subsurface structures.

Goal: to find what has not been excavated. No artifact needs to be lifted during this phase — only to record the location.

---

## Team

A multidisciplinary expedition requires a multidisciplinary team.

Core: Georgian archaeologists (with a license to work in the protected zone), climbers (for inaccessible areas), a photogrammetry specialist, a LiDAR specialist.

Partners: an AMS dating laboratory (Oxford Radiocarbon Accelerator Unit or BETA Analytic), a paleogenetics laboratory (Max Planck Institute for Evolutionary Anthropology or equivalent).

Observers: representatives of the Georgian Ministry of Culture, UNESCO (for potential inclusion in the tentative list).

---

## Success Criteria

The expedition will be successful if it provides answers to three questions.

First: when were the petroglyphs created — or, at least, the earliest of them?

Second: are there burials in the Khvamli area older than the 10th century with analyzable aDNA?

Third: are there data in Fund No. 1738 that change what we know about the 1948–1949 expedition?

Three questions. Three answers. This is enough to either confirm the KHT as a research program or close it.

What is lacking is the will to begin.

---

*Next chapter: Legal Status.*

---

# Chapter 56. Legal Status

> "The law protects what society recognizes as valuable. The main battle is not in court, but in minds."

---

In 2020, the government of Georgia adopted Decree No. 123.

The document assigned the status of "monument of national significance" to Mount Khvamli and its immediate surroundings.
This is a step. But only the first.

---

## What the Status of "Monument of National Significance" Means

Georgian legislation on the protection of cultural heritage distinguishes three levels: monuments of world significance (UNESCO sites), monuments of national significance, and monuments of local significance.

The status of "national significance" means the following.

Conducting any earthworks, construction, or landscape alterations within the protected zone requires prior approval from the National Agency for Cultural Heritage Preservation of Georgia.

Items found on the territory of the monument are state property. Unauthorized removal of artifacts is a criminal offense.

The state is obligated to ensure the development of a protection zone project and the registration (passportization) of the monument.

This is legal protection. Real, with enforcement mechanisms.

---

## What the Status Does NOT Mean

The status of "national significance" is not funding for research.

Not the creation of accessible infrastructure.

Not public access to archival materials related to this monument.

Not international recognition.

Not an obligation to conduct comprehensive documentation.

In other words: the law says "do not touch without permission," but it does not say "research." The gap between protection and study — it is precisely in this space that Khvamli resides.

---

## What is Needed for an Expedition

The multidisciplinary expedition described in the previous chapter requires the following permits.

**Permit for scientific research work** — issued by the National Agency for Cultural Heritage Preservation of Georgia. A standard procedure for any work on monuments. Requires an expedition project specifying methods, scope, and a list of participating organizations.

**Permit for sample collection** — a separate item. Removing material from the surface of a petroglyph — even a microscopic scraping — requires a special permit. This is correct: uncontrolled sampling destroys monuments. The procedure for issuing such a permit exists.

**Permit for drone flights** within the protected zone — a separate issue, also involving the Civil Aviation Agency of Georgia.

**Access to the caves**: The Tekenteli and Boga caves are part of the protected zone. For a scientific expedition, access is arranged through the same National Agency.

All these permits exist. The procedures for obtaining them are standard. To do this, there is no need to change laws, adopt new acts, or lobby parliament. Only initiative is needed.

---

## The Path to UNESCO

Gobustan is a UNESCO World Heritage Site since 2007.

Khvamli is not — yet. But the path to this status is open.

Inclusion on the UNESCO list begins with inclusion on the national Tentative List. Georgia submits and updates such lists. Khvamli is absent from the current Tentative List.

Inclusion on the Tentative List requires nomination documentation: a history of study, description of Outstanding Universal Value (OUV), justification of compliance with criteria.

A multidisciplinary expedition with verified dating results would be precisely the documentary base needed to prepare this nomination.

Without an expedition — no data. Without data — no nomination. Without nomination — no UNESCO. Without UNESCO — no resources for large-scale research.

A vicious circle that must be broken at one point: start the expedition.

---

## The Legal Paradox of the Archive

The status of "national significance" applies to the physical object — the mountain and its surroundings.

Archival materials about the object — Fund No. 1738 — exist in a separate legal sphere. Archival legislation regulates access to them separately from heritage protection legislation.

This means: even with full compliance with all Khvamli protection norms, the archive of the 1948–1949 expedition may remain partially classified — under different legislation.

Synchronizing these two legal tracks — protecting the object and opening access to its documentation — requires separate efforts. This is the subject of the next chapter.

---

*Next chapter: The Battle for the Archive.*

---

# Chapter 57. The Battle for the Archive

> "A secret that no one knows eventually ceases to be a secret. It simply ceases to exist. But for that to happen, someone must start asking questions."

---

Fund No. 1738 exists in the Central State Archive of Georgia. This is a fact confirmed by the archival inventory. It contains documents related to the 1948–1949 expedition to Khvamli. The order of October 4, 1948, which we managed to study, is just one of them.

What do we know for certain? The fund's composition is partially closed. The status of a number of files is marked with the stamp "Restricted Access" or refers to departmental instructions from the Soviet period.

What we do not know — and have no right to assume — is the specific content of these closed folders. Any extrapolation about what "certainly" lies inside is speculation. Our task is not to guess, but to gain access and establish the facts.

Why is access restricted? The story of this archive is not a detective story about sensational content, but a classic story about bureaucratic legacy, legal uncertainty, and a silence that has lasted longer than the secrecy itself was relevant.

---

## Historical Context: Why Things Were Classified

To understand the situation with Fund No. 1738, it is necessary to abandon a modern perspective and immerse oneself in the logic of the late 1940s. The classification of expedition materials in the USSR was rarely related solely to scientific sensationalism. More often, it obeyed a departmental game, mixing several factors:

1.  **Departmental affiliation.** The expedition could have been organized or supervised by structures initially related to "regime" objects: the Academy of Sciences (which worked for defense), the geographical society (connected to cartography), or local party organs. The mere fact that documents passed through such instances could automatically impose a classification stamp.
2.  **Geopolitical paranoia.** Any research in border or strategically important mountainous areas (and Khvamli certainly belonged to such) easily fell under suspicion. Even a report on petroglyphs could be classified "just in case," as potentially containing data on terrain, approaches, caves.
3.  **Economic hypothesis.** There was a practice of classifying reports if they mentioned signs of mineral resources. A geologist or archaeologist might casually note "the presence of rocks characteristic of..." and that was enough to remove the document from open circulation.
4.  **Bureaucratic inertia.** Applying a classification stamp was often a routine procedure. Removing it was not. After the expedition closed and its staff disbanded, no one took responsibility for reviewing the documents' status. They were simply sent to the archive with the existing markings.

Thus, the "restricted access" status of the 1948–1949 materials is highly likely an artifact of this system, not an indicator of their explosive content today.

---

## Legal Basis and the Bureaucratic Labyrinth

The Law of Georgia "On the National Archival Fund" declares the principle of openness. Access to Soviet archival materials transferred to Georgia after 1991 should be regulated by national legislation, not outdated Soviet instructions.
However, between declaration and practice lies a chasm filled with legal uncertainty. Which specific Soviet-era classifications are considered automatically void? Who, and on what basis, should declassify them? The law provides a general framework but does not prescribe a clear procedure for the "archival lustration" of millions of files.

Our path to Fond No. 1738 began not with speculation about its contents, but with a series of official inquiries and the responses received, which paint a picture of the contemporary impasse.

*   **Inquiry No. 1 (Individual Historian):** Submitted in 2021. **Response:** "The requested documents contain information, access to which is restricted in accordance with the legislation of the Georgian SSR and the current procedures of the Archive." Citing the legislation of a non-existent state is a striking example of bureaucratic absurdity.
*   **Inquiry No. 2 (On Behalf of a University Department):** Submitted in 2022, requesting clarification of the legal grounds for the restriction. **Response:** The matter has been referred to the interdepartmental expert commission on declassification for consideration. The review is ongoing "in the order of the general queue."
*   **Inquiry No. 3 (Request for the Fond's Inventory):** A parallel request was made for the provision of the complete, uncensored inventory of Fond No. 1738. This is a key point: even if we cannot see the documents, we must know *how many there are and what they are titled*. **Response:** The same open inventory was provided, where a dash or the note "see p. 2" appears against a number of entries—a page missing from the provided copy.

These responses clearly delineate the problem: there is no malicious intent, only a bureaucratic quagmire. The documents are not guarded as state secrets; they are simply buried under a layer of procedural inertia.

---

## Precedents and Arguments for Declassification

The situation is not hopeless. In post-Soviet Georgia, there are successful precedents for declassification that can be relied upon:

*   **Archives of the MGB of the Georgian SSR.** Part of these documents, concerning political repressions, was opened to researchers and victims of the regime. If materials about people's fates can be declassified, then materials about the archaeological survey of a mountain—all the more so.
*   **Declassification of Scientific Fonds.** In the 2000s, many reports from Soviet geological and botanical expeditions, classified for formal or economic reasons, were declassified and made accessible.

Based on this, we are forming not speculative conjectures, but concrete, legally and ethically impeccable arguments for a substantiated request:

1.  **Historical-Scientific Value.** The materials are of interest exclusively for the history of science and cultural studies. Their closed status impedes the study of an important stage in the development of Georgian archaeology.
2.  **Cultural Heritage.** Any documentation of monuments from 1949 possesses enduring value as a historical snapshot of their condition. The loss of this data is a loss of part of the national cultural capital.
3.  **Absence of a Contemporary Threat.** Information from 75 years ago about petroglyphs and caves cannot constitute a state, economic, or ecological secret for sovereign 21st-century Georgia.
4.  **Principle of Open Science.** Declassification would align with international standards of scientific ethics and enhance the academic authority of Georgian institutions.

---

## Tactics and a Concrete Plan of Action

The detective intrigue here shifts from the question "what is inside?" to the question "how to open it?". Our plan consists of sequential, realistic steps.

**Step 1: Establishing the Exact Composition of the Fond.**
Goal: To obtain the complete, official inventory of Fond No. 1738, clearly listing the titles of all files, their dates, physical volume, and the current legal status of each. Without this, any appeal will be vague.
Action: Submitting an official inquiry, appealing to the researcher's right to know the structure of a fond, even if access to its content is temporarily restricted. If necessary—appealing to the ombudsman or the courts to protect this right to information.

**Step 2: Preparing an Institutional Request for Declassification.**
Goal: To initiate the formal procedure for reviewing the status of specific files.
Action: Forming a consortium of authoritative organizations to submit a joint, weighty request. The ideal configuration: the National Academy of Sciences of Georgia, the Institute of History and Ethnology, and one of the international partner universities. The request must be extremely specific: not "declassify the fond," but "consider the issue of removing restrictions from files No.... in connection with...."

**Step 3: Parallel Search.**
Goal: To find possible duplicates or related materials outside the main closed fond.
Action: A systematic search in the personal fonds of expedition members (Japaridze, others), in the archives of the organizing institutions (Tbilisi State University, the Museum of the History of Georgia), in the regional archives of Racha. Often, copies of reports were kept in "non-secret" institutional archives.

**Step 4: Academic Mediatization.**
Goal: To create legitimate public interest around the fate of the fond, not around its hypothetical contents.
Action: Publishing a scholarly article or an open letter in a peer-reviewed journal, presenting not a sensation but a case study: the history of the expedition, the fact of the existence of closed materials, a description of the attempts made to access them, and a justification of their scientific importance. This shifts the issue from the bureaucratic plane to the plane of academic reputation.

---

## What Needs to Be Done (Plan Summary)

1.  **Document the Refusals.** Gather a complete set of official responses from the archive—this is the foundation for further action.
2.  **Obtain the Complete Inventory.** Through inquiries, appeals, or the courts, obtain an exhaustive list of files in Fond No. 1738.
3.  **Form an Alliance.** Combine the efforts of Georgian and international scientific institutions to prepare a substantial joint request.
4.  **Focus on Procedure.** Demand not to "reveal the secret," but to conduct, in accordance with established legal procedure, an expert review of the justification for maintaining the classification on specific documents.
5.  **Seek Alternative Paths.** Conduct archival searches in related, open fonds.

An archive is not a treasure chest guarded by a sentinel with a sword. It is an abandoned room, the door to which has jammed from time and neglect. Our task is not to break down the door shouting "there's gold inside!", but to methodically, using laws and procedures, find the very lever that will lift the rusty latch.

We do not know what lies inside. But we know for certain that the time has come to find out.

---
*Next Chapter: Related Fonds and Parallel Search: Where Else Traces of the Expedition Might Be Stored.*
---

# Chapter 58. What Could Be in the Special Repository

> "Documents do not lie. They are silent. But when the silence is broken—a whole world is heard."

---

This is a chapter of assumptions. Informed assumptions, based on what we know about Soviet scientific expeditions of the 1940s, about documentation practices, about the personality of Aliosha Japaridze.

What exactly could have been in the final report submitted to Stalin?

---

## What Soviet Expeditions of the 1940s Documented

Soviet academic practice required strict accountability from field expeditions.

The standard package of documents included: a field diary (the leader's daily notes), a technical report (description of methods, routes, work conducted), a scientific report (interpretation of findings), photographic materials, sketches and tracings, maps and diagrams, an inventory of artifacts and samples.

For a speleological expedition, the following were added: survey diagrams of caves (topographic surveys), description of hydrology, description of biological objects (if any).

The expedition to Khvamli under Japaridze's leadership had an explicit order to pay "special attention to ancient drawings." Consequently, the report must have had a separate section on petroglyphs.

---

## Hypothesis One: Tracings of Petroglyphs

In 1949, there was no digital photography. There was film photography—expensive, limited in the number of shots, requiring laboratory processing under conditions unavailable in the mountains.

Soviet field expeditions often documented petroglyphs by tracing: a sheet of tracing paper was placed on the rock surface, and the researcher carefully transferred the contours of the images. This is technically straightforward and yields an accurate, scaled drawing.

Parallel hand-drawn sketches were made—for interpretation and description.

If Japaridze conducted such documentation—the archive holds tracings from 1949. Tracings of petroglyphs in the condition they existed 75 years ago.

This is invaluable material. Especially if over 75 years the surfaces have faded, been damaged, or changed due to environmental factors.

---

## Hypothesis Two: Photographs

Photographic materials from Soviet expeditions have partially survived in archives. The famous Khvalynsk expedition, materials from the Turkestan expeditions, the photo chronicle of Siberian exploration—much has been preserved and entered scholarly circulation after declassification.

Photographs of Khvamli from 1949—if they exist—could show:
- General views of the mountain from the approaches (routes used by the expedition)
- Cave entrances
- Interiors of cave systems
- The petroglyphs themselves under 1949 conditions

Even if the shots are technically imperfect by 21st-century standards—they contain historically valuable information: the state of the object before 75 additional years of temporal impact.

---

## Hypothesis Three: Description of the "Mountain's Books"
This is the most intriguing and most speculative hypothesis.

Local folklore persistently speaks of "books" hidden inside the mountain. This expression could be a metaphor for petroglyphs. But it could also be something else.

Soviet expeditions frequently documented artifact finds in caves—pottery, metal objects, bones. If handwritten materials or artifacts with inscriptions were discovered in the Khvamli caves, this would have required special documentation.

Medieval Georgian monasteries practiced hiding libraries in inaccessible places during the threat of invasion. If Khvamli was used as a refuge for valuables—perhaps parchment manuscripts were hidden in its caves. Good preservation at a constant temperature of around 10 degrees Celsius—ideal conditions for organic materials.

This is a hypothesis, not a statement. But if Japaridze's report contains a description of such a find—this explains why it was classified so thoroughly.

---

## Hypothesis Four: Maps

Soviet speleological expeditions compiled detailed topographic maps of cave systems.

Maps of the Khvamli caves—if they are in the archive—are a tool for the next expedition. Instead of re-mapping the systems from scratch, one could take the ready-made 1949 base and refine it with modern methods.

Soviet field maps were made with high precision: the engineering training of expedition members was professional.

---

## What This Changes

Even one of the components described above—tracings, photographs, maps—would make the materials of Fund No. 1738 scientifically significant.

All four together would turn them into a foundational document for all subsequent research on Khvamli.

We do not know what exactly is there. But we know: there is something there. Otherwise, there wouldn't be such meticulous storage.

Secrets are not kept in empty boxes. Secrets are kept in what is important.

---

*Next chapter: The Legend of the "Books of Ubar".*

---

# Chapter 59. The Legend of the "Books of Ubar"

> "Folk memory does not preserve the invented. It preserves the misunderstood."

---

In Georgian folklore, Khvamli holds books.

This is not the only motif. The mountain holds Amiran. The mountain holds regalia. The mountain holds treasures. But "books"—is a special motif that appears in several independent versions.

What are these books? And what does this word actually mean?

---

## The Origin of the Motif

Field records of the "Green Alternative" organization (2011 expedition) documented several testimonies from local residents about the Soviet period on Khvamli. One testimony includes the phrase: "they went up to take the books from the mountain."

This is not a direct reference to Japaridze's expedition. It is an oral transmission of a rumor or observation, passed through several generations.

But what is interesting is this: the folkloric motif of "books in the mountain" is not isolated. It is present in the broader Georgian tradition.

---

## The Sacred Library in Georgian Narratives

Georgian literary tradition has preserved several cases of the actual concealment of manuscripts.

The most famous is the Khelta library. According to legend, when Persians or Mongols threatened monasteries, monks would wall up manuscripts in walls or hide them in mountain caves. Some of these repositories were discovered in the 20th century; some, according to researchers, remain unfound to this day.

A real precedent exists: in 1940, during the restoration of a church in Shiomgvime, a cache with manuscripts from the 11th–12th centuries was discovered, which no one knew about. They lay there for several centuries in satisfactory condition.

The mountain as a repository—is not a metaphor in Georgian tradition. It is a literal practice.

---

## Ubar: A Parallel from the Arab World

The comparison with "Ubar" in this chapter's title is an analogy, not a direct connection.

Ubar—a legendary Arabian city mentioned in the Quran as "Iram of the Pillars," hidden beneath the sands. In the 1990s, its presumed location was discovered via satellite imagery in Oman. Excavations confirmed: behind the legend lies a real fortified site.

"Books of Ubar" in the context of this chapter—is a metaphor for the folkloric narrative about hidden treasures of knowledge, behind which a real object may stand.

The Georgian legend of the "mountain's books"—is the same kind of narrative. It requires not a literal reading, but an investigative one: what exactly in the real history of Khvamli gave rise to this motif?

---

## Three Possible Explanations

**First**: "books" are petroglyphs. For an illiterate bearer of tradition, rock drawings are the "writing of the mountain." Incomprehensible writing made by unknown people in times immemorial. The word "book" could be a metaphor for "inscriptions."

This explanation is the most economical. Occam's razor favors it.

**Second**: "books" are actual manuscripts, concealed in the caves during one of the many invasions of Western Georgia. Colchis, Egrisi, the Abkhazian Kingdom, Imereti—all these states experienced periods when preserving manuscripts required their physical concealment.

This explanation is realistic and has precedents. The conditions of the Khvamli caves (constant temperature, protection from direct light) are suitable for the long-term storage of parchment manuscripts.

**Third**: "books" are a metaphor for knowledge in general. In the folkloric consciousness, Khvamli holds that which must not be lost. This could be knowledge of blacksmithing, sacred formulas, astral observations—everything that in oral tradition is "held by the mountain." The materialization of this immaterial knowledge—in the image of a book.

---

## Why This Matters for KHT

The Khvamli Hypothesis of Transfer (KHT), at its core, is a theory about the transfer of knowledge.

The knowledge that is transferred—from Khvamli through Colchis to Near Eastern civilizations—is not merely information. It is an understanding of the world encoded in symbols, myths, and rituals.

The "mountain's books" in folklore—are the memory that the mountain held something that could be "read." Petroglyphs that the initiated "read." Ritual texts that priests "read." Astral observations that astronomer-priests "read" in the night sky above the summit.

This is one of the most persistent pieces of folkloric evidence that Khvamli functioned as a place of knowledge, not just a place of power.

---

## Metaphor and Reality

The boundary between metaphor and reality in folk memory is thin.

When peasants spoke of "books in the mountain," they could have meant petroglyphs they saw with their own eyes. They could have been retelling a story about manuscripts they heard from their ancestors. They could have used the word "book" for something that had no name in their language.

The researcher's task is not to reject the folkloric narrative as a "legend" nor to accept it literally as "historical evidence." The task is to understand what exactly in people's real experience gave rise to this image.
The answer to this question can only be found on an expedition.

---

*End of Part VI. Next part: Conclusion.*

---

# Chapter 60. Synthesis of the Four Lines

> "Four rivers flow from a single source. This is not a coincidence—it is geography."

---

The investigation has followed a sequential path: from the geology of the double cuesta to the petroglyphs, from the petroglyphs to the Soviet archive, from the archive to linguistics, and then to astral theology, metallurgy, mythology, and genetics.

The present moment requires a comprehensive review of the collected data.

The four lines of inquiry represent different methodological approaches to a single question. Their independent convergence on one geographical point deserves special attention.

---

## First Line: Ancient Texts

Strabo indicated the location of Colchis. Apollonius of Rhodes described the route of the Argonauts. Vakhushti Bagrationi in the 18th century recorded the toponym "Star of Khomli," linking the astral image with the mountain.

These texts, separated by centuries and composed in different languages, demonstrate a persistent connection. They indicate that the area of Mount Khvamli was perceived as a significant place over a long historical period.

Ancient and later sources do not directly confirm the hypothesis, but they form a logical question: why does this specific location consistently appear in such heterogeneous evidence?

---

## Second Line: The Soviet Archive

October 1948. An order was issued mandating "special attention to ancient drawings" and requiring personal reports to I.V. Stalin.

The scale of state attention is indicative. The organization of an expedition to a hard-to-reach area and the level of reporting indicate that the object of study—Mount Khvamli—was considered to be of exceptional importance. This, in turn, suggests that the Soviet leadership relied on data that remains outside contemporary scholarly circulation.

The Soviet archive does not serve as direct proof of the hypothesis, but it points to the existence of a sustained interest in this place at the highest administrative level in the last century.

---

## Third Line: Parallels with Civilizations

A series of conceptual parallels is observed: the Sumerian goddess of the morning star on a mountain; the Caucasian goddess of the hunt in a golden cave on a summit; the Egyptian symbolism of the Primeval Mound; the floods of the Rioni River in the Neolithic as a possible geographical context.

The architectural form of the ziggurat is interpreted as a "longing for the mountain," and Khvamli can be considered its natural prototype. The motif of "hunters at the pillar" in the petroglyphs potentially correlates with the Mesopotamian concept of *dur-an-ki*.

Each individual comparison could be explained by coincidence or cultural convergence. However, the totality of these parallels forms a consistent pattern requiring a systemic explanation.

---

## Fourth Line: Material Heritage

Material evidence includes: the Gokhnari necropolis; traces of an ancient Caucasian metallurgical tradition dating back to the 5th millennium BCE; the 2024 Bashplem tablet containing 60 signs, some of which show graphic parallels with Phoenician, Proto-Sinaitic, and Proto-Kartvelian scripts.

Material culture provides objective data. This data testifies to the presence in the region in deep antiquity of a complex society possessing technological and, possibly, proto-literate skills.

---

## The Point of Intersection

Four independent lines of inquiry converge on a single geographical point: Mount Khvamli (1987 m), Western Georgia, Imereti Range.

This location focuses:
- Data from astral toponymy ("Star of Khomli").
- Mythological narratives (the place of Amiran's chaining).
- Traces of classified state interest in the 20th century.
- A complex of petroglyphs requiring precise dating.
- A center of ancient Caucasian metallurgy.
- Genetic data indicating connections within a West Eurasian network.

The coincidence of four independent research trajectories at one point goes beyond chance and indicates the promise of further study.

---

## Propositions of the Hypothesis

The Khvamli Hypothesis of Transfer (KHT) is not a theory of ethnic or civilizational primacy. It does not claim that Georgian culture became the basis for Sumerian culture.

Its main proposition is as follows: Khvamli likely functioned as one of the nodes in a network of cultural and ideational connections that spanned the Near East, the Caucasus, and the Eastern Mediterranean during the Neolithic and Bronze Age. Mythological images, astronomical knowledge, and technological innovations circulated through this network. In this model, the Caucasus acts as an active participant in the exchange, not a peripheral recipient.

This formulation is more restrained than some public interpretations, which aligns with the principles of scientific rigor.

---

## Directions for Verification

To test the hypothesis, three specific steps are necessary:

1. Conduct precise dating of the Khvamli petroglyphs using modern physico-chemical methods.
2. Ensure full access to and study of the materials from Foundation No. 1738.
3. Implement an expanded program of paleogenetic research involving early archaeological samples from the region.

If the petroglyphs are dated to the Neolithic or Bronze Age, the hypothesis will gain a material foundation. If the archival materials contain reports from the 1949 expedition, primary field data will be revealed. If paleogenetics confirms the existence of deep historical connections with Near Eastern populations, a biodemographic context will be established.

None of these steps require breakthrough methodologies—only decisions to implement them.

---

*Next chapter: Rethinking the Role of the Caucasus.*
---

# Chapter 61. Rethinking the Role of the Caucasus

> "The periphery is not a geographical concept. It is a mental one. Changing it must begin with the maps in our minds."

---

The Caucasus has long been viewed as a corridor—a territory through which peoples passed from Asia to Europe and back, a buffer zone between major civilizations, and a mountain barrier dividing North and South.

This paradigm is not merely outdated. It appears to have been erroneous from the very beginning.

---

## The Origins of the "Periphery" Paradigm

The European historical tradition, formed in the 19th century, focused on centers such as Egypt, Greece, Rome, and Mesopotamia. The selection of these regions was dictated by the availability of deciphered written sources and the scale of preserved material monuments.

The Caucasus did not fit this model for several reasons.
The Georgian written sources that have reached us begin in the 5th century AD. This is a late date compared to the monuments of the Ancient East. However, this indicates only the preservation of specific texts, not the absence of a written tradition in earlier periods.

The monumental architecture of the Caucasus — towers, fortresses, churches — mostly dates to the Middle Ages. Neolithic and Bronze Age structures are less massive than pyramids or ziggurats, but their modest scale is not a marker of cultural significance.

Systematic international archaeological research in the Caucasus has been conducted on a smaller scale than in the regions of the Near East or the Mediterranean. Consequently, our knowledge of this region is limited not by a lack of material, but by a lack of its study.

---

## Contemporary Data

Contemporary research points to a different role for the Caucasus.

**Metallurgy.** Copper metallurgy in the Caucasus dates to the 5th millennium BC, earlier than in most regions of the Near East. The Satskhenisi mining zone in Georgia is one of the oldest known centers of copper extraction in the world.

**Winemaking.** The earliest evidence of grape domestication and winemaking comes from the territory of Georgia (6th–5th millennium BC). The *kvevri* technology — the making and storage of wine in clay vessels buried in the ground — was widespread here long before the appearance of similar practices in the Near East.

**Culture.** Polyphonic singing, whose tradition dates back to deep antiquity and is recognized by UNESCO as intangible heritage, represents a unique phenomenon on a world scale, formed in the Caucasus.

---

## Generators, Not Corridors

Research in the field of population genetics has significantly adjusted the understanding of the role of Caucasian hunter-gatherers (CHG).

The work of Wang et al. (2019) in the journal *Nature Communications* points to a significant genetic contribution of Caucasian hunter-gatherers to steppe populations, which in the 3rd millennium BC spread across Europe and Central Asia. CHG act not as passive recipients, but as one of the sources of migration flows.

The study by Lazaridis et al. (2016) in *Nature* records the presence of a Caucasian genetic component in the founders of European Neolithic culture.

Thus, genetic data allow us to consider the Caucasus as an active node in Eurasian history, not as a periphery.

---

## Metallurgical Center

A substantial argument is that the Caucasus was an independent center for the development of metallurgy.

In the 3rd millennium BC, the bronze metallurgy of the Colchian and Karan-Mil cultures produced items distributed throughout the Near East. Lead isotope analysis of a number of Near Eastern bronze artifacts points to a Caucasian origin of the ore.

Blacksmithing in the Caucasus had a sacral character. The blacksmith in Georgian tradition is not merely a craftsman, but a mediator between worlds. This likely explains the mythological image of Amiran-Prometheus as a blacksmith.

The knowledge spreading from this region was primarily metallurgical — practical, economically significant, and deeply embedded in the cultural context.

---

## From Periphery to Node

The rethinking of the Caucasus's role is not an attempt to attribute non-existent merits to it. It is about restoring scientific objectivity regarding a region that has been studied unevenly.

If the Khvamli petroglyphs date to the Neolithic, this should be considered as data about a place that was part of an extensive cultural space.

If Archive Fund No. 1738 contains documents capable of changing the historical picture, they must be introduced into scholarly circulation. This requirement is dictated not by political considerations, but by the necessity of achieving data completeness.

The Caucasus awaits not a re-evaluation, but an unbiased analysis.

---

*Next chapter: Not Just a Mountain.*
---

# Chapter 62. Not Just a Mountain

> "A place becomes sacred not because the gods chose it. But because people could not pass it by."

---

Khvamli is a mountain massif.

It is a limestone massif with an elevation of 1987 meters above sea level, representing a double cuesta with vertical cliffs. Its characteristics include developed cave systems, petroglyphs, and a church at the summit, which is reflected in local legends.

However, if Khvamli possessed only these physical-geographical features, it would hardly have become the subject of a separate study.

---

## Refuge

The primary and most obvious function of Khvamli is defensive.

The vertical rock walls, making the ascent to the mountain extremely difficult without special preparation, represent not only a symbolic barrier. They form an effective natural fortification system.

Historical sources attest that in the 14th century, during the invasion of Timur's army into Western Georgia, the local population found salvation on the slopes of Khvamli. This forced retreat, associated with the loss of property and lands, ensured the physical survival of communities.

Mentions of storing royal regalia in the caves of Khvamli also relate to this function. Such symbols of power as the "diamond chain" or the "unseen pearl" were placed in hard-to-reach locations for the purpose of their preservation during periods of external threat.

Thus, the refuge function is likely the most ancient. The first evidence of human activity on the mountain may be connected to the search for safety.

---

## Treasury

The second function logically follows from the first: Khvamli as a place for preserving valuables.

A location perceived as safe naturally begins to accumulate various forms of capital. This includes material values (regalia, treasures), cultural ones (symbols, rituals, knowledge), and symbolic ones (memory, collective identity, connection with the past).

The caves of Khvamli in the role of a repository are not only a cultural metaphor but also a practical solution. The stable microclimate of the caves, protection from external influences, and difficulty of access created optimal conditions for the long-term preservation of objects.

In this aspect, Khvamli can be considered alongside other monuments that performed the function of a sacred treasury: Umm el-Qa'ab in Egypt, Gobustan in Azerbaijan, Altamira in Spain. They are united not by similarity of details, but by a common functional program: placing significant artifacts in hard-to-reach places.

---

## Ritual Center

The third function is sacral: Khvamli as a place for performing rituals.

Ritual practice requires a specially marked space, separated from the everyday. The inaccessibility of Khvamli itself creates such a separation: the path to it requires effort, which contributes to a change in the psychophysiological state of the participants.

The petroglyphs on the mountain slopes are part of this ritual communication. They can be interpreted not simply as artistic expression, but as a message — either to otherworldly forces associated with the place, or to future generations, recording the significance of what transpired here.

The annual ritual of blacksmiths, symbolically "restoring" the links of the chain of the mythical hero Amiran with a strike on the anvil, served not the literal recreation of an object, but the actualization of the connection between the community, the sacred topography of the mountain, and the foundational myths shaping identity.

---

## Knowledge Transmitter

The fourth function is the most complex and significant for this research: Khvamli as a node in a network of knowledge transmission.

This refers not to instantaneous transmission, but to a centuries-long process during which people who came into contact with this place spread the images, technologies, and narratives associated with it.

Metallurgists who mastered the craft in the Caucasian region migrated to Anatolia and the Near East, spreading technological skills and the accompanying cultural codes.
The trade routes through which Caucasian metal moved across the Black Sea region and the Levant simultaneously served as channels for the transmission of stories—about the sacred mountain, the goddess, the chained hero, and hidden treasures.

Priests or keepers of knowledge, who possessed, for example, astronomical observations linked to landmarks on Khvamli (the risings of Venus, certain stars), could share this esoteric knowledge within the framework of corresponding traditions.

Transmission occurred through human mobility. Data from genetic studies, archaeology, and comparative narrative analysis point to active population movements from the Caucasus in antiquity.

---

## Sacred Topography as a Recurring Structure

Various cultural traditions exhibit a repetition of a specific spatial model.

It can be described as a sequence: Mountain → Cave → Tower → River.

In the Georgian context, this is: Khvamli (mountain) → the Tekenteli cave (cave) → Svan residential towers (vertical architectural dominant) → the Rioni River (water source).

In the ancient Egyptian context: pyramid (artificial mountain) → burial chamber (cave) → obelisk (tower) → the Nile (river).

In the Sumero-Akkadian context: ziggurat (mountain) → sanctuary at the summit (cave) → cultic pillar (tower) → the Euphrates (river).

This parallelism does not serve as proof of direct historical or cultural contacts. Rather, it testifies to the existence of a universal or archetypal matrix for organizing sacred space, built upon oppositions: high/low (deep), light/dark, static (vertical)/dynamic (fluid).

Khvamli represents a natural realization of this matrix, while the ziggurat or pyramid is its man-made architectural embodiment.

---

## Khvamli as a Typological Case

Khvamli is a specific geographical and historical object. However, within the framework of this analysis, it also acquires the significance of a typological case.

It represents a type of place that in various cultures was chosen as a symbolic axis of the world, the center of a cosmological model.

Such places are numerous and found on all continents. Not all of them are historically connected to each other. However, all of them, it seems, realize a similar complex of functions: marking the point of connection between the celestial and the earthly, preserving the legacy of the past for the future, ensuring the transmission of knowledge through generations.

Khvamli is one such place. Therefore, its significance extends beyond a purely geographical or geological description.

---

*Next chapter: Myth and Reality.*
---

# Chapter 63. Myth and Reality

> "Myth is not a lie about the past. It is a truth told in a language we have forgotten how to understand."

---

What is hidden in the caves of Khvamli?

Throughout this book, we have examined petroglyphs, royal regalia, Soviet archives, the "books of the mountain." It is time to answer: what exactly is real, and what is myth?

And—what does this division mean?

---

## What is Definitely Real

Geological facts are real: the double cuesta, vertical cliffs, cave systems with constant temperature.

The petroglyphs are real: red images of horsemen, dated to the 12th–13th centuries, recorded by expeditions in 1939 and 1945.

The 14th-century chronicle mentioning royal regalia on Khvamli is real: *Kartlis Tskhovreba* is a verified historical source.

The order dated October 4, 1948, is real: Fund No. 1738, Central State Archive of Georgia.

The astral connotations of the mountain are real: Vakhushti Bagrationi in the 18th century recorded the connection of Khvamli with Venus.

It is real that the petroglyphs have not been dated by modern methods.

---

## What is Presumed

The existence of older, prehistoric petroglyphs, predating the medieval red images, is presumed.

The final report of Japaridze's expedition is presumed to contain documentation of these petroglyphs.

A connection between Khvamli symbols and Sumerian, Canaanite, or Egyptian analogues is presumed.

The functioning of Khvamli as an astronomical observatory in prehistoric times is presumed.

---

## What is Myth

Myth is that which has no verifiable basis but carries an important function.

Amiran is chained in the cave of Khvamli and breaks his chains every New Year. This is a myth. A real Amiran did not exist. Real chains do not break.

But this myth performs a real function: it encodes humanity's relationship with metal, fire, knowledge. It preserves the memory that to be a blacksmith is to possess a dangerous and sacred skill.

The "books of the mountain" are a myth in the sense that literal books most likely do not lie in the caves of Khvamli. But this myth encodes a reality: the mountain preserved something that people perceived as knowledge. The petroglyphs *are* the "books," written in a language we have forgotten how to read.

---

## The Boundary Between Myth and Reality is Fluid

Troy was considered a myth—until Schliemann found its ruins.

Ubar was considered a myth—until satellite images revealed its outlines beneath the Arabian sands.

Jericho was considered a legend—until excavations confirmed the existence of a city destroyed in the middle of the Bronze Age.

Each time, history repeats itself in the same way: what seemed like a myth, upon closer examination, turns out to be a distant memory of a real event. The memory is deformed by time, metaphorized, mixed with later layers—but at its core, it is real.

The data indicate that Khvamli is no exception to this rule.

---

## Deciphering the Metaphor of the "Books"

The "books of the mountain" are the petroglyphs.

This explanation is simple but not simplistic. In the prehistoric and early historical consciousness, rock images were perceived as writing in the literal sense: messages left by ancestors, gods, spirits. In the Georgian language, the very word *nishani*—sign, mark—simultaneously means both a symbol and a landmark and a trace.

A person seeing rows of images in the cave of Khvamli—human figures by a pillar, astral signs, zoomorphic forms—perceived this as writing. The writing of those who were here before him.

The "books of the mountain" are reality, told in the language of metaphor.

---
## What Needs to Be Deciphered

The solution is not to find literal books.

The solution is to read the "books" that already exist: petroglyphs, dated and systematically described; archival documents, declassified and studied; genetic data, obtained from a sufficient number of samples; folklore, interpreted not literally, but as a code.

When these four "books" are read—we will understand what the mountain has kept.

And then myth and reality will cease to oppose each other. They will merge into what is called history.

---

*Next chapter: A Call to the Scientific Community.*
---

# Chapter 64. A Call to the Scientific Community

> "Science does not move by itself. It is moved by people who have decided to ask the next question."

---

This chapter is not a preface to a conclusion. It is a direct address.

To archaeologists. To geneticists. To linguists. To astronomers. To archivists. To those who study the history of the Near East, the Caucasus, Egypt, Mesopotamia.

Khvamli is your problem. Not just a Georgian one. A global one.

---

## Why This Is Not Just a Georgian Problem

Gobustan is in Azerbaijan. But its petroglyphs have been studied by international expeditions. It was dated at the Oxford laboratory. It became a UNESCO site.

Altamira is in Spain. But its discovery changed the understanding of prehistoric art worldwide.

Khvamli is in Georgia. But if its petroglyphs date to the 6th–4th millennium BCE—this is data on prehistoric Caucasus that is significant for understanding all of Eurasian history.

There is no "Georgian version" of Caucasian history. There is the history of the Caucasus, which is either studied or not.

---

## What Is Specifically Needed

Not funding out of thin air. Concrete partnerships.

**Dating Laboratories**. Oxford Radiocarbon Accelerator Unit, BETA Analytic, CEA Saclay—any of these laboratories process samples from Caucasian sites. A partner in a Georgian scientific institution is needed to initiate the process.

**Paleogenetics Institutes**. Max Planck Institute for Evolutionary Anthropology—a leader in aDNA research on the Eurasian Caucasus. In 2022–2024, they published works on Caucasian genetics. The Khvamli region is not included. This is a lacuna that can be filled.

**Specialists in Rock Art**. International Federation of Rock Art Organizations (IFRAO) deals with the systematization and study of petroglyphs worldwide. Including Khvamli in their database is a technically simple task.

**Archivists and Historians of the Soviet Period**. People specializing in the declassification of Soviet scientific archives know how to work with such material. This is a procedure, not a feat.

---

## An Address to Georgian Scientists

You are the closest to this site.

You have the legal framework: the 2020 decree. You have the institutions: the Institute of History and Ethnology, the Georgian State Museum, the National Archives. You have young researchers who know modern methods and want to work.

What is needed is a decision. The decision to place Khvamli at the center of the research agenda. Not as a tourist site, but as a scientific priority.

Georgia has demonstrated this ability with other sites. The Telavi necropolises, Dmanisi (the site of the oldest hominins outside Africa), Neolithic grape culture—all have undergone serious international research initiated by Georgian scientists.

Khvamli awaits the same initiative.

---

## An Address to International Organizations

UNESCO funds the preparation of nominations for inclusion in the World Heritage List. Preliminary inclusion on the Tentative List is the first step.

The European Research Council (ERC) funds precisely such projects: multidisciplinary, linking several countries, opening new directions.

Horizon Europe—the EU funding program—supports cultural heritage projects. A Khvamli multidisciplinary project fits directly into the thematic priorities: Culture, Creativity and Inclusive Society.

Grants exist. Application mechanisms exist. People are needed to submit them.

---

## What We Are Not Asking For

We are not asking to change history textbooks before obtaining data.

We are not asking to recognize KHT as a proven theory.

We are not asking to declare the Caucasus the cradle of all civilizations.

We ask for one thing: to give Khvamli the same level of scientific attention that Gobustan, Altamira, Göbekli Tepe received.

To research. To date. To open the archive. To publish the data.

After that, the data will speak for themselves.

---

*Next chapter: Ready Tools.*

---

# Chapter 65. Ready Tools

> "We have a map, a compass, and a road. All that remains is to set out."

---

Sometimes the problem is not the lack of tools. The problem is that no one has picked them up.

With Khvamli—this is precisely the case.

I will list what is already ready.

---

## Legal Framework — Ready

Decree of the Government of Georgia No. 123 (2020): Khvamli is a monument of national significance.
The Law of Georgia "On the National Archival Fund": the procedure for requesting access to archival materials is standard.

Georgia has signed the UNESCO Convention for the Protection of Cultural Heritage. The procedure for submitting to the Tentative List is described and open.

All of this is current legislation. Nothing needs to be changed.

---

## Scientific Methods — Ready

AMS dating: the technology is established, laboratories are operational. The cost of one analysis ranges from several hundred to several thousand dollars. For a preliminary study, 10–15 samples are sufficient.
