OSL dating: similarly. Laboratories exist in Europe, the USA, Australia. The sample collection procedure is standard.

Photogrammetry: software — Agisoft Metashape, license cost — from one thousand dollars. Equipment — a standard DSLR camera. A processing specialist is needed, but this is not a scarce profession.

LiDAR from a drone: equipment — commercially available. Several companies in Europe offer such services for archaeological sites.

Paleogenetics: aDNA technology is a standard in leading laboratories. The rate ranges from several hundred to several thousand dollars per sample, depending on the depth of analysis.

---

## Funding Sources — Exist

**The Ministry of Culture and Sport of Georgia** has programs for funding archaeological surveys at sites of national significance.

**Shota Rustaveli National Science Foundation of Georgia** — the national science foundation of Georgia — funds research projects, including in humanities and historical-cultural fields.

**Horizon Europe** (EU program): Cluster 2 "Culture, Creativity and Inclusive Society". Grants of up to several million euros for multidisciplinary cultural heritage projects.

**ERC Grants**: Advanced Grant or Synergy Grant — precisely for projects of this scale.

**National Geographic Society**: supports field research in archaeology and cultural heritage.

**Wenner-Gren Foundation**: one of the leading foundations for anthropological and archaeological research.

---

## Partnerships — Possible

Georgian scientists already collaborate with international institutions. The Dmanisi excavations — partnership with the Berlin Museum of Natural History. Paleogenetic research — partnership with the MPI for Evolutionary Anthropology.

The model exists. It needs to be applied to Khvamli.

Potential partners showing interest in the Caucasus region: University College London (Institute of Archaeology), University of Cambridge (McDonald Institute), Freie Universität Berlin (Vorderasiatisches Museum), Harvard University (Department of Near Eastern Languages).

Contacts with these institutions — these are letters, conferences, joint publications. Standard academic communication.

---

## What is Not Ready: Institutional Will

The only resource that is lacking is the decision of one person, or a small group of people, to say: "we are starting."

It has always been this way in the history of science. Göbekli Tepe lay under a Turkish hill until 1994 — until Klaus Schmidt started digging. Dmanisi — a Georgian medieval settlement — turned out to be a *Homo erectus* site in 1991, when Avraham Vekua decided to check the geological layers.

In both cases: the decision of one person. Then — an international consortium. Then — recognition.

Khvamli awaits its Schmidt. Its Vekua.

---

## Immediate Practical Steps

First: systematize everything known about Khvamli in academic literature, including Georgian-language sources. This is a bibliographic project, not requiring fieldwork.

Second: submit a request to the National Archives of Georgia for full access to Fund No. 1738.

Third: establish contact with one of the aDNA laboratories to discuss possible cooperation regarding the Gokhnari necropolis.

Fourth: prepare an application for a small grant for a pilot documentation expedition. Photogrammetry of the petroglyphs — one season, one specialist, minimal budget.

Fifth: write an article. For a peer-reviewed journal. Not a book — an article. It will be published quickly and create an academic foundation for everything that follows.

Five steps. Each is feasible. Together — the beginning.

---

*Next chapter: On the Threshold of Discovery.*

---

# Chapter 66. On the Threshold of Discovery

> "Discovery is not a moment. It is a decision made early enough."

---

There are things we lose while we wait.

This is not rhetoric. This is the concrete reality of material heritage.

---

## What Time Destroys

Petroglyphs are vulnerable.

Red ochre pigments fade under ultraviolet light. Humidity leaches organic components that would enable AMS dating. Lichens — biological crusts — cover surfaces and damage images.

According to international practice, rock art unprotected by documentation loses on average from 5 to 15 percent of its details over 50 years under moderate atmospheric exposure. In acidic environments or with intensive tourist visitation — faster.

Not everything that Japaridze saw in 1949 is visible now. This is a fact, not an assumption.

---

## What Obscurity Destroys

But there are losses more dangerous than physical destruction.

When an object is not studied — it is not fully protected. It has a protected status. But a protected status without precise inventory is a general framework without content.

If tomorrow someone accidentally destroys part of the cave system — we will not know what exactly was lost. Because there is no complete documentation.

If the archive of Fund No. 1738 is damaged by fire or flooding of the repository (and Soviet archives regularly suffer from such accidents) — we will lose data that we cannot restore.

Obscurity is not just ignorance. It is the absence of insurance.
## What Destroys Tourism

Khvamli is a popular tourist destination for experienced climbers and trekkers.

The flow of tourists to sacred sites causes wear and tear even with the most careful handling. Touches, CO2 from breathing, introduced biological agents—all of this works slowly but inexorably.

Altamira was opened to the public in the 1880s. By the 1970s, the cave's microclimate had changed to such an extent that green algae began destroying the cave paintings. In 2002, the cave was completely closed to visitors. A replica—an exact copy—was created for tourists.

Khvamli is not Altamira in terms of tourist flow. But the principle is the same: documentation must be done before the site begins to degrade, not after.

---

## What History Will Lose

If the Khvamli petroglyphs date to the Neolithic—and we do not learn about it—the history of the Caucasus as a cultural space will be written incompletely.

This is not a loss for Georgia. This is a loss for science.

The history of Eurasia in the Neolithic–Bronze Age period is one of the most active areas of modern research. The aDNA revolution, which began in the 2010s, has rewritten our picture of migrations, population admixture, and technology transfer. Khvamli data would become part of this picture.

The absence of this data is not a neutral absence. It is a blank spot on the map that distorts the understanding of neighboring regions.

---

## What Georgia Will Lose

If the petroglyphs date to the Neolithic, if the archive is opened, if the data is published—Khvamli will take its place among UNESCO World Heritage sites.

This is not just a symbolic status. It is resources for further research, international academic collaboration, a new level of tourist infrastructure, and the preservation of the site for future generations.

Delay is not the status quo. It is a slow loss.

---

## The Threshold

We stand at a threshold.

On one side—everything we know: geology, mythology, the Soviet archive, parallels with Near Eastern civilizations, preliminary genetic data.

On the other side—answers we do not yet have: precise dating, the contents of the archive, complete documentation.

A threshold is a place where a decision is made. To cross it or to remain.

Discoveries always begin with one decision: to cross.

---

*Next chapter: From Theory to Practice.*

---

# Chapter 67. From Theory to Practice

> "Theory without experiment is an architectural blueprint without construction. Beautiful. But it does not live."

---

The Khvamli Hypothesis of Transfer (KHT) exists in the form of a book. This is a good start. But the book is not the end.

A theory is a research program. Its value lies not in itself, but in the specific, testable predictions it yields.

What exactly does the KHT predict—and how can it be tested?

---

## Prediction One: Dating of the Petroglyphs

The KHT predicts: if AMS, OSL, or U-Th dating of the Khvamli petroglyphs is conducted, at least some of them will prove to be older than the 12th–13th centuries AD. Possibly—significantly older.

Test: Conduct dating.

Result: Either the prediction is confirmed—and the KHT gains a foundation. Or all images turn out to be medieval—and the KHT, regarding the petroglyphs, is closed. Both results are valuable: one opens new avenues, the other closes a false path.

---

## Prediction Two: Contents of the Archive

The KHT predicts: In Fund No. 1738, there is documentation of the petroglyphs from 1949—tracings, photographs, or descriptions.

Test: Open the archive, study its contents.

Result: Either what we assume is there—and this changes everything. Or the archive turns out to be incomplete—but then we at least know what exactly has been lost.

---

## Prediction Three: Genetic Connections

The KHT predicts: aDNA from Bronze Age burials in the Khvamli area will show genetic connections with Near Eastern populations of the corresponding period—signs of real contacts, not isolation.

Test: Conduct expanded aDNA research including earlier periods.

Result: The data will either support the contact model or refute it. Both outcomes represent progress in understanding.

---

## Prediction Four: Structural Systematics of the Petroglyphs

The KHT predicts: Systematic documentation of the Khvamli petroglyphs will reveal stable, recurring symbols that have a structural (non-random) similarity to proto-writing systems of the Near East.

Test: Conduct RTI photography and photogrammetry; systematically describe all discovered symbols; apply comparative semiotic analysis.

Result: Either the similarity proves to be statistically significant and systematic—an argument in favor of the KHT. Or the symbols turn out to be random or exclusively local—an argument against.

---

## What Makes a Theory Scientific

A theory is scientific not because it is beautiful. And not because it is bold. But because it is falsifiable: it has specific conditions under which it would be refuted.

The KHT is falsifiable.

If dating shows: all petroglyphs are medieval. If the archive contains only administrative documents without scientific documentation. If aDNA shows complete genetic isolation of the Khvamli area. If systematic analysis of symbols reveals nothing but random similarities.

With all these results—the KHT is refuted. Honestly. Cleanly. By the rules of science.

This is not a weakness of the theory. It is its strength. A theory that cannot be refuted is not a theory. It is a belief.

---
## Research Program: Five Years

A realistic five-year program.

**Year One**: archival work (Fund No. 1738), bibliographic review, preparation of grant applications.

**Year Two**: pilot expedition — photogrammetry and RTI of petroglyphs. Initial selection of samples for dating.

**Year Three**: dating of samples in laboratories. Expanded aDNA research of the Gokhnari necropolis.

**Year Four**: data processing, comparative analysis, preparation of publications.

**Year Five**: publications in peer-reviewed journals. Conferences. International presentation of results.

Five years is a standard cycle for a research project of this scale. During this cycle, the KHT (Khvamli Hypothesis of Transfer) will either be verified, significantly refined, or refuted.

In any case — we will know more than we do now.

---

## The Researcher's Testament

I am writing this book not as a person who has all the answers.

I am writing it as a physician and researcher who posed a question and is convinced that this question deserves an answer. Deserves it enough to spend years formulating it and more years — searching for the tools to answer it.

The Khvamli Hypothesis of Transfer is an invitation. An invitation to look at one specific piece of a mountain in western Georgia and ask: what if something important happened here?

If after this book at least one researcher — just one — decides that this question is worth testing, I have done my job.

---

*End of Part VII. Next part: Appendices and Epilogue.*

---

# Chapter 68. Appendix A: Chronology

> A detailed timeline from geological processes to the present day.

---

## Geological Time

**~5–10 million years ago**: uplift of the Greater Caucasus Range due to the collision of the Arabian and Eurasian plates. Formation of the limestone massif of the future Mount Khvamli.

**~2–3 million years ago**: intense karstification of limestone. Formation of cave systems — prototypes of Tekenteli and Boga.

**~12,000 years ago**: end of the Last Glacial Maximum. Climate warming; rise in the water level of the Rioni River. The valley around Khvamli was periodically flooded, the mountain stood as an island in the swollen waters.

---

## Prehistoric Period

**8th–6th millennium BCE**: Neolithic communities in the Caucasus and Western Georgia. First use of wild cereals; beginning of animal husbandry. Possible beginning of ritual use of Khvamli — unverified.

**~6000–5500 BCE**: early evidence of viticulture in Georgia (Shulaveri-Shomutepe culture). The Racha-Lechkhumi region, where Khvamli is located, falls within the range of this culture.

**5th millennium BCE**: beginning of copper metallurgy in the Caucasus. The Satskhenisi mining zone — one of the oldest copper mining sites in Eurasia. The Caucasus becomes a metallurgical center.

**~3500–3200 BCE**: emergence of early writing in Sumer (proto-cuneiform). Concurrently in the Caucasus — possible proto-script practices (hypothesis requiring verification).

**~3000 BCE**: flourishing of the Chalcolithic-Bronze Age cultures of Transcaucasia (Kura-Araxes culture). Caucasus-produced metal spreads across the Near East.

---

## Bronze and Iron Ages

**~2300–2000 BCE**: period of intensive contacts between the Caucasus and Anatolia/Mesopotamia via trade routes. Caucasian metal found in Near Eastern artifacts.

**~1200 BCE**: "Bronze Age Collapse" — collapse of many Near Eastern civilizations. The Caucasus remains relatively stable; iron metallurgy in the Caucasus is documented by this time.

**~1200–800 BCE**: the state of Diauehi in Eastern Anatolia — the first political entity associated with Georgian ancestors. Territorial conflicts with Urartu.

**8th–6th centuries BCE**: Greek colonization of the Black Sea coast. Colchis in Greek sources: Apollonius of Rhodes mentions the land of Aeëtes, son of Helios. First mentions of the Golden Fleece.

---

## Antiquity and Middle Ages

**~500–300 BCE**: Kingdom of Colchis at its peak. Export of metals, flax, timber. Contacts with the Greek world via the Pontus Euxinus.

**~65 BCE**: Pompey passes through Colchis during his eastern military campaign. Brief description of the region in Roman sources.

**1st–4th centuries CE**: spread of Christianity in Iberia (Kartli) — 337 CE, official date.

**5th century CE**: creation of the Georgian alphabet (traditional date attributed to Mesrop Mashtots or King Pharnavaz). Beginning of the Georgian written tradition.

**9th–10th centuries**: unification of Georgian kingdoms. Flourishing of Georgian statehood.

**14th century**: first documented mention of Khvamli as a repository for royal regalia in *Kartlis Tskhovreba*.

**12th–13th centuries**: creation of the red rider images on Khvamli — verified date based on expeditions of 1939 and 1945.

---

## Modern Era

**1718**: Vakhushti Bagrationi compiles maps of Georgia with a description of the "Star of Khomli" — astral identification of the mountain.

**1836–1927**: active recording of Georgian folklore in the Russian press. The motif of Amiran and Khvamli appears in the records of several expeditionary ethnographers.

**1939**: first recorded scientific expedition to Khvamli, which sketched the red images.

**1945**: follow-up expedition. Confirmation of the medieval dating of the red drawings.

**1948, October 4**: order to equip a secret expedition to Khvamli under the leadership of Aliosha Japaridze.

**1948–1949**: Japaridze expedition. Results classified; stored in Fund No. 1738, Central State Archive of Georgia.

---

## Contemporary Period

**2011**: expedition by the organization "Green Alternative". Recording of oral testimonies from local residents about Soviet-era operations on the mountain.

**2019**: publication by Tsintsadze and colleagues with genetic data from the Gokhnari necropolis.

**2020**: Decree of the Government of Georgia No. 123 — granting Khvamli the status of "Monument of National Significance".
**2021**: Discovery of the Bashplemi Tablet at Lake Bashplemi (Dmanisi region).

**2024**: Publication of the description of the Bashplemi Tablet in the journal JAHA. 60 characters, 39 unique; possible connection to Proto-Kartvelian script.

**Present time**: The Khvamli petroglyphs have not been dated by modern methods. Archive Fund No. 1738 is partially classified. Research awaits.

---

*Next appendix: Glossary of Terms.*

---

# Chapter 69. Appendix B: Glossary of Terms

> Explanation of key concepts used in the book.

---

**AMS** (Accelerator Mass Spectrometry) — a radiocarbon dating method that allows for the analysis of samples of extremely small volume (from 1 mg). Used for dating organic pigments in rock art. Accuracy ranges from several tens to several hundreds of years, depending on the sample's condition.

---

**Axis mundi** (world axis) — in religious studies and cultural anthropology: a vertical axis connecting heaven, earth, and the underworld. Sacred mountains, trees, pillars, towers, and wells can serve as an axis mundi. The ziggurat, the Scandinavian Yggdrasil, the Indian Mount Meru, and Khvamli in the Caucasian context are examples of axis mundi in different traditions.

---

**Haplogroup** — in genetics: a branch of the genealogical tree uniting people who share a common ancestor along one line. Mitochondrial haplogroups are passed down the maternal line; Y-chromosomal haplogroups are passed down the paternal line. Used for tracking population migrations and genetic connections.

---

**Double cuesta** — a geomorphological landform consisting of two asymmetric ridges formed by the selective weathering of inclined rock strata. Khvamli is a classic example of a double cuesta: steep outer slopes create the "walls" of an almost impregnable natural fortress.

---

**Dur-an-ki** (Sumerian: "bond of heaven and earth") — a Sumerian concept of a sacred center, a point where heaven connects with earth. Nippur with its temple Ekur was considered the primary dur-an-ki. The concept is related to the idea of axis mundi and was architecturally realized in the form of the ziggurat.

---

**Ideational diffusion** — in cultural anthropology: the spread of ideas, symbols, and mythological structures without the obligatory physical movement of large groups of people. Ideas can be transmitted through traders, craftsmen, pilgrims, and stories — without mass migrations.

---

**Karstification** — the process of dissolution of soluble rocks (limestone, dolomite, gypsum) by underground and surface waters. The result is caves, sinkholes, collapses, and underground rivers. The cave systems of Khvamli are the result of multi-million-year karstification.

---

**Cuesta** (from Spanish *cuesta* — slope) — a ridge formed by inclined rock strata. One side of a cuesta is gentle (along the dip of the strata), the other is steep. In a double cuesta, both sides are steep — as at Khvamli.

---

**LiDAR** (Light Detection and Ranging) — a remote sensing method using laser light to create a three-dimensional model of the terrain. Allows for the detection of structures hidden under vegetation or soil. Used in archaeology to discover hidden features.

---

**OSL** (Optically Stimulated Luminescence) — a dating method for mineral grains based on the dose of ionizing radiation accumulated since their last exposure to light. Used for dating sediments, buried surfaces, and building materials.

---

**Proto-Kartvelian script** — a hypothetical pictographic writing system, presumed to have existed in the Caucasus in the 4th–3rd millennium BCE. There is a lack of direct, verified sources; the hypothesis is based on the analysis of a series of symbols and possible predecessors of the Georgian alphabet.

---

**Proto-cuneiform** — the earliest stage of cuneiform development, recorded in Uruk around 3300–3000 BCE. It was a pictographic system for recording primarily economic information. Gradually transformed into a full phonetic cuneiform script.

---

**Proto-writing** — a system of signs with stable meaning, used for recording information but not reproducing speech sounds. An intermediate stage between iconic symbols and phonetic writing. Petroglyphs can be a form of proto-writing.

---

**RTI** (Reflectance Transformation Imaging) — a method for documenting surfaces using a series of photographs taken under different lighting angles. Allows for the detection of subtle relief details invisible under standard lighting.

---

**Speleothem** — any mineral formation in a cave resulting from the deposition of minerals from water. Includes stalactites, stalagmites, and calcite crusts. Used for U-Th dating.

---

**Spetskhran** (Special Storage) — the Soviet system of classified collections in libraries and archives, where materials not intended for public access were placed. Paradoxically, the spetskhran often ensured the best preservation of materials precisely due to restricted access.

---

**KHT** (Khvamli Hypothesis of Transfer) — a research hypothesis proposing that Mount Khvamli functioned as a node of ideational connections within a network encompassing the Caucasus, the Near East, and Egypt during the Neolithic–Bronze Age period. It does not claim a direct origin of Near Eastern civilizations from the Caucasus, but suggests active interaction within a shared cultural space.

---

**U-Th dating** (Uranium-Thorium dating) — a radiometric dating method for carbonate deposits (speleothems, corals) based on the ratio of uranium and thorium isotopes. High accuracy (error from 1% and less for young samples); range — from several hundred to several hundred thousand years.

---

**aDNA** (ancient DNA) — DNA extracted from fossil remains — bones, teeth, hair. Allows for the study of the genetics of long-deceased individuals and populations. A revolutionary technology that has changed the understanding of prehistoric migrations.

---

*Next appendix: Maps and Diagrams.*

---

# Chapter 70. Appendix C: Maps and Diagrams

> Visualization of routes, cave locations, and vectors of knowledge transmission.

---

*Note: In the present edition, maps are presented as textual descriptions with coordinates and landmarks. The illustrated edition will contain graphic versions.*

---

## Map 1. Khvamli in the Caucasus System

**General Orientation**

Mount Khvamli is located in western Georgia, in the Racha-Lechkhumi region, approximately at 42°39' N, 43°01' E.

To the west of Khvamli (about 80 km) — the Colchis Lowland and the Black Sea. To the north — the Greater Caucasus Range. To the east — central Georgia and Tbilisi (about 350 km). To the south — the valley of the Rioni River.

**Nearest settlements**: village of Abari (at the foot of the mountain, northwestern slope), village of Gebi.

**Key geographical landmarks within a 100 km radius**: Tskhviri and Nakhar passes; confluence of the Rioni and Tskhenistskali rivers; Kutaisi (70 km to the west).
---

## Map 2. The Four Vectors of KHT

**Vector I: Sumerian (Southeast)**

Route: Khvamli → Kura Valley → Armenian Highlands → Upper Mesopotamia → Lower Mesopotamia (Uruk, Nippur, Ur).

Distance: approximately 1500–2000 km as the crow flies.

Key intermediate points: Tbilisi (320 km), Yerevan (450 km), Lake Van (550 km), Nineveh (900 km), Baghdad (1400 km).

Presumed period of route activity: 5th–3rd millennium BCE. *Note: the range is intentionally broad; the most likely period of active interaction is the 4th millennium BCE (Uruk IV–III period, 3500–2900 BCE).*

**Vector II: Canaanite (Southwest via the Black Sea Region and Anatolia)**

Route: Khvamli → Black Sea → Anatolian coast → Eastern Mediterranean → Levant (Ugarit, Byblos, Tyre, Jerusalem).

Distance: approximately 1500–2000 km.

Presumed period of route activity: 3rd–2nd millennium BCE.

**Vector III: Egyptian (via the Levant)**

Route: Khvamli → (Canaanite Vector) → Levant → Sinai Peninsula → Nile Valley.

Distance: approximately 3000 km.

Presumed period of route activity: 3rd–2nd millennium BCE (mediated by the Canaanite cultural zone).

**Vector IV: Caucasian-Iberian (Local)**

Route: Khvamli → Colchis (western Georgia) → Iberia (eastern Georgia) → Diauehi (Eastern Anatolia).

This vector is local and the most well-documented. It includes a metallurgical network, political formations, and technology exchange within the Caucasian region.

---

## Diagram 1. The Cave Systems of Khvamli

**Tekenteli (Tekanateli) Cave**

Location: northern slope of Khvamli, approximately 1400 m above sea level.

Type: vertical karst shaft with horizontal halls at depths of 30, 60, and more meters.

Features: constant temperature ~10°C, high humidity, underground lake, waterfall at mid-levels.

Scientific significance: ideal conditions for preserving organic materials; possible speleothems over petroglyphs.

**Cave of God**

Location: eastern slope, altitude ~1600 m.

Type: horizontal cave with several halls.

Features: presence of rock carvings (documented in 1939 and 1945); difficult-to-access entrance.

---

## Diagram 2. Location of Petroglyphs

Based on data from the 1939 and 1945 expeditions:

**Zone A (Cave of God, eastern entrance)**: red depictions of riders, abstract signs. Dating: 12th–13th centuries CE.

**Zone B (northern walls, interior grottoes)**: possible earlier depictions (mentioned in non-scholarly sources; not academically verified).

**Zone C (ledge on the southern wall)**: "hunters at a pillar" — described in several sources. Exact location requires field verification.

---

## Diagram 3. Near Eastern Parallels

| Site | Coordinates | Dating | Parallel with Khvamli |
|------|-------------|--------|----------------------|
| Gobustan (Azerbaijan) | 40°04' N, 49°22' E | 7850 ± 30 years | Petroglyphs; AMS dating |
| Çatalhöyük (Turkey) | 37°40' N, 32°49' E | ~7500–5700 BCE | Sacred mountain site; goddess |
| Göbekli Tepe (Turkey) | 37°13' N, 38°55' E | ~9600–8000 BCE | Sacred center on an elevation |
| Uruk (Iraq) | 31°19' N, 45°38' E | ~4000–3000 BCE | Proto-cuneiform; Inanna |
| Umm el-Qa'ab (Egypt) | 26°11' N, 31°55' E | ~3000 BCE | Sacred inaccessibility |

---

*Next appendix: Full text of the 1948 directive.*

---

# Chapter 71. Appendix G: The 1948 Directive

> Publication and analysis of a document from Fund No. 1738 of the Central State Archive of Georgia.

---

## Source Note

The document reproduced in this appendix is held in Fund No. 1738 of the Central State Archive of Georgia (CSAG).

The document was discovered during archival research. The original is in Russian, a typescript with handwritten annotations.

Date: October 4, 1948.

Classification: the original bears a note on limited distribution; the full classification at the time of writing this book is being clarified through a declassification request.

---

## Document Text (reconstruction based on studied fragments)

> ⚠️ **Warning:** What follows is a reconstruction of the document based on partially studied fragments. This is **NOT** an exact copy of the original. Certain formulations may differ from the authentic text. The full verified text of the archival document will be published upon completion of the official declassification procedure for Fund No. 1738 of the Central State Archive of Georgia.

*Note: a reconstruction is published based on partially accessible materials. The full text will be published upon completion of the declassification procedure for Fund No. 1738.*

---

**DIRECTIVE**

**Academy of Sciences of the Georgian SSR**

**October 4, 1948**
**To Comrade Japaridze, Aliosha**

In accordance with the objectives of the scientific research work of the Academy of Sciences of the Georgian SSR, you are instructed to conduct reconnaissance work on Mount Khvamli (Racha district).

Include a group of the best mountaineer-speleologists in the expedition.

During the work, pay special attention to ancient drawings.

Submit the results of the work in the prescribed manner.

---

*[Signature; deciphering and attribution are being clarified]*

---

## Textual Analysis

**Addressee: "To Comrade Japaridze, Aliosha"**

The use of a first name (rather than a surname or title) in an official document of Soviet record-keeping is non-standard. This indicates the personal nature of the assignment: Aliosha Japaridze was chosen not as a faceless executor, but as a specific individual with a specific reputation.

By 1948, Aliosha Japaridze was a well-known Georgian mountaineer who had made a number of first ascents, including the ascent of Ushba in 1934. His selection as the leader of an expedition to a difficult-to-access mountain is professionally justified.

**"A group of the best mountaineer-speleologists"**

The task required dual competence: high-altitude mountaineering (to overcome the vertical walls of Khvamli) and speleology (for work in cave systems). This indicates good preliminary knowledge of the nature of the object.

**"Special attention to ancient drawings"**

A key phrase. The Soviet leadership knew about the petroglyphs before the expedition began. The source of this knowledge was likely reports from the 1939 and 1945 expeditions, which had reached academic structures.

The formulation "special attention" signifies priority: among everything that could be found on the mountain, the drawings were of the greatest importance to the client.

**"Submit the results of the work in the prescribed manner"**

This formulation, combined with data about the resolution "to report personally to Comrade Stalin" (mentioned in other materials from Fund No. 1738), indicates the non-standard nature of the reporting. The standard procedure for submitting academic reports did not involve a personal report to Stalin.

---

## Contextualization

October 1948 — a specific historical moment.

The USSR had completed post-war reconstruction and was actively investing resources in science. Lysenkoism was reaching its peak in biology — but geology, archaeology, and speleology remained relatively free from ideological pressure.

In 1948, Georgia was experiencing a period of cultural nationalism under the cautious patronage of Stalin (himself Georgian by origin). The Georgian Academy of Sciences enjoyed a certain authority.

The choice of 1948 as the moment for this expedition is not accidental. This was a time when Soviet science was sufficiently organized to conduct such work, and sufficiently closed to allow its results to be classified.

---

## What Further Researchers Are Looking For

The full text of the order. A possible resolution about reporting to Stalin. Japaridze's final report. Photographic and graphic materials from the expedition.

All of this is or was stored in Fund No. 1738. Opening this fund is a priority for the next stage of research.

---

*Next and final chapter: Epilogue.*

---

---

# Chapter 73. Appendix D: Table of Facts and Hypotheses

> Methodological apparatus: distinguishing between verified facts, substantiated hypotheses, and scientifically productive assumptions.

---

## 15 Verified Facts

| No. | Fact | Source/Method of Verification |
|---|---|---|
| 1 | Mount Khvamli has the form of a double cuesta with vertical walls 200–400 m high | Geological maps; field research |
| 2 | Petroglyphs — images carved in stone — have been recorded on Mount Khvamli | Expeditions of 1939, 1945, 1948; modern field data |
| 3 | The "Red Figures" in the Tekenteli cave are dated to the 12th–13th centuries AD | Iconographic and stylistic analysis |
| 4 | The cave systems of Khvamli (Tekenteli, "Cave of God") actually exist and are accessible | Speleological expeditions |
| 5 | The 1948 Soviet expedition led by A. Japaridze was conducted under a directive from the Academy of Sciences of the GSSR | Partially studied materials from Fund No. 1738 |
| 6 | Kartvelian languages form an isolated language family without proven relationship to other language families | Comparative-historical linguistics (Gamkrelidze, Klimov) |
| 7 | The Colchian Bronze Age culture (2nd–1st millennium BC) is an independent metallurgical tradition | Archaeological data; metallographic analysis |
| 8 | The motif "hunter by a vertical object" is present in the petroglyphs of Khvamli and in Sumerian glyptics | Visual analysis; publications by Anati, Frankfort |
| 9 | The *axis mundi* (world axis) motif is a universal element of archaic cosmology | Religious studies; Eliade, "Shamanism" 1998 |
| 10 | Shulaveri-Shomutepe (6th–5th millennium BC) is one of the oldest agricultural cultures of the Caucasus | Radiocarbon dating; field data |
| 11 | The Sumerian language contains words for which Kartvelian etymologies are proposed | Linguistic analysis; controversial — see Rubio 1999 |
| 12 | Khvamli appears in Georgian epic as the place of imprisonment of Amiran | Georgian folklore; comparative mythology |
| 13 | AMS, OSL, U-Th methods allow for high-precision dating of organic matter and mineral deposits | Applied to similar sites; not applied to Khvamli |
| 14 | The mountain has received the status of a monument of national significance of Georgia | Official legal act |
| 15 | Transcaucasian trade routes existed in the Bronze Age and connected the Caucasus with Anatolia and Mesopotamia | Archaeological data; imported artifacts |

---

## 15 Working Hypotheses

| No. | Hypothesis | Verification Status |
|---|---|---|
| 1 | Petroglyphs on open rock surfaces date to the 6th–4th millennium BC | Not verified: AMS/OSL has not been applied to them |
| 2 | Khvamli functioned as a sacral center in the Neolithic and Bronze Age | Plausible, not proven |
| 3 | Depictions of the "hunter by a pillar" motif reflect a ritual practice analogous to the Sumerian one | Typologically substantiated; direct connection not proven |
| 4 | Kartvelian vocabulary in Sumerian testifies to contacts, not typological coincidence | Controversial; criticism by Rubio 1999 is substantial |
| 5 | Khvamli is one of the nodes of an ideational network that included the Caucasus, Mesopotamia, and Canaan | Central hypothesis of the book; not verified |
| 6 | Manuscripts or other artifacts are stored in the cave systems of Khvamli | Based on folklore; not confirmed by field data |
| 7 | Soviet expeditions of the 1940s documented more material than was published | Indirect data; Fund No. 1738 is not fully open |
| 8 | The Georgian and Sumerian word "Amateri" share a common root | Typologically possible; methodologically controversial |
| 9 | The name "Khvamli" is semantically connected to heliacal cults (Venus/morning star) | Presumptive; onomastic analysis not completed |
| 10 | The "Tekenteli" cave was used as a ritual space in the prehistoric period | Possible; no stratigraphic data |
| 11 | Colchis was not a "periphery," but an active participant in Near Eastern cultural exchange | Plausible; requires systematic proof |
| 12 | Mythological parallels (Prometheus/Amiran, Dali/Ishtar) reflect real cultural exchange | Possible; convergent explanation also not excluded |
| 13 | The 1948 directive contained an instruction to report to Stalin | Based on unverified information |
| 14 | Genetic data will confirm significant connections between the population of Khvamli and Near Eastern populations | Prediction; research not conducted |
| 15 | LiDAR survey will reveal hidden structures in the vicinity of Khvamli | Prediction; based on analogies with other sites |

---

## 3 Conditions for Verifying the KHT
### Condition 1. Dating of the Petroglyphs

The KHT can be verified or falsified based on chronological criteria:

**Confirmation Criterion:** AMS/OSL/U-Th dating of petroglyphs on open rock surfaces yields an age older than 4000 BCE.

**Refutation Criterion:** All undated petroglyphs prove to be medieval (up to the 12th–13th centuries CE).

### Condition 2. Content of the Archive

Full disclosure of Fund No. 1738 and related materials:

**Confirmation Criterion:** Discovery of systematic documentation from Soviet expeditions with previously unpublished findings.

**Refutation Criterion:** The archive contains only administrative documents without scientific data.

### Condition 3. Analysis of Contact Traces

Genetic, isotopic, or artefactual investigation:

**Confirmation Criterion:** Isotopic or aDNA analysis reveals markers of population mobility between the Khvamli region and Mesopotamia/Anatolia in the 4th–3rd millennium BCE.

**Refutation Criterion:** Genetics and isotope analysis demonstrate complete local population isolation with no signs of long-distance contacts.

# Chapter 72. Epilogue: The Call of the Ancestors

> "The mountains do not call. It is we who hear them."

---

I am Georgian.

This means I belong to a people who carry within themselves — in their genes, language, and the names of mountains and rivers — the memory of millennia lived at the crossroads of civilizations.

When I look at Khvamli — I am not simply looking at a mountain.

---

## Mountain → Cave → Tower → River

This is the sacred matrix. It repeats everywhere people built their world.

The mountain is the vertical. A point from which the horizon is visible. A place one ascends to, not descends from. A place of labor — and therefore a place of meaning.

The cave is the inversion of the vertical. A descent downward. Darkness that preserves. The space before birth and after death. In the cave lie treasures, the dead, secrets.

The tower is the man-made mountain. A person who builds a tower says: "I can create a vertical where there is none." Svan towers, Egyptian obelisks, Sumerian ziggurats — one program, three manifestations.
A river is a horizontal line. Movement, connection, boundary. Along rivers travel merchants, along rivers move armies, along rivers is conveyed everything too heavy to carry across mountains.

Khvamli → Tekenteli → Svan towers → Rioni.

This is not a metaphor. This is the structure of the world that people have built here over millennia.

---

## The Khvamli Transfer: A Source, Not a Periphery

History is written by those who have access to sources. For a long time, the sources were Greek and Latin texts, Egyptian hieroglyphs, Sumerian tablets. In these sources, the Caucasus was a periphery—a distant place where the Argonauts sailed, where Prometheus was chained.

But periphery is a point of view. For the Argonaut, Colchis was "far away." For an inhabitant of Colchis, it was "here."

We are not asking to rewrite history. We are asking to pose the next question: what was happening "here," before the Argonauts sailed in?

The KHT provides a preliminary answer: here was a complex culture, connected to the Near Eastern world through metallurgy, astral theology, mythological imagery, and trade routes. Not a periphery—a node. Not a recipient—a participant.

---

## What the Ancestors Left in Stone

Petroglyphs are letters.

An illiterate Neolithic hunter did not know he was leaving "letters." He carved signs into the rock because it was important. Because something happened in that place that required recording.

Thousands of years later, we stand before these signs and try to read them. We do not know the language. We do not know the context. We do not even know precisely when it was written.

But we know this: someone wanted to tell us something important.

"Here was a person. Here happened something that must not be forgotten. Here the sky met the earth."

---

## The Responsibility of the Living

We bear a responsibility to those who left these signs.

Not because they are "our" ancestors in an ethnic sense. The ancestors of all humanity left petroglyphs—in Africa, Australia, Asia, Europe. We all bear this responsibility.

But each people has its own specific place where this obligation becomes personal.

For Georgians—it is Khvamli.

The mountain guarded Amiran. The mountain guarded the regalia. The mountain guarded the "books." The mountain guarded the signs we still cannot read.

Now it is our turn to guard the mountain.

To guard means to research. To document. To open archives. To date. To publish. To protect from destruction.

Not in order to "prove" a theory. But in order to learn the truth.

---

## The Call

I do not literally hear the voice of the ancestors. I am not a mystic.

But I hear something else: the logic of history, which says—here is unfinished business. Here is a question awaiting an answer. Here is a blank spot on the map of science that living researchers can and must fill in.

This is not the call of the ancestors. This is the call of incompleteness.

Incompleteness demands completion. A question demands an answer. A blank spot demands an expedition.

---

## The Last Word

Khvamli stands.

It has stood for several million years. It has survived ice ages, invasions, empires, revolutions. It has survived Soviet secrecy and post-Soviet oblivion.

Its caves hold what lies within them: petroglyphs, possibly manuscripts, certainly—questions.

Its name still means something we cannot precisely translate. Perhaps—"shining." Perhaps—"beacon." Perhaps—something for which our language has no word.

In a world where most mysteries have been solved, Khvamli remains untouched.

This is not a shortcoming. This is an invitation.

The mountain calls.

---

*End of the book*

---

**«Kartvely: The Mountain Fortress of the Caucasus—A Hypothesis on the Origins of Caucasus Connections with the Civilizations of Sumer, Canaan, and Egypt»**

*Author: Jaba Tkemaladze*

*Tbilisi — 2024–2026*
